Pharmacology

Pharmacology

Complete edition · all 14 chapters · by Dr. Vidya Aggrawal · Nightingale College
Pharmacology · Chapter 1

Pharmacological Principles & Herbal Supplements

Every drug you will ever give is a bargain. You accept a wanted effect and you accept, in the same breath, the unwanted ones that ride along with it. Pharmacology is the study of that bargain — what the body does to a drug, what the drug does to the body, and how a nurse tips the odds toward benefit and away from harm. This chapter builds the vocabulary and the reasoning you will use for every medication in the rest of the book: how drugs are named and grouped, how they move through a patient, how they act once they arrive, how to give them safely, and why the bottle of ginkgo in your patient's purse belongs on the medication list too.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Distinguish a drug's chemical, generic, and trade names, and explain why nurses work in generic names.
  • Define a drug class and a prototype drug, and use a prototype to predict a whole class's effects.
  • Describe the four pharmacokinetic processes — absorption, distribution, metabolism, excretion — and the first-pass effect.
  • Use half-life to reason about dosing intervals, steady state, and trough levels.
  • Explain pharmacodynamics: receptors, agonists and antagonists, dose–response, potency versus efficacy.
  • Interpret the therapeutic index and name the common narrow-therapeutic-index drugs.
  • Apply the rights of medication administration, match a drug to an appropriate route, and name concrete error-prevention practices.
  • Identify common herbal supplements and their clinically important drug interactions.

Section 1Drugs, names & classes

One drug, three names

A drug is any chemical that changes the way the body works. Every approved drug carries three names, and confusing them is one of the quiet sources of medication error. The chemical name describes the molecule — N-acetyl-para-aminophenol — and you will essentially never use it. The generic name (also called the nonproprietary or United States Adopted Name) is the drug's one true name: acetaminophen. It belongs to no company, it never changes, and it is what the order, the chart, and the reference book use. The trade name (brand or proprietary name) is the marketing name the manufacturer owns — Tylenol, and a dozen others.

Here is why this matters at the bedside. One generic drug can wear many trade names, and a patient arriving from home may know only the brand. A patient taking Tylenol, Percocet, and a nighttime cold remedy is taking acetaminophen three times and may sail past the maximum daily dose without ever recognizing the drug. Nurses work in generic names precisely because the generic name is the only name that cannot hide a duplicate.

Generic names also carry built-in clues. Most classes share a stem — a fragment of the name that tells you what the drug is before you look it up. If it ends in -olol it is a beta blocker; -pril, an ACE inhibitor; -statin, an HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor; -prazole, a proton pump inhibitor; -cillin, a penicillin; -sartan, an angiotensin II receptor blocker. Learning stems is one of the highest-yield hours you can spend in this book.

Classes: sorting drugs by what they do

A drug class (or pharmacologic class) is a group of drugs that share a mechanism of action, and therefore share effects, adverse effects, and nursing implications. This is the whole reason pharmacology is learnable. There are thousands of drugs and only a few dozen mechanisms that matter. You are not memorizing a list; you are learning a small number of stories and then recognizing which story a new drug belongs to.

Drugs can be grouped two ways, and both appear in practice. A therapeutic classification says what the drug is used for — antihypertensive, anticoagulant, analgesic. A pharmacologic classification says how it works — calcium channel blocker, vitamin K antagonist, opioid receptor agonist. One drug lives in both systems at once: metoprolol is therapeutically an antihypertensive and antianginal, and pharmacologically a beta1-selective adrenergic blocker.

Prototype drugs

Because a class shares a mechanism, each class has a prototype — the well-studied, representative member you learn cold, then use as the template for every other drug in the family. Learn morphine and you have most of hydromorphone, fentanyl, and oxycodone. Learn furosemide and bumetanide and torsemide hold few surprises. When you meet an unfamiliar drug, your first question is not "what does this do?" but "what class is this, and what is the prototype?"

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Beta blockers (-olol)metoprololBlocks beta1 receptors → lower heart rate and blood pressure, less cardiac workloadHold and notify for HR under 60 or SBP under 90; never stop abruptly (rebound tachycardia, angina, MI); may blunt hypoglycemia warning signs
ACE inhibitors (-pril)lisinoprilBlocks formation of angiotensin II → vasodilation, lower BP, renal protectionDry hacking cough, hyperkalemia, first-dose hypotension; angioedema is an emergency; contraindicated in pregnancy
Loop diureticsfurosemideBlocks Na-K-2Cl reabsorption in the loop of Henle → brisk diuresisWatch hypokalemia, hypotension, dehydration; push IV slowly (ototoxicity); daily weight is the best measure of fluid loss
Opioid agonistsmorphineMu-receptor agonist → analgesia, sedation, euphoriaRespiratory depression is the killer — monitor rate and sedation; constipation never resolves, treat it; antidote is naloxone
Benzodiazepines (-azepam, -azolam)lorazepamEnhances GABAA → sedation, anxiolysis, seizure controlAdditive CNS and respiratory depression with opioids and alcohol; fall risk in older adults; antidote is flumazenil (rarely used — it can precipitate seizures)
AnticoagulantswarfarinInhibits vitamin K epoxide reductase → less synthesis of factors II, VII, IX, X → less clottingMonitor INR (usual target 2–3); antidote is vitamin K (phytonadione); teach consistent — not zero — leafy-green intake; enormous interaction list. Contrast heparin: acts immediately by potentiating antithrombin, monitored by aPTT (1.5–2.5 times control), reversed by protamine sulfate; watch for HIT (heparin-induced thrombocytopenia)
Statins (-statin)atorvastatinInhibits HMG-CoA reductase → lower LDL cholesterolReport unexplained muscle pain or weakness (myopathy, rhabdomyolysis); avoid grapefruit juice with several agents; monitor liver enzymes
Proton pump inhibitors (-prazole)omeprazoleIrreversibly blocks the gastric H+/K+-ATPase → much less acidGive before the first meal of the day; long-term use is linked to low magnesium and B12, fractures, and C. difficile; deprescribe when the indication ends
Count the drug, not the box. Before you give anything, translate every product on the patient's list into its generic name. Duplicate therapy hides behind brand names and combination products — acetaminophen inside an opioid combination, an NSAID inside a cold remedy, aspirin inside an antacid tablet. The generic name is the only place a duplicate cannot hide.
Interactive Tap a drug-name stem — -olol, -pril, -statin, -azole, -cillin — to split a generic name apart and reveal its class, mechanism, and nursing priorities, then test yourself in Match mode. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Every drug has a chemical, a generic, and one or more trade names; nurses work in generic names.
  • Generic stems (-olol, -pril, -statin, -prazole, -sartan) identify the class on sight.
  • A drug class shares a mechanism, so it shares effects, adverse effects, and nursing implications.
  • Learn the prototype and you have the family; classify an unfamiliar drug before you try to memorize it.

Section 2Pharmacokinetics — what the body does to the drug

The four-step journey

Pharmacokinetics is the study of drug movement: how a drug gets in, where it goes, how it is chemically changed, and how it leaves. Four processes, remembered as ADME, run at once from the moment of administration: Absorption, Distribution, Metabolism, Excretion. Everything that determines how much drug reaches its target and for how long lives in these four steps — which is why the same 5 mg tablet behaves very differently in a young athlete and in an 88-year-old with heart failure and kidney disease.

Absorption is the movement of drug from the site of administration into the bloodstream. It depends on the route (IV is instant and complete; oral is slow and partial), on blood flow to the site (a shocked patient absorbs almost nothing from an intramuscular injection), on the drug's ability to cross membranes (lipid-soluble and un-ionized crosses easily), and on the presence of food. The fraction of a dose that actually reaches the systemic circulation intact is its bioavailability. By definition an IV dose is 100% bioavailable; an oral dose is always less.

Distribution is the drug's travel from blood into tissue. Blood flow decides who gets it first — heart, brain, liver, and kidney are perfused richly; fat and resting muscle far less. Two barriers matter clinically: the blood–brain barrier, which only lipid-soluble drugs cross easily (a reason some antihistamines sedate and others do not), and the placenta, which stops almost nothing. Distribution is also governed by protein binding: many drugs ride on albumin, and only the unbound (free) fraction is active. A patient with low albumin — malnourished, cirrhotic, elderly — has more free drug from the same dose and is at risk for toxicity at a "normal" dose.

Metabolism and the first-pass effect

Metabolism (biotransformation) chemically alters a drug, usually in the liver, usually via the cytochrome P450 (CYP450) enzyme family. Most of the time metabolism inactivates the drug and makes it water-soluble enough to be excreted. Sometimes it does the opposite: a prodrug is given inactive and must be metabolized to work — codeine must be converted to morphine by CYP2D6, and a patient who is a poor metabolizer gets little relief while an ultrarapid metabolizer can be dangerously overdosed.

The CYP450 system is where most drug–drug interactions live. An enzyme inducer (rifampin, phenytoin, carbamazepine, St. John's wort) speeds metabolism, so partner drugs fall to subtherapeutic levels — the classic disaster is an oral contraceptive or warfarin failing. An enzyme inhibitor (many azole antifungals, clarithromycin, amiodarone, grapefruit juice) slows metabolism, so partner drug levels climb toward toxicity.

The first-pass effect is the reason a route change is never a straight swap. Anything absorbed from the stomach or intestine drains into the portal vein and is carried through the liver before it reaches the rest of the body. The liver may destroy most of the dose on that first trip. Oral morphine is the textbook case: roughly three times the IV dose is needed by mouth for the same effect. Routes that bypass the portal circulation — IV, sublingual, transdermal, rectal (partially), inhaled — escape the first pass, which is exactly why nitroglycerin is placed under the tongue and not swallowed.

Excretion, half-life, and steady state

Excretion removes the drug, overwhelmingly through the kidneys. Reduced renal function — from age, dehydration, or kidney disease — means drug accumulates, so renally cleared drugs are dose-adjusted to creatinine clearance or eGFR. Smaller routes matter for specific drugs: bile and feces (some antibiotics), lungs (inhaled anesthetics), and breast milk, which is trivial in quantity but never trivial for the nursing infant.

The single most useful number in kinetics is the half-life (t½): the time for plasma concentration to fall by half. Half-life sets the dosing interval, and it drives two rules worth memorizing. It takes about four to five half-lives to reach steady state — the plateau where the amount going in equals the amount coming out, and where the drug is finally doing its full job. It also takes about four to five half-lives to clear a drug after the last dose. That is why a loading dose exists: when a patient cannot wait days for steady state, a large first dose fills the tank immediately, and smaller maintenance doses keep it there.

StepWhat happensWhat slows or changes itWhat the nurse does
AbsorptionDrug enters the bloodstream from the administration siteRoute, blood flow to the site, food, gastric pH, lipid solubilityGive on an empty stomach or with food as ordered; recognize that a hypotensive patient absorbs IM and SubQ drugs unreliably
DistributionDrug travels from blood into tissuePerfusion, protein binding, blood–brain barrier, placenta, body fatSuspect toxicity at normal doses when albumin is low; assume the drug crosses the placenta and into breast milk
MetabolismLiver enzymes (CYP450) alter the drug; prodrugs are activatedLiver disease, age (neonates and elders), inducers and inhibitors, geneticsScreen for CYP interactions; watch LFTs; know which drugs are prodrugs
ExcretionDrug leaves, mostly in urineRenal function, urine pH, competition for tubular transportCheck creatinine and eGFR before renally cleared drugs; monitor intake and output
Draw the trough right before the next dose. A trough is the lowest concentration in a dosing interval — drawn 30 minutes before the next dose — and it is the level that tells you whether the drug is accumulating toward toxicity. Draw it early and you will report a falsely high level; draw it after the dose is hung and you have measured nothing useful at all. For vancomycin and aminoglycosides, the trough is the safety check.
Interactive Follow one dose through absorption, distribution, metabolism, and excretion — switch between oral, sublingual, and IV routes to see why the first-pass effect makes the same drug need three different doses. Open full ↑
Key points
  • ADME — absorption, distribution, metabolism, excretion — is what the body does to the drug.
  • Bioavailability is the fraction reaching circulation intact; IV is 100%, oral is always less.
  • Only the free (unbound) drug is active — low albumin means more free drug and more toxicity risk.
  • The first-pass effect destroys much of an oral dose in the liver; sublingual, transdermal, and IV routes bypass it.
  • It takes 4–5 half-lives to reach steady state and 4–5 to clear; a loading dose shortcuts the wait.

Section 3Pharmacodynamics — what the drug does to the body

Receptors: the lock and the key

Pharmacodynamics is the other half of the story: once the drug arrives, what does it actually do? Almost always, it binds something. Most drugs act on a receptor — a protein, usually on a cell membrane, that the body built for its own signaling molecule. Drugs do not invent new functions. They borrow existing ones, turning the body's own switches up or down. Albuterol works because your lungs already have beta2 receptors waiting for epinephrine; morphine works because your nervous system already has mu receptors waiting for endorphins.

Two properties describe the fit. Affinity is how tightly a drug binds its receptor. Intrinsic activity is whether binding actually triggers a response. That pair generates the entire vocabulary below — and it explains selectivity, too. A drug is called selective when it prefers one receptor subtype, but selectivity is dose-dependent and never absolute: metoprolol is beta1-selective at ordinary doses, which is why it is the safer choice if a patient with asthma must take a beta blocker at all, but at higher doses it spills onto the beta2 receptors in the airway and can trigger bronchospasm anyway. Nonselective agents such as propranolol block beta2 from the first dose and are avoided in reactive airway disease.

Agonists, antagonists, and everything between

An agonist binds and activates — it mimics the body's own messenger and turns the response on. An antagonist binds and blocks — it has affinity but no intrinsic activity, so it occupies the receptor and prevents the natural messenger (or another drug) from acting. Antagonists have no effect of their own; their entire effect is the absence of someone else's. A partial agonist binds and activates only weakly, producing a submaximal response no matter how high the dose — a ceiling that can be a safety feature.

TypeWhat it does at the receptorExampleWhat it means at the bedside
AgonistBinds and activates; mimics the natural ligandalbuterol (beta2), morphine (mu)Expect the natural signal, amplified — albuterol gives bronchodilation and the tremor and tachycardia that come with it
AntagonistBinds and blocks; affinity, no intrinsic activitynaloxone (mu), metoprolol (beta1)The classic antidote mechanism; naloxone evicts opioid from the receptor and reverses respiratory depression — but it is shorter-acting than most opioids, so re-sedation is the danger and the patient must be watched
Partial agonistBinds, activates weakly; has a ceilingbuprenorphine (mu)Less respiratory depression at high doses, but it will displace a full agonist and can precipitate withdrawal
Non-receptor actionWorks by physical or chemical means, no receptorantacids, osmotic laxatives, mannitol, chelatorsDo not look for a receptor story — the mechanism is chemistry or osmosis

Dose–response, potency, efficacy, and the therapeutic index

Plot dose against effect and you get the dose–response curve: little happens at the bottom (below threshold), the response rises steeply through the useful middle, then flattens into a plateau where the receptors are saturated and more drug buys no more effect — only more toxicity. That plateau is why "the pain is still there, give more" eventually stops being pharmacology and starts being harm.

Two words students routinely swap. Potency is how much drug it takes to produce a given effect — a more potent drug works at a smaller dose. Efficacy is the maximum effect the drug can produce, no matter the dose. Efficacy is what you want; potency is only a milligram number. Hydromorphone is more potent than morphine (fewer milligrams needed) but not more efficacious. Meanwhile ibuprofen has a hard analgesic ceiling that no dose can escape: for severe pain, an opioid's greater efficacy is the point.

The therapeutic index (TI) compares the dose that poisons to the dose that helps — TD50/ED50, the dose toxic to half the population divided by the dose effective in half of it. A high TI means a wide, forgiving margin. A low (narrow) therapeutic index means the effective dose and the toxic dose are uncomfortably close, and these are the drugs that get serum levels drawn, get double-checked, and land patients in the ED. Learn the list: warfarin, digoxin, lithium, phenytoin, levothyroxine, theophylline, carbamazepine, vancomycin, aminoglycosides (gentamicin), and insulin. The therapeutic range is the concentration window between "not working" and "poisoning" — and for these drugs, that window is a canyon you can fall out of in either direction.

Digoxin is the narrow-index drug that teaches the lesson. Therapeutic range is roughly 0.5–2 ng/mL, and toxicity looks like anorexia, nausea, visual halos or yellow-green vision, confusion, and bradycardia or new dysrhythmias. Now add the trap: hypokalemia potentiates digoxin toxicity, and hypokalemia is exactly what furosemide causes — and the two are prescribed together constantly. Check the apical pulse for a full minute, hold for a rate under 60, and read the potassium as part of the digoxin assessment.
Interactive Drag the dose to climb a dose–response curve while morphine, buprenorphine and naloxone compete at the mu-opioid receptor — then switch to the therapeutic index and see why digoxin, lithium, warfarin and phenytoin demand levels, exact dosing and constant assessment. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Drugs act by binding receptors and turning the body's own signals up or down; affinity is the fit, intrinsic activity is whether it fires.
  • Agonist activates, antagonist blocks (the antidote mechanism), partial agonist activates weakly with a ceiling.
  • Potency = how many milligrams; efficacy = the maximum effect possible. Efficacy is what the patient feels.
  • A narrow therapeutic index drug — warfarin, digoxin, lithium, phenytoin, insulin — needs levels, monitoring, and double-checks.

Section 4Safe medication administration

The rights of medication administration

Everything above is theory until you are standing at a bedside with a cup in your hand. The rights of medication administration are the checklist that turns knowledge into safety. The traditional five are right patient, right drug, right dose, right route, right time, and most programs and employers add several more: right documentation, right reason (indication), right response, right to refuse, and right education. Verify them at three points — when you pull the medication, when you prepare it, and again at the bedside before it goes in.

Two of these deserve emphasis because they are the ones most often skipped. Right patient means two identifiers — name and date of birth against the armband, never the room number, and never the patient's nod when you say the wrong name aloud. Right response means the job is not finished when the drug is given: you gave metoprolol, so you recheck the heart rate; you gave morphine, so you reassess pain and respiratory rate and sedation; you gave insulin, so you know when the glucose will bottom out. Documentation comes after administration, never before — charting a drug you have not yet given is how a dose gets given twice.

Routes

The route decides speed, completeness, and reversibility. The single most important safety principle in the table below is this: once it is in, you cannot take it back, and the faster the route, the more absolutely that is true. An oral overdose can sometimes be intercepted; an IV push cannot.

RouteOnsetFirst pass?Nursing notes
Oral (PO)Slow (30–60 min)YesSafest and cheapest; needs a swallow and a working gut. Never crush extended-release or enteric-coated tablets — crushing an ER product delivers the whole day's dose at once
Sublingual / buccalFast (1–5 min)NoNothing to swallow, nothing to drink; nitroglycerin is the prototype — a tingle suggests the tablet is still potent. Absolutely contraindicated with a PDE-5 inhibitor (sildenafil, tadalafil, vardenafil) — both drugs vasodilate and the combination causes catastrophic hypotension; ask before you give
SubcutaneousSlow, sustainedNoSmall volumes (usually 1 mL or less); insulin and heparin; rotate sites; do not massage after heparin (bruising)
IntramuscularModerateNoLarger volumes; ventrogluteal is the safest adult site; absorption fails in shock or poor perfusion
IntravenousImmediateNo100% bioavailable and irreversible — know the push rate, the compatibility, and the antidote before you start; watch for infiltration and extravasation
TransdermalSlow, steadyNoRemove the old patch before applying the new one (stacked fentanyl patches have killed patients); date and initial; wear gloves; rotate sites
InhaledFast, localNoLocal effect with small systemic dose; if both are ordered, bronchodilator before corticosteroid; rinse the mouth after inhaled steroids to prevent thrush
RectalVariablePartialUseful when the patient is vomiting or unconscious; absorption is unpredictable

Orders and error prevention

A complete medication order names the patient, the date and time, the drug, the dose, the route, the frequency, and the prescriber's signature. If any element is missing, ambiguous, or simply does not make sense for this patient, the order is not valid and you do not give it — you clarify it. "The prescriber wrote it" has never been a defense; the nurse who administers is independently accountable for the drug.

High-alert medications are those that cause disproportionate harm when they go wrong: insulin, anticoagulants (heparin, warfarin), opioids, concentrated electrolytes (especially potassium chloride), chemotherapy, neuromuscular blockers, and sedatives. They earn an independent double-check — a second nurse who verifies the drug, dose, pump settings, and patient without being told what you already calculated. And a permanent rule: concentrated potassium chloride is never given IV push, ever. It must be diluted and infused.

Then there is how the numbers are written. Use a leading zero (0.5 mg, not .5 mg — a missed decimal point becomes a tenfold overdose) and never a trailing zero (5 mg, not 5.0 mg — which reads as 50). Spell out "units" rather than writing "U," which is misread as a zero. Avoid the abbreviations on your facility's do-not-use list. And know your look-alike/sound-alike (LASA) pairs: hydralazine and hydroxyzine, celecoxib and citalopram, metformin and metronidazole, lamotrigine and lamivudine, and — the pair that reaches the ICU — morphine and hydromorphone. Read the label three times — pulling it, preparing it, and returning or discarding it.

Most errors are interruption errors. The research is consistent and unglamorous: the more times a nurse is interrupted while preparing medications, the more errors occur. That is the entire reason for "do not disturb" vests, quiet zones, and no-interruption med passes. Protecting your own attention during a med pass is not rudeness — it is a clinical intervention with an evidence base.
Interactive Verify six live medication orders against the ten rights — armband, order, and label side by side — and find the one broken right hiding in each. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The rights — patient, drug, dose, route, time, plus documentation, reason, response, refusal, education — are checked three times, and right patient means two identifiers.
  • Route determines onset and reversibility; IV is immediate and cannot be undone; never crush ER or enteric-coated tablets.
  • High-alert drugs (insulin, anticoagulants, opioids, concentrated KCl, chemo) need independent double-checks; KCl is never IV push.
  • Use a leading zero, never a trailing zero; spell out "units"; know your LASA pairs.
  • An unclear or incomplete order is clarified, not given — the administering nurse is independently accountable.

Section 5Herbal supplements & interactions

Supplements are drugs, whatever the label says

Roughly one in five adults takes an herbal product, and the majority never mention it to a clinician — because in their minds a supplement is food, not medicine. That belief is the hazard. A dietary supplement in the United States is regulated under the Dietary Supplement Health and Education Act (DSHEA) of 1994 as a food, not a drug. It requires no pre-market proof of safety or efficacy, and the FDA can act only after harm appears. Nobody verified the potency, the purity, or the contents of the bottle in your patient's bag.

Meanwhile, the pharmacology is entirely real. Digoxin comes from foxglove, aspirin from willow bark, morphine from the poppy, atropine from belladonna, and paclitaxel from the yew tree. Plants are chemists. "Natural" has never meant safe — hemlock is natural — and a compound potent enough to help is by definition potent enough to interact.

The interactions that actually matter

Herbal interactions cluster into three predictable mechanisms, which means you can reason instead of memorize. First, CYP450 and P-glycoprotein effects: St. John's wort is a powerful inducer and can wreck a drug regimen by driving levels down. Second, bleeding risk: the products with antiplatelet activity stack on top of anticoagulants, antiplatelets, and NSAIDs — the memorable cluster is the four G's plus feverfew (garlic, ginger, ginkgo, ginseng, feverfew). Third, additive pharmacology: kava and valerian pile onto CNS depressants; ginseng pushes glucose down on top of antidiabetics.

SupplementCommon claimed useMechanism / concernNursing alert
St. John's wortDepression, moodPotent CYP3A4 and P-glycoprotein inducer; serotonergic activityThe most dangerous common supplement. Lowers levels of warfarin, oral contraceptives, cyclosporine, HIV protease inhibitors, digoxin, anticonvulsants — transplant rejection and unintended pregnancy are documented. With an SSRI or triptan, risk of serotonin syndrome
Ginkgo bilobaMemory, circulationInhibits platelet-activating factor → antiplateletBleeding with warfarin, aspirin, clopidogrel, NSAIDs; lowers seizure threshold; typically stop 1–2 weeks before surgery
GarlicCholesterol, blood pressureAntiplatelet; can reduce saquinavir levelsAdditive bleeding risk; stop before surgery; may add to antihypertensives
GingerNausea, motion sicknessAntiplatelet at higher dosesReasonably safe in culinary amounts; bleeding risk with anticoagulants at supplement doses
GinsengEnergy, "immune support"Antiplatelet; lowers blood glucose; may reduce INRHypoglycemia with insulin or sulfonylureas; unpredictable warfarin effect; stimulant — insomnia, hypertension; interacts with MAOIs
FeverfewMigraine preventionAntiplateletBleeding risk; abrupt discontinuation causes rebound headache
KavaAnxiety, insomniaHepatotoxicity; CNS depressionLinked to severe liver injury and transplant; additive with benzodiazepines, alcohol, opioids; avoid with any hepatotoxic drug
ValerianInsomniaCNS depression (GABAergic)Additive sedation with benzodiazepines, opioids, alcohol, antihistamines; fall risk in older adults
EchinaceaColds, "immune support"Immune stimulation; possible hepatotoxicityAvoid in immunosuppression, transplant, autoimmune disease, and HIV; may oppose immunosuppressants
Black cohoshMenopausal symptomsHepatotoxicity reportsMonitor liver enzymes; avoid with other hepatotoxic drugs; caution in hormone-sensitive cancer
Saw palmettoBenign prostatic hyperplasiaMild antiplatelet effect; hormonal activityBleeding risk; may lower PSA and mask prostate cancer screening — the prescriber must know the patient takes it
Milk thistleLiver supportGenerally well tolerated; modest CYP effectsAmong the safer products; still verify against the patient's regimen, and it is not a treatment for liver disease
Grapefruit juice (not an herb, same problem)FoodIntestinal CYP3A4 inhibitorRaises levels of simvastatin, atorvastatin, amiodarone, many calcium channel blockers, some immunosuppressants; a single glass can matter and the effect lasts about 24 hours

How to ask

The interview technique changes the answer. "Are you taking any medications?" reliably gets a "no" from a patient with three supplement bottles at home, because they do not classify them as medications. Ask instead: "What do you take that you buy without a prescription — vitamins, herbs, teas, powders, protein shakes, anything from the health-food aisle, anything a family member recommends?" Then ask them to bring in the actual bottles. Document each one on the medication list. And teach without judgment: the goal is not to talk the patient out of a supplement, it is to keep them from a bleed, a rejected transplant, or a serotonin crisis they never saw coming. Two teaching points earn their place in nearly every discharge: tell every prescriber about every supplement, and stop most herbal products one to two weeks before surgery.

The purse check is a nursing intervention. The medication reconciliation that catches a supplement is worth more than the one that perfectly lists eight prescriptions and misses the St. John's wort quietly driving the transplant patient's cyclosporine to zero. Ask about the bottles nobody wrote a prescription for — that is where the interaction is hiding.
Interactive Tap a common herbal supplement to build its interaction web, then tap any drug on it to see why the collision happens and what the nurse does about it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Supplements are regulated as food, not drugs — no pre-market proof of safety, efficacy, potency, or purity.
  • St. John's wort is the highest-stakes common supplement: a CYP3A4/P-gp inducer that drives warfarin, contraceptives, cyclosporine, and antiretrovirals to failure, plus serotonin syndrome risk.
  • The bleeding cluster — garlic, ginger, ginkgo, ginseng, feverfew — stacks with anticoagulants, antiplatelets, and NSAIDs; stop 1–2 weeks before surgery.
  • Kava and black cohosh are hepatotoxic; valerian and kava add to CNS depressants; echinacea is a problem in immunosuppression.
  • Ask specifically about non-prescription products and put every one on the medication list.

The chapter in one breath

  • Every drug has a chemical, generic, and trade name; nurses work in generic names, and stems like -olol and -pril announce the class on sight.
  • A class shares a mechanism, so it shares effects and nursing implications — learn the prototype and you own the family.
  • Pharmacokinetics (ADME) is what the body does to the drug; bioavailability, protein binding, and the first-pass effect explain why the same dose behaves differently in different patients.
  • Half-life drives the dosing interval: about 4–5 half-lives to reach steady state and 4–5 to clear; a loading dose skips the wait.
  • Pharmacodynamics is what the drug does to the body: agonists activate, antagonists block (the antidote mechanism), partial agonists have a ceiling.
  • Potency is milligrams, efficacy is the maximum effect; a narrow therapeutic index (warfarin, digoxin, lithium, phenytoin, insulin) demands levels and double-checks.
  • Safe administration is the rights, two identifiers, the right route, an independent double-check for high-alert drugs, and never IV push potassium.
  • Herbal supplements are unregulated drugs: St. John's wort induces CYP3A4 and wrecks regimens, the four G's plus feverfew bleed, kava and black cohosh injure the liver — so ask about the bottles no one prescribed.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Absorption
Movement of a drug from its administration site into the bloodstream; the first step of pharmacokinetics.
Adverse drug reaction (ADR)
A harmful, unintended response to a drug given at a normal dose; broader and more serious than a predictable side effect.
Agonist
A drug that binds a receptor and activates it, mimicking the body's own messenger.
Antagonist
A drug that binds a receptor and blocks it, producing no effect of its own; the mechanism behind antidotes such as naloxone.
Bioavailability
The fraction of an administered dose that reaches the systemic circulation intact; 100% for IV, always less for oral.
Distribution
Movement of a drug from the blood into tissues; shaped by perfusion, protein binding, and barriers such as the blood–brain barrier and placenta.
Efficacy
The maximum effect a drug can produce regardless of dose — what the drug is capable of.
Excretion
Removal of drug and metabolites from the body, chiefly by the kidneys.
First-pass effect
Metabolism of an orally absorbed drug by the liver before it reaches systemic circulation; bypassed by IV, sublingual, and transdermal routes.
Half-life (t½)
The time required for plasma drug concentration to fall by 50%; sets the dosing interval and predicts steady state and clearance.
High-alert medication
A drug that causes disproportionate harm when used in error — insulin, anticoagulants, opioids, concentrated electrolytes, chemotherapy — warranting an independent double-check.
Loading dose
A large initial dose given to reach a therapeutic concentration quickly, rather than waiting four to five half-lives.
Metabolism (biotransformation)
Chemical alteration of a drug, mostly by hepatic CYP450 enzymes; usually inactivates the drug, but activates a prodrug.
Pharmacodynamics
What the drug does to the body — receptor binding, mechanism of action, and the dose–response relationship.
Pharmacokinetics
What the body does to the drug — absorption, distribution, metabolism, and excretion.
Potency
The amount of drug required to produce a given effect; a more potent drug works at a lower dose. Not the same as efficacy.
Prodrug
An inactive drug that must be metabolized into its active form — codeine converted to morphine, for example.
Prototype drug
The representative, well-studied member of a class used as the template for the rest of the family.
Side effect
A predictable, dose-related effect other than the intended one; often tolerable, sometimes the reason a patient quits the drug.
Steady state
The plateau at which drug entering the body equals drug leaving it; reached after roughly four to five half-lives of regular dosing.
Therapeutic index
The ratio of the toxic dose to the effective dose; a low (narrow) index means a dangerously small margin for error.
Trough level
The lowest drug concentration in a dosing interval, drawn about 30 minutes before the next dose; the level that reveals accumulation.

Check yourself

1. A patient's home list reads Tylenol, Percocet, and NyQuil. Why does a nurse translate that list into generic names before giving anything?
All three contain acetaminophen — the patient is triple-dosing one drug. Trade names and combination products hide duplicates. Only the generic name reveals that the same drug appears three times, risking a hepatotoxic total daily dose.
2. You have never seen the drug "carvedilol" before. What are your first two questions, and what does the name already tell you?
"What class is it?" and "what is the prototype?" — the -olol stem says beta blocker, prototype metoprolol. From the class you can predict lower heart rate and blood pressure, the need to hold for bradycardia or hypotension, and the rule against abrupt discontinuation.
3. Distinguish a therapeutic classification from a pharmacologic classification, using one drug.
Therapeutic = what it treats; pharmacologic = how it works. Metoprolol is therapeutically an antihypertensive/antianginal and pharmacologically a beta1-selective adrenergic blocker. Every drug carries both labels at once.
4. Name the four pharmacokinetic processes and define bioavailability.
Absorption, distribution, metabolism, excretion (ADME). Bioavailability is the fraction of a dose that reaches systemic circulation intact. IV is 100% bioavailable by definition; oral is always less, because of incomplete absorption and the first-pass effect.
5. A malnourished patient with an albumin of 2.1 g/dL is started on a usual dose of a highly protein-bound drug. What is the risk and why?
Toxicity — only the free (unbound) drug is active, and with less albumin to bind it, a larger free fraction circulates. The "normal" dose behaves like a bigger one. Expect exaggerated effects and monitor closely.
6. Explain the first-pass effect and give one clinical consequence.
Orally absorbed drug travels through the portal vein to the liver and is partly metabolized before reaching the body. Consequence: oral doses must be larger — oral morphine is roughly three times the IV dose. Routes that bypass the liver first pass (sublingual, transdermal, IV) act faster and need less drug, which is why nitroglycerin goes under the tongue.
7. A drug has a half-life of 12 hours. Roughly how long until steady state, and how long until it is cleared after the last dose? What if the patient cannot wait?
About 4–5 half-lives each way — roughly 48–60 hours to steady state, and the same to clear. If the patient needs a therapeutic level now, a loading dose fills the tank immediately and maintenance doses hold it there.
8. Contrast an agonist, an antagonist, and a partial agonist, and explain why naloxone requires ongoing monitoring after it works.
Agonist binds and activates; antagonist binds and blocks with no effect of its own; partial agonist activates weakly and plateaus. Naloxone is a mu antagonist with a shorter duration than most opioids — when it wears off, opioid still on board can re-bind and the patient can re-sedate and stop breathing. Keep monitoring; repeat dosing may be needed.
9. Hydromorphone is more potent than morphine. Does that make it a better analgesic? Explain using potency and efficacy.
No — potency only means fewer milligrams are needed; efficacy is the maximum effect achievable. Both are full mu agonists with comparable efficacy. Potency matters for dosing math and for the danger of confusing the two drugs; efficacy is what determines how much pain can be relieved.
10. Define therapeutic index, and explain why a patient on both furosemide and digoxin needs a potassium check.
Therapeutic index compares the toxic dose to the effective dose; a narrow index means little margin for error — and digoxin's is narrow (about 0.5–2 ng/mL). Furosemide causes hypokalemia, and hypokalemia potentiates digoxin toxicity. Watch for anorexia, nausea, visual halos or yellow-green vision, confusion, and bradycardia; check the apical pulse for a full minute and hold for a rate under 60.
11. An order reads "Insulin 10.0 U SubQ now." Name three problems and state what you do.
The trailing zero can be read as 100; "U" can be misread as a zero (making it 100 units); and the order lacks the insulin type. Insulin is a high-alert drug: clarify the order with the prescriber, have it rewritten as "10 units" with the specific insulin named, and obtain an independent double-check before administering.
12. A transplant patient on cyclosporine mentions she started St. John's wort for her mood. Why is this an emergency-level finding, and what else would you screen for?
St. John's wort is a potent CYP3A4 and P-glycoprotein inducer — it accelerates cyclosporine metabolism, drops the level below therapeutic, and can cause graft rejection. Notify the prescriber immediately. Also screen for warfarin (falling INR), oral contraceptives (unintended pregnancy), antiretrovirals, digoxin, and anticonvulsants — and for SSRIs or triptans, where the combination risks serotonin syndrome.
Pharmacology · Chapter 2

Antimicrobial Drugs

Antimicrobials are the only drugs we give that are aimed at something that is not the patient. Everything else in the medication room — the beta blocker, the insulin, the opioid — changes the person in the bed. An antibiotic is meant to kill an invader while leaving that person untouched, and how well it manages that trick is the difference between a cure and a kidney injury. This chapter walks through how these drugs pick their target, how they are grouped by the machinery they break, why the microbes are learning to shrug them off, and what the nurse does at the bedside to make the whole thing work safely. Learn the classes as families and the details stop being memorization — they start being predictions you can make.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain selective toxicity and use it to predict which drugs are safe and which are not.
  • Distinguish narrow- from broad-spectrum and bactericidal from bacteriostatic therapy.
  • Describe the role of culture and sensitivity testing and why empiric therapy exists.
  • Group antibacterials by mechanism — cell wall, protein synthesis, nucleic acid, and metabolic inhibitors — and name a prototype for each.
  • Explain how resistance arises and identify MRSA, VRE, and other resistant organisms and their treatments.
  • State the nurse's role in antimicrobial stewardship.
  • Compare the major antifungal and antiviral classes and their signature toxicities.
  • Describe the major antiprotozoal and anthelmintic drugs and the nursing care common to all antimicrobial therapy.

Section 1Principles of antimicrobial therapy

Selective toxicity: the whole game

Selective toxicity is the principle that makes antimicrobial therapy possible: the drug attacks a structure the microbe has and the patient does not. Bacteria build a peptidoglycan cell wall; your cells build no wall at all, so a drug that sabotages wall construction is nearly invisible to you. Bacteria run 70S ribosomes assembled from 30S and 50S subunits; your cells run 80S ribosomes, so a drug shaped to jam the bacterial one mostly ignores yours. Bacteria have to synthesize their own folate from scratch; you eat yours, so a drug that blocks the synthesis pathway starves the bacterium and skips you entirely.

Read that list backwards and you have predicted the side effects. Selective toxicity is never perfect. Human mitochondria carry ribosomes that resemble bacterial ones — which is exactly why linezolid suppresses bone marrow and why aminoglycosides damage the hair cells of the inner ear. The narrower the gap between microbe and host, the more toxic the drug. That is the single sentence that explains why penicillin is safe enough to give a toddler and amphotericin B has a nickname involving rigors.

Spectrum, and cidal versus static

Narrow-spectrum drugs hit a limited range of organisms; broad-spectrum drugs hit many. Students often assume broad is better. It is not. Broad-spectrum therapy clear-cuts the patient's normal flora along with the pathogen, opening the door to superinfection — Clostridioides difficile colitis, Candida thrush, vaginal yeast — and it applies selection pressure across a far wider bacterial population. Broad is a starting guess, not a goal. The goal is always the narrowest drug that covers the actual organism.

Bactericidal drugs kill outright (beta-lactams, aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, vancomycin, metronidazole). Bacteriostatic drugs stall replication and let the immune system finish the job (tetracyclines, macrolides, clindamycin, sulfonamides, linezolid). The distinction matters most when the immune system cannot help — endocarditis, meningitis, neutropenia — where a cidal drug is generally required.

Culture and sensitivity, and the empiric window

A culture and sensitivity (C&S) has two halves. The culture identifies the organism. The sensitivity panel exposes it to a series of drugs and reports the minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC) — the lowest concentration that stops growth — then labels each drug susceptible, intermediate, or resistant. A low MIC number alone does not mean "best drug"; the interpretation next to it does, because it factors in what concentration the drug actually reaches at the site of infection.

Cultures take 24 to 72 hours. A septic patient does not have 72 hours. So we start empiric therapy — an educated, broad guess based on the suspected site, the likely organisms for that site, the local antibiogram, and the patient's recent exposures. Then, when the C&S returns, therapy is de-escalated to a narrow drug that the organism is actually susceptible to. Empiric therapy is a bridge, not a destination, and a broad antibiotic still running on day five with a susceptibility report on the chart is a stewardship failure.

TermWhat it meansWhy it matters at the bedside
Selective toxicityAttacks a microbial-only targetThe narrower the microbe–host gap, the more toxic the drug and the more you monitor
Narrow-spectrumFew organisms coveredPreferred once the organism is known — spares flora, less resistance
Broad-spectrumMany organisms coveredEmpiric starting point; watch for C. difficile and yeast
BactericidalKills the organismRequired when host defenses can't help: endocarditis, meningitis, neutropenia
BacteriostaticStops replicationNeeds a working immune system to finish the job
MICLowest concentration that inhibits growthRead the susceptible/resistant label, not just the number
Empiric therapyBest guess before resultsStart fast in sepsis — but plan to de-escalate
ProphylaxisPrevention, not treatmentSurgical dose timed within 60 min before incision
Cultures before the first dose — but never at the cost of the dose. One dose of antibiotic can sterilize a blood culture and cost the team the organism's name for the rest of the admission. Draw the set first. The exception is a crashing septic patient: in sepsis, antibiotics inside the first hour save lives, so you draw fast, hang faster, and do not let the phlebotomy delay the drug.
Interactive Tap through three real culture reports — a UTI, a MRSA wound, and a septic patient on empiric broad coverage — to read S, I, and R off the plate and learn why the narrowest drug that works is the right one. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Selective toxicity — a microbial-only target — makes the drug possible; where microbe and host overlap, toxicity appears.
  • Narrow-spectrum is the goal; broad-spectrum is a temporary guess that costs flora and breeds resistance.
  • Bactericidal kills; bacteriostatic stalls and needs an immune system.
  • C&S names the organism and reports the MIC; empiric therapy covers the gap and is then de-escalated.

Section 2Antibiotics by mechanism

Cell wall inhibitors: break the box

Bacteria live under enormous internal osmotic pressure and are held together by a peptidoglycan mesh. Interrupt the cross-linking and the cell swells and bursts. All of these drugs are therefore bactericidal, and because human cells have no wall, they are among the safest antimicrobials we own.

The beta-lactams — named for the four-membered beta-lactam ring they share — include penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems, and the monobactam aztreonam. They bind the penicillin-binding proteins that do the cross-linking. Penicillins run from narrow (penicillin G, for strep and syphilis) to broader (amoxicillin, ampicillin) to antipseudomonal (piperacillin-tazobactam). Cephalosporins climb through generations — cefazolin (first, the classic surgical prophylaxis drug), ceftriaxone (third, meningitis and pneumonia), cefepime (fourth, Pseudomonas), ceftaroline (fifth, active against MRSA). Carbapenems (meropenem, ertapenem, imipenem-cilastatin) are the heavy artillery reserved for resistant gram-negatives. Aztreonam covers gram-negatives only and is the escape hatch for a patient with true penicillin anaphylaxis.

The non-beta-lactam wall drugs matter too. Vancomycin, a glycopeptide, blocks wall building at a different step and is a workhorse for MRSA. Daptomycin is a lipopeptide that punches holes in the gram-positive membrane rather than the wall; it is inactivated by lung surfactant, so it is never the answer for pneumonia.

Protein synthesis inhibitors: jam the ribosome

The bacterial 70S ribosome is a target with two halves, and the drugs sort by which half they grab. Hitting the 30S subunit: aminoglycosides (gentamicin, tobramycin, amikacin) and tetracyclines (doxycycline, minocycline). Hitting the 50S subunit: macrolides (azithromycin, erythromycin, clarithromycin), clindamycin, linezolid, and chloramphenicol. A useful mnemonic: "buy AT 30, CELL at 50" — Aminoglycosides and Tetracyclines at 30S; Chloramphenicol, Erythromycin/macrolides, Lincosamides (clindamycin), and Linezolid at 50S.

This is where selective toxicity gets thin, and the toxicities are the reason. Aminoglycosides are nephrotoxic and ototoxic — the hearing loss can be permanent — so they demand peak and trough levels and daily creatinine. Tetracyclines chelate calcium: they stain developing teeth (avoid under age 8 and in pregnancy) and are inactivated if the patient swallows them with milk, antacids, or an iron pill. Macrolides prolong the QT interval. Clindamycin is the classic C. difficile offender. Linezolid, working on a ribosome that looks like the one in your mitochondria, causes thrombocytopenia, optic and peripheral neuropathy, and — because it is a weak MAO inhibitor — serotonin syndrome when stacked on an SSRI.

Nucleic acid and metabolic inhibitors: break the blueprint, starve the factory

Fluoroquinolones (ciprofloxacin, levofloxacin, moxifloxacin) poison DNA gyrase and topoisomerase IV, the enzymes that untangle bacterial DNA. They are potent, oral, and carry more boxed warnings than almost anything else on the shelf: tendinitis and tendon rupture (Achilles, worse over 60, on steroids, or post-transplant), aortic aneurysm and dissection, peripheral neuropathy, CNS effects, QT prolongation, and blood glucose swings. Rifampin blocks bacterial RNA polymerase, turns urine, sweat, and tears orange (stains contact lenses), is hepatotoxic, and is a ferocious CYP450 inducer that can quietly defeat oral contraceptives and warfarin. Metronidazole is activated inside anaerobes and protozoa where it shreds DNA; it produces a metallic taste and a disulfiram-like reaction with alcohol.

The metabolic inhibitors starve the bacterium of folate. Sulfamethoxazole blocks dihydropteroate synthase and trimethoprim blocks dihydrofolate reductase — two sequential steps in one pathway, which is why the combination (TMP-SMX) is synergistic and bactericidal. Watch for rash that could become Stevens-Johnson syndrome, hyperkalemia, crystalluria (push fluids), photosensitivity, and a sharp rise in the INR of anyone on warfarin. Isoniazid, a first-line TB drug, blocks mycolic acid synthesis and causes peripheral neuropathy — which is why it is co-prescribed with pyridoxine (vitamin B6) — and hepatitis.

Vancomycin flushing is a rate problem, not an allergy. Red face, neck, and torso with itching during a vancomycin infusion is a direct histamine release from infusing too fast — it is not IgE-mediated. Stop the infusion, give an antihistamine per order, and restart at a slower rate over a longer time. If this gets charted as "vancomycin allergy," the patient may lose the best MRSA drug they have for the rest of their life. Document what actually happened.
Interactive Tap the seven antibiotic target sites on a bacterial cell — wall, membrane, 30S, 50S, DNA gyrase, RNA polymerase, and the folate pathway — to see which drug class attacks each, why it spares human cells, and what the nurse monitors; includes a match-the-drug quiz mode. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Cell wall drugs (beta-lactams, vancomycin) are bactericidal and the safest — humans have no cell wall.
  • Protein synthesis drugs split by subunit: aminoglycosides and tetracyclines at 30S; macrolides, clindamycin, and linezolid at 50S.
  • Nucleic acid drugs: fluoroquinolones (gyrase — tendon rupture), rifampin (RNA polymerase — orange fluids, CYP inducer), metronidazole (no alcohol).
  • Metabolic drugs: TMP-SMX blocks two sequential folate steps — watch rash, potassium, and INR.

Section 3Resistance and stewardship

How resistance arises

Antibiotics do not create resistance. They select for it. In any large bacterial population a few cells already carry a gene that happens to blunt the drug — through random mutation, or borrowed wholesale from a neighbor. Give the antibiotic and you kill the susceptible majority, which is exactly the outcome you wanted, but you also hand the survivors an empty room with no competition. They multiply. This is ordinary natural selection running at bacterial speed, and every unnecessary dose accelerates it.

Bacteria are also promiscuous with DNA. Horizontal gene transfer lets a resistance gene jump between organisms that are not even related: conjugation passes a plasmid through a direct bridge, transformation scoops up free DNA from a dead cell, and transduction ferries genes by bacteriophage. One gut organism can donate a resistance plasmid to a passing pathogen overnight.

The mechanisms themselves come in four shapes, and you can read them straight off the resistance figure. Enzymatic inactivation — beta-lactamases chew open the beta-lactam ring; extended-spectrum beta-lactamases (ESBLs) chew a wider menu; carbapenemases chew even carbapenems. Target modification — MRSA's mecA gene builds an altered penicillin-binding protein (PBP2a) that ordinary beta-lactams cannot grip; VRE's vanA gene rebuilds the wall precursor so that its terminal D-alanine-D-alanine becomes D-alanine-D-lactate, which vancomycin can no longer bind. Efflux pumps bail the drug back out before it works. Reduced permeability shuts the porin doors so the drug never gets in.

The organisms you will meet

MRSA is Staphylococcus aureus that has become immune to the entire traditional beta-lactam class at once — not just methicillin — because PBP2a ignores penicillins, cephalosporins, and carbapenems alike. That single gene knocks out the safest, cheapest drugs you had and forces a move to vancomycin, linezolid, daptomycin, or ceftaroline — the one beta-lactam specifically engineered to bind PBP2a anyway. VRE pushes further, defeating the drug we reached for when MRSA appeared. ESBL-producing gram-negatives push therapy toward carbapenems; CRE defeat the carbapenems and leave a genuinely thin bench. And C. difficile, while not a resistance mechanism itself, is the price of collateral damage: the antibiotic wipes out the gut community and C. diff blooms in the vacancy.

Stewardship and the nurse

Antimicrobial stewardship is the coordinated effort to use these drugs only when needed, and then in the right drug, dose, route, and duration. It is often described as a pharmacy-and-physician program, which badly understates the nurse's role — the nurse is the person who actually gets the culture, notices the report, gives the dose, and hears the patient's story.

Concretely: get the cultures before the first dose. Take a real allergy history — "penicillin gave me an upset stomach at age six" is not an allergy, and roughly 9 in 10 people labeled penicillin-allergic are not, so that label costs them broader, more toxic, more expensive drugs for decades. Flag day 3: is the culture back, and is the patient still on the broad empiric drug? Push the IV-to-PO switch when the patient is eating and stable (linezolid, fluoroquinolones, metronidazole, doxycycline, and fluconazole are all essentially fully absorbed orally). Question the duration — evidence keeps shortening courses. Teach the patient that antibiotics do nothing for a cold, that leftovers are not to be saved or shared, and that the prescribed course should be taken as directed rather than stopped the moment they feel better. And do the unglamorous thing that prevents more resistant infections than any drug decision: wash your hands.

OrganismResistance mechanismTreatment optionsNursing alert
MRSAmecA → altered PBP2a; all traditional beta-lactams failvancomycin, linezolid, daptomycin, ceftaroline; TMP-SMX or doxycycline for skinContact precautions; nasal decolonization protocols; a standard cephalosporin such as cefazolin or ceftriaxone will not cover MRSA — ceftaroline is the lone exception
VREvanA/vanB → altered D-Ala-D-Ala binding sitelinezolid, daptomycinContact precautions; survives on surfaces — dedicated equipment
ESBL gram-negativesExtended-spectrum beta-lactamase inactivates penicillins/cephalosporinsCarbapenems (meropenem, ertapenem)Escalation is appropriate here — a "susceptible" cephalosporin on the report may still fail
CRECarbapenemase (e.g., KPC)ceftazidime-avibactam, meropenem-vaborbactam, sometimes colistinReport to infection prevention; strict precautions; colistin is nephrotoxic
C. difficileNot resistance — overgrowth after flora are destroyedOral vancomycin or fidaxomicin; fecal transplant for recurrenceSoap and water — alcohol gel does not kill spores; stop the offending antibiotic if possible; do not give antimotility agents
Oral vancomycin and IV vancomycin are not interchangeable — in either direction. Oral vancomycin is essentially not absorbed, which is precisely why it works for C. difficile: it stays in the gut where the organism is. It will do nothing for a bloodstream infection. IV vancomycin, conversely, does not reach the gut lumen in useful amounts and will not treat C. diff. If the route on the order looks wrong for the indication, that is a call to the prescriber, not a transcription error to fix quietly.
Interactive Follow an antibiotic across the bacterial envelope to see the four defenses that stop it — reduced entry, drug-destroying enzymes, efflux pumps, and altered targets — then work the ward and watch which nursing practices actually slow resistance down. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Antibiotics select for resistance rather than causing it; horizontal gene transfer spreads it between unrelated organisms.
  • Four mechanisms: enzymes (beta-lactamase, ESBL, carbapenemase), target modification (MRSA's PBP2a, VRE's vanA), efflux pumps, reduced permeability.
  • MRSA defeats every beta-lactam at once; VRE defeats the drug we replaced it with; CRE leaves very few options.
  • The nurse's stewardship work: cultures first, accurate allergy history, day-3 review, IV-to-PO, right duration, patient teaching, hand hygiene.

Section 4Antifungals and antivirals

Antifungals: a much smaller gap

Fungi are eukaryotes. Their cells are built like yours — nucleus, 80S ribosomes, similar membranes — which means the pool of microbe-only targets is small and antifungal drugs are, as a group, markedly more toxic than antibacterials. Almost every antifungal exploits one of two genuine differences: fungal membranes use ergosterol where yours uses cholesterol, and fungal cell walls contain beta-(1,3)-D-glucan, which you do not make at all.

Amphotericin B (a polyene) binds ergosterol directly and rips pores in the membrane. It is powerful, and it is rough: infusion-related fevers, rigors, and shaking chills earned it the nickname "shake and bake," plus dose-limiting nephrotoxicity with potassium and magnesium wasting. Liposomal formulations are considerably gentler — and this is a genuine safety trap, because the conventional and liposomal doses are very different and are not interchangeable. Verify the formulation on the label against the order. The azoles (fluconazole, itraconazole, voriconazole, posaconazole, isavuconazole) block lanosterol 14-alpha-demethylase, the enzyme that makes ergosterol; they are the mainstay of oral therapy but bring hepatotoxicity, QT prolongation, and heavy CYP450 interactions with warfarin and statins. Voriconazole adds a distinctive transient visual disturbance — blurred or bright-tinged vision — and photosensitivity. The echinocandins (caspofungin, micafungin, anidulafungin) attack the beta-glucan wall, a target humans simply do not have, and are correspondingly well tolerated; they are first-line for invasive candidemia but are IV-only. Terbinafine treats nail and skin fungus over long courses — monitor liver function and warn about taste changes. Nystatin is topical: swish and swallow for thrush, and teach the patient to hold it in the mouth before swallowing.

Antivirals: the narrowest gap of all

A virus replicates using your cellular machinery, so there is almost nothing to attack that is not also yours. Antivirals work by targeting the handful of enzymes the virus brings itself — a viral polymerase, a protease, an integrase, a neuraminidase. They also suppress rather than cure most of the time, and they are exquisitely time-sensitive: antivirals started late usually do very little.

Acyclovir and its better-absorbed prodrug valacyclovir treat herpes simplex and varicella-zoster. The elegance is in the activation: acyclovir must be phosphorylated by viral thymidine kinase before it works, so it is essentially inert in an uninfected cell. IV acyclovir crystallizes in renal tubules, so hydrate the patient well and infuse over at least an hour. Ganciclovir and valganciclovir handle CMV and are bone-marrow suppressive. Oseltamivir blocks influenza neuraminidase and must start within about 48 hours of symptom onset to matter. For HIV, antiretroviral therapy combines drug classes — NRTIs (tenofovir, emtricitabine, abacavir), NNRTIs (efavirenz), protease inhibitors (usually boosted with ritonavir), and integrase inhibitors (dolutegravir, bictegravir), which are now first-line. Two nursing points dominate: abacavir requires HLA-B*5701 screening before the first dose because a positive result predicts a potentially fatal hypersensitivity reaction, and adherence is not a soft outcome — missed doses let the virus mutate into resistance. Hepatitis C is now curable in most patients with 8–12 weeks of direct-acting antivirals such as sofosbuvir-based or glecaprevir-pibrentasvir regimens; screen for hepatitis B first, since these drugs can reactivate it.

AllylamineterbinafineBlocks squalene epoxidaseLong courses for nails; baseline and periodic LFTs; taste and smell disturbance
Topical polyenenystatinSame as amphotericin, applied locallySwish and swallow for thrush; hold in mouth, no food/drink 30 min after; treat the denture too
Anti-herpesacyclovir, valacyclovirGuanosine analog activated by viral thymidine kinaseHydrate — IV form crystallizes in the kidney; infuse over ≥1 h; start within 72 h of a shingles rash
Anti-CMVganciclovir, valganciclovirInhibits CMV DNA polymeraseMyelosuppression — monitor CBC; hazardous drug handling
