Integrase inhibitordolutegravir, bictegravirBlocks integration into host DNAFirst-line, well tolerated; weight gain; separate from antacids/calcium; adherence is everything
Protease inhibitorritonavir-boosted regimensBlocks viral protein maturationRitonavir is a potent CYP3A4 inhibitor — screen every other medication; metabolic effects
HCV direct-actingsofosbuvir-based, glecaprevir-pibrentasvirBlocks the HCV NS3/4A protease, NS5A, or NS5B polymerase — regimens combine two targetsCures >95% in 8–12 weeks; screen for hepatitis B — risk of HBV reactivation
Antivirals are a stopwatch, not a prescription. Oseltamivir inside 48 hours of the first influenza symptom shortens illness; on day four it is close to pointless. Acyclovir for shingles inside 72 hours of the rash meaningfully reduces the odds of post-herpetic neuralgia; a week later, the window is gone. When a patient calls describing a new rash or new flu symptoms, the clinically important question is not "how bad is it" but "when did it start."
Interactive Switch between a fungal cell and the viral replication cycle, then tap any target — ergosterol, glucan synthase, reverse transcriptase, integrase, viral polymerase, protease, neuraminidase — to see what the drug blocks, its key toxicity, and what the nurse monitors. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Fungi are eukaryotes, so antifungals are more toxic; the real targets are ergosterol and beta-glucan.
  • Amphotericin B = rigors and nephrotoxicity; azoles = liver, QT, CYP interactions; echinocandins = safest, IV only.
  • Antivirals hit viral-only enzymes, mostly suppress rather than cure, and are highly time-dependent.
  • Hydrate for IV acyclovir; screen HLA-B*5701 before abacavir; ART adherence prevents resistance.

Section 5Antiparasitics and nursing care

Antiprotozoals

Protozoa are eukaryotes too, so once again the safety margin is narrow and the drugs are chosen around the parasite's life stage rather than just its name. Malaria is the clearest example. Artemisinin-based combination therapy (artemether-lumefantrine) is first-line for uncomplicated P. falciparum and should be taken with fatty food, which dramatically improves absorption. Atovaquone-proguanil and doxycycline serve for prophylaxis; chloroquine still works where resistance is absent, and long-term use risks irreversible retinopathy. The critical piece is the dormant liver form, the hypnozoite, of P. vivax and P. ovale: treating the blood stage alone leaves it in place and the patient relapses months later. Clearing it requires primaquine or tafenoquine — and both cause severe hemolysis in G6PD deficiency, so screen G6PD before the dose, without exception.

Beyond malaria, metronidazole or tinidazole covers giardiasis, amebiasis, and trichomoniasis. For trichomoniasis, treat the partner too or the patient simply gets it back — and reinforce the alcohol warning, since these are the drugs behind the disulfiram-like reaction. Nitazoxanide covers Giardia and Cryptosporidium. TMP-SMX remains the drug of choice for Pneumocystis pneumonia (an organism reclassified as a fungus but still taught alongside this group), with pentamidine as an alternative that can cause severe hypoglycemia followed by hyperglycemia.

Anthelmintics

Worms are animals, and the drugs against them are mostly aimed at neuromuscular or cytoskeletal machinery the worm depends on. Albendazole and mebendazole block the worm's microtubule assembly, starving it; albendazole is absorbed far better with a fatty meal. Ivermectin opens glutamate-gated chloride channels, paralyzing the parasite — mammals have no such channels at all, and the blood-brain barrier keeps the drug away from the similar channels in the human CNS — it treats strongyloidiasis, onchocerciasis, and scabies. Praziquantel makes the tegument of flukes and tapeworms leak calcium, causing paralysis; take with food and warn about dizziness. Pyrantel pamoate is the over-the-counter pinworm option.

The teaching matters as much as the drug. Pinworm is a household diagnosis: treat all household members, repeat the dose in two weeks to catch newly hatched eggs, wash bed linens and underwear in hot water, cut and scrub fingernails, and collect the diagnostic tape test first thing in the morning before bathing. That set of instructions prevents more reinfection than any second prescription.

Nursing care that applies to every antimicrobial

Strip away the class differences and the same nursing spine holds up all of it. Before: take a real allergy history, get the culture, check renal and hepatic function against the drug and the dose, review interactions, and confirm pregnancy status where it matters. During: give doses on schedule and evenly spaced around the clock so blood levels stay above the MIC — a "late" antibiotic is not a trivial late medication. Stay with the patient for the first dose of anything new. Draw troughs immediately before the next dose and peaks at the specified interval after it, and chart the exact times, because a level drawn at the wrong moment is worse than no level at all: it will be acted upon. After: watch for the response you expect (fever curve, white count, the wound), for the toxicity you were warned about (creatinine, CBC, LFTs, hearing, tendons, QT), and for the superinfection you invited — new diarrhea, white patches in the mouth, vaginal itching.

Nursing actionWhyWatch for
Real allergy history"Rash" vs. "throat closed" are different drugs and different risksMislabeled penicillin allergy pushing the patient to broader, more toxic therapy
Cultures before first doseOne dose can sterilize the specimen and cost the organism's nameIn sepsis: draw fast, but antibiotics still go in within the hour
Check creatinine and LFTsMost antimicrobials are renally cleared or hepatically metabolizedRising creatinine on vancomycin, gentamicin, amphotericin B, colistin
Even, around-the-clock dosingKeeps concentration above the MIC between dosesClustered or missed doses — a real cause of treatment failure
Correct level timingTrough immediately before the next dose; peak at the specified time afterMis-timed levels lead to wrong dose changes — chart exact draw times
Stay for the first doseAnaphylaxis usually shows itself within 30 minFlushing, itching, wheeze, throat tightness, drop in BP
Assess for superinfectionAntibiotics destroy the protective normal floraNew diarrhea (C. diff), oral white plaques, vaginal itching
Teach the courseAdherence prevents relapse and resistanceStopping early when they feel better; saving or sharing leftovers
Teach the specific warningEvery class has one that changes daily lifeSun (doxycycline, TMP-SMX), alcohol (metronidazole), orange fluids (rifampin), tendon pain (fluoroquinolones)
The nurse is the last checkpoint the drug passes through. The prescriber chose the drug, the pharmacist checked the dose, but you are the only one holding the bag while looking at the patient — and the only one who knows their creatinine rose overnight, that they said "penicillin makes me swell," and that their diarrhea started this morning. Every antimicrobial safety story eventually comes down to someone at the bedside who stopped and asked a question. Be that person.
Interactive Tap a body site or an antiprotozoal/anthelmintic drug to see where the parasite hides and watch the nine-square nursing monitoring grid light up — G6PD before primaquine, CBC and LFTs on long benzimidazole courses, no alcohol with metronidazole, and the food rules that decide whether a dose works at all. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Antimalarials are chosen by life stage; hypnozoite clearance needs primaquine/tafenoquine — screen G6PD first.
  • Metronidazole covers giardiasis, amebiasis, trichomoniasis — treat the partner, no alcohol.
  • Albendazole/mebendazole (microtubules), ivermectin (chloride channels), praziquantel (calcium influx); pinworm needs household treatment plus a repeat dose in two weeks.
  • Universal nursing spine: allergy history, culture first, organ function, on-time evenly spaced dosing, correct level timing, watch for toxicity and superinfection, teach the course.

The chapter in one breath

  • Selective toxicity makes antimicrobials possible; where microbe and host overlap, toxicity appears — which is why antifungals and antivirals are rougher than antibacterials.
  • Narrow-spectrum is the goal, broad-spectrum is a temporary guess; bactericidal drugs are required when the immune system cannot help.
  • C&S names the organism and reports the MIC; empiric therapy bridges the wait and must then be de-escalated.
  • Antibacterials sort by mechanism: cell wall (beta-lactams, vancomycin), protein synthesis (30S: aminoglycosides, tetracyclines; 50S: macrolides, clindamycin, linezolid), nucleic acid (fluoroquinolones, rifampin, metronidazole), metabolic (TMP-SMX).
  • Antibiotics select for resistance; enzymes, target modification, efflux, and reduced permeability produce MRSA, VRE, ESBL, and CRE.
  • Stewardship is nursing work: cultures first, honest allergy history, day-3 review, IV-to-PO, right duration, teaching, and hand hygiene.
  • Antifungals target ergosterol and beta-glucan; antivirals target viral-only enzymes, suppress rather than cure, and only help if started early.
  • Across every antimicrobial, the nurse monitors organ function and levels, times doses evenly, watches for superinfection, and teaches the one warning that class carries.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

The vocabulary of antimicrobial therapy — skim it now, then come back whenever a term on a culture report or a drug label stops you.

Aminoglycoside
A bactericidal class (gentamicin, tobramycin, amikacin) that binds the 30S ribosomal subunit; nephrotoxic and ototoxic, requiring peak and trough monitoring.
Anthelmintic
A drug that kills or expels parasitic worms, e.g., albendazole, ivermectin, praziquantel.
Antimicrobial stewardship
The coordinated effort to use antimicrobials only when indicated, and then in the right drug, dose, route, and duration.
Bactericidal
Kills bacteria outright; required when host defenses are unavailable (endocarditis, meningitis, neutropenia).
Bacteriostatic
Inhibits bacterial replication and relies on the immune system to clear the organism.
Beta-lactam
Any drug built around the beta-lactam ring — penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems, and the monobactam aztreonam — that blocks cell wall cross-linking.
Beta-lactamase
A bacterial enzyme that inactivates beta-lactam drugs by opening the ring; extended-spectrum forms (ESBLs) inactivate a much wider range.
Broad-spectrum
Active against a wide range of organisms; useful empirically but destroys normal flora and drives resistance.
Culture and sensitivity (C&S)
A laboratory test that identifies the organism and reports which drugs inhibit it, with susceptible/intermediate/resistant interpretations.
De-escalation
Narrowing therapy from an empiric broad-spectrum drug to a targeted one once the culture and sensitivity result returns.
Empiric therapy
Treatment started before results are available, based on the likely organisms for that site and patient.
Ergosterol
The sterol in fungal cell membranes (humans use cholesterol); the target of polyenes and the azoles' synthesis pathway.
Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus (MRSA)
S. aureus carrying mecA, which builds an altered penicillin-binding protein (PBP2a) so that every beta-lactam fails at once.
Minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC)
The lowest drug concentration that prevents visible growth of an organism in vitro.
Narrow-spectrum
Active against a limited range of organisms; the goal once the pathogen is identified.
Nephrotoxicity
Drug-induced kidney injury; a signature risk of vancomycin, aminoglycosides, amphotericin B, and colistin.
Ototoxicity
Drug-induced damage to hearing or balance, classically from aminoglycosides; may be permanent, so report tinnitus or vertigo at once.
Peak and trough
Serum drug levels drawn at the expected maximum (peak) and immediately before the next dose (trough) to keep therapy effective and non-toxic.
Prophylaxis
Giving an antimicrobial to prevent infection rather than treat it, e.g., cefazolin within 60 minutes before surgical incision.
Selective toxicity
Harming the microbe while sparing the patient by attacking a structure the microbe has and human cells lack.
Superinfection
A new infection that appears during antimicrobial therapy because the protective normal flora were destroyed, e.g., C. difficile colitis or thrush.
Vancomycin-resistant Enterococcus (VRE)
Enterococcus that has altered the D-alanine binding site vancomycin depends on; usually treated with linezolid or daptomycin.

Check yourself

1. Explain selective toxicity, and use it to explain why penicillin is remarkably safe while linezolid suppresses bone marrow.
Selective toxicity means attacking a structure the microbe has and the patient does not. Penicillin blocks cell wall synthesis, and human cells have no cell wall at all — a perfect gap, hence a very safe drug. Linezolid binds the bacterial 50S ribosomal subunit, but human mitochondrial ribosomes resemble bacterial ones, so the gap is narrow and marrow suppression follows. The rule: the smaller the microbe–host difference, the more toxic the drug.
2. A patient with an identified organism is still on a broad-spectrum antibiotic on hospital day five. Why is that a problem, and what should have happened?
The empiric broad-spectrum drug should have been de-escalated to a narrow drug the organism is susceptible to. Continuing broad therapy needlessly destroys normal flora (inviting C. difficile and Candida) and applies selection pressure to a much wider bacterial population. Empiric therapy is a bridge until the C&S returns, not a destination.
3. Why does the choice between a bactericidal and a bacteriostatic drug matter more in endocarditis or neutropenia than in a simple skin infection?
Bacteriostatic drugs only stall replication — the immune system has to finish the kill. In endocarditis (a vegetation immune cells cannot reach), meningitis, or neutropenia (no functioning immune cells), there is no one to finish the job, so a bactericidal drug is generally required.
4. Sort these by ribosomal target and give the toxicity you would monitor for each: gentamicin, doxycycline, azithromycin, linezolid.
30S: gentamicin (nephrotoxicity and ototoxicity — creatinine, peak/trough, tinnitus) and doxycycline (photosensitivity, teeth staining under 8, esophagitis). 50S: azithromycin (QT prolongation) and linezolid (thrombocytopenia, serotonin syndrome with SSRIs, optic neuropathy). Mnemonic: "buy AT 30, CELL at 50."
5. A patient turns red across the face and torso and itches 20 minutes into a vancomycin infusion. What is happening, what do you do, and what must you be careful about documenting?
This is vancomycin flushing syndrome — direct histamine release from too-rapid infusion, not an IgE allergy. Stop the infusion, give an antihistamine per order, and restart at a slower rate over a longer time (at least 60 minutes, longer for larger doses). Do not chart it as a vancomycin allergy — that label could cost the patient the best MRSA drug they have for life. Document exactly what occurred and its relation to the rate.
6. Your patient on ciprofloxacin reports new heel pain when walking. Why does this matter, and what else on the fluoroquinolone warning list would you review?
Heel pain suggests Achilles tendinitis, which can progress to tendon rupture — hold the drug and notify the prescriber. Risk is highest over age 60, on corticosteroids, or after transplant. Also review aortic aneurysm/dissection risk, peripheral neuropathy, CNS effects, QT prolongation, and blood glucose swings — and remember to separate the dose from calcium, iron, and antacids by several hours.
7. Name three teaching points you must cover before a patient leaves on rifampin, and one before they leave on metronidazole.
Rifampin: (1) urine, sweat, and tears turn orange and will permanently stain soft contact lenses — harmless but expected; (2) it is a potent CYP450 inducer and can make oral contraceptives fail — use a backup method; (3) it is hepatotoxic — report dark urine, jaundice, RUQ pain, and avoid alcohol. Metronidazole: no alcohol during therapy and for three days after — a disulfiram-like reaction (flushing, vomiting, tachycardia) follows.
8. Explain in one paragraph how giving an antibiotic leads to a resistant population, and name the four broad mechanisms bacteria use.
The antibiotic does not create resistance — it selects for it. A few cells in the population already carry a resistance gene from mutation or horizontal gene transfer (conjugation, transformation, transduction). Killing the susceptible majority removes their competition, so the survivors multiply into a resistant population. The four mechanisms: enzymatic inactivation (beta-lactamase, ESBL, carbapenemase), target modification (MRSA's PBP2a, VRE's altered D-Ala site), efflux pumps, and reduced permeability.
9. Why does one gene (mecA) make MRSA resistant to every beta-lactam rather than just methicillin, and which drugs remain?
mecA builds an altered penicillin-binding protein, PBP2a, that ordinary beta-lactams cannot bind — and since all penicillins, cephalosporins, and carbapenems work by binding PBPs, the traditional class fails at once. Options: vancomycin, linezolid, daptomycin, ceftaroline (a fifth-generation cephalosporin specifically engineered to bind PBP2a, which is why it is the one beta-lactam that still works); TMP-SMX or doxycycline for uncomplicated skin infections.
10. A patient on clindamycin develops watery diarrhea. What are you thinking, and name three specific nursing actions.
Think Clostridioides difficile — clindamycin is the classic trigger, and this is superinfection from destroyed normal flora. Actions: (1) notify the prescriber and send a stool sample; (2) place on contact precautions and use soap and water — alcohol gel does not kill spores; (3) hold antimotility agents and anticipate stopping the offending antibiotic. Treatment is oral vancomycin or fidaxomicin.
11. Why is amphotericin B so much harder on a patient than meropenem, and what two specific safety checks does it demand?
Fungi are eukaryotes, so their cells resemble human cells and the selective-toxicity gap is narrow; amphotericin B binds ergosterol but also interacts with human cholesterol. Result: infusion fevers and rigors ("shake and bake") plus dose-limiting nephrotoxicity with potassium and magnesium wasting. Checks: (1) verify conventional vs. liposomal formulation against the order — the doses are very different and not interchangeable; (2) monitor creatinine, potassium, and magnesium, and premedicate per order.
12. Two "before the first dose" screening tests appear in this chapter — one before abacavir and one before primaquine. Name each and say what it prevents.
Abacavir requires HLA-B*5701 testing — a positive result predicts a severe, potentially fatal hypersensitivity reaction, so the drug is never given. Primaquine (and tafenoquine) requires G6PD screening — deficient patients suffer severe acute hemolysis. Both are examples of the same principle: for a handful of drugs, the safety decision is made by a test result before the first dose, not by monitoring afterward.
Pharmacology · Chapter 3

Immunomodulators & Antineoplastic Agents

Most drugs help a patient by turning a body system up or down a little. The drugs in this chapter do something far more consequential: they rewrite the terms on which the immune system operates, or they poison cell division itself. That power is exactly why they work — a transplanted kidney survives, a lupus flare quiets, a lymphoma melts away — and exactly why they hurt. An immunosuppressed patient cannot mount a fever the way you expect. A chemotherapy patient loses the lining of the gut and the blood-forming marrow along with the tumor. This chapter walks you through the drugs that quiet immunity, the drugs that wake it up, and the drugs that kill dividing cells — and, at every step, what the nurse watches, teaches, and refuses to let slide.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Name the major immunosuppressant classes, their prototypes, and their signature adverse effects.
  • Explain why infection is the defining risk of immunosuppression and how that changes your assessment.
  • Distinguish immunostimulants, biologics, and the main vaccine types, and state who must not receive a live vaccine.
  • Describe the cell cycle and contrast cell-cycle–specific with cell-cycle–nonspecific antineoplastics.
  • Explain why chemotherapy damages normal rapidly dividing tissues, and predict which ones.
  • Identify the five chemotherapy classes with their prototypes and class-defining toxicities.
  • Manage myelosuppression, nausea and vomiting, mucositis, alopecia, and extravasation at the bedside.
  • Apply hazardous drug handling precautions to protect yourself, the patient, and the family.

Section 1Immunosuppressants

Why we deliberately weaken a defense system

The immune system is superb at one job: telling self from not-self and destroying the not-self. That instinct becomes a problem in exactly two situations. In transplantation, the donated organ is not-self, and an untreated immune system will reject it within days. In autoimmune disease — rheumatoid arthritis, lupus, Crohn disease, multiple sclerosis, psoriasis — the system has misfiled the patient's own tissue as foreign and is attacking it. In both cases we give drugs to turn the response down. There is no free lunch here: every bit of immunity we take away is immunity that was also guarding against bacteria, viruses, fungi, and cancer cells.

Transplant regimens are usually built in two stages. Induction therapy is heavy and brief, given around the time of surgery — basiliximab (an interleukin-2 receptor antagonist) or antithymocyte globulin. Maintenance therapy is lifelong and usually combines three drugs from different classes at lower doses each: a calcineurin inhibitor, an antiproliferative, and a corticosteroid. Using three mechanisms at modest doses beats using one at a huge dose, because the toxicities do not stack in the same organ.

The classes worth knowing cold

Calcineurin inhibitors — cyclosporine and tacrolimus — are the backbone of transplant care. They block the calcineurin signal that a T cell needs to make interleukin-2, so the T cell never proliferates. Both have a narrow therapeutic index: too little and the graft rejects, too much and the kidneys fail. Trough levels are drawn immediately before the next dose, and the whole regimen lives or dies on the patient taking it at the same times every day. Both are metabolized by CYP3A4, which is why grapefruit juice, azole antifungals, and clarithromycin raise levels dangerously, while rifampin, phenytoin, and St. John's wort drop them toward rejection.

Antiproliferatives starve lymphocytes of the DNA building blocks they need to divide. Mycophenolate is the modern standard; azathioprine is older and carries one of the most famous interactions in pharmacology — giving allopurinol alongside it blocks xanthine oxidase, the enzyme that clears azathioprine's active metabolite, so drug levels climb and can cause catastrophic marrow failure. mTOR inhibitors (sirolimus, everolimus) spare the kidney but impair wound healing, so they are typically held in the immediate post-op period. Corticosteroids blunt nearly every arm of inflammation and are effective, cheap, and toxic in a dozen directions; they are tapered, never stopped abruptly, because chronic use suppresses the adrenal axis.

On the autoimmune side, low-dose methotrexate is the anchor drug for rheumatoid arthritis, and here is the fact that saves lives: it is dosed once weekly, not daily. Daily dosing of a weekly methotrexate order has killed patients. Folic acid is given on the other days to reduce mucositis and marrow toxicity. Biologic DMARDs — the TNF inhibitors etanercept, adalimumab, and infliximab — are extraordinarily effective and require latent tuberculosis screening before the first dose, because knocking out TNF lets walled-off TB break loose.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Calcineurin inhibitorTacrolimus; cyclosporineBlocks IL-2 production → T cells cannot proliferateNarrow TI — trough levels before the dose; nephrotoxicity, hypertension, tremor, hyperkalemia; hyperglycemia (tacrolimus); gum overgrowth and hirsutism (cyclosporine); no grapefruit juice
AntiproliferativeMycophenolate; azathioprineBlocks purine synthesis → lymphocytes cannot divideGI upset, leukopenia; mycophenolate is teratogenic (pregnancy prevention required); azathioprine + allopurinol = marrow failure
mTOR inhibitorSirolimus; everolimusBlocks the growth signal downstream of IL-2Impaired wound healing, hyperlipidemia, mouth ulcers, pneumonitis; kidney-sparing
CorticosteroidPrednisone; methylprednisoloneBroad anti-inflammatory and lymphocyte suppressionHyperglycemia, osteoporosis, mood change, masks fever and infection; taper — never stop abruptly
Biologic (induction)Basiliximab; antithymocyte globulinTargets IL-2 receptor or depletes T cells at transplantInfusion reactions; premedicate; monitor closely during and after the infusion
Antimetabolite DMARDMethotrexateFolate antagonist; anti-inflammatory at low doseWEEKLY dosing — a daily order is a lethal error; give folic acid; monitor CBC, LFTs, creatinine; teratogenic

JAK inhibitorTofacitinib; baricitinibBlocks intracellular cytokine signaling (oral)Boxed warning: serious infection, thrombosis, cardiovascular events, malignancy; monitor CBC and lipids

Infection is the whole risk

An immunosuppressed patient does not present the way your textbook patient presents. Steroids blunt fever. Neutropenia removes the cells that make pus, so a wound infection may show no drainage and pneumonia may show a nearly clear chest film. The classic signs of inflammation are, in part, the immune system working — and you have turned it off. This means the threshold for concern drops through the floor: a low-grade temperature, a new cough, a sore throat, a small area of redness, or simply "I don't feel right" all get taken seriously and reported.

Prophylaxis is standard rather than optional in this population — typically trimethoprim–sulfamethoxazole against Pneumocystis jirovecii, an antiviral against cytomegalovirus, and an antifungal in high-risk patients. Live vaccines are contraindicated in significant immunosuppression, and they should be brought up to date before transplant or before starting a biologic. Long-term, these patients also carry a real excess risk of malignancy — skin cancer above all — so sunscreen, protective clothing, and annual skin checks are legitimate nursing teaching, not filler.

A fever in an immunosuppressed patient is never "just a fever." In someone on tacrolimus, prednisone, or a TNF inhibitor, a temperature that you would watch overnight in anyone else may be the only sign of an infection that is already systemic. Report it, culture it, and treat it as an emergency until proven otherwise.
Interactive Build a transplant regimen class by class on the T-cell activation cascade — corticosteroids, calcineurin inhibitors, antiproliferatives, mTOR inhibitors, basiliximab, and antithymocyte globulin — and watch rejection control and infection risk climb as the same number. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Transplant regimens layer three classes at lower doses — calcineurin inhibitor, antiproliferative, corticosteroid — so toxicities do not stack.
  • Calcineurin inhibitors have a narrow therapeutic index: trough levels, nephrotoxicity, CYP3A4 interactions, no grapefruit.
  • Methotrexate is weekly; azathioprine plus allopurinol causes marrow failure; TNF inhibitors require TB screening first.
  • Immunosuppressed patients hide infection — blunted fever, no pus — and must not receive live vaccines.

Section 2Immunostimulants and vaccines

Drugs that wake the immune system up

If immunosuppressants turn the dial down, immunostimulants turn it up — either by rebuilding cells the immune system has lost, or by releasing a brake that a tumor has been exploiting. The most common ones you will hang are the colony-stimulating factors. Filgrastim and its long-acting cousin pegfilgrastim are manufactured versions of G-CSF; they tell the marrow to pump out neutrophils and are given after chemotherapy to shorten the window of dangerous neutropenia. The classic side effect is deep, aching bone pain — the marrow is literally working overtime — and it is usually managed with acetaminophen or an NSAID rather than by stopping the drug. Timing matters: these are given at least twenty-four hours after chemotherapy, never alongside it, because you do not want to hand freshly stimulated dividing marrow cells to a drug that kills dividing cells.

Erythropoiesis-stimulating agents (epoetin alfa, darbepoetin) raise hemoglobin but carry a boxed warning: pushed too high, they shorten survival in some cancers and cause thrombosis and stroke. They are used at the lowest dose that avoids transfusion, not to make the number look pretty. Interferons (interferon alfa-2b; interferon beta for multiple sclerosis) reliably produce flu-like symptoms — fever, chills, myalgia — and can cause depression severe enough to require screening. Interleukin-2 (aldesleukin) is powerful and dangerous, with capillary leak syndrome as its defining toxicity.

The most important recent class is the immune checkpoint inhibitors — pembrolizumab and nivolumab (anti–PD-1), atezolizumab (anti–PD-L1), and ipilimumab (anti–CTLA-4). Tumors survive partly by pressing the immune system's own "stand down" buttons; these drugs cut that wire. The result is a reawakened immune system that sometimes attacks the patient too, producing immune-related adverse events: colitis, pneumonitis, hepatitis, thyroiditis, dermatitis, hypophysitis. Any new diarrhea, cough, or shortness of breath in a patient on a checkpoint inhibitor is reported — these are treated with corticosteroids, and delay does real harm.

A quick decoder for biologic names, because it genuinely helps: -mab means monoclonal antibody, -cept means a receptor fusion protein, -nib means a small-molecule kinase inhibitor, and -kin or -grastim flags a cytokine or growth factor.

Vaccines: borrowing the memory without the disease

A vaccine is the most cost-effective drug there is. It shows the adaptive immune system a harmless preview of a pathogen so that memory B and T cells are already standing by when the real thing arrives — active immunity, slow to build but long-lasting. Contrast that with passive immunity, where we simply inject someone else's ready-made antibodies: immune globulin, Rho(D) immune globulin, rabies or tetanus immune globulin. Passive protection works within minutes and fades within weeks, because the patient made no memory of their own. Use active for prevention, passive for a threat that is already here.

Vaccine typePrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Live attenuatedMMR; varicella; rotavirus; intranasal influenza; yellow feverWeakened but replicating organism — strongest, longest immunityContraindicated in pregnancy and significant immunosuppression; give before transplant or biologics, not during
Inactivated (killed)Hepatitis A; injectable polio (IPV); rabiesWhole organism, killed — cannot replicateSafe in immunosuppression; often needs boosters
Subunit / recombinantHepatitis B; HPV; recombinant zoster (Shingrix)Only the pieces that matter, made in the labSafe in immunosuppression; recombinant zoster is notably reactogenic — warn about arm soreness and a day of feeling rough
ToxoidTetanus; diphtheria (Td, Tdap)Inactivated toxin — immunity to the poison, not the bugBooster every 10 years; Tdap in every pregnancy (27–36 weeks) to protect the newborn
ConjugateHib; pneumococcal (PCV); meningococcalSugar coat linked to a protein so infant immune systems respondEssential for the very young and the asplenic
mRNA / viral vectorCOVID-19 vaccinesDelivers the instructions; the patient's cells make the antigenNot a live vaccine and does not alter DNA — a common patient question worth answering plainly
Passive (not a vaccine)Immune globulin; Rho(D) immune globulinBorrowed antibodies — immediate, temporaryWorks within minutes, no memory made; used after exposure or for Rh-negative mothers

Vaccine administration is a nursing skill with a few non-negotiables. Give the Vaccine Information Statement before the injection and document that you did. Have epinephrine immediately available and observe for at least fifteen minutes afterward, longer if there is any history of reaction. Document lot number, expiration, site, and route — this is one of the few places where charting is itself a public health function. Teach that soreness, a low-grade temperature, and fatigue for a day mean the vaccine is working, and treat them with acetaminophen; do not give aspirin to a child. And know the real contraindications from the false ones: a serious allergy to a vaccine component or a previous anaphylactic reaction is a genuine contraindication; a mild cold, a runny nose, breastfeeding, or a family history of reactions is not.

Ask the immune-status question before every live vaccine. "Are you pregnant, could you be pregnant, are you on steroids or a biologic, or does anyone in your household have a weakened immune system?" A live vaccine given to the wrong person — or occasionally to someone living with the wrong person — can cause the disease it was meant to prevent.
Interactive Toggle between vaccines and immunostimulants and tap any agent to see what's given, what the immune system does with it, and what the nurse watches — seven vaccine types (live, inactivated, subunit, conjugate, toxoid, mRNA, plus passive immune globulin) and five immunostimulant classes (interferons, aldesleukin, colony-stimulating factors, checkpoint inhibitors, BCG/TLR agonists). Open full ↑
Key points
  • Filgrastim/pegfilgrastim rebuild neutrophils after chemo — expect bone pain, and never give within 24 hours of chemotherapy.
  • Checkpoint inhibitors (pembrolizumab, nivolumab, ipilimumab) release the immune brake; new diarrhea or dyspnea means an immune-related adverse event until proven otherwise.
  • Live vaccines (MMR, varicella, rotavirus, LAIV, yellow fever) are contraindicated in pregnancy and immunosuppression.
  • Active immunity (vaccines) is slow and lasting; passive immunity (immune globulin) is immediate and temporary.

Section 3Antineoplastic principles

The cell cycle, and why it is the target

Cancer is, at bottom, a disease of division that will not stop. So the oldest and still most widely used strategy against it is to attack division itself. To understand where each drug lands, you need the cycle. G1 is growth: the cell enlarges and manufactures the enzymes it will need. S is synthesis: DNA is copied, and the cell is at its most chemically vulnerable here. G2 is the second growth phase, building the spindle apparatus and proofreading the new DNA. M is mitosis, when the chromosomes are pulled apart and the cell splits in two. And G0 is the resting state — the cell is alive and working but not dividing. G0 is the hiding place. Cell-cycle–specific drugs cannot touch a cell in G0 at all, and even the nonspecific ones work on it poorly — a resting cell is not copying DNA and has time to repair the damage it does take. That sanctuary is a large part of why cancers relapse years later.

That geography splits antineoplastics into two groups. Cell-cycle–specific (CCS) drugs only work on a cell that is in a particular phase: antimetabolites act in S, mitotic inhibitors act in M, bleomycin acts in G2. Because they need to catch the cell in the act, they work best against fast-growing tumors and are often given as a continuous infusion or in divided doses to widen the window. Cell-cycle–nonspecific (CCNS) drugs — alkylating agents, antitumor antibiotics, platinum compounds — damage DNA whenever they find it, including in G0. They are typically given as a single large bolus, because the damage is dose-dependent rather than timing-dependent.

Log kill, growth fraction, and why chemo comes in cycles

Here is the idea that explains the entire structure of a chemotherapy regimen: a given dose of a drug kills a constant percentage of tumor cells, not a constant number. This is the log kill hypothesis, sometimes called fractional kill. If a dose kills 99.9 percent of a billion cancer cells, a million are still alive. The next cycle kills 99.9 percent of the million, leaving a thousand. You can see immediately why one round is never enough, why the schedule is a schedule and not a suggestion, and why a patient who feels wonderful after two cycles still needs the other four.

The gaps between cycles are equally deliberate. Normal marrow recovers from chemotherapy faster than tumor does, so a rest period of two to three weeks lets the patient's blood counts climb back while the tumor gets comparatively less benefit. Combination chemotherapy follows the same logic from a different angle: drugs are chosen with different mechanisms, different resistance patterns, and — crucially — non-overlapping toxicities, so that no single organ takes the whole hit. Dosing is calculated by body surface area in mg/m2 rather than by weight, and carboplatin is dosed by target AUC using the patient's renal function. Small errors in these calculations are not small; this is why chemotherapy orders require an independent double-check by a second qualified clinician.

Why chemotherapy hurts the patient too

Traditional chemotherapy cannot tell a cancer cell from a healthy cell. It can only tell a dividing cell from a resting one. That is the entire tragedy of the drug class in one sentence — and it lets you predict every major side effect before you ever open the drug book. Ask yourself which normal tissues in the body divide fast, and you have your answer.

Fast-dividing normal tissueWhat chemo does to itWhat the patient and nurse see
Bone marrowHalts production of neutrophils, platelets, RBCsMyelosuppression — infection risk, bleeding, fatigue; the dose-limiting toxicity of most regimens
GI mucosa (mouth to anus)Lining is not replaced as it sloughsMucositis, stomatitis, esophagitis, diarrhea, nausea
Hair folliclesMatrix cells stop dividing; the shaft breaksAlopecia — scalp, brows, lashes, body; temporary
Gonadal germ cellsSperm and oocyte production impairedInfertility, sometimes permanent — discuss banking before treatment starts
Skin and nailsBasal layer and nail matrix disruptedRash, photosensitivity, ridged or darkened nails, hand–foot syndrome
Fetal cellsEverything is dividingTeratogenicity — pregnancy must be excluded; effective contraception is required

Two more principles round out the picture. Growth fraction — the proportion of a tumor's cells actively cycling — predicts responsiveness: leukemias and lymphomas with high growth fractions melt away, while slow, bulky solid tumors respond far less. And drug resistance develops, most notoriously through multidrug resistance proteins such as P-glycoprotein, which sit in the cell membrane and pump the drug straight back out. Resistance is another reason we combine drugs and another reason we do not stretch out the schedule.

You can predict chemo toxicity without memorizing it. "Which of my patient's normal tissues divide fastest?" Marrow, gut lining, hair, gonads, skin. That single question gives you myelosuppression, mucositis and diarrhea, alopecia, infertility, and rash — the whole list, derived rather than memorized.
Interactive Tap a cell-cycle phase or a chemo drug class on an interactive cycle wheel to see which phases each class strikes — cell-cycle-specific vs nonspecific — with the nursing priority for every agent. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The cycle is G1 → S → G2 → M, with G0 as the resting phase that cell-cycle–specific drugs cannot reach at all and nonspecific drugs reach poorly — the source of relapse.
  • CCS drugs hit one phase (antimetabolites in S, mitotic inhibitors in M); CCNS drugs damage DNA in any phase, including G0.
  • The log kill hypothesis — each dose kills a percentage, not a number — is why treatment comes in repeated cycles and why finishing the regimen matters.
  • Chemo cannot distinguish cancer from healthy; it distinguishes dividing from resting, which is why marrow, gut, hair, and gonads suffer.

Section 4Chemotherapy classes

Damaging the DNA: alkylating agents and antitumor antibiotics

Alkylating agents are the oldest class — descendants of the sulfur mustard gas of the First World War, whose habit of destroying the bone marrow of exposed soldiers was the clue that led to the nitrogen mustards being tried against lymphoma in the 1940s. They attach an alkyl group to DNA, cross-linking the strands so the double helix can no longer unzip and copy itself. They are cell-cycle–nonspecific and broadly effective, and each subgroup carries a signature toxicity. Cyclophosphamide breaks down into acrolein, which sits in the bladder and burns it — hemorrhagic cystitis, prevented with aggressive hydration, frequent voiding, and the rescue agent mesna. Ifosfamide does the same and adds neurotoxicity. The nitrosoureas such as carmustine cross the blood–brain barrier (useful for brain tumors) and cause a notoriously delayed myelosuppression that nadirs four to six weeks out. The platinum compounds are grouped here by behavior: cisplatin is nephrotoxic, ototoxic, and one of the most emetogenic drugs in medicine; carboplatin trades that for marrow suppression; oxaliplatin produces a strange cold-triggered neuropathy — patients must be taught not to drink cold liquids or touch cold metal for days after a dose.

Antitumor antibiotics come from soil bacteria and intercalate into DNA, generating free radicals that shred it. The anthracyclines — doxorubicin, daunorubicin, epirubicin — are among the most useful drugs in oncology and among the most feared, because their cardiotoxicity is cumulative and largely irreversible. A lifetime dose ceiling (roughly 550 mg/m2 for doxorubicin) is tracked across the patient's entire history, a baseline ejection fraction is measured by echocardiogram or MUGA, and dexrazoxane may be given as a cardioprotectant. Doxorubicin is also a potent vesicant and turns urine red-orange for a day or two — harmless, but terrifying if nobody warned the patient. Bleomycin is the exception that proves the rule: it causes almost no myelosuppression, and instead causes pulmonary fibrosis, with its own cumulative lifetime limit and a baseline pulmonary function test.

Starving and stalling: antimetabolites and mitotic inhibitors

Antimetabolites are molecular impostors. They look enough like the natural building blocks of DNA that the cell picks them up and jams its own machinery — so they act in the S phase and are strongly cell-cycle–specific. Methotrexate blocks dihydrofolate reductase and starves the cell of folate; high-dose regimens are followed by leucovorin rescue, which restores folate to normal cells. Never give NSAIDs or salicylates with high-dose methotrexate — they delay its clearance and can cause fatal toxicity. 5-fluorouracil and its oral prodrug capecitabine cause mucositis, diarrhea, and the palms-and-soles redness of hand–foot syndrome. Cytarabine adds a distinctive cerebellar toxicity and a chemical conjunctivitis prevented with corticosteroid eye drops.

Mitotic inhibitors attack the spindle in the M phase, and they do it from opposite directions. The vinca alkaloids (vincristine, vinblastine) prevent microtubules from assembling; the taxanes (paclitaxel, docetaxel) do the reverse, freezing microtubules so they cannot disassemble. Either way, mitosis stops. Vincristine's dose-limiting problem is peripheral neuropathy — numb fingertips, foot drop, and an autonomic constipation that can progress to ileus, which is why a bowel regimen goes out with the drug. And vincristine carries the most absolute rule in oncology nursing: it is labeled "For intravenous use only — fatal if given by other routes," because intrathecal administration causes ascending paralysis and death. Taxanes cause hypersensitivity reactions from their solvent, so patients are premedicated with a corticosteroid, an antihistamine, and an H2 blocker, and paclitaxel is infused through non-PVC tubing with an in-line filter.

Aiming instead of carpet-bombing: targeted and hormonal therapy

Everything above kills dividing cells indiscriminately. Targeted therapy asks a better question: what is broken in this tumor specifically? Imatinib shuts down the BCR-ABL kinase that drives chronic myeloid leukemia and turned a fatal disease into a manageable one. Trastuzumab targets HER2-positive breast cancer and causes cardiotoxicity that is, unlike doxorubicin's, usually reversible — but the two together are a bad combination. Rituximab targets CD20 on B cells and can reactivate hepatitis B, so screening comes first. Bevacizumab starves tumors of blood supply by blocking VEGF, at the cost of hypertension, bleeding, impaired wound healing, and rare GI perforation. Targeted drugs are gentler on the marrow but not gentle — they simply trade one toxicity profile for another. Hormonal agents work the same logic on hormone-driven tumors: tamoxifen blocks estrogen at the breast (and raises the risk of endometrial cancer and clots), the aromatase inhibitors anastrozole and letrozole cut estrogen production in postmenopausal women (at the cost of joint pain and bone loss), and leuprolide suppresses testosterone in prostate cancer after an initial tumor flare.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Alkylating agentCyclophosphamideCross-links DNA (CCNS)Hemorrhagic cystitis — hydrate aggressively, void often, give mesna; report pink or red urine
Platinum compoundCisplatin; carboplatin; oxaliplatinCross-links DNA (CCNS)Cisplatin: nephro- and ototoxic, highly emetogenic — pre-hydrate, monitor creatinine and hearing; oxaliplatin: avoid all cold — drinks, air, metal
AntimetaboliteMethotrexate; 5-fluorouracil; cytarabineFake nucleotides jam DNA synthesis (S phase)Leucovorin rescue after high-dose methotrexate; no NSAIDs/salicylates with it; mucositis and hand–foot syndrome; assess the mouth daily
Antitumor antibioticDoxorubicinIntercalates DNA, generates free radicals (CCNS)Cumulative irreversible cardiotoxicity — track lifetime dose, baseline echo/MUGA; potent vesicant; red-orange urine is harmless — warn the patient

Vinca alkaloidVincristineBlocks microtubule assembly (M phase)FATAL IF GIVEN INTRATHECALLY — IV use only; peripheral neuropathy and constipation/ileus — start a bowel regimen; vesicant
