Alcohol and benzodiazepine withdrawal can kill; opioid withdrawal usually cannot. That single line reorders your priorities on admission. But the patient sweating through opioid withdrawal deserves treatment too — not because the withdrawal is dangerous, but because untreated withdrawal sends them back out the door to a dose their body can no longer survive.
Interactive Tap any of six substances and switch between intoxication, withdrawal, and treatment to watch the patient's pupils and vital signs change — and learn which agent each one calls for. Open full ↑
Key points
  • All substances of misuse converge on the mesolimbic reward pathway; use person-first, non-stigmatizing language — disclosure is a safety intervention.
  • Alcohol withdrawal: tremor → seizures → delirium tremens (48–96 h) and can be fatal. Treat with benzodiazepines guided by CIWA-Ar, and give thiamine before glucose.
  • Opioid withdrawal is rarely fatal but drives relapse into overdose; buprenorphine, methadone, and naltrexone are the evidence-based treatments — and buprenorphine given too early precipitates withdrawal.
  • Benzodiazepine withdrawal can be fatal — always taper. Stimulant withdrawal is not physically dangerous, but screen for suicidality.
  • Smoking cessation: NRT, varenicline, bupropion — and bupropion is contraindicated with seizure or eating disorders.

The chapter in one breath

  • Nociception runs transduction → transmission → perception → modulation, and each analgesic class interrupts a different step — which is exactly why multimodal analgesia works better than a bigger dose of one drug.
  • Self-report is the gold standard; match the scale to the patient, assess function, and reassess after every dose — vital signs are not a pain scale.
  • Non-opioids have a ceiling effect: more milligrams buy toxicity, not relief. Acetaminophen hides in combination products and kills livers (antidote acetylcysteine); NSAIDs bleed stomachs, injure kidneys, and raise cardiovascular risk.
  • Opioid mu agonism gives analgesia and respiratory depression from the same receptor, with no ceiling on either — and sedation always comes before the respiratory rate falls.
  • Naloxone reverses in minutes but wears off before the opioid does; titrate to respiratory rate and keep monitoring for re-sedation. Tolerance never develops to constipation — order the laxative on day one.
  • Neuropathic pain needs adjuvants — gabapentinoids, TCAs, duloxetine, lidocaine patch — not more opioid. Triptans abort migraine and are contraindicated in coronary artery disease; preventives are taken daily and judged over weeks.
  • Tolerance and physical dependence are expected pharmacology; substance use disorder is compulsive use despite harm — and the words you choose determine whether the patient tells you the truth.
  • Alcohol and sedative withdrawal can be fatal (benzodiazepines, CIWA-Ar, thiamine before glucose); opioid withdrawal rarely is, but it drives the relapse that kills — treat it, and send naloxone home.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adjuvant analgesic
A drug whose primary indication is not pain but which relieves it in certain conditions — gabapentinoids, TCAs, SNRIs, corticosteroids; first-line for neuropathic pain.
Agonist-antagonist
An opioid that activates kappa receptors while blocking mu (nalbuphine, butorphanol); has a ceiling on respiratory depression and can precipitate withdrawal in opioid-dependent patients.
Breakthrough pain
A transient flare of pain over an otherwise controlled baseline, treated with a short-acting opioid in addition to the long-acting regimen.
Ceiling effect
The dose beyond which more drug produces no further effect. With non-opioids the ceiling is on analgesia while toxicity keeps climbing, so more milligrams buy only harm; with partial agonists such as buprenorphine the ceiling is on respiratory depression, which is a safety advantage.
CIWA-Ar
A validated scoring tool for alcohol withdrawal severity used to trigger benzodiazepine dosing.
COWS
The Clinical Opiate Withdrawal Scale, used to grade opioid withdrawal — including the moderate threshold required before starting buprenorphine.
Cyclooxygenase (COX)
The enzyme that makes prostaglandins; COX-1 protects stomach, kidney, and platelets, COX-2 drives inflammation, and NSAIDs inhibit one or both.
Delirium tremens
Severe alcohol withdrawal at roughly 48–96 hours — confusion, hallucinations, fever, autonomic instability; a medical emergency with real mortality.
Endorphin
An endogenous opioid peptide released as part of descending pain modulation; acts at the same receptors as morphine.
Gate control theory
The idea that stimulating large touch fibers partially closes a dorsal-horn "gate" on pain transmission — the rationale for massage, heat, cold, and TENS.
Mu receptor
The opioid receptor responsible for analgesia, euphoria, sedation, respiratory depression, constipation, miosis, and physical dependence — analgesia and respiratory depression come from the same receptor, which is why they cannot be separated.
Multimodal analgesia
Combining drugs with different mechanisms plus non-drug measures so each contributes at a lower, safer dose; the basis of opioid sparing.
NAPQI
The toxic acetaminophen metabolite normally neutralized by glutathione; in overdose it accumulates and causes hepatic necrosis.
Neuropathic pain
Pain from damaged or misfiring nerves — burning, shooting, electric — that responds poorly to opioids and NSAIDs and well to adjuvants.
Nociception
The physiologic detection and transmission of a noxious stimulus: transduction, transmission, perception, and modulation.
Nociceptive pain
Pain from actual tissue damage sensed by intact nerves; subdivided into somatic and visceral.
Opioid-induced constipation
Slowed gut motility from mu receptor activation; the one opioid effect to which tolerance does not develop, requiring a scheduled stimulant laxative.
Physical dependence
Physiologic adaptation such that abrupt cessation or an antagonist produces withdrawal; expected with sustained opioid therapy and not the same as addiction.
Pseudoaddiction
Drug-seeking behavior caused by inadequate pain treatment that resolves once the pain is adequately controlled.
Reye syndrome
Acute encephalopathy with fatty liver linked to aspirin use in children and teens during a viral illness; the reason aspirin is avoided in that group.
Substance use disorder
A diagnosable pattern of compulsive use, craving, loss of control, and continued use despite harm — distinct from tolerance and physical dependence.
Tolerance
A diminishing effect from the same dose over time, requiring dose escalation; expected pharmacology, not addiction.

Check yourself

1. Name the four steps of nociception and state where NSAIDs, opioids, and antidepressant adjuvants each intervene.
Transduction, transmission, perception, modulation. NSAIDs act at transduction (blocking the prostaglandins that sensitize nociceptors); opioids act mainly at transmission and perception (dorsal horn and brain); antidepressant adjuvants act at modulation, strengthening the descending serotonin/norepinephrine brake.
2. A patient with chronic low back pain reports 8/10 pain. Her heart rate is 70, her blood pressure is normal, and she is laughing with a visitor. What do you do, and why?
Believe her and treat the pain. In chronic pain the sympathetic response adapts away, so normal vital signs and a calm appearance are expected findings — not evidence against pain. Self-report is the gold standard; vital signs are not a pain scale.
3. Which pain scale would you choose for (a) a 3-year-old, (b) an intubated, sedated ICU patient, and (c) a patient with advanced dementia?
(a) Wong-Baker FACES or FLACC, (b) CPOT or BPS, (c) PAINAD. A number is meaningless if the patient cannot give you one — defaulting to 0–10 for these patients is how pain gets missed.
4. Explain the ceiling effect and use it to answer a patient who says, "Two ibuprofen didn't work, so I took four."
Past the maximum dose a non-opioid gives no additional analgesia, but toxicity keeps rising. The extra tablets add GI bleeding and kidney risk without adding relief. The right move is to add a different mechanism — scheduled acetaminophen, an adjuvant, heat or ice — not more of the same drug.
5. A patient takes oxycodone/acetaminophen every 6 hours plus a nighttime cold remedy plus 650 mg of over-the-counter acetaminophen for headaches. What is your concern, what is the mechanism, and what is the antidote?
Cumulative acetaminophen overdose from hidden sources, causing hepatotoxicity; the antidote is acetylcysteine. Excess acetaminophen produces NAPQI faster than glutathione can neutralize it, and NAPQI causes centrilobular hepatic necrosis. Max is 4 g/day in a healthy adult (many teach 3 g/day; 2 g/day or less with liver disease or heavy alcohol use), and acetylcysteine works best within 8–10 hours of ingestion.
6. Why does an NSAID cause GI bleeding and acute kidney injury and impaired clotting — from one mechanism?
Because COX-1 blockade removes the housekeeping prostaglandins. Those prostaglandins protect gastric mucosa (so bleeding), dilate the renal afferent arteriole (so GFR falls — especially with an ACE inhibitor plus a diuretic, the "triple whammy"), and enable thromboxane-driven platelet aggregation (so clotting is impaired). One enzyme, three organs.
7. Your postoperative patient on IV morphine is hard to rouse but has a respiratory rate of 14. What is happening and what do you do?
This is advancing opioid-induced sedation — the warning sign that precedes respiratory depression. The rate of 14 is reassuring only if you misread it: sedation comes first, and a rate that is still normal is exactly what you expect to see before it falls. Hold the next dose, stimulate and monitor the patient, apply oxygen and continuous monitoring, escalate the sedation score (POSS), notify the provider, and have naloxone available. Waiting for the rate to fall is waiting too long.
8. You give naloxone to a patient found unresponsive with pinpoint pupils. Two minutes later he is awake and wants to leave. Why is this the most dangerous moment?
Naloxone lasts only about 30–90 minutes — shorter than most opioids — so the patient can re-sedate as it wears off while opioid is still on board. Keep him under observation, be prepared to redose or start an infusion, and titrate naloxone to respiratory rate rather than full consciousness to avoid precipitating severe withdrawal and pain.
9. Which opioid adverse effect does tolerance not develop to, and what does that mean for your very first order set?
Constipation. The bowel never adapts, so a stimulant laxative such as senna is started with the first opioid dose — not after the patient is impacted. For refractory cases, peripherally acting antagonists (methylnaltrexone, naloxegol) relieve the constipation without reversing analgesia.
10. A patient on chronic morphine for cancer pain needs escalating doses and would go into withdrawal if it stopped. Is this addiction? Define your terms.
No — that is tolerance plus physical dependence, which are expected pharmacology. Tolerance = the same dose produces less effect; physical dependence = withdrawal on abrupt cessation. Substance use disorder requires compulsive use, craving, loss of control, and continued use despite harm. Confusing these leads to under-treated pain.
11. A patient with burning, shooting foot pain from diabetic neuropathy asks for "something stronger." Why is a bigger opioid dose the wrong answer, and what is the right one?
Neuropathic pain responds poorly to opioids; adjuvants are first-line. Reach for a gabapentinoid (gabapentin or pregabalin — renally dosed, watch dizziness and falls, taper on discontinuation) or duloxetine, or a topical lidocaine patch. Teach that adjuvants take days to weeks and are given for the pain, not because the pain is imagined.
12. A patient admitted 60 hours after his last drink becomes confused, febrile, hallucinating, and hypertensive. Name the syndrome, the treatment, and the vitamin rule.
Delirium tremens — treat with benzodiazepines, guided by CIWA-Ar, and give thiamine before glucose. DTs appear at roughly 48–96 hours and can be fatal. Benzodiazepines restore the GABA tone that chronic alcohol suppressed; giving glucose to a thiamine-depleted patient can precipitate Wernicke encephalopathy, so thiamine goes first. Also replace folate and magnesium.
Pharmacology · Chapter 5

Mental Health, Antiepileptic & Anesthetic Drugs

Every drug in this chapter works in the same place: the tiny gap between two nerve cells. Speed a chemical signal up, slow it down, or silence it entirely, and you change mood, thought, movement, awareness, and pain. That is enormous power, and it comes with enormous responsibility — these are the drugs that cause serotonin syndrome, that stop a seizure, that stop breathing. This chapter walks you from the synapse outward: what the neurotransmitters do, what each drug class does to them, and what you — the nurse holding the syringe and watching the monitor — do about it.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe how a chemical synapse works and name the four neurotransmitters most psychotropic drugs target.
  • Compare SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, and MAOIs by mechanism, adverse effects, and teaching priorities.
  • Recognize serotonin syndrome early and describe the nursing response.
  • Explain why lithium is a high-alert drug and state its therapeutic range, toxicity signs, and monitoring plan.
  • Distinguish typical from atypical antipsychotics, and identify EPS, tardive dyskinesia, and neuroleptic malignant syndrome.
  • Describe how benzodiazepines work, why they are habit-forming, and what to do in an overdose.
  • Explain the three mechanisms by which antiepileptic drugs stop seizures, and monitor the major agents safely.
  • Compare general and local anesthesia and describe nursing priorities in each stage of anesthesia.

Section 1Neurotransmitters & drug targets

The synapse is the whole story

Neurons do not touch. Where one ends and the next begins there is a microscopic gap — the synaptic cleft — and the message crosses it as a puff of chemical called a neurotransmitter. The sending (presynaptic) neuron packages the transmitter into vesicles, dumps them into the cleft when an electrical impulse arrives, and the transmitter drifts across and binds receptors on the receiving (postsynaptic) neuron. Then it must be cleared, or the signal never turns off. Clearance happens two ways: the transmitter is vacuumed back into the sending neuron by a reuptake pump, or it is chopped up by an enzyme.

That handful of steps is your entire map of psychiatric pharmacology. There is no drug that "fixes depression." There are drugs that block a reuptake pump, drugs that inhibit a degrading enzyme, drugs that occupy a receptor so the real transmitter cannot, and drugs that make a receptor more responsive than it would otherwise be. Once you know which step a drug touches, its effects — wanted and unwanted — stop being a list to memorize and start being something you can reason out.

Two vocabulary words earn their keep here. An agonist binds a receptor and turns it on, imitating the natural transmitter. An antagonist binds the receptor and turns nothing on — it just sits there, blocking the real transmitter from docking. Most antipsychotics are antagonists. Most antidepressants are neither: they leave more transmitter in the cleft by blocking its removal.

The four transmitters that matter most

Serotonin (5-HT) is the mood, sleep, appetite, and anxiety transmitter — and, importantly, it is not confined to the brain. Most of the body's serotonin lives in the gut, which is exactly why serotonergic drugs cause nausea and diarrhea, and why too much serotonin produces the gastrointestinal storm you will read about shortly. Dopamine runs four different circuits: reward and motivation, fine motor control (the basal nuclei), hormone regulation (it suppresses prolactin), and a brainstem trigger zone for vomiting. Block dopamine and you will hit all four, which is why antipsychotics cause both the effect you want and the movement disorders you do not.

Norepinephrine (NE) is alertness, drive, energy, and blood pressure — the internal accelerator. Boost it and depressed patients gain energy; boost it too much and they get tachycardic, tremulous, and hypertensive. GABA (gamma-aminobutyric acid) is the brain's brake pedal, the main inhibitory transmitter. When GABA binds its receptor, chloride flows into the neuron and makes it harder to fire. Nearly every sedative, anxiolytic, many antiepileptics, and most general anesthetics work by pushing on that brake — which is also why they all stack on top of one another and why combining them can stop a patient's breathing.

TransmitterWhat it doesToo little looks likeToo much looks like
SerotoninMood, sleep, appetite, anxiety, GI motilityDepression, anxiety, poor sleepSerotonin syndrome — agitation, clonus, hyperthermia, diarrhea
DopamineReward, movement, prolactin suppression, vomiting triggerParkinsonism, flat affect, high prolactin (blockade)Psychosis, hallucinations, dyskinesia
NorepinephrineAlertness, drive, blood pressureFatigue, low motivationTachycardia, tremor, hypertension, insomnia
GABAMain inhibitory brake; raises seizure thresholdAnxiety, seizures, withdrawal statesSedation, ataxia, respiratory depression
AcetylcholineMemory, GI motility, secretions, bladder emptyingDry mouth, constipation, urinary retention, blurred vision, confusionSLUDGE — salivation, lacrimation, urination, defecation, GI upset, emesis

Notice that last row. Acetylcholine is not usually the target of these drugs — it is the innocent bystander. Older antidepressants and older antipsychotics block acetylcholine receptors by accident, and that accident produces the anticholinergic effects your patient will actually complain about: the cotton mouth, the constipation, the inability to urinate, and in an older adult, the confusion that gets mistaken for dementia.

Ask "which step?" before you memorize anything. Every drug in this chapter blocks a pump, blocks an enzyme, blocks a receptor, or modulates an ion channel. Fluoxetine blocks a pump. Phenelzine blocks an enzyme. Haloperidol blocks a receptor. Lorazepam modulates a channel — it does not open the chloride channel itself, it makes GABA better at opening it. Name the step and you can predict the side effects without a flashcard.
Interactive Tap serotonin, dopamine, norepinephrine, GABA, glutamate, or acetylcholine to explore a live synapse, then tap any drug class to light up exactly where it acts — the pump, the enzyme, the vesicle, the ion channel, or the receptor. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Transmitters cross the synaptic cleft, bind receptors, and are cleared by a reuptake pump or an enzyme — every psychotropic drug touches one of those steps.
  • Agonists turn a receptor on; antagonists block it. Antidepressants mostly do neither — they block clearance.
  • Serotonin = mood & gut; dopamine = reward, movement, prolactin, vomiting; norepinephrine = drive & blood pressure; GABA = the brake.
  • Anticholinergic effects (dry mouth, constipation, retention, blurred vision, confusion) are usually an accident of older drugs, not their purpose.

Section 2Antidepressants & mood stabilizers

Four generations of the same idea

All four antidepressant classes raise the amount of serotonin and/or norepinephrine hanging around in the synapse. They differ almost entirely in how selectively they do it — and selectivity is safety. The classes came in the order they did because each generation learned to hit the target and miss the bystanders.

SSRIs (selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors) — fluoxetine, sertraline, escitalopram, paroxetine, citalopram — block only the serotonin pump. They are first-line because they are hard to die from, not because they work better than the old drugs. Expect nausea and headache in the first week (the gut is full of serotonin receptors), insomnia or drowsiness, and, in the long run, sexual dysfunction — the single most common reason patients quietly stop taking them. Ask about it directly and without embarrassment; a patient who feels able to say "this is ruining my sex life" is a patient who can be switched to something else instead of relapsing in silence.

SNRIs (venlafaxine, duloxetine) block the serotonin pump and the norepinephrine pump. That second target adds energy and blunts neuropathic pain — duloxetine is genuinely useful in diabetic neuropathy and fibromyalgia — but it also adds hypertension. Check a blood pressure at every visit on venlafaxine.

TCAs (amitriptyline, nortriptyline, imipramine) block both pumps too, but they also blunder into histamine, acetylcholine, and alpha-1 receptors. So: sedation, anticholinergic misery, and orthostatic hypotension. Their real danger is cardiac — in overdose they block sodium channels in the heart, widen the QRS, and cause fatal dysrhythmias. A widening QRS is the signal to act, and the treatment is IV sodium bicarbonate, which overcomes the sodium-channel blockade. A one-week supply can kill, which makes them a poor choice for a patient at risk of suicide. MAOIs (phenelzine, tranylcypromine, selegiline) take the enzyme route: they disable monoamine oxidase, the enzyme that destroys serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine. Effective, and hemmed in by a diet.

The MAOI diet, and why it is not optional

Monoamine oxidase in your gut wall exists to destroy tyramine, an amine in aged and fermented food. Disable that enzyme and tyramine walks into the bloodstream, floods nerve terminals, and dumps stored norepinephrine all at once. The result is a hypertensive crisis: a pounding occipital headache, blood pressure that can exceed 180/120, stiff neck, sweating, and the real possibility of a stroke. Teach the list — aged cheeses, cured and smoked meats, tap and craft beer, red wine, soy sauce, sauerkraut, fava beans, overripe fruit, yeast extracts — and teach it as though it were an allergy list, because functionally it is.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
SSRIFluoxetine, sertralineBlocks serotonin reuptake onlyNausea & headache early; sexual dysfunction long-term; taper — do not stop abruptly
SNRIVenlafaxine, duloxetineBlocks serotonin and norepinephrine reuptake; helps neuropathic painMonitor blood pressure — venlafaxine raises it dose-dependently
TCAAmitriptyline, nortriptylineBlocks both pumps; also blocks histamine, ACh, alpha-1Lethal in overdose — widened QRS, dysrhythmias; treat with IV sodium bicarbonate. Sedation, anticholinergic effects, orthostasis
MAOIPhenelzineInhibits the enzyme that destroys all three monoaminesTyramine-free diet or hypertensive crisis; 2-week washout to/from serotonergic drugs — but 5 weeks after fluoxetine (long half-life)
AtypicalBupropionNE/dopamine reuptake; no sexual dysfunction; aids smoking cessationLowers seizure threshold — avoid in seizure disorder, bulimia, anorexia
AtypicalMirtazapineSedating; increases appetiteUseful when depression comes with insomnia and weight loss; give at bedtime
Mood stabilizerLithiumDamps the highs and lows of bipolar disorderHigh-alert: narrow therapeutic range 0.6–1.2 mEq/L; steady salt & fluid intake

The two-week lie, and the black box

Every antidepressant raises transmitter levels within hours, yet mood does not lift for two to six weeks — the therapeutic effect requires slow receptor adaptation downstream. Patients do not know this. They take a pill for eight days, feel nothing but nausea, and quit. Telling them up front — "the side effects come first and fade, the benefit comes later and lasts" — is one of the highest-yield things a nurse says all shift.

The gap is also dangerous. Antidepressants carry a boxed warning for increased suicidal thinking in patients under 25, and there is a mechanical reason the risk is front-loaded: energy returns before mood does. A profoundly depressed patient may lack the drive to act on suicidal thoughts; two weeks in, the drive is back and the hopelessness is not. Assess suicidality at every contact during that window, not just at intake.

Two more habits. Do not stop an SSRI or SNRI abruptly — discontinuation syndrome (dizziness, flu-like aches, electric-shock "brain zaps," irritability) is miserable and easily avoided by tapering; paroxetine and venlafaxine are the worst offenders because of short half-lives. And screen for bipolar disorder before starting: an antidepressant given alone to a patient with undiagnosed bipolar disorder can flip them into mania.

Serotonin syndrome

Serotonin syndrome is too much serotonergic activity, usually from combining agents — an SSRI plus tramadol, plus triptans for migraine, plus linezolid, plus St. John's wort, plus an MAOI. It arrives fast, typically within hours of the new drug or dose increase, and it is recognized by a triad: mental status change (agitation, confusion), autonomic instability (fever, tachycardia, diaphoresis, diarrhea), and neuromuscular hyperactivity (tremor, hyperreflexia, and clonus — the most specific sign, often worst in the legs). Stop the serotonergic drugs, call the provider, support with cooling and IV fluids, benzodiazepines for agitation and rigidity, and cyproheptadine as the serotonin-blocking antidote in severe cases.

Lithium is salt, and the kidney cannot tell the difference. Lithium is reabsorbed by the same tubular pathway as sodium, so anything that makes the body hoard sodium makes it hoard lithium — dehydration, vomiting, a low-salt diet, a new thiazide, an NSAID, an ACE inhibitor, a hot day with heavy sweating. Teach a consistent intake: 2–3 liters of fluid and a steady, normal salt intake every day. "I started a diet" or "I'm on ibuprofen for my back" is a lithium level waiting to climb.

Lithium levels track a narrow road: 0.6–1.2 mEq/L for maintenance, with anything over 1.5 mEq/L counting as toxic and over 2.0 mEq/L a medical emergency. Draw levels as troughs, 12 hours after the evening dose. Expected effects at therapeutic levels are a fine hand tremor, mild thirst, and increased urination. Early toxicity is GI — nausea, vomiting, diarrhea — plus a coarse tremor and slurred speech; later comes ataxia, confusion, seizures, and cardiovascular collapse. There is no antidote; treatment is fluids and, at high levels, hemodialysis. Because lithium is cleared renally and affects the thyroid, baseline and periodic renal function, TSH, and pregnancy status belong in every monitoring plan. Valproate and lamotrigine also serve as mood stabilizers — both carry their own warnings, covered next section over.

Interactive Compare SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, MAOIs and the atypicals at a clickable synapse, then drive a lithium level from therapeutic to dialysis and build the drug combinations that tip a patient into serotonin syndrome. Open full ↑
Key points
  • All antidepressants raise synaptic serotonin and/or norepinephrine; selectivity equals safety, which is why SSRIs are first-line.
  • Mood lifts in 2–6 weeks while side effects come first — and the boxed warning for suicidality in patients under 25 exists because energy returns before mood.
  • Serotonin syndrome = mental status change + autonomic instability + neuromuscular hyperactivity (clonus); stop the drug, cool, hydrate, benzodiazepines, cyproheptadine.
  • Lithium is high-alert: range 0.6–1.2 mEq/L, trough draws, steady salt and fluid, watch NSAIDs, thiazides, and dehydration; monitor renal function and TSH.

Section 3Antipsychotics & anxiolytics

Typical antipsychotics: dopamine blockade, and the price of it

Typical (first-generation) antipsychotics — haloperidol, fluphenazine, chlorpromazine — are dopamine D2 antagonists, and they are good at exactly one thing: the positive symptoms of psychosis. Hallucinations, delusions, and disorganized thought are the "added" symptoms, and blocking dopamine strips them away. What these drugs barely touch are the negative symptoms — the flat affect, poverty of speech, social withdrawal, and lost motivation. That distinction matters at the bedside, because a patient whose voices are gone but who still cannot get out of bed is not treatment-resistant; they are inadequately treated.

The price is movement. Dopamine blockade in the basal nuclei — the circuit in the reference figure above — produces extrapyramidal symptoms (EPS), and they arrive in a predictable order that is worth knowing because the timing tells you what you are looking at.

EPS typeWhenWhat you seeNursing action
Acute dystoniaHours to daysSudden muscle spasm — neck twisting, eyes rolled up (oculogyric crisis), laryngospasmEmergency. Airway first; give IM/IV diphenhydramine or benztropine
AkathisiaDays to weeksUnbearable inner restlessness; pacing, cannot sit stillOften mistaken for worsening psychosis — do not raise the dose. Beta blocker or benzodiazepine; consider switching
ParkinsonismWeeksTremor, rigidity, bradykinesia, mask-like face, shuffling gaitFall risk. Anticholinergic (benztropine) or amantadine; dose reduction
Tardive dyskinesiaMonths to yearsInvoluntary lip-smacking, tongue-thrusting, facial grimacing, writhingOften irreversible. Screen with AIMS — early detection is what preserves the chance of reversal. A VMAT2 inhibitor (valbenazine, deutetrabenazine) can treat it; anticholinergics make it worse

Two other consequences follow straight from the dopamine map. Dopamine normally suppresses prolactin, so blocking it raises prolactin: expect galactorrhea, amenorrhea, gynecomastia, and sexual dysfunction — embarrassing effects that patients hide and then stop the drug over. And neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS) is the rare, life-threatening reaction: fever (often above 40 °C), lead-pipe rigidity, altered consciousness, autonomic instability, and a sky-high creatine kinase from muscle breakdown. Stop the antipsychotic, cool aggressively, hydrate, and expect dantrolene or bromocriptine. NMS and serotonin syndrome look alike; the tell is that NMS is rigid and slow (days, lead-pipe rigidity, hyporeflexia), while serotonin syndrome is fast and jumpy (hours, clonus, hyperreflexia, diarrhea).

Atypicals: fewer movements, more metabolism

Atypical (second-generation) antipsychotics — risperidone, olanzapine, quetiapine, aripiprazole, ziprasidone, clozapine — block dopamine D2 and serotonin 5-HT2A receptors (aripiprazole is the exception: it is a D2 partial agonist, which is why it is less sedating and rarely raises prolactin), and that second action buys them two advantages: less EPS and some genuine effect on negative symptoms. They did not, however, come free. They traded movement problems for metabolic syndrome: weight gain, new-onset type 2 diabetes, and dyslipidemia. Olanzapine and clozapine are the worst; ziprasidone and aripiprazole are gentler. That means metabolic monitoring is nursing work — baseline and periodic weight, waist circumference, blood pressure, fasting glucose or A1C, and a lipid panel.

Clozapine is the one to know cold. It is the most effective antipsychotic that exists, reserved for treatment-resistant schizophrenia, and it can cause severe neutropenia (agranulocytosis) — which is why it is dispensed only through a REMS program with mandatory absolute neutrophil count monitoring. A fever or a sore throat in a patient on clozapine is not a cold until an ANC proves it. It also causes myocarditis, seizures at higher doses, severe constipation that can progress to ileus, and, oddly, drooling. All antipsychotics carry a boxed warning against use in elderly patients with dementia-related psychosis because of increased mortality.

Benzodiazepines and the GABA brake

Benzodiazepines — lorazepam, diazepam, alprazolam, midazolam, clonazepam — do not open the chloride channel themselves. They are positive allosteric modulators: they sit alongside the GABA receptor and make GABA's own effect stronger, increasing how frequently the chloride channel opens when GABA binds. That mechanism has a built-in ceiling, and it is the reason a benzodiazepine alone is fairly hard to die from — without GABA present, the drug does nothing. Add an opioid or alcohol, which push on the same brake by other routes, and the ceiling disappears. Most benzodiazepine deaths are combination deaths.

They are excellent short-term drugs: rapid anxiety relief, sedation, muscle relaxation, seizure termination, and alcohol-withdrawal management. They are poor long-term drugs: tolerance develops, dependence follows, and abrupt withdrawal after chronic use can cause seizures and death — a benzodiazepine taper is not a courtesy, it is a safety measure. In older adults they are on the Beers list for a reason: falls, hip fractures, and confusion. Flumazenil reverses them, but use it cautiously — in a chronic user or a mixed overdose it can precipitate intractable seizures. The safer non-benzodiazepine option for chronic anxiety is buspirone: no sedation, no dependence, no reversal needed — but it takes weeks to work and is useless as a rescue.

Restlessness on an antipsychotic is a side effect until proven otherwise. Akathisia gets misread as agitation, the dose goes up, the akathisia gets worse, and the cycle repeats. Ask the patient where the restlessness lives: psychotic agitation is in the mind, akathisia is in the legs — "I have to move, I can't explain it." That one question prevents a great deal of harm, and akathisia is linked to suicidality.
Interactive Drag D2 receptor occupancy down all four dopamine pathways to see why typicals cause EPS and atypicals cause metabolic syndrome, step the EPS clock from acute dystonia to tardive dyskinesia and NMS, then open the GABA-A chloride channel with a benzodiazepine. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Typicals block dopamine — good for positive symptoms, heavy on EPS: dystonia (hours) → akathisia (days) → parkinsonism (weeks) → tardive dyskinesia (months, often irreversible).
  • Atypicals block dopamine and serotonin — less EPS, more metabolic syndrome; monitor weight, glucose, and lipids.
  • Clozapine is the most effective and the most dangerous — REMS-mandated ANC monitoring for agranulocytosis; treat fever or sore throat as an emergency.
  • NMS = fever + lead-pipe rigidity + altered mental status; it is slow and stiff, while serotonin syndrome is fast and jumpy with clonus.
  • Benzodiazepines amplify GABA (frequency of channel opening); dangerous with opioids/alcohol, never stop abruptly, reversal is flumazenil with caution.

Section 4Antiepileptic drugs

Three ways to stop a seizure

A seizure is a neuronal riot: a population of neurons firing too fast, too synchronously, and spreading that firing to their neighbors. There are only three fundamental ways a drug can quiet it, and essentially every antiepileptic drug (AED) — the word anticonvulsant is used interchangeably — uses at least one of them.

Slow the sodium channels. A neuron cannot fire without opening voltage-gated sodium channels, and after each firing those channels need a moment to reset. Phenytoin, carbamazepine, lamotrigine, and others prolong that inactive state, so a neuron can still fire at a normal rate but cannot fire at a runaway rate. That is elegant: normal traffic passes, the riot does not. Slow the calcium channels. The T-type calcium channels in the thalamus drive the rhythmic 3-per-second discharge of absence seizures; ethosuximide blocks them, which is why it works for absence seizures and nothing else. Boost GABA. More inhibition raises the seizure threshold for the whole brain — the mechanism of benzodiazepines, phenobarbital, and part of what valproate does.

Two nursing consequences fall out of this. First, most AEDs are broad brakes on brain excitability, so sedation, dizziness, and ataxia are class effects, and fall precautions and a no-driving-until-stable conversation belong in the plan. Second, AEDs are prevention, not rescue — missed doses are the single most common reason a controlled patient seizes. Never abruptly discontinue an AED; abrupt withdrawal can trigger status epilepticus in a patient with epilepsy, and withdrawal seizures even in a patient taking the drug for pain or mood who has never had a seizure in their life.

DrugMechanismKey effect / useNursing alert
PhenytoinNa channelTonic-clonic & focal seizuresLevel 10–20 mcg/mL; gingival hyperplasia (teach flossing), hirsutism, ataxia/nystagmus = toxicity; IV only in normal saline (precipitates in dextrose), push slowly — hypotension & dysrhythmia; many interactions
FosphenytoinNa channelIV/IM prodrug of phenytoinSafer IV option; dosed in PE (phenytoin equivalents) — a classic dosing error
CarbamazepineNa channelFocal seizures; trigeminal neuralgia; bipolarStevens–Johnson syndrome (screen HLA-B*1502 in patients of Asian descent); agranulocytosis, aplastic anemia; hyponatremia (SIADH); autoinduces its own metabolism
Valproic acidNa, Ca, & GABABroad spectrum; bipolar; migraine prophylaxisLevel 50–100 mcg/mL; hepatotoxicity and pancreatitis; highly teratogenic — neural tube defects; monitor LFTs and ammonia
LamotrigineNa channelBroad spectrum; bipolar maintenanceTitrate slowly — rapid escalation causes SJS/TEN; any rash stops the drug and gets reported same day
LevetiracetamSV2A vesicle proteinBroad spectrum; few interactions; a workhorseBehavioral effects — irritability, agitation, mood change; ask the family, not just the chart
EthosuximideT-type Ca channelAbsence seizures onlyGI upset, drowsiness; ineffective for tonic-clonic
PhenobarbitalGABA (duration)Refractory seizures; neonatal seizuresHeavy sedation; strong enzyme inducer; respiratory depression
Lorazepam / diazepamGABA (frequency)Acute seizure & status epilepticus — the rescue drugsWatch respirations; have airway support ready; not for maintenance

Monitoring, teaching, and the seizure itself

Phenytoin and valproic acid have narrow therapeutic ranges and get serum levels; lamotrigine and levetiracetam generally do not. Draw levels as troughs, just before the next dose, and remember that phenytoin is heavily protein-bound — in a patient with low albumin, the total level can look normal while the free (active) drug is toxic. If the number says fine and the patient has nystagmus, ataxia, and slurred speech, believe the patient.

Teaching carries real weight here. Take doses at the same times every day. Do not stop, even if seizure-free for years, without a supervised taper. Carry medical identification. Know your state's driving restrictions. Alcohol lowers the seizure threshold and stacks sedation. Many AEDs — carbamazepine, phenytoin, phenobarbital — are enzyme inducers that can render oral contraceptives ineffective, and valproate and carbamazepine are teratogenic, so contraception counseling is not optional for anyone of childbearing potential. AEDs also carry a class warning for suicidal thoughts; screen for mood changes at follow-up.

And when a seizure happens: your job is not to stop it with your hands. Stay, protect the head, turn the patient to the side, loosen tight clothing, clear the area, time it, and never force anything into the mouth. A generalized seizure lasting more than five minutes, or repeated seizures without a return to baseline, is status epilepticus — a true emergency treated with IV benzodiazepines first, then a loading agent such as fosphenytoin, valproate, or levetiracetam.

A rash on lamotrigine or carbamazepine is not "just a rash." These are the two AEDs most associated with Stevens–Johnson syndrome and toxic epidermal necrolysis, which begin as an unremarkable rash days to weeks in and end in sloughing skin and an ICU. Teach every patient to report any new rash the day it appears, and never let anyone speed a lamotrigine titration to "catch up" on missed doses — a missed run means restarting the titration from the beginning.
Interactive Tap the four drug targets on a firing neuron to watch the seizure burst calm, explore 15 antiepileptics grouped by mechanism, then drag a therapeutic-level gauge for phenytoin, carbamazepine, valproic acid, phenobarbital and ethosuximide. Open full ↑
Key points
  • AEDs stop seizures three ways: prolong sodium channel inactivation, block T-type calcium channels (absence), or boost GABA.
  • Sedation, dizziness, and ataxia are class effects; never stop an AED abruptly — it can trigger status epilepticus.
  • Phenytoin 10–20 mcg/mL, valproate 50–100 mcg/mL, drawn as troughs; check albumin before trusting a phenytoin level.
  • Signature toxicities: phenytoin → gingival hyperplasia; carbamazepine → SJS, blood dyscrasias, hyponatremia; valproate → hepatotoxicity, pancreatitis, teratogenicity; lamotrigine → SJS if titrated fast; levetiracetam → behavior change.
  • Status epilepticus = >5 minutes or no return to baseline — IV benzodiazepine first.

Section 5Anesthetics

Two entirely different jobs

The words sound related, but general and local anesthesia are opposites in strategy. General anesthesia takes away the brain: unconsciousness, amnesia, analgesia, and immobility, delivered systemically, with the patient's protective reflexes — including breathing — deliberately abolished. Local anesthesia leaves the brain entirely alone and cuts the wire instead: the drug blocks sodium channels in a specific nerve so the pain signal never leaves the tissue. The patient is awake and knows exactly what is happening. One takes the receiver offline; the other cuts the phone line.

Modern general anesthesia is almost never one drug — it is balanced anesthesia, a deliberate combination: an IV induction agent to go to sleep fast, an inhaled agent to stay asleep, an opioid for pain, and a neuromuscular blocker for immobility. Combining lets each drug be given at a lower, safer dose. Inhaled agents (sevoflurane, desflurane, isoflurane, nitrous oxide) are titrated breath by breath and eliminated by the lungs. IV agents include propofol — fast, smooth, antiemetic, and a potent respiratory depressant and vasodilator; ketamine — a dissociative that preserves airway reflexes and respiratory drive, valuable in trauma, with emergence delirium as its signature; etomidate — hemodynamically kind, so favored in unstable patients, but it suppresses adrenal function; and midazolam — for anxiolysis and amnesia.

Neuromuscular blockers deserve special fear. Succinylcholine (depolarizing, ultra-short, with fasciculations, hyperkalemia risk, and a link to malignant hyperthermia) and rocuronium or vecuronium (non-depolarizing, reversed with neostigmine or sugammadex) cause paralysis without any sedation or analgesia whatsoever. A paralyzed patient who is not adequately sedated is fully awake, in pain, and unable to signal it. These are high-alert drugs, stored separately, and given only where an airway can be secured immediately.

The stages of anesthesia

Guedel's four stages were described in the era of ether, when induction was slow enough to watch. Modern agents blow through the first three in under a minute — but the stages are still taught because they name what you are watching for, and because Stage II is where a patient can be hurt.

StageNameWhat you seeNursing priority
IAnalgesia / inductionAwake but drowsy; pain sense dulled; can still follow commandsQuiet room, reassurance; the patient can still hear you
IIExcitement / deliriumLoss of consciousness with excitation — irregular breathing, struggling, dilated pupils; vomiting & laryngospasm riskThe dangerous stage. No noise, no stimulation. Suction ready, restrain gently for safety, get through it fast
IIISurgical anesthesiaRegular breathing, relaxed muscles, reflexes lost, pupils constrictedThe target plane; positioning, warming, continuous monitoring
IVMedullary depression / overdoseRespiratory arrest, circulatory collapse, fixed dilated pupilsNever intended. Stop the agent; ventilate and support circulation

Emergence reverses the sequence, so the patient passes back through Stage II on the way out — which is why the PACU is quiet, why side-lying with suction at hand is standard, and why an emerging patient who thrashes is not "being difficult." Two emergencies live in this territory. Malignant hyperthermia is a genetic reaction to volatile agents and succinylcholine: uncontrolled calcium release in muscle produces masseter rigidity, a fast-rising end-tidal CO₂ (the earliest sign), rigidity, tachycardia, and a temperature that climbs alarmingly — treated with dantrolene, immediate cessation of the trigger, cooling, and treatment of hyperkalemia. Ask about family history of anesthesia problems before surgery; it is a question that saves lives. And aspiration is why NPO status exists — anesthesia strips the gag reflex.

Local anesthetics: the wire cut

Locals come in two chemical families and the difference is clinical. Esters (procaine, benzocaine, tetracaine) are broken down in plasma and cause more allergic reactions; benzocaine sprays also cause methemoglobinemia — suspect it when a patient is cyanotic with a normal PaO₂ and chocolate-brown blood, and treat with methylene blue. Amides (lidocaine, bupivacaine, ropivacaine — note the tell-tale second "i" in the name) are metabolized by the liver and rarely cause true allergy. They are given topically, by local infiltration, as a nerve block, epidurally, or spinally.

Epinephrine is often added to a local. It constricts vessels, which keeps the drug where you put it: longer block, less bleeding, less systemic absorption. The old teaching to avoid epinephrine-containing locals in fingers, toes, nose, ears, and penis has been substantially revised for the digits by modern evidence, but it remains a caution worth knowing and honoring wherever facility policy says so.

The danger of locals is systemic absorption — too much drug, or an accidental intravascular injection. Local anesthetic systemic toxicity (LAST) announces itself in the brain first: circumoral (around-the-mouth) numbness, a metallic taste, tinnitus, dizziness, and agitation, then seizures, then cardiac arrest. Bupivacaine is the most cardiotoxic and can go straight to arrest. Treatment is airway support, benzodiazepines for seizures, and IV lipid emulsion — which every unit doing blocks keeps at hand. After a spinal or epidural, watch for hypotension from sympathetic blockade, monitor the level of the block, keep the patient flat per protocol, and remember that the block recedes in the reverse of how it came on: the level drops from the top down, so the highest dermatomes wake up first and the toes come back last. A patient with moving toes and a wearing-off block is exactly the patient who tries to stand and falls — sensation and motor strength in the feet are the last things to return, and the fall risk lasts until they are fully back.

The first sign of local anesthetic toxicity is a taste and a tingle. Circumoral numbness, ringing ears, and a metallic taste come before the seizure and long before the arrest. A patient during a block who says "my lips feel funny" is telling you the drug is in the bloodstream. Stop injecting, get help, get the lipid emulsion. That sentence is the whole warning you get.
Interactive Slide a patient down through Guedel's four stages of general anesthesia — watching pupils, breathing, reflexes and muscle tone change — then switch modes to explore how local anesthetics block the nerve. Open full ↑
Key points
  • General anesthesia abolishes consciousness and reflexes systemically; local anesthesia blocks sodium channels in one nerve and leaves the patient awake.
  • Balanced anesthesia combines induction agent + inhaled agent + opioid + neuromuscular blocker so each can be dosed lower and safer.
  • Neuromuscular blockers paralyze without sedating — high-alert, airway-ready use only.
  • Stage II (excitement) is the dangerous stage — vomiting and laryngospasm; the patient passes back through it on emergence.
  • Malignant hyperthermia (volatiles, succinylcholine) → dantrolene; LAST (circumoral numbness, metallic taste, tinnitus → seizures → arrest) → IV lipid emulsion.

The chapter in one breath

  • Every drug here acts at the synapse — blocking a reuptake pump, an enzyme, a receptor, or an ion channel. Name the step and you can predict the effects.
  • Serotonin drives mood and the gut; dopamine drives reward, movement, prolactin, and vomiting; norepinephrine is drive and blood pressure; GABA is the brake.
  • Antidepressants take 2–6 weeks to lift mood while side effects come first — and suicide risk rises early because energy returns before mood does.
  • Serotonin syndrome is fast, jumpy, and clonic; NMS is slow, rigid, and febrile. Telling them apart is a bedside skill.
  • Lithium is high-alert: 0.6–1.2 mEq/L, steady salt and fluid, beware NSAIDs, thiazides, and dehydration; monitor renal function and TSH.
  • Typicals cause EPS in a predictable timeline ending in irreversible tardive dyskinesia; atypicals trade that for metabolic syndrome; clozapine demands ANC monitoring.
  • AEDs slow sodium or calcium channels or boost GABA, are never stopped abruptly, and carry signature toxicities — and any rash on lamotrigine or carbamazepine is reported today.
  • General anesthesia removes the brain, local anesthesia cuts the wire; the emergencies to know are malignant hyperthermia (dantrolene) and LAST (lipid emulsion).

ReferenceWords worth knowing

The vocabulary of this chapter is the vocabulary of the report you will give and the order you will question — skim it now, and come back whenever a term stops you.

Agonist
A drug that binds a receptor and activates it, imitating the body's own transmitter.
Akathisia
An extrapyramidal effect of intolerable inner restlessness and an inability to sit still; commonly mistaken for worsening psychosis.
Antagonist
A drug that binds a receptor without activating it, blocking the natural transmitter from acting.
Antiepileptic drug (AED)
A drug that raises the seizure threshold by slowing sodium or calcium channels or enhancing GABA; also called an anticonvulsant.
Balanced anesthesia
Combining an induction agent, an inhaled agent, an opioid, and a neuromuscular blocker so each can be given at a lower, safer dose.
Discontinuation syndrome
Dizziness, flu-like aches, irritability, and "brain zaps" after abruptly stopping an SSRI or SNRI; prevented by tapering.
Dystonia
Sudden sustained muscle spasm — neck twisting, oculogyric crisis, or laryngospasm — occurring within hours to days of an antipsychotic; an emergency.
Extrapyramidal symptoms (EPS)
Movement disorders caused by dopamine blockade in the basal nuclei: dystonia, akathisia, parkinsonism, and tardive dyskinesia.
Flumazenil
The benzodiazepine reversal agent; used cautiously, as it can precipitate seizures in chronic users or mixed overdoses.
GABA
Gamma-aminobutyric acid, the brain's main inhibitory neurotransmitter; opening its chloride channel makes neurons harder to fire.
Local anesthetic systemic toxicity (LAST)
Toxicity from systemic absorption of a local anesthetic: circumoral numbness, metallic taste, and tinnitus progressing to seizures and cardiac arrest; treated with IV lipid emulsion.
Malignant hyperthermia
A genetic, life-threatening reaction to volatile anesthetics or succinylcholine causing rigidity, rising end-tidal CO₂, and hyperthermia; treated with dantrolene.
Monoamine oxidase (MAO)
The enzyme that degrades serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine — and dietary tyramine in the gut wall.
Negative symptoms
The "subtracted" symptoms of psychosis — flat affect, poverty of speech, withdrawal, lost motivation — poorly treated by typical antipsychotics.
Neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS)
A rare antipsychotic reaction of high fever, lead-pipe rigidity, altered consciousness, autonomic instability, and elevated creatine kinase.
Neuromuscular blocker
A high-alert drug that paralyzes skeletal muscle without producing any sedation or analgesia; requires a secured airway.
Positive symptoms
The "added" symptoms of psychosis — hallucinations, delusions, disorganized thought — that respond best to dopamine blockade.
Reuptake pump
The transporter that vacuums a neurotransmitter back into the presynaptic neuron; the target of SSRIs, SNRIs, and TCAs.
Serotonin syndrome
Excess serotonergic activity producing mental status change, autonomic instability, and neuromuscular hyperactivity with clonus; treated by stopping the agents, supportive care, and cyproheptadine.
Status epilepticus
A seizure lasting more than five minutes, or repeated seizures without a return to baseline; treated first with an IV benzodiazepine.
Tardive dyskinesia
Late-onset involuntary lip-smacking, tongue-thrusting, and grimacing after months to years of dopamine blockade; often irreversible.
Tyramine
An amine in aged, fermented, and cured foods that triggers a hypertensive crisis in a patient taking an MAOI.

Check yourself

1. A drug blocks a reuptake pump, another blocks an enzyme, and a third blocks a receptor. Give a prototype for each and explain why identifying "the step" is more useful than memorizing side effects.
Pump: fluoxetine (SSRI). Enzyme: phenelzine (MAOI). Receptor: haloperidol (dopamine antagonist). Every effect — wanted and unwanted — follows from which synaptic step the drug touches and which transmitters that step controls, so the step lets you reason out the side effects rather than recall them.
2. Your patient started sertraline eight days ago, feels no better, and is nauseated. She wants to stop. What do you teach?
Side effects come first and usually fade; the mood benefit takes 2–6 weeks. And she should not stop abruptly — SSRIs are tapered to avoid discontinuation syndrome. Nausea reflects serotonin acting on the gut, where most of the body's serotonin lives, and it typically settles within a week or two. Set that expectation at the first dose, not the tenth.
3. Why do antidepressants carry a boxed warning for suicidality, and when in therapy is the risk highest?
Because energy and motivation return before mood does — typically in the first weeks of therapy, and especially in patients under 25. A severely depressed patient may lack the drive to act on suicidal thoughts; once the drug restores drive while hopelessness persists, the risk peaks. Assess suicidality at every contact during that window.
4. A patient on an SSRI is started on tramadol. Hours later he is agitated and diaphoretic, temperature 39.4 °C, with hyperreflexia and clonus in both legs. What is happening, and what do you do?
Serotonin syndrome — mental status change + autonomic instability + neuromuscular hyperactivity, with clonus as the most specific sign. Stop all serotonergic drugs, notify the provider, support with cooling and IV fluids, give benzodiazepines for agitation and rigidity, and expect cyproheptadine (a serotonin blocker) in severe cases.
5. How would you distinguish serotonin syndrome from neuroleptic malignant syndrome at the bedside?
Serotonin syndrome is fast and jumpy: onset within hours, clonus, hyperreflexia, diarrhea. NMS is slow and stiff: onset over days, lead-pipe rigidity, hyporeflexia, very high fever and creatine kinase. The drug history clinches it — a new serotonergic agent versus a dopamine-blocking antipsychotic. Treatment differs: cyproheptadine versus dantrolene/bromocriptine.
6. Your patient on lithium started a low-sodium diet and is taking ibuprofen for back pain. Why does this worry you, and what teaching follows?
Both make the kidney retain sodium — and lithium is reabsorbed by the same pathway, so the lithium level will climb toward toxicity. Teach a consistent normal salt intake and 2–3 L of fluid daily, avoid NSAIDs (and thiazides, and ACE inhibitors) unless the prescriber approves, and report vomiting, diarrhea, or heavy sweating. Therapeutic range is 0.6–1.2 mEq/L; over 1.5 is toxic.
7. A patient on a MAOI reports a pounding occipital headache and stiff neck after dinner out. BP is 196/118. What happened and what was on the plate?
A tyramine-induced hypertensive crisis — likely aged cheese, cured or smoked meat, red wine, draft beer, soy sauce, or sauerkraut. With MAO inhibited, dietary tyramine reaches the circulation and causes a massive norepinephrine release. This is an emergency with stroke risk; teach the tyramine-free diet like an allergy list, and honor the 2-week washout between MAOIs and serotonergic drugs.
8. Two days after starting haloperidol, your patient's neck twists to one side and his eyes roll upward. What is this, and what is your first action?
Acute dystonia with oculogyric crisis — an EPS emergency because it can progress to laryngospasm. Assess and protect the airway first, then give IM/IV diphenhydramine or benztropine as ordered and notify the provider. Dystonia appears within hours to days; akathisia follows in days to weeks; parkinsonism in weeks; tardive dyskinesia in months to years.
9. Why is akathisia so often mismanaged, and what question sorts it out?
It looks like agitation, so the antipsychotic dose gets increased — which makes it worse. Ask where the restlessness is: psychotic agitation lives in the mind, akathisia lives in the legs ("I have to move and I can't explain why"). Treat with a beta blocker or benzodiazepine, or switch agents. Akathisia is distressing and has been linked to suicidality.
10. Your patient on clozapine calls with a sore throat and a fever of 38.6 °C. Why is this not "just a cold"?
Clozapine causes severe neutropenia (agranulocytosis) — fever and sore throat are the classic presentation of a patient with no neutrophils to fight with. This needs an urgent absolute neutrophil count; clozapine is dispensed only through a REMS program precisely because of this risk. Also watch for myocarditis, seizures at higher doses, severe constipation, and drooling.
11. A patient on phenytoin has a total level of 14 mcg/mL but shows nystagmus, ataxia, and slurred speech. Albumin is 2.1 g/dL. Explain.
Phenytoin is highly protein-bound; with low albumin, more drug is free and active, so the total level looks therapeutic while the patient is clinically toxic. Believe the patient over the number, report it, and expect a free phenytoin level. Also remember: dilute IV phenytoin in normal saline only (it precipitates in dextrose) and push it slowly to avoid hypotension and dysrhythmias.
12. During a nerve block a patient says her lips feel numb, she has a metallic taste, and her ears are ringing. What is this, and what happens next?
Early local anesthetic systemic toxicity (LAST) — the drug has reached the bloodstream. Stop the injection immediately, call for help, support the airway, give benzodiazepines if she seizes, and get the IV lipid emulsion. These early neurologic signs precede seizures and cardiac arrest — bupivacaine is the most cardiotoxic and can go straight to arrest, so this sentence from the patient is the entire warning you get.
Pharmacology · Chapter 6

Bone & Skin Drugs

Bone and skin look like the quiet parts of the body — scaffolding and wrapping paper. They are neither. Bone is a living, constantly rebuilt mineral bank that the body raids whenever blood calcium dips, and skin is a barrier that fails loudly and expensively when it is burned, inflamed, or broken. The drugs in this chapter are unusually dependent on you: a bisphosphonate swallowed the wrong way can burn a hole in an esophagus, methotrexate taken daily instead of weekly can kill, and a burn cream chosen carelessly can tip a patient into metabolic acidosis. Almost nothing here is about the pill itself. It is about how it is given, what you watch, and what you teach.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain bone remodeling and describe how PTH, vitamin D, and calcitonin hold serum calcium steady.
  • Compare calcium salts and vitamin D preparations, apply the concept of elemental calcium, and give IV calcium safely.
  • Teach the administration rules for oral bisphosphonates and recognize osteonecrosis of the jaw and atypical femoral fracture.
  • Contrast antiresorptive and anabolic osteoporosis drugs, including denosumab, raloxifene, teriparatide, and romosozumab.
  • Distinguish drugs that treat an acute gout flare from drugs that lower urate long term, and name the dangerous interactions of each.
  • Describe the monitoring and safety teaching for methotrexate, hydroxychloroquine, and biologic DMARDs.
  • Apply topical corticosteroid potency, vehicle, and quantity rules, and explain the iPLEDGE requirements for isotretinoin.
  • Select and monitor topical burn antimicrobials and debriding agents, and state the signature toxicity of each.

Section 1Bone, calcium & vitamin D

Bone is never finished

The skeleton you have today is not the skeleton you had ten years ago. Bone is torn down and rebuilt continuously in a process called remodeling, carried out by two cell crews working in opposition. Osteoclasts are the demolition crew: they attach to a patch of bone, acidify it, and dissolve the mineral, releasing calcium into the blood. Osteoblasts are the construction crew: they lay down new collagen matrix and mineralize it. In a healthy adult the two are balanced. Tip the balance toward the clasts and bone thins; tip it toward the blasts and bone thickens.

The signal that decides the balance is worth memorizing because half the drugs in the next section act on it. Osteoblasts secrete a protein called RANK ligand (RANKL), which binds RANK receptors on osteoclast precursors and tells them to mature and start resorbing. Osteoblasts also secrete a decoy, osteoprotegerin, that mops up RANKL and shuts resorption down. Estrogen restrains RANKL — which is precisely why bone loss accelerates after menopause, when estrogen falls and the demolition crew works unsupervised.

The calcium thermostat

Serum calcium is defended within a narrow band (roughly 8.5–10.5 mg/dL total) because so much depends on it: nerve conduction, muscle contraction, clotting, and cardiac rhythm. Three hormones do the defending.

Parathyroid hormone (PTH) is the emergency responder. When calcium falls, the parathyroid glands release PTH, which pulls calcium out of bone, tells the kidney to reabsorb calcium (while dumping phosphate), and switches on the enzyme that activates vitamin D. Everything PTH does raises calcium. Vitamin D, once activated, is the absorber — it makes the gut take calcium in from food. Calcitonin, from the thyroid, is the brake: it "tones down" calcium by blocking osteoclasts. A simple way to hold it: PTH pulls calcium up, calcitonin tones it down.

Vitamin D deserves a moment because it is not really a vitamin — it is a hormone made in two steps. Skin makes cholecalciferol from sunlight, or the patient swallows cholecalciferol (D3) or ergocalciferol (D2). The liver then hydroxylates it to 25-hydroxyvitamin D — the storage form, and the one the lab measures when someone orders a "vitamin D level." Finally the kidney performs the activating step, producing calcitriol (1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D). That last step is the clinical punchline: a patient with advanced kidney disease cannot activate ordinary vitamin D no matter how much you give, so they are prescribed calcitriol or a related activated analog instead.

Giving calcium and vitamin D without causing harm

Calcium supplements are labeled by salt weight, but only part of that weight is usable elemental calcium. Calcium carbonate is about 40% elemental calcium — the most calcium per tablet, and cheap — but it needs stomach acid to dissolve, so it must be taken with food and it works poorly in patients on proton pump inhibitors or H2 blockers, or after gastric surgery. Calcium citrate is only about 21% elemental (more tablets), but it dissolves without acid, so it can be taken with or without food and is the right choice for the acid-suppressed patient. Either way, the gut can only absorb roughly 500–600 mg of elemental calcium at one time, so a 1,200 mg daily target must be split into two or three doses. Constipation is the near-universal side effect; fluids, fiber, and activity are part of the teaching, not an afterthought.

AgentClass / formKey effectNursing alert
Calcium carbonateOral calcium salt (~40% elemental)Highest calcium per tablet; also an antacidGive with food — needs acid. Ineffective with PPIs. Constipation. Separate from levothyroxine, tetracyclines, fluoroquinolones, and iron by 2–4 h.
Calcium citrateOral calcium salt (~21% elemental)Absorbed without gastric acidChoice for patients on acid suppression or post-gastrectomy; may be taken any time. More tablets per dose.
Calcium gluconateIV calcium (~9% elemental)Corrects symptomatic hypocalcemiaPreferred peripherally. Give slowly by pump; cardiac monitor. Extravasation causes necrosis.
Calcium chlorideIV calcium (~27% elemental)Three times the calcium of gluconate; fastCentral line strongly preferred — severe vesicant. Emergency/code use. Never confuse the two salts.
Cholecalciferol (D3) / ergocalciferol (D2)Vitamin D precursorNeeds liver and kidney activationFat-soluble — it accumulates. Excess causes hypercalcemia. Check 25-OH vitamin D, not calcitriol.
CalcitriolActivated vitamin DAlready active — bypasses the kidney stepFor chronic kidney disease. Monitor calcium, phosphate, PTH. Hypercalcemia comes on faster than with D3.
Calcitonin-salmonHormone (nasal, SC)Inhibits osteoclasts; lowers calciumAdjunct in hypercalcemia and Paget disease; tachyphylaxis in days. Alternate nares; rhinitis is common.

Two clinical pictures should be automatic. Hypocalcemia makes nerves twitchy: numbness and tingling around the mouth and fingers, muscle cramps, positive Chvostek (facial tap) and Trousseau (cuff-induced carpal spasm) signs, tetany, laryngospasm, seizures, and a prolonged QT. Hypercalcemia makes everything sluggish — "stones, bones, groans, thrones, and psychiatric overtones": kidney stones, bone pain, nausea and constipation, polyuria, lethargy and confusion, and a shortened QT.

Calcium and digoxin are a dangerous pair. Calcium potentiates digoxin at the myocardial level; a rapid IV calcium push in a digitalized patient can precipitate lethal dysrhythmias. Give IV calcium slowly, on a pump, with continuous cardiac monitoring — and never run calcium through a line with sodium bicarbonate or phosphate, which will precipitate into visible crystals right in the tubing.

Open full ↑
Key points
  • Osteoclasts resorb bone, osteoblasts build it; RANKL drives resorption and osteoprotegerin blocks it — the target of several drugs.
  • PTH raises calcium (bone, kidney, vitamin D activation); calcitonin lowers it.
  • Vitamin D is activated in the liver then kidney; kidney failure means giving calcitriol, not cholecalciferol.
  • Only elemental calcium counts; carbonate needs acid and food, citrate does not; absorb no more than 500–600 mg per dose.

Section 2Osteoporosis drugs

Bisphosphonates: the first-line drugs, and the fussiest

Bisphosphonates — alendronate, risedronate, ibandronate, and IV zoledronic acid — are antiresorptive drugs. They bind tightly to the hydroxyapatite mineral of bone and sit there. When an osteoclast comes along and dissolves that patch, it swallows the drug, which poisons an enzyme (farnesyl pyrophosphate synthase) the osteoclast needs to survive. The osteoclast undergoes apoptosis. Demolition slows, the blasts keep building, and bone density climbs.

The problem is that oral bisphosphonates are absorbed miserably — well under 1% of the dose — and they are corrosive to the esophagus. That combination generates the most heavily tested administration rules in all of pharmacology, and they are not negotiable:

  • Take first thing in the morning, on a completely empty stomach, before any other food, drink, or medication.
  • Swallow with a full glass (6–8 oz) of plain water — not coffee, juice, mineral water, or milk. Calcium, iron, magnesium, and antacids block absorption entirely.
  • Remain fully upright — sitting or standing, never lying down — for at least 30 minutes (a full 60 minutes for ibandronate).
  • Wait that same interval before eating, drinking anything else, or taking other drugs.
  • Do not give to a patient who cannot sit or stand upright for that long, or who has esophageal stricture, achalasia, or delayed emptying.
  • Correct hypocalcemia and vitamin D deficiency first — an antiresorptive given to a calcium-depleted patient drives calcium lower.

Two rarer adverse effects earn their own teaching. Osteonecrosis of the jaw (ONJ) is exposed, non-healing jaw bone, most often after a dental extraction; the risk is highest with IV, high-dose, oncology-level therapy, and the nursing action is to arrange a dental exam and any needed extractions before therapy starts, then reinforce meticulous oral hygiene. Atypical femoral fracture is a transverse fracture of the femoral shaft after years of therapy, and it announces itself first: teach every patient that new, dull, aching thigh or groin pain must be reported immediately, because that prodrome is the window to catch it before the bone snaps.

IV zoledronic acid is once yearly, which solves adherence, but check renal function first (generally avoid if creatinine clearance is under about 35 mL/min), hydrate the patient well, and warn them about the acute phase reaction — a flu-like day or two of fever, myalgia, and headache after the first infusion, blunted by acetaminophen and unlikely to recur.

When a bisphosphonate is not the answer

Denosumab is a monoclonal antibody against RANKL — a manufactured osteoprotegerin. Given subcutaneously every 6 months, it is not cleared by the kidney, so it can be used at renal function where bisphosphonates are contraindicated — but that convenience comes with a warning. It carries a boxed warning for severe hypocalcemia in patients with advanced chronic kidney disease (including those on dialysis), so calcium and vitamin D must be repleted and serum calcium checked before each injection and monitored closely afterward in those patients. It also carries a rule that patients under-appreciate: it must not be stopped or delayed without a replacement antiresorptive, because withdrawal triggers a rebound surge of bone resorption and multiple vertebral fractures. ONJ occurs here too.

Raloxifene is a selective estrogen receptor modulator (SERM): an estrogen agonist at bone (density up) and an antagonist at breast (invasive breast cancer risk down), while sparing the uterus. Its price is estrogen's thrombotic risk — a boxed warning for venous thromboembolism and fatal stroke in women at risk. Teach that it should be stopped 72 hours before prolonged immobilization (surgery, long travel) and restarted only when fully mobile. Hot flashes and leg cramps are common.

Everything above slows demolition. Two drug groups actually build bone. Teriparatide and abaloparatide are PTH-pathway analogs, given as a daily subcutaneous injection; the trick is that intermittent PTH exposure stimulates osteoblasts, whereas the continuous exposure of hyperparathyroidism destroys bone. Watch for hypercalcemia, and warn about orthostatic hypotension with the first several doses — the patient should inject where they can sit or lie down immediately. Avoid in patients at baseline increased risk of osteosarcoma (Paget disease, prior skeletal radiation, unexplained elevated alkaline phosphatase). Romosozumab inhibits sclerostin, building bone while also slowing resorption, given monthly for 12 months only — but it carries a boxed warning for myocardial infarction, stroke, and cardiovascular death and must not be used in anyone who has had an MI or stroke within the past year.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
BisphosphonateAlendronate (also risedronate, ibandronate, IV zoledronic acid)Antiresorptive — osteoclast apoptosisEmpty stomach, plain water, upright 30 min (60 for ibandronate). Report thigh/groin pain (atypical fracture). Dental exam first (ONJ).
RANKL inhibitorDenosumabAntiresorptive monoclonal antibody; SC q6 monthsNot renally cleared — an option when bisphosphonates are not. SERMRaloxifeneEstrogen agonist at bone, antagonist at breastBoxed warning: VTE and fatal stroke. Stop 72 h before immobilization. Hot flashes, leg cramps.

Calcitonin-salmonCalcitonin nasal sprayWeak antiresorptive; some analgesia in vertebral fractureLast-line. Rhinitis, epistaxis — alternate nares daily. Effect fades (tachyphylaxis).
The 30-minute rule is a safety rule, not a convenience rule. A patient who takes alendronate with a sip of coffee and goes back to bed can develop ulcerating esophagitis — the drug sits against the mucosa and burns. If a patient tells you they "just lie back down after," that is a report-worthy finding, not a shrug. Any new dysphagia, retrosternal pain, or heartburn on a bisphosphonate means hold the drug and call.
Interactive Step through how bisphosphonates bind bone mineral and shut the osteoclast down, then build a patient's morning dose and watch the wrong choices cause esophagitis or a wasted, unabsorbed tablet. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Bisphosphonates are first-line: empty stomach, plain water, upright 30 min (60 for ibandronate), nothing else by mouth for that interval.
  • Teach the two long-term signals: thigh/groin pain (atypical femoral fracture) and jaw pain or exposed bone (ONJ, so dental work goes first).
  • Denosumab is not renally cleared, but carries a boxed warning for severe hypocalcemia in advanced CKD — correct calcium before each dose — and it cannot be stopped abruptly.
  • Teriparatide, abaloparatide, and romosozumab build bone; everything else mostly slows its loss.

Section 3Gout & antiarthritis drugs

Gout: put out the fire, then lower the fuel

Gout is uric acid crystallizing in a joint. Two entirely separate jobs follow, and mixing them up is the classic error. Job one is stopping the acute flare — an anti-inflammatory problem. Job two is lowering serum urate for the long run, which does nothing for today's pain and can even provoke a flare when it starts.

For the flare, three options: an NSAID (naproxen, indomethacin), a corticosteroid (oral, or injected into a single hot joint), or colchicine. Colchicine is the interesting one. It binds tubulin, wrecking the microtubules that neutrophils need in order to crawl into the joint and unleash inflammation. It has no analgesic and no urate-lowering effect at all — it simply keeps the neutrophils away. Modern dosing is low: 1.2 mg at the first hint of a flare, then 0.6 mg one hour later. The signature toxicity is gastrointestinal — nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea are the first sign of toxicity, and the instruction is to stop the drug, not push through. Colchicine is cleared by CYP3A4 and P-glycoprotein, so combining it with clarithromycin, verapamil, diltiazem, cyclosporine, or a strong azole in a patient with kidney impairment has caused fatal marrow suppression and myopathy. Note also that aspirin is avoided in gout: low doses raise urate.

For the long run, allopurinol is the anchor. It inhibits xanthine oxidase, the enzyme that manufactures uric acid, so less is produced. Start low (100 mg, less in kidney disease) and titrate slowly to a serum urate under 6 mg/dL; encourage 2–3 L of fluid daily. Because starting it mobilizes urate and can set off a flare, patients are covered with low-dose colchicine or an NSAID for the first several months. Two allopurinol facts must be reflexes: any rash means stop the drug and call immediately (allopurinol is a leading cause of Stevens–Johnson syndrome, toxic epidermal necrolysis, and DRESS), and allopurinol blocks the metabolism of azathioprine and mercaptopurine, causing life-threatening bone marrow suppression unless those doses are slashed. Febuxostat is an alternative xanthine oxidase inhibitor for allopurinol-intolerant patients, but it carries a boxed warning for cardiovascular death and is second-line. Probenecid takes the other route — it is a uricosuric, blocking urate reabsorption in the renal tubule so more is excreted; it needs decent kidney function and generous fluids, and it is avoided if the patient has a history of stones. Pegloticase, an IV enzyme that dissolves urate directly, is reserved for refractory tophaceous gout: boxed warning for anaphylaxis, and it must never be given in G6PD deficiency (hemolysis, methemoglobinemia) — so screening comes first.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Anti-inflammatory (flare)ColchicineBlocks neutrophil migration into the jointGI upset = toxicity — stop the drug. Fatal interactions with clarithromycin, verapamil, cyclosporine. Reduce in renal impairment. No analgesic effect.
NSAID (flare)Naproxen, indomethacinBlocks prostaglandin-driven inflammationGI bleeding, renal injury, fluid retention. Take with food. Do not use aspirin as the NSAID — low doses raise urate.
Corticosteroid (flare)Prednisone; intra-articular triamcinoloneBroad anti-inflammatoryHyperglycemia, infection risk, insomnia. Useful when NSAIDs and colchicine are contraindicated.
Xanthine oxidase inhibitorAllopurinolMakes less uric acid; target urate <6 mg/dLAny rash → stop and report (SJS/TEN/DRESS). Deadly with azathioprine/mercaptopurine. Fluids 2–3 L/day. Expect early flares — cover with prophylaxis.
Xanthine oxidase inhibitorFebuxostatAlternative when allopurinol fails or is not toleratedBoxed warning: cardiovascular death. Monitor LFTs. Second-line.
UricosuricProbenecidExcretes more uric acidNeeds adequate renal function; push fluids; avoid with stone history. Salicylates cancel it. Raises penicillin levels.
Recombinant uricasePegloticase IVEnzymatically destroys urate; for refractory tophaceous goutBoxed warning: anaphylaxis — premedicate, monitor. Screen for G6PD deficiency first — contraindicated.

DMARDs: changing the disease, not just the symptoms

Rheumatoid arthritis is not gout and it is not wear-and-tear osteoarthritis — it is an autoimmune attack on the synovium that erodes joints permanently. NSAIDs and steroids make an RA patient feel better while the joints quietly dissolve. Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs (DMARDs) slow the disease itself, and the guiding principle is to start them early, within weeks of diagnosis, before erosion is done. They work slowly — weeks to months — which is a teaching point, because a patient who expects ibuprofen-speed relief will stop taking them.

Methotrexate is the anchor DMARD and the single most dangerous prescribing error in this chapter. At RA doses it is given once weekly. Taken daily by mistake — and this has happened, and patients have died — it produces catastrophic mucositis and pancytopenia. Confirm the weekly schedule out loud with the patient and with anyone taking a verbal order, and check that the pill bottle and the MAR say weekly. Daily folic acid (usually 1 mg, not on the methotrexate day in some regimens) blunts the mouth sores, GI upset, and marrow effects without blocking the benefit. Methotrexate carries boxed warnings for bone marrow suppression, hepatotoxicity, pulmonary toxicity, and embryo-fetal toxicity — it is contraindicated in pregnancy, and effective contraception is required for both partners. Monitor CBC, LFTs, and creatinine. Teach no alcohol, report a persistent dry cough or dyspnea (pneumonitis) and mouth ulcers (the earliest toxicity sign). Avoid trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole, which is an additive antifolate and can produce pancytopenia. Leucovorin is the rescue agent.

Among the other conventional DMARDs, hydroxychloroquine is the gentlest — and the one with a memorable rule: it can deposit in the retina, so patients need a baseline and annual eye exam and must report changes in vision or color perception. Sulfasalazine is avoided in sulfa allergy and G6PD deficiency and turns urine orange-yellow (harmless, but tell them).
