Leflunomide is hepatotoxic and a potent teratogen with a very long half-life, requiring a cholestyramine washout if pregnancy occurs or is planned.

The biologic DMARDs — TNF inhibitors (etanercept, adalimumab, infliximab), plus IL-6, T-cell, and B-cell agents — are extraordinarily effective and share one governing safety idea: they take the brakes off infection. Every patient is screened for latent tuberculosis and hepatitis B before the first dose, boxed warnings cover serious infections and malignancy including lymphoma, live vaccines are contraindicated during therapy, and the drug is held during any active infection and usually around surgery. The oral JAK inhibitors (tofacitinib, baricitinib, upadacitinib) add boxed warnings for major adverse cardiovascular events, thrombosis, malignancy, and mortality.

"Weekly, not daily" is the whole methotrexate lesson. Say it to the patient, write it on the discharge sheet, and have the patient say it back to you. Then add the second sentence they will actually use: mouth sores are the early warning — call before they get worse. Both of those beat any amount of memorized pharmacokinetics.
Interactive Tap any of 13 drugs to see exactly where it acts — the flaring joint, the uric acid pathway, or the rheumatoid synovium — and why the drug that stops an attack is never the drug that lowers the urate. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Gout has two separate jobs: quench the flare (colchicine, NSAID, steroid) and lower urate long term (allopurinol, febuxostat, probenecid) — urate-lowering drugs do nothing for today's pain.
  • Colchicine GI upset = toxicity, stop; allopurinol rash = stop and report; allopurinol plus azathioprine/mercaptopurine can be fatal.
  • Methotrexate is weekly with daily folic acid; monitor CBC/LFTs, no alcohol, contraindicated in pregnancy, mouth ulcers are the first sign of trouble.
  • Before any biologic DMARD: screen for latent TB and hepatitis B, no live vaccines, hold for active infection.

Section 4Skin drugs

Acne: four problems, four drug jobs

Acne is not dirt. Four things go wrong in a follicle: sebum production rises (androgen-driven), the follicle lining sheds abnormally and plugs the pore, Cutibacterium acnes multiplies behind the plug, and inflammation follows. Every acne drug attacks one of those four, which is exactly why combinations work better than any single agent.

Benzoyl peroxide kills the bacteria by releasing free radicals — and crucially, bacteria do not develop resistance to it. Teach that it bleaches fabric: white towels and pillowcases, or ruined ones. Topical retinoids (tretinoin, adapalene, tazarotene) fix the plugging by normalizing follicular shedding; apply a pea-sized amount at bedtime to dry skin, expect an initial worsening and peeling for several weeks, use non-comedogenic moisturizer, and use sunscreen because they cause photosensitivity. Topical antibiotics (clindamycin, erythromycin) should essentially never be used alone — always pair with benzoyl peroxide to prevent resistance. Oral antibiotics for moderate inflammatory acne are usually doxycycline or minocycline: photosensitivity again, pill-induced esophagitis (full glass of water, stay upright), and contraindicated in pregnancy and in children under 8 (tooth staining). Hormonal options — combined oral contraceptives, spironolactone (monitor potassium) — target the androgen driver.

Isotretinoin is the only drug that hits all four mechanisms, and it is the most tightly controlled drug most nurses will ever handle. It is a severe teratogen, and dispensing runs through the iPLEDGE risk management program: patients who can become pregnant must use two forms of contraception beginning one month before, throughout, and for one month after therapy, with monthly negative pregnancy tests and monthly program authorization. There is no blood donation during therapy or for a month afterward, because a transfused unit could reach a pregnant recipient. Expected effects: cheilitis in nearly everyone (dry cracked lips), dry eyes and nose, epistaxis, myalgia, night-vision changes, elevated triglycerides, and hepatotoxicity — so monitor lipids and LFTs and teach no alcohol. Mood changes should be reported. And a hard rule: never combine isotretinoin with a tetracycline — the pair raises intracranial pressure (pseudotumor cerebri, presenting as headache, visual changes, and nausea).

Psoriasis: turning down an overdriven immune system

Psoriasis is immune-driven hyperproliferation — keratinocytes turning over in days instead of weeks, producing thick silvery plaques. Mild disease is topical: corticosteroids (the workhorse), vitamin D analogs like calcipotriene (do not exceed the weekly maximum — systemic absorption causes hypercalcemia; it is inactivated by salicylic acid, so do not layer them), tazarotene, coal tar, and calcineurin inhibitors (tacrolimus, pimecrolimus ointment) for the face and skin folds where steroids thin the skin. Moderate to severe disease escalates to phototherapy (narrowband UVB; or PUVA, where oral methoxsalen plus UVA requires UV-blocking eyewear for 24 hours after the dose) and systemic drugs: methotrexate, cyclosporine, acitretin (a retinoid, and a teratogen so persistent that pregnancy must be avoided for 3 years after and alcohol is forbidden because it regenerates a long-lived metabolite), apremilast, and the biologics — TNF inhibitors, IL-17 blockers (secukinumab, ixekizumab), and IL-23 blockers (guselkumab, risankizumab). All of the biologics carry the same TB-screening and infection precautions as in rheumatoid arthritis.

Topical corticosteroids: potency, vehicle, quantity, and time

Topical steroids are the most used and most misused drugs in dermatology, and the nursing content is four variables. Potency is ranked in classes from 1 (superpotent, e.g., clobetasol) to 7 (low, e.g., hydrocortisone 1%). Vehicle changes potency for the same molecule: ointments are the most potent because they occlude and hydrate (best for thick, dry, scaly skin), creams are middle-ground and cosmetically acceptable, and lotions, gels, solutions, and foams are for hairy areas and the scalp. Quantity is measured in the fingertip unit — the ribbon squeezed from the tip of an adult index finger to the first crease, about 0.5 g, which covers an area equal to two adult palms. Time matters because effects accumulate.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Superpotent / high (I–II)Clobetasol propionate 0.05%Thick plaques on palms, soles, scalp, trunkNever on face, groin, or axilla. Limit to ~2 weeks. HPA-axis suppression with large areas or occlusion. Do not exceed the labeled weekly gram limit.
Medium (III–V)Triamcinolone acetonide 0.1%Everyday eczema/dermatitis on the trunk and limbsThin layer, limited duration. Watch for atrophy, striae, telangiectasia in flexures.
Low (VI–VII)Hydrocortisone 1–2.5%Face, eyelids, groin, infantsSafest for thin skin. Even here, prolonged facial use causes perioral dermatitis and rebound.
Steroid-sparing topicalTacrolimus / pimecrolimus ointmentCalcineurin inhibitor — no skin atrophyFor face and folds. Transient burning/stinging is expected. Boxed warning: possible malignancy risk. Strict sun protection.
Topical vitamin D analogCalcipotrieneNormalizes keratinocyte turnover in psoriasisRespect the weekly maximum — hypercalcemia. Inactivated by salicylic acid. Irritating on the face.
Systemic retinoidIsotretinoin (acne); acitretin (psoriasis)Definitive but teratogeniciPLEDGE, two contraceptives, monthly pregnancy tests, no blood donation. Never with a tetracycline (pseudotumor cerebri). Acitretin: no pregnancy for 3 years, no alcohol.

The adverse effects follow directly from what steroids do. Locally: skin atrophy, striae, telangiectasia, hypopigmentation, acneiform eruption, and tachyphylaxis (the drug stops working, so patients use more, which is the trap). Systemically, absorption is greatest where skin is thin (face, eyelids, genitals), where skin is inflamed and barrier-broken, where the surface area is large, and wherever the site is occluded. Children absorb disproportionately more because their body-surface-to-weight ratio is high. The rule set is short: lowest potency that works, thin layer, shortest duration, no occlusion unless it is ordered, and do not put a potent steroid on the face or in a skin fold.

A diaper is an occlusive dressing. Hydrocortisone under a diaper is not a class-7 steroid anymore — the plastic backing traps heat and moisture and can multiply absorption many times over, which is exactly how an infant ends up with HPA suppression from an over-the-counter cream. Same logic for plastic wrap, a sock over a foot, or a steroid under a compression stocking. If the parent says "I put a little on and then put his pajamas and the diaper right on," that is the finding you escalate.
Interactive Tap a condition — acne, psoriasis, or topical steroids — then a drug, and climb the corticosteroid potency ladder that decides where on the body it's safe. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Acne drugs target four things: sebum, plugging (retinoids), bacteria (benzoyl peroxide — no resistance, bleaches fabric), and inflammation. Never use a topical antibiotic without benzoyl peroxide.
  • Isotretinoin = iPLEDGE, two contraceptives, monthly pregnancy tests, no blood donation, monitor lipids/LFTs, and never with a tetracycline.
  • Topical steroid strength depends on potency class, vehicle, quantity (fingertip unit), and occlusion — ointment > cream > lotion for the same molecule.
  • Keep potent steroids off the face, groin, and axilla; watch for atrophy, tachyphylaxis, and HPA suppression, especially in children.

Section 5Burns & wound agents

Why burns get topical drugs instead of IV antibiotics

A deep burn produces eschar — dead, leathery, and, most importantly, avascular. No blood supply means no delivery: an IV antibiotic cannot reach the bacteria colonizing that surface, because the drug travels in blood and the blood does not go there. So the antimicrobial is put on the wound. Systemic antibiotics in burn care are reserved for documented invasive infection or sepsis, not prophylaxis — prophylactic systemic antibiotics simply select for resistant organisms while the wound surface stays untouched.

Three classic topical agents divide the work, and each has one toxicity that defines it.

Silver sulfadiazine is the traditional workhorse: broad-spectrum, soothing, painless to apply, spread about 1/16 inch thick with a sterile gloved hand. Its defining problem is transient leukopenia, typically appearing two to three days in — so the WBC count gets checked, and a dip is usually self-limiting. It is a sulfonamide, so it is contraindicated in sulfa allergy, and it is contraindicated in infants under 2 months and in near-term pregnancy because it displaces bilirubin and risks kernicterus. It does not penetrate eschar well, and it forms a gray pseudo-eschar that must be cleaned off before reapplication.

Mafenide acetate is the one that penetrates eschar and cartilage, which makes it the agent for burned ears and noses where cartilage is at risk. Two things follow. It hurts — a real burning pain on application, so premedicate. And it is a carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, so it causes metabolic acidosis; the patient compensates by hyperventilating, and a burn patient who becomes tachypneic on mafenide is telling you their pH is falling. Monitor acid-base status and watch the respiratory rate.

Silver nitrate 0.5% solution is applied on wet dressings that must be kept continuously moist (drying concentrates it into a caustic). It stains everything — skin, linens, walls — black or gray, which is alarming but harmless. Its real hazard is that it leaches electrolytes out of the wound: hyponatremia and hypochloremia, plus a risk of methemoglobinemia. Monitor electrolytes.

AgentClassKey effectNursing alert
Silver sulfadiazineTopical sulfonamide + silverBroad-spectrum; painless; poor eschar penetrationTransient leukopenia — check CBC. Contraindicated in sulfa allergy, infants <2 months, near-term pregnancy. Remove pseudo-eschar before reapplying.
Mafenide acetateTopical sulfonamidePenetrates eschar and cartilage — ears, nosePainful — premedicate. Carbonic anhydrase inhibition → metabolic acidosis; watch for tachypnea, monitor ABGs.
Silver nitrate 0.5%Topical antiseptic solutionBroad surface antisepsis on wet dressingsKeep dressings continuously moist. Hyponatremia/hypochloremia — monitor electrolytes. Stains black. Methemoglobinemia risk.
Nanocrystalline silver dressingSustained-release silverBroad-spectrum; stays on for several daysMoisten with sterile water, not saline — chloride inactivates silver. Fewer dressing changes means less pain.
MupirocinTopical antibacterialExcellent anti-staphylococcal, including MRSAImpetigo, small superficial wounds, nasal MRSA decolonization. Limit duration — resistance develops.
Bacitracin / polymyxin BTopical antibacterial ointmentSuperficial and facial burns; keeps the wound moistContact dermatitis is common with bacitracin. Not for deep or large burns.
CollagenaseEnzymatic debriding agentDigests denatured collagen anchoring sloughInactivated by silver, iodine, and other heavy metals — do not layer. Apply nickel-thick, once daily, to moist wound bed.
BecaplerminTopical PDGF growth factorPromotes granulation in diabetic foot ulcersBoxed warning: increased malignancy mortality with ≥3 tubes. Adjunct only — offloading and debridement still do the work.

Getting the dead tissue off

Nothing granulates under slough. Debridement is the removal of nonviable tissue, and it comes in flavors. Autolytic debridement uses the body's own enzymes trapped under a moisture-retentive dressing (hydrogel, hydrocolloid) — painless and selective, but slow, and not for infected wounds. Enzymatic debridement uses collagenase, applied daily; remember it is destroyed by silver and iodine products, so those two cannot share a wound bed. Mechanical debridement — the old wet-to-dry gauze — is nonselective and painful: it rips out healthy granulation along with the slough, and it has largely been abandoned for exactly that reason. Sharp/surgical debridement is fastest and is the standard for extensive burns. Biologic (larval) debridement is a real, occasionally used option for chronic wounds.

What actually heals a wound

The single most important idea in modern wound care is that a moist wound heals faster than a dry one. Old teaching let wounds "air out" and form a scab; we now know epithelial cells migrate across a moist bed and stall at a dry crust. The dressing's job is to hold moisture at the wound while wicking away excess — hence the working shorthand: if it's dry, moisten it; if it's wet, absorb it. Antiseptics deserve the same skepticism you would apply to any drug: full-strength povidone-iodine and hydrogen peroxide are cytotoxic to fibroblasts and have no place being poured routinely into a healing granulating wound.

The nursing work around these agents is as consequential as the agents themselves. Premedicate for pain 30 minutes before a dressing change — burn dressing changes are among the most painful procedures in the hospital, and in the acute phase with massive fluid shifts, analgesia must be given IV, because IM and subcutaneous absorption is unreliable and doses stack up unabsorbed until perfusion returns. Maintain strict aseptic technique, since the burn patient's greatest threat after the first 48 hours is infection. Verify tetanus status on every wound. Support nutrition aggressively — protein and calories are literally the raw material for granulation, and healing fails without them.

Match the toxicity to the agent and you will never mix them up. Silver sulfadiazine → the white cells drop (leukopenia). Mafenide → the pH drops (acidosis, so the patient breathes fast). Silver nitrate → the sodium drops (and everything turns black). Three agents, three falling numbers, three different labs to check.
Interactive Tap six topical burn and wound antimicrobials to watch how far each one penetrates dead eschar — then match the right agent to a deep burn, a sulfa allergy, a facial burn, and MRSA-colonized nares. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Eschar is avascular, so IV antibiotics cannot reach it — burn antimicrobials are topical; systemic antibiotics are for documented infection, not prophylaxis.
  • Silver sulfadiazine → leukopenia, no sulfa allergy, no infants <2 months. Mafenide → penetrates eschar/cartilage, painful, metabolic acidosis. Silver nitrate → keep moist, stains, hyponatremia.
  • Collagenase is inactivated by silver and iodine; wet-to-dry debridement is nonselective and damages granulation tissue.
  • Moist wounds heal faster; premedicate 30 minutes before dressing changes and give burn analgesia IV in the acute phase.

The chapter in one breath

  • Bone is a living bank: osteoclasts withdraw, osteoblasts deposit, RANKL sets the rate, and PTH raises while calcitonin lowers serum calcium.
  • Vitamin D needs liver then kidney activation — kidney failure means giving calcitriol; only elemental calcium counts, and no more than 500–600 mg absorbs per dose.
  • Bisphosphonates are first-line for osteoporosis and live or die by administration: empty stomach, plain water, upright 30 minutes; teach thigh pain (atypical fracture) and pre-treatment dental care (ONJ).
  • Denosumab must never be stopped abruptly; raloxifene risks clots; teriparatide, abaloparatide, and romosozumab are the drugs that actually build bone.
  • Gout is two jobs: quench the flare (colchicine — GI upset means stop) and lower urate (allopurinol — rash means stop, and never casually with azathioprine).
  • Methotrexate is weekly, not daily, with folic acid, CBC/LFT monitoring, no alcohol, and no pregnancy; every biologic DMARD gets TB and hepatitis B screening first.
  • Topical steroid strength is potency × vehicle × quantity × occlusion — keep the potent ones off face and folds, and remember a diaper is an occlusive dressing.
  • Burn eschar is avascular, so antimicrobials go on the wound: silver sulfadiazine drops the WBC, mafenide drops the pH, silver nitrate drops the sodium — and moist wounds heal fastest.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Antiresorptive
A drug that slows bone breakdown by suppressing osteoclasts; bisphosphonates, denosumab, raloxifene, and calcitonin. Contrast with anabolic drugs, which build new bone.
Atypical femoral fracture
A transverse fracture of the femoral shaft associated with long-term antiresorptive therapy, typically preceded for weeks by dull thigh or groin pain — a symptom patients must be taught to report.
Bisphosphonate
A drug that binds bone mineral and triggers osteoclast apoptosis when the cell resorbs it; first-line for osteoporosis, and requiring strict upright, empty-stomach, plain-water administration.
Calcitriol
1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D — the fully activated hormone form of vitamin D, produced by the kidney. Given directly when the kidney can no longer perform that step.
Debridement
Removal of nonviable tissue from a wound; may be autolytic, enzymatic, mechanical, sharp/surgical, or biologic.
Disease-modifying antirheumatic drug (DMARD)
A drug that slows the underlying autoimmune joint destruction of rheumatoid arthritis rather than only relieving symptoms; works over weeks to months and is started early.
Elemental calcium
The amount of usable calcium in a supplement, as opposed to the total salt weight — roughly 40% for calcium carbonate and 21% for calcium citrate.
Eschar
Dead, leathery, avascular tissue over a deep burn; because no blood reaches it, systemic antibiotics cannot treat its surface bacteria.
Fingertip unit
The ribbon of topical medication from an adult fingertip to the first crease (about 0.5 g), which covers an area equal to two adult palms — the standard way to teach quantity.
Hypercalcemia
Serum calcium above the normal range, producing "stones, bones, groans, thrones, and psychiatric overtones," constipation, lethargy, and a shortened QT interval.
Osteoblast
The bone-building cell that lays down and mineralizes new matrix, and that also secretes RANKL and osteoprotegerin to regulate osteoclasts.
Osteoclast
The bone-resorbing cell that dissolves mineral and releases calcium into the blood; the target of every antiresorptive drug.
Osteonecrosis of the jaw (ONJ)
Exposed, non-healing jaw bone associated with bisphosphonates and denosumab, most often triggered by dental extraction — hence dental clearance before therapy.
Parathyroid hormone (PTH)
The hormone that raises serum calcium by resorbing bone, increasing renal calcium reabsorption, and activating vitamin D; its analogs, given intermittently, build bone.
Photosensitivity
Exaggerated sunburn response caused by a drug; expected with retinoids, tetracyclines, and several other agents in this chapter, and managed with sunscreen and covering clothing.
RANK ligand (RANKL)
The signal from osteoblasts that drives osteoclast maturation and bone resorption; blocked naturally by osteoprotegerin and pharmacologically by denosumab.
Retinoid
A vitamin A derivative that normalizes epithelial turnover; topical (tretinoin, adapalene) or systemic (isotretinoin, acitretin). All are photosensitizing and the systemic ones are potent teratogens.
Selective estrogen receptor modulator (SERM)
A drug that acts as an estrogen agonist in some tissues and an antagonist in others; raloxifene builds bone and blocks breast tissue while carrying estrogen's clot risk.
Teratogen
A substance that causes fetal malformation. Isotretinoin, acitretin, methotrexate, and leflunomide are among the most dangerous teratogens a nurse will handle.
Tophus
A visible deposit of urate crystals in soft tissue, seen in chronic untreated gout; its presence signals long-standing hyperuricemia needing urate-lowering therapy.
Uricosuric
A drug that lowers serum urate by increasing renal excretion rather than reducing production; probenecid is the prototype, and it requires adequate kidney function and generous fluids.
Xanthine oxidase inhibitor
A drug that blocks the enzyme making uric acid, lowering urate at the source; allopurinol is the prototype and febuxostat the alternative.

Check yourself

1. Explain in one sentence each what PTH and calcitonin do to serum calcium, and name the two organs that must work for dietary vitamin D to become active.
PTH raises serum calcium (pulls it from bone, reabsorbs it in the kidney, activates vitamin D); calcitonin lowers it by inhibiting osteoclasts. Activation requires the liver, then the kidney. That last point is clinical: a patient in kidney failure cannot perform the final activating step, so they must receive calcitriol (already active) rather than cholecalciferol.
2. A patient on omeprazole is prescribed a calcium supplement and asks whether the cheap calcium carbonate is fine. What do you advise and why?
Recommend calcium citrate instead. Calcium carbonate needs gastric acid to dissolve, and a proton pump inhibitor removes that acid, so absorption is poor. Calcium citrate dissolves without acid and can be taken with or without food. Also teach that only about 500–600 mg of elemental calcium absorbs per dose, so a larger daily target must be split.
3. Your patient is digitalized and needs IV calcium for symptomatic hypocalcemia. List three safety measures.
Any three: infuse slowly on a pump; use continuous cardiac monitoring (calcium potentiates digoxin and can trigger lethal dysrhythmias); prefer calcium gluconate peripherally (chloride is a severe vesicant needing central access); assess the site for extravasation, which causes necrosis; never run calcium with sodium bicarbonate or phosphate, which precipitate.
4. Write out, as you would teach it, the administration instructions for alendronate — and state one condition that would make you hold the drug entirely.
First thing in the morning, on an empty stomach, with a full glass of plain water only, then stay sitting or standing upright for at least 30 minutes with nothing else by mouth. Hold it if the patient cannot remain upright for 30 minutes, or has esophageal stricture, achalasia, or delayed emptying — the drug is corrosive and will ulcerate a stagnant esophagus. Also correct hypocalcemia and vitamin D deficiency before starting.
5. A patient three years into bisphosphonate therapy mentions a dull ache in her thigh for the past few weeks. Why does this matter?
It is the prodrome of an atypical femoral fracture and must be reported and worked up immediately. That aching thigh or groin pain precedes the transverse femoral-shaft fracture by weeks — it is the window in which the fracture can still be prevented. Every patient starting long-term antiresorptive therapy should be taught this specific symptom.
6. Why is a missed or delayed dose of denosumab a bigger problem than a missed dose of alendronate?
Stopping denosumab causes a rebound surge of bone resorption and multiple vertebral fractures; bisphosphonates linger in bone and have no such rebound. Denosumab must never be discontinued without transitioning to another antiresorptive. Also verify calcium and vitamin D are repleted and serum calcium is checked before each dose — denosumab carries a boxed warning for severe hypocalcemia in advanced chronic kidney disease.
7. Your patient with an acute gout flare on colchicine calls to say she has had vomiting and diarrhea since yesterday but is "pushing through it." What do you tell her?
Stop the colchicine and call the prescriber — GI symptoms are the first sign of colchicine toxicity, not a side effect to tolerate. Beyond that, ask what else she takes: clarithromycin, verapamil, diltiazem, cyclosporine, or an azole (CYP3A4/P-glycoprotein inhibitors) plus kidney impairment can push colchicine to fatal marrow suppression and myopathy.
8. Give two reasons a patient starting allopurinol might call you, and state the correct response to each.
(1) A rash — stop the drug and report immediately; allopurinol is a leading cause of Stevens–Johnson syndrome, TEN, and DRESS. (2) A gout flare — expected on initiation as urate mobilizes; it is not a sign the drug is failing, and patients are covered with low-dose colchicine or an NSAID for the first several months. Also confirm the patient is not on azathioprine or mercaptopurine, whose metabolism allopurinol blocks, causing life-threatening marrow suppression.
9. A patient with rheumatoid arthritis is discharged on methotrexate. State the dosing schedule, the co-prescribed vitamin, three monitoring labs, and the earliest sign of toxicity you would teach.
Once weekly (never daily — daily dosing has killed patients); with daily folic acid; monitoring CBC, LFTs, and creatinine; and mouth ulcers are the earliest sign of toxicity. Also teach no alcohol, report persistent dry cough or dyspnea (pneumonitis), avoid trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole (additive antifolate), and strict contraception — methotrexate is contraindicated in pregnancy. Leucovorin is the rescue agent.
10. What must be screened for before the first dose of a TNF inhibitor, and name two ongoing rules for a patient on one.
Screen for latent tuberculosis and hepatitis B. Ongoing: no live vaccines during therapy; hold the drug during any active infection (and usually around surgery); teach the patient to report fever, cough, or any sign of infection promptly. The boxed warnings are serious infection and malignancy, including lymphoma.
11. A parent reports using an over-the-counter hydrocortisone cream on her infant's diaper rash, applying it and putting the diaper right back on. Why is this a concern, and what four variables determine a topical steroid's true strength?
The diaper is an occlusive dressing — it dramatically increases absorption, and infants already absorb more because of a high body-surface-to-weight ratio, risking HPA-axis suppression and skin atrophy. The four variables: potency class (1 superpotent to 7 low), vehicle (ointment > cream > lotion for the same molecule), quantity (fingertip unit ≈ 0.5 g covers two palms), and occlusion/duration. Keep potent steroids off the face, groin, and axilla.
12. A burn patient on mafenide acetate becomes tachypneic. What is happening, and what would you expect if the same patient were on silver sulfadiazine or silver nitrate instead?
Mafenide inhibits carbonic anhydrase, causing metabolic acidosis — the tachypnea is respiratory compensation. Check ABGs and acid-base status. Silver sulfadiazine instead causes transient leukopenia around days 2–3 (check the CBC; it is contraindicated in sulfa allergy, infants under 2 months, and near-term pregnancy). Silver nitrate leaches electrolytes, causing hyponatremia and hypochloremia (check electrolytes; keep the dressings continuously moist and expect black staining). Remember, mafenide is the one that penetrates eschar and cartilage, which is why it is used on ears and noses despite the pain.
Pharmacology · Chapter 7

Autonomic Drugs

Your patient's heart rate, blood pressure, pupils, bladder, gut, and airways are all being quietly managed — second by second — by a nervous system nobody chose to switch on. Autonomic drugs work by pushing on that system: turning up the gas, stepping on the brake, or cutting a wire. Once you understand which receptor a drug lands on and what that receptor does to the organ underneath it, the adverse effects stop being a list to memorize and start being something you can predict from first principles. This is the chapter that makes the rest of pharmacology click, because so many drugs — for hypertension, asthma, glaucoma, overactive bladder, shock, and anesthesia — are simply autonomic drugs wearing a different name badge.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Contrast the sympathetic and parasympathetic divisions and predict each one's effect on the major organs.
  • Match alpha-1, alpha-2, beta-1, beta-2, beta-3, muscarinic, and nicotinic receptors to their locations and responses.
  • Explain how adrenergic agonists work, and describe the dose-dependent behavior of epinephrine.
  • Describe the nursing implications of alpha blockers and beta blockers, including first-dose hypotension and abrupt-withdrawal rebound.
  • Compare cholinergic agonists, acetylcholinesterase inhibitors, and anticholinergics, and recognize crisis states of each.
  • Distinguish depolarizing from non-depolarizing neuromuscular blockade and explain why only one is reversible.
  • Name the antidotes for cholinergic crisis, anticholinergic toxicity, and non-depolarizing blockade.
  • Identify the high-alert autonomic drugs and the monitoring a nurse owns for each.

Section 1The autonomic nervous system

Two divisions, one body

The autonomic nervous system (ANS) runs everything you do not consciously decide: heart rate, vessel tone, breathing passages, digestion, sweating, pupil size, bladder emptying. It has two divisions that mostly oppose each other, and the classic shorthand is worth keeping because it is genuinely predictive. The sympathetic division is fight or flight — the body preparing to run from something. The parasympathetic division is rest and digest — the body settling down to housekeeping. Ask yourself what a person sprinting from danger needs, and sympathetic effects fall out on their own: faster heart, higher pressure, open airways, dilated pupils to see the threat, glucose released for fuel, blood shunted to muscle and away from gut and bladder. Ask what a person napping after a meal needs, and the parasympathetic list appears: slower heart, constricted pupils, active gut, a bladder that empties.

The two divisions differ in their chemistry, and that is where drugs get their leverage. Every autonomic pathway uses two neurons in series with a ganglion (a synapse) between them. At every ganglion, in both divisions, the transmitter is acetylcholine (ACh) acting on nicotinic receptors. What changes is the last step, at the organ itself. Parasympathetic nerves release acetylcholine onto muscarinic receptors — these are the cholinergic synapses. Most sympathetic nerves release norepinephrine onto adrenergic receptors. Two important sympathetic exceptions are worth memorizing now, because they explain odd drug effects later: the adrenal medulla is a modified ganglion that dumps epinephrine straight into the blood as a hormone, and the sweat glands are sympathetic but use acetylcholine on muscarinic receptors — which is exactly why anticholinergic drugs make patients hot and dry.

The receptor map — the single most useful table in pharmacology

Nearly everything in this chapter is an application of the table below. A drug does not have effects because of its name; it has effects because of which receptor it hits and where that receptor lives. Learn the receptor and you have learned the drug, the therapeutic use, and the adverse effect profile in one stroke.

ReceptorTransmitterWhere it livesWhat happens when stimulated
Alpha-1NorepinephrineVascular smooth muscle, pupil (radial muscle), bladder neck & prostateVasoconstriction → raised BP; pupil dilation (mydriasis); urinary retention
Alpha-2NorepinephrinePresynaptic nerve terminals; CNS (brainstem)Negative feedback — less norepinephrine released → lower BP, sedation
Beta-1NorepinephrineHeart (“1 heart”); kidney juxtaglomerular cellsFaster rate, stronger contraction, faster conduction; renin release
Beta-2Epinephrine (circulating)Lungs (“2 lungs”); skeletal-muscle vessels; uterus; liverBronchodilation; vasodilation; uterine relaxation; glycogenolysis
Beta-3NorepinephrineBladder detrusor muscle; fat cellsDetrusor relaxation (bladder holds more); lipolysis
MuscarinicAcetylcholineHeart, gut, bladder detrusor, airways, glands, eye, sweat glandsSLUDGE — slower heart, gut motility, bladder emptying, secretions, bronchoconstriction, miosis
Nicotinic (NN)AcetylcholineAll autonomic ganglia; adrenal medullaTransmission through the ganglion; catecholamine release
Nicotinic (NM)AcetylcholineSkeletal muscle end plate (somatic, not autonomic)Muscle contraction — the target of neuromuscular blockers

Two memory hooks pay for themselves all semester. “One heart, two lungs” puts beta-1 in the heart and beta-2 in the lungs and never lets you mix them up — and it instantly explains why a nonselective beta blocker is dangerous in asthma. SLUDGE (Salivation, Lacrimation, Urination, Defecation, GI upset, Emesis) is the muscarinic picture; the anticholinergic picture is simply its mirror image, which students remember as dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter, hot as a hare.

Four things a drug can do

Every drug in this chapter falls into one of four buckets, and the vocabulary is worth pinning down now. A sympathomimetic (adrenergic agonist) mimics the sympathetic system. A sympatholytic (adrenergic antagonist, or blocker) blocks it. A parasympathomimetic (cholinergic agonist) mimics the parasympathetic system. A parasympatholytic (anticholinergic) blocks it. Notice the elegance hiding in there: blocking one division looks a great deal like stimulating the other. A beta blocker slows the heart, and so does a cholinergic drug — different receptor, same bradycardia on your monitor. Do not let the clinical picture alone tell you which drug your patient took.

Predict, don't memorize. Before you look up an autonomic drug's side effects, name its receptor and ask what that receptor's organ does. Alpha-1 agonist? Vasoconstriction — so expect hypertension, reflex bradycardia, and tissue damage if it infiltrates an IV site. You will be right far more often than not, and on an exam you can reconstruct a fact you never memorized.
Interactive Tap any of ten organs and flip between rest-and-digest and fight-or-flight to see what each autonomic branch does, which receptor carries the signal, and what the nurse assesses because of it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The sympathetic division is fight-or-flight (mostly norepinephrine on adrenergic receptors); the parasympathetic is rest-and-digest (acetylcholine on muscarinic receptors).
  • All ganglia in both divisions use acetylcholine on nicotinic receptors; the divisions differ at the organ, not the ganglion.
  • One heart (beta-1), two lungs (beta-2); alpha-1 constricts vessels; alpha-2 is presynaptic negative feedback that lowers BP.
  • Sympathetic sweat glands are cholinergic — the reason anticholinergics cause dry, hot skin.

Section 2Adrenergic agonists

Turning up the sympathetic gas

An adrenergic agonist, or sympathomimetic, produces the effects the sympathetic nervous system would produce. Some do it directly, by binding the receptor themselves (epinephrine, norepinephrine, phenylephrine, albuterol). Others do it indirectly, by causing the nerve terminal to dump its own stored norepinephrine or by blocking its reuptake (ephedrine, which is mixed direct and indirect; amphetamines; cocaine). Either way the receptor sees more signal, and the organ responds.

What makes this class confusing at first is that a single drug can hit several receptors at once, so its effect depends on which receptors it reaches and at what dose. Selectivity is the whole game. A drug that hits only beta-2 opens airways without racing the heart; one that hits everything opens airways and races the heart and clamps down the vessels. Selectivity is also dose-dependent — push the dose high enough and a “selective” drug starts spilling onto its neighbors. This is precisely why an asthmatic patient who overuses albuterol develops tachycardia and tremor: at high doses the beta-2 selectivity leaks onto beta-1.

Epinephrine: the drug that changes personality with dose

Epinephrine is the prototype and the one to know cold. It is a nonselective agonist — alpha-1, alpha-2, beta-1, and beta-2 — but its receptors have different sensitivities, so the drug behaves differently at different doses. At low doses, beta effects dominate: the heart speeds up, contractility rises, and airways open while skeletal-muscle vessels actually dilate, which can drop the diastolic pressure. At high doses, alpha-1 effects take over and vasoconstriction wins, driving blood pressure up hard. This dose-dependent split is why the same molecule is used for anaphylaxis, for cardiac arrest, for shock, and (in dental and local anesthetic mixtures) to keep lidocaine from washing away.

In anaphylaxis, epinephrine is doing three jobs simultaneously, and the nurse should be able to name all three: alpha-1 constricts vessels to reverse the catastrophic drop in blood pressure and reduce airway edema; beta-1 supports cardiac output; beta-2 dilates the bronchi and stabilizes mast cells against further mediator release. No other drug does all of that. Antihistamines and steroids are adjuncts and never the first move. The first-line route for anaphylaxis is intramuscular into the vault of the lateral thigh (vastus lateralis), because that muscle's rich blood supply absorbs the drug faster and more reliably than subcutaneous tissue or the deltoid.

The workhorses, drug by drug

DrugReceptorsMain useNursing alert
EpinephrineAlpha-1/2, beta-1/2Anaphylaxis, cardiac arrest, severe asthma, shockHigh alert. Anaphylaxis dose is IM lateral thigh. Concentration errors are classic and lethal — verify with an independent double-check.
NorepinephrineAlpha-1 >> beta-1Septic shock — first-line vasopressorHigh alert, central line preferred. Watch the site continuously; extravasation causes tissue necrosis — antidote is phentolamine infiltrated locally.
DopamineDose-dependent: dopaminergic → beta-1 → alpha-1Shock, symptomatic bradycardiaSame extravasation risk; monitor urine output and rhythm continuously.
DobutamineBeta-1 selectiveAcute decompensated heart failure — inotropic supportRaises contractility; can cause tachycardia and drop BP through mild beta-2 vasodilation.
PhenylephrineAlpha-1 selectiveHypotension, nasal decongestant, mydriatic dropsRaises BP with reflex bradycardia; caution in BPH (retention) and glaucoma.
AlbuterolBeta-2 selective (short-acting)Rescue bronchodilator in asthma/COPDTremor, tachycardia, hypokalemia (drives K+ into cells). Rescue, not maintenance — rising use signals poor control.
Salmeterol, formoterolBeta-2 selective (long-acting)Asthma/COPD maintenanceNever for an acute attack; in asthma must be paired with an inhaled corticosteroid, never used alone.
ClonidineAlpha-2 agonist (central)Hypertension, ADHD, opioid/nicotine withdrawalCounterintuitive: stimulating alpha-2 lowers BP. Sedation, dry mouth; never stop abruptly — rebound hypertensive crisis.
TerbutalineBeta-2 selectiveBronchospasm; off-label short-term tocolysisMaternal tachycardia and hyperglycemia; not for prolonged tocolysis.

Look again at clonidine, because it trips up nearly everyone. It is an agonist, yet it lowers blood pressure. The reason is where its receptor sits: alpha-2 receptors are presynaptic brakes. Stimulating them tells the nerve terminal to release less norepinephrine. So an alpha-2 agonist functionally behaves like a sympathetic blocker — a good reminder that receptor location matters as much as receptor type.

Watch the IV site like it's the patient. Norepinephrine, dopamine, and epinephrine constrict the vessels they sit in. If one infiltrates, the tissue loses its blood supply and dies. Blanching, coolness, or pain at the site means stop the infusion, aspirate what you can, leave the catheter in, and call for phentolamine to infiltrate the area — an alpha blocker that reopens the strangled vessels. Central access is preferred for exactly this reason.
Interactive Choose alpha-1, alpha-2, beta-1, or beta-2 and watch its target organs light up on the body — each with its signature response, the agonists that hit it, and what the nurse monitors. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Sympathomimetics mimic the sympathetic system directly (receptor binding) or indirectly (transmitter release/reuptake block).
  • Epinephrine is dose-dependent: beta effects at low doses, alpha vasoconstriction at high doses; anaphylaxis dose is IM lateral thigh.
  • Beta-2 agonists (albuterol) rescue airways but cause tremor, tachycardia, and hypokalemia; long-acting agents are never rescue drugs.
  • Clonidine is an alpha-2 agonist that lowers BP via presynaptic feedback — and rebounds dangerously if stopped abruptly.
  • Vasopressor extravasation causes necrosis; the antidote is local phentolamine.

Section 3Adrenergic antagonists

Alpha blockers: releasing the vessels

An alpha-1 blocker stops norepinephrine from constricting vascular smooth muscle. The vessels relax, resistance falls, and blood pressure comes down. The same receptor sits in the bladder neck and prostatic smooth muscle, so blocking it also relaxes the outflow tract — which is why the “-osin” drugs are workhorses in benign prostatic hyperplasia (BPH), letting men empty a bladder that a big prostate had been squeezing shut. Tamsulosin is relatively uroselective and drops pressure less; doxazosin, terazosin, and prazosin lower blood pressure more and are sometimes used for that purpose (prazosin is also used off-label for PTSD-related nightmares, though the trial evidence is mixed).

The signature hazard is first-dose orthostatic hypotension. The first dose — or the first dose after any increase — can drop a patient's pressure sharply enough to cause syncope, and a syncopal fall in an older adult is a hip fracture waiting to happen. The nursing response is concrete: give the first dose at bedtime, teach the patient to rise slowly and to sit on the edge of the bed before standing, warn them not to drive after the first dose, and check orthostatic vitals. Phentolamine is a nonselective alpha blocker used differently — as the antidote for vasopressor extravasation and in the management of catecholamine excess such as pheochromocytoma.

Beta blockers: the “-olol” family

A beta blocker occupies beta receptors so catecholamines cannot. Blocking beta-1 slows the heart rate, weakens contraction, slows AV conduction, and suppresses renin release — the combination that lowers blood pressure, quiets angina by cutting myocardial oxygen demand, controls rate in atrial fibrillation, and, most importantly, improves survival after myocardial infarction and in chronic heart failure. That last point deserves emphasis because it is counterintuitive: a drug that weakens contraction helps the failing heart, because it protects it from years of catecholamine flogging. In heart failure the beta blocker is started at a low dose and titrated up slowly — and never started during an acute decompensation.

The critical distinction in this class is cardioselectivity. Cardioselective agents (metoprolol, atenolol, bisoprolol, esmolol — the ones that start with A through M) block mainly beta-1 and largely spare the lungs. Nonselective agents (propranolol, nadolol, timolol, sotalol — N through Z) block beta-2 as well, which means they can constrict bronchi. That is the mechanism behind the caution in asthma and COPD. The A–M letter trick is a study aid, not a rule — carvedilol and labetalol are the classic exceptions, since both begin in the first half of the alphabet yet block beta-1, beta-2, and alpha-1. Cardioselectivity is also relative, not absolute, and is lost at higher doses, so even a “cardioselective” blocker gets used cautiously in reactive airway disease.

Class / drugBlocksMain useNursing alert
TamsulosinAlpha-1 (uroselective)BPHOrthostasis, dizziness, retrograde ejaculation; flag before cataract surgery (floppy iris syndrome).
Prazosin, doxazosin, terazosinAlpha-1Hypertension, BPH; prazosin off-label for PTSD nightmaresFirst-dose syncope — give at bedtime, rise slowly, check orthostatics.
PhentolamineAlpha-1 & alpha-2Vasopressor extravasation; pheochromocytomaReflex tachycardia; infiltrate the extravasation site promptly.
Metoprolol, atenolol, bisoprololBeta-1 (cardioselective)Hypertension, angina, post-MI, heart failure, rate controlHold and reassess if HR under 60 or SBP low per parameters; never stop abruptly.
EsmololBeta-1 (ultra-short IV)Acute rate control, perioperativeContinuous cardiac and BP monitoring; effect ends within minutes of stopping.
Propranolol, nadololBeta-1 & beta-2 (nonselective)Hypertension, migraine prophylaxis, tremor, performance anxiety, thyroid stormCaution in asthma/COPD (bronchospasm); masks hypoglycemia signs.
Timolol (ophthalmic)Beta-1 & beta-2Glaucoma — reduces aqueous productionEye drops are systemic drugs. Apply punctal occlusion for 1–2 minutes; bradycardia and bronchospasm are real.
Carvedilol, labetalolBeta plus alpha-1Heart failure (carvedilol); hypertensive urgency and pregnancy (labetalol)Added alpha blockade means more orthostasis; take carvedilol with food.

The three cautions a nurse must own

First, never stop a beta blocker abruptly. Chronic blockade upregulates beta receptors, and yanking the drug away exposes a hypersensitized heart to circulating catecholamines — rebound tachycardia, severe hypertension, angina, and infarction have all been documented. Taper over one to two weeks, and teach the patient this explicitly, because a patient who feels fine and stops on their own is the one who ends up in the emergency department.

Second, beta blockers mask hypoglycemia. The trembling, racing heart, and anxiety that warn a diabetic patient their glucose is falling are beta-mediated sympathetic signals — block beta and the warning goes silent. Sweating is the one sign that survives, because sweat glands are cholinergic. Teach patients on beta blockers with diabetes to check their glucose rather than trust how they feel, and never assume a patient without tremor is safe.

Third, hold parameters are yours to enforce. Take an apical pulse for a full minute before every dose. If the heart rate is under 60 or the blood pressure is below the ordered threshold, hold the dose and notify the prescriber — do not simply give it and chart the vitals. Beta blockers also blunt the compensatory tachycardia of hypovolemia, so a bleeding patient on metoprolol may not mount the heart rate you would otherwise expect. In beta blocker overdose — profound bradycardia and hypotension unresponsive to atropine — the specific antidote is glucagon, which raises cardiac cAMP by a route that bypasses the blocked receptor entirely.

Eye drops count as drugs. A patient with glaucoma on timolol who becomes bradycardic or wheezy is not a mystery — the drop drained through the tear duct into systemic circulation. Teach punctal occlusion (gentle finger pressure at the inner corner for a minute or two after instilling), and list ophthalmic drops on the medication reconciliation. Patients almost never think of them as “medications.”
Interactive Pick an alpha or beta blocker and watch which receptor wires it cuts — then tap the heart, vessels, airways, kidney, or glucose response to see the benefit, the danger, and what the nurse does about it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Alpha-1 blockers (“-osin”) relax vessels and the bladder outlet — hypertension and BPH — and cause first-dose orthostatic syncope; dose at bedtime.
  • Cardioselective beta blockers (A–M: metoprolol, atenolol) spare beta-2; nonselective ones (N–Z: propranolol) risk bronchospasm in asthma/COPD.
  • Beta blockers improve survival post-MI and in chronic heart failure, but are started low and titrated slowly — not during acute decompensation.
  • Never stop abruptly (rebound crisis); they mask hypoglycemia except sweating; check apical pulse and honor hold parameters.
  • Beta blocker overdose antidote: glucagon. Vasopressor extravasation antidote: phentolamine.

Section 4Cholinergic and anticholinergic drugs

Cholinergic agonists: turning up rest-and-digest

A cholinergic agonist (parasympathomimetic) reproduces what acetylcholine does at muscarinic receptors, and the resulting picture is SLUDGE: salivation, lacrimation, urination, defecation, GI cramping, emesis — plus bradycardia, bronchoconstriction, and miosis. Written out that way it sounds purely like a poisoning, and at high doses it is. Used deliberately at therapeutic doses, though, two of those effects are useful. Bethanechol selectively stimulates the bladder detrusor and gut, which makes it the drug for urinary retention that is not caused by an obstruction — a distinction that matters enormously, because squeezing a bladder against a blocked outlet risks rupture. Pilocarpine, given as eye drops, constricts the pupil and opens the drainage angle in glaucoma, and orally it stimulates saliva in xerostomia. Cholinergic agonists are contraindicated in asthma (bronchoconstriction), in bradycardia or heart block, in peptic ulcer disease, and in bowel or bladder obstruction.

AChE inhibitors: making the body's own acetylcholine last longer

Acetylcholinesterase (AChE) is the enzyme that chews up acetylcholine in the synapse within milliseconds. Block the enzyme and acetylcholine lingers, so the same signal hits harder — an indirect cholinergic agonist. This is the mechanism behind three quite different therapies. In myasthenia gravis, where autoantibodies have destroyed nicotinic receptors at the muscle end plate, pyridostigmine keeps acetylcholine around long enough for the surviving receptors to work, restoring strength; the timing of doses around meals matters so patients can chew and swallow safely. In Alzheimer disease, donepezil, rivastigmine, and galantamine raise central acetylcholine — they modestly improve or stabilize symptoms for a time, but they neither cure the disease nor halt the underlying neurodegeneration. And in anesthesia, neostigmine reverses non-depolarizing neuromuscular blockade, which Section 5 takes up.

Push cholinergic stimulation too far and you get cholinergic crisis: profound SLUDGE with bradycardia, bronchoconstriction, copious secretions, and — the part that kills — muscle weakness and respiratory failure from a depolarized, overwhelmed end plate. This is the mechanism of organophosphate poisoning (insecticides and nerve agents), which irreversibly inactivates AChE. The treatment is two drugs, not one: atropine to block the muscarinic effects and dry the secretions, and pralidoxime to pull the organophosphate off the enzyme before the bond “ages” and becomes permanent. Atropine alone will not fix the nicotinic muscle weakness — a fact worth understanding rather than memorizing.

The cruel clinical trap in myasthenia is that myasthenic crisis (too little drug) and cholinergic crisis (too much) both present as severe weakness and respiratory failure. The differentiator is the company the weakness keeps: cholinergic crisis brings SLUDGE — drooling, tearing, cramping, tiny pupils, bradycardia — while myasthenic crisis does not. Either way the airway comes first.

Anticholinergics: blocking rest-and-digest

An anticholinergic (antimuscarinic, parasympatholytic) blocks muscarinic receptors, so the picture is SLUDGE in reverse: dry mouth, no sweat, blurred near vision and dilated pupils, tachycardia, constipation, and urinary retention, with confusion when the drug reaches the brain. Atropine is the prototype — the drug you push for symptomatic bradycardia, use to dry secretions preoperatively, and reach for in organophosphate poisoning. Ipratropium and tiotropium are inhaled anticholinergics that bronchodilate in COPD by a route completely different from albuterol's, which is why the two are often combined. Oxybutynin, tolterodine, and solifenacin quiet an overactive bladder. Scopolamine patches prevent motion sickness. Benztropine and trihexyphenidyl rebalance the dopamine–acetylcholine seesaw in Parkinson disease and in drug-induced extrapyramidal symptoms.

Class / drugActionMain useNursing alert
BethanecholDirect muscarinic agonistNon-obstructive urinary retentionContraindicated in obstruction (rupture risk) and asthma; keep a urinal/commode within reach — it works fast.
PilocarpineDirect muscarinic agonistGlaucoma; xerostomiaMiosis causes dim, blurred vision — fall risk, caution at night.
PyridostigmineAChE inhibitorMyasthenia gravisTime doses 30–60 min before meals; watch for cholinergic vs myasthenic crisis.
Donepezil, rivastigmine, galantamineCentral AChE inhibitorsAlzheimer diseaseEases symptoms modestly; does not cure or stop progression. Nausea, diarrhea, bradycardia, weight loss, vivid dreams.
NeostigmineAChE inhibitorReverses non-depolarizing blockadeAlways paired with an antimuscarinic (glycopyrrolate/atropine) to prevent bradycardia.
AtropineAntimuscarinicSymptomatic bradycardia; preop drying; organophosphate poisoningBradycardia dose is 1 mg IV push rapidly, repeat q3–5 min to a 3 mg max — a slow push or an underdose can paradoxically worsen bradycardia. Anticholinergic toxicity antidote: physostigmine.
Ipratropium, tiotropiumInhaled antimuscarinicCOPD — ipratropium short-acting (also added to albuterol in acute exacerbation); tiotropium long-acting maintenanceDry mouth — sugar-free gum, sips of water; caution with peanut/soy allergy for some inhaler formulations.
Oxybutynin, tolterodine, solifenacinAntimuscarinicOveractive bladderDry mouth, constipation, retention, confusion in older adults; a Beers-list concern.
ScopolamineAntimuscarinic (patch)Motion sickness, secretionsWash hands after handling — touching the eye causes unilateral mydriasis; remove old patch before applying new.
BenztropineCentral antimuscarinicParkinson disease; drug-induced EPSAnticholinergic burden, especially cognitive effects in older adults.

Every one of those anticholinergic effects is predictable from the table in Section 1, and so is the danger. Anticholinergic burden is the additive load of every drug with antimuscarinic activity a patient is taking — and it is not just the drugs above. Many antihistamines, tricyclic antidepressants, and antipsychotics carry hidden antimuscarinic activity. Stack three or four in an 80-year-old and you get confusion, falls, retention, and constipation that get misread as “dementia” or “just getting older.” Counting up anticholinergic burden on a medication list is one of the highest-yield things a nurse can do for an older patient.

Dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter, hot as a hare. That old rhyme is the complete anticholinergic toxidrome, and every line is mechanistic: no secretions (dry), flushed skin shunting heat because sweating is blocked (red, hot), paralyzed accommodation and dilated pupils (blind), central muscarinic blockade (mad). Because sweating fails, these patients cannot cool themselves — heat stroke is a genuine risk, so teach every patient on an anticholinergic to avoid heat and exertion. The antidote for severe toxicity is physostigmine.
Interactive Flip the muscarinic switch between cholinergic and anticholinergic and watch eight organs change at once — then tap any organ to compare SLUDGE against the "dry" effects side by side, with the nursing action for each. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Cholinergic agonists produce SLUDGE; bethanechol for non-obstructive retention, pilocarpine for glaucoma and dry mouth.
  • AChE inhibitors raise the body's own acetylcholine: pyridostigmine (myasthenia), donepezil (Alzheimer), neostigmine (reversal).
  • Cholinergic crisis = SLUDGE plus weakness and respiratory failure; organophosphate poisoning is treated with atropine plus pralidoxime.
  • Anticholinergics are the mirror image: dry, hot, red, blurred, tachycardic, retaining, confused. Antidote for toxicity: physostigmine.
  • Anticholinergic burden stacks across drug classes and drives confusion, falls, and retention in older adults.

Section 5Neuromuscular blockers

A different synapse, a much higher stake

Neuromuscular blocking agents (NMBAs) are technically somatic, not autonomic, but they belong here because they work on the same molecule — acetylcholine — at a nicotinic receptor. They act at the neuromuscular junction, on the NM receptor of the skeletal muscle end plate, to produce flaccid paralysis for intubation, surgery, and mechanical ventilation.

Three facts must be absolutely fixed before any of the pharmacology matters. These drugs do not sedate. They do not relieve pain. They do not cause amnesia. A paralyzed patient who has not been given adequate sedation and analgesia is fully awake, feeling everything, and unable to move a finger or make a sound to tell you — among the most terrifying experiences medicine can inflict. NMBAs are high-alert medications, stored separately with auxiliary warnings, and they are never given except where the airway can be secured and mechanical ventilation is immediately available. Paralysis always includes the diaphragm. The nurse's obligations are non-negotiable: confirm deep sedation and analgesia are running first, never allow paralysis without a secured airway, provide eye lubrication and taping (the blink is gone), reposition to prevent pressure injury, provide DVT prophylaxis, and keep talking to the patient because hearing is unaffected.

Depolarizing blockade: succinylcholine

Succinylcholine is the only depolarizing agent in common use, and it works by an odd trick: it is an agonist that paralyzes. It binds the nicotinic receptor and opens it — producing an initial wave of disorganized contractions called fasciculations — but unlike acetylcholine it is not broken down by acetylcholinesterase, so it sits on the receptor and holds the end plate depolarized. A depolarized membrane cannot fire again, and the muscle goes flaccid. Onset is under a minute and the effect wears off in five to ten minutes as plasma pseudocholinesterase metabolizes it, which is exactly the profile you want for rapid sequence intubation.

Three hazards define it. First, there is no reversal agent — you wait it out, ventilating the patient the whole time. Giving an AChE inhibitor would only prolong the block by adding more acetylcholine to an already depolarized plate. Second, the depolarization leaks potassium out of every muscle cell in the body, so succinylcholine is contraindicated in patients with existing hyperkalemia, and in burns, crush injury, denervation, spinal cord injury, or prolonged immobility beyond roughly 24–48 hours, where receptor upregulation can produce a potassium surge large enough to cause cardiac arrest. Third, succinylcholine is a leading trigger of malignant hyperthermia in genetically susceptible patients — a hypermetabolic crisis whose earliest reliable sign is a rising end-tidal CO2, with masseter rigidity, tachycardia, and a late temperature spike. The antidote is dantrolene, which must be immediately available wherever the drug is used. A rare patient with atypical pseudocholinesterase stays paralyzed for hours; the management is simply continued ventilation and sedation until it clears.

Non-depolarizing blockade: the “-curium” and “-curonium” drugs

Rocuronium, vecuronium, cisatracurium, and atracurium take the more intuitive route: they are competitive antagonists that occupy the nicotinic receptor without activating it, so acetylcholine cannot get in. There are no fasciculations, onset is slower (though a large dose of rocuronium approaches succinylcholine's speed), and the block lasts much longer. Because the blockade is competitive, it is reversible — flood the junction with enough acetylcholine and it outcompetes the drug. That is what neostigmine does, and it is always given with glycopyrrolate or atropine, because the extra acetylcholine spilling onto muscarinic receptors would otherwise cause bradycardia and drowning secretions. Sugammadex is the newer and cleaner option: it physically encapsulates rocuronium or vecuronium molecules and drags them out of circulation, reversing even deep blockade in minutes without touching the cholinergic system at all. Cisatracurium earns a special mention because it degrades spontaneously in plasma (Hofmann elimination) rather than through liver or kidney, making it the agent of choice in organ failure.

FeatureDepolarizing (succinylcholine)Non-depolarizing (rocuronium, vecuronium, cisatracurium)
MechanismAgonist — opens the receptor and holds it depolarizedCompetitive antagonist — occupies the receptor, blocks ACh
FasciculationsYes, then flaccid paralysisNo
Onset / durationUnder 1 min / 5–10 min1–3 min / 30–60+ min
ReversalNone — ventilate and waitNeostigmine + glycopyrrolate, or sugammadex (roc/vec)
Effect of AChE inhibitorProlongs the blockReverses the block
Key dangersHyperkalemia, malignant hyperthermia, myalgias, bradycardia in childrenProlonged paralysis, residual weakness; potentiated by aminoglycosides, magnesium, hypothermia
Typical useRapid sequence intubationSurgical relaxation, ventilated ICU patients

Monitoring is where the nurse lives with these drugs. Train-of-four (TOF) peripheral nerve stimulation delivers four small shocks and counts the resulting twitches; the depth of blockade is titrated to a target, commonly one to two twitches out of four for a continuous infusion. Fewer twitches means deeper block and a longer, harder recovery. Before extubation, recovery is judged by objective criteria — sustained head lift, adequate tidal volume and grip, the ability to protect the airway — and never by appearance alone, because a patient can look awake while still too weak to breathe. Remember too that several common drugs deepen and prolong blockade: aminoglycoside antibiotics, magnesium sulfate, calcium channel blockers, inhaled anesthetics, hypothermia, and acidosis. A postpartum patient on a magnesium drip is a different pharmacologic problem than the same patient without it.

Paralysis is not sedation, and the patient can hear you. Sedation and analgesia go in first and stay running — check the pumps and the volume in the bag before you check anything else, and treat an unexplained rise in heart rate or blood pressure in a paralyzed patient as pain or awareness until proven otherwise, because it is the only signal they have left. Then keep speaking to them by name and explaining what you are doing. Hearing is the last sense standing, and it costs you nothing.
Interactive Block the neuromuscular junction with succinylcholine, rocuronium, or cisatracurium — watch the receptors, read the train-of-four, then try each reversal agent and find out which one saves the patient and which one deepens the block. Open full ↑
Key points
  • NMBAs act at the nicotinic (NM) receptor of the motor end plate; they are high-alert drugs that paralyze the diaphragm.
  • They provide no sedation, no analgesia, no amnesia — sedation and pain control must run first and continuously.
  • Succinylcholine depolarizes: fasciculations, ultra-short action, no reversal; risks hyperkalemia and malignant hyperthermia (antidote dantrolene).
  • Non-depolarizers compete: no fasciculations, longer action, reversible with neostigmine + glycopyrrolate or sugammadex.
  • Monitor depth with train-of-four; aminoglycosides and magnesium prolong blockade; confirm real strength before extubation.

The chapter in one breath

  • The autonomic nervous system has a sympathetic (fight-or-flight, adrenergic) and a parasympathetic (rest-and-digest, cholinergic) division; all ganglia are nicotinic.
  • The receptor map does the work: alpha-1 constricts, alpha-2 feeds back to lower BP, beta-1 is the heart, beta-2 is the lungs, muscarinic is SLUDGE.
  • Epinephrine is dose-dependent (beta low, alpha high) and is the first-line, IM-lateral-thigh drug in anaphylaxis; vasopressor extravasation is reversed with phentolamine.
  • Beta-2 agonists rescue airways at the price of tremor, tachycardia, and hypokalemia; long-acting agents are maintenance only.
  • Alpha blockers cause first-dose syncope (dose at bedtime); beta blockers save lives post-MI and in heart failure but must never be stopped abruptly and mask hypoglycemia except sweating.
  • AChE inhibitors amplify the body's own acetylcholine (myasthenia, Alzheimer, reversal); overshoot is cholinergic crisis, treated with atropine + pralidoxime in organophosphate poisoning.
  • Anticholinergics are dry, hot, red, blurred, tachycardic, and confused — and their burden stacks dangerously in older adults; the antidote is physostigmine.
  • Neuromuscular blockers paralyze without sedating: succinylcholine depolarizes and cannot be reversed (hyperkalemia, malignant hyperthermia → dantrolene); non-depolarizers compete and reverse with neostigmine + glycopyrrolate or sugammadex.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Acetylcholine (ACh)
The transmitter at all autonomic ganglia, at parasympathetic organ synapses, at sympathetic sweat glands, and at the skeletal muscle end plate.
Acetylcholinesterase (AChE)
The enzyme that breaks down acetylcholine in the synapse; blocking it amplifies and prolongs cholinergic signaling.
Alpha-1 receptor
Receptor on vascular smooth muscle, pupil, and bladder neck; stimulation causes vasoconstriction, mydriasis, and urinary retention.
Alpha-2 receptor
Presynaptic and central receptor whose stimulation reduces norepinephrine release, lowering blood pressure (clonidine).
Anticholinergic (antimuscarinic)
A drug that blocks muscarinic receptors, producing dry mouth, blurred vision, tachycardia, constipation, retention, and confusion.
Anticholinergic burden
The cumulative antimuscarinic load from all of a patient's drugs, a major cause of confusion and falls in older adults.
Autonomic nervous system
The involuntary nervous system controlling heart, vessels, airways, gut, bladder, glands, and pupils.
Beta-1 receptor
Cardiac (“one heart”) and renal receptor; stimulation raises rate, contractility, conduction, and renin release.
Beta-2 receptor
Pulmonary (“two lungs”), vascular, uterine, and hepatic receptor; stimulation causes bronchodilation and vasodilation.
Cardioselective
Describing a beta blocker that acts mainly on beta-1 and largely spares beta-2; selectivity is relative and lost at high doses.
Cholinergic crisis
Excess cholinergic stimulation causing SLUDGE plus muscle weakness and respiratory failure; the mirror trap of myasthenic crisis.
Depolarizing blocker
A neuromuscular blocker (succinylcholine) that activates and then holds the end plate depolarized; has no reversal agent.
Extravasation
Leakage of a vesicant such as a vasopressor into surrounding tissue, risking necrosis; reversed locally with phentolamine.
Malignant hyperthermia
A hypermetabolic crisis triggered by succinylcholine or volatile anesthetics; earliest sign is rising end-tidal CO2; antidote is dantrolene.
Muscarinic receptor
The acetylcholine receptor on parasympathetic target organs; stimulation produces the SLUDGE picture.
Neuromuscular blocking agent (NMBA)
A high-alert drug that paralyzes skeletal muscle at the nicotinic end plate without sedating or relieving pain.
Nicotinic receptor
The acetylcholine receptor at autonomic ganglia (NN) and at the skeletal muscle end plate (NM).
Non-depolarizing blocker
A competitive nicotinic antagonist (rocuronium, vecuronium, cisatracurium) reversible with neostigmine or sugammadex.
Parasympathetic division
The rest-and-digest division; acetylcholine on muscarinic receptors slows the heart and activates gut and bladder.
SLUDGE
Salivation, Lacrimation, Urination, Defecation, GI upset, Emesis — the muscarinic (cholinergic excess) picture.
Sympathetic division
The fight-or-flight division; mostly norepinephrine on adrenergic receptors, plus adrenal epinephrine release.
Train-of-four (TOF)
Peripheral nerve stimulation delivering four impulses to gauge the depth of neuromuscular blockade by counting twitches.

Check yourself

1. A patient asks why their asthma inhaler makes their hands shake and their heart pound if it is “just for the lungs.” Explain in receptor terms.
Beta-2 selectivity is relative, not absolute — at higher doses albuterol spills over onto beta-1 receptors in the heart, and beta-2 receptors in skeletal muscle cause tremor. Also watch for hypokalemia, since beta-2 stimulation drives potassium into cells. Escalating rescue-inhaler use signals poor asthma control and should be reported.
2. Why is epinephrine, and not an antihistamine or a steroid, the first drug in anaphylaxis — and by what route and site?
Epinephrine does three things at once: alpha-1 vasoconstriction reverses hypotension and airway edema, beta-1 supports cardiac output, and beta-2 dilates bronchi and stabilizes mast cells. Give it IM into the vault of the lateral thigh (vastus lateralis). Antihistamines and steroids are adjuncts that work far too slowly to be first-line.
3. Clonidine is an alpha-2 agonist, yet it lowers blood pressure. Resolve the apparent contradiction, and state the teaching point that keeps a patient out of the ED.
Alpha-2 receptors are presynaptic and central brakes — stimulating them tells the nerve terminal to release less norepinephrine, so the net effect is sympatholytic. Teaching: never stop clonidine abruptly — rebound hypertensive crisis. Receptor location matters as much as receptor type.
4. A norepinephrine infusion site is pale, cool, and painful. What are your actions, and what is the antidote?
This is extravasation of a vasoconstrictor. Stop the infusion, aspirate what you can, leave the catheter in place, notify the prescriber, and infiltrate the area with phentolamine (an alpha blocker that reopens the constricted vessels). Restart the vasopressor at another site — ideally central access, which is preferred precisely for this reason.
5. Your patient with COPD is prescribed propranolol for migraine prophylaxis. What is your concern, and what would you ask about?
Propranolol is nonselective — it blocks beta-2 in the lungs and can cause bronchospasm in reactive airway disease. Clarify with the prescriber whether a cardioselective agent (metoprolol, atenolol) would be safer, and note that even cardioselectivity is dose-dependent and relative.
6. A patient with type 1 diabetes is started on metoprolol. What specific hypoglycemia teaching is required, and why?
Beta blockade masks the beta-mediated warning signs of hypoglycemia — tremor, palpitations, anxiety. Sweating still occurs because sweat glands are cholinergic. Teach the patient to check blood glucose on a schedule rather than relying on how they feel, and to treat diaphoresis as a warning sign.
7. Your patient's apical pulse is 52 and metoprolol is due. What do you do, and what should you never advise the patient to do?
Hold the dose and notify the prescriber — standard hold parameters are a heart rate under 60 or a systolic BP below the ordered threshold. Take the apical pulse for a full minute. Never advise stopping a beta blocker abruptly: chronic blockade upregulates receptors, and abrupt withdrawal causes rebound tachycardia, hypertension, angina, or infarction. Taper over 1–2 weeks.
8. Why is the first dose of doxazosin given at bedtime, and what else do you teach?
Because of first-dose orthostatic hypotension and syncope — alpha-1 blockade abruptly relaxes vessels, and the resulting fall can fracture a hip. Teach rising slowly, sitting on the edge of the bed before standing, no driving after the first dose, and expect the same risk after any dose increase. Check orthostatic vitals.
9. A patient with myasthenia gravis on pyridostigmine develops severe weakness, drooling, tearing, abdominal cramping, and pinpoint pupils. Which crisis is this, and how do you know?
Cholinergic crisis — too much drug. Both crises cause weakness and respiratory failure, but only cholinergic crisis brings the SLUDGE picture (salivation, lacrimation, cramping, miosis, bradycardia). Myasthenic crisis (too little drug) has weakness without SLUDGE. Either way, airway and ventilation come first.
10. A farmworker arrives with copious secretions, bradycardia, pinpoint pupils, and muscle weakness after pesticide exposure. Name the mechanism and both antidotes, and explain why one is not enough.
Organophosphate poisoning irreversibly inhibits acetylcholinesterase, so acetylcholine accumulates. Give atropine to block the muscarinic effects and dry the secretions, and pralidoxime to reactivate the enzyme before the bond “ages.” Atropine alone cannot fix the nicotinic effects — the skeletal muscle weakness and respiratory failure — because atropine only blocks muscarinic receptors.
11. An 82-year-old on oxybutynin, diphenhydramine, and amitriptyline becomes confused and cannot urinate. Name the phenomenon, and explain why she is also at risk in hot weather.
This is anticholinergic burden — antimuscarinic activity stacked across three drug classes, causing confusion and urinary retention. Because sympathetic sweat glands are cholinergic, blocking muscarinic receptors abolishes sweating, so she cannot dissipate heat — heat stroke is a genuine risk. Recognize the toxidrome: dry, red, blind, mad, hot. Severe toxicity is treated with physostigmine.
12. Why can neostigmine reverse rocuronium but not succinylcholine, and what must always accompany neostigmine?
Rocuronium is a competitive antagonist, so raising acetylcholine with neostigmine outcompetes it at the receptor. Succinylcholine is a depolarizing agonist — adding more acetylcholine only deepens and prolongs the block, so there is no reversal; you ventilate and wait. Neostigmine is always paired with glycopyrrolate or atropine to block the muscarinic spillover (bradycardia, secretions). Sugammadex is an alternative that encapsulates rocuronium or vecuronium directly.
Pharmacology · Chapter 8

Endocrine Drugs

The endocrine system runs on chemical messages sent slowly, in tiny amounts, through the bloodstream — and endocrine pharmacology is mostly the art of replacing a message the body has stopped sending, or muffling one it is sending too loudly. That sounds simple until you meet the feedback loop, which quietly turns off the patient's own gland the moment you supply the hormone from outside. Almost every endocrine drug error a nurse will ever see traces back to that one fact, or to a decimal point in an insulin dose. This chapter walks through the pituitary, the thyroid, the adrenal cortex, and the pancreas — the four glands you will medicate most — and the safety habits that keep those drugs from hurting the person you gave them to.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain negative feedback and predict what happens to a patient's own gland when you give the hormone from outside.
  • Describe the major pituitary agents — including desmopressin and octreotide — and their monitoring.
  • Teach a patient how to take levothyroxine correctly and recognize under- and over-replacement.
  • Compare methimazole and propylthiouracil and name the adverse effect that must be reported immediately.
  • List the major adverse effects of glucocorticoids and explain why long-term therapy must be tapered.
  • State the onset, peak, and duration of each insulin class and use peak times to anticipate hypoglycemia.
  • Recognize and treat hypoglycemia, including glucagon rescue in an unresponsive patient.
  • Describe metformin and the other oral antidiabetic classes, with the nursing alert unique to each.

Section 1Hormone principles and the pituitary

What makes a hormone drug different

A hormone is a chemical messenger released by a gland into the blood, carried to a distant tissue, and recognized there by a receptor built to fit it. Two features of that arrangement matter enormously at the bedside. First, hormones work at vanishingly small concentrations — a therapeutic dose of levothyroxine is measured in micrograms, a thousandth of the milligram doses you are used to — so a decimal slip is not a small error, it is a hundredfold one. Second, hormones come in two chemical families that behave completely differently as drugs.

Lipid-soluble hormones (thyroid hormone and the steroid hormones such as cortisol) slip straight through the cell membrane and act on receptors inside the cell, changing which genes get transcribed. Because they work by building new proteins, their effects come on over hours to days and linger after the drug is gone — and because they are fat-soluble, they survive the gut and can be given by mouth. Water-soluble hormones (insulin, growth hormone, glucagon, vasopressin, and the other peptides) cannot cross the membrane; they dock on a receptor on the cell surface and trigger a fast intracellular signaling cascade. They act in minutes — and they are proteins, which means digestive enzymes destroy them. That single chemical fact is why insulin cannot be a pill. It is not a marketing decision or a gap in the science; a swallowed peptide is simply lunch.

Negative feedback: the idea the whole chapter rests on

Endocrine glands are governed by negative feedback, a thermostat arrangement. The hypothalamus signals the anterior pituitary, the pituitary signals the target gland, the target gland releases its hormone — and that circulating hormone travels back and switches off the hypothalamus and pituitary that ordered it. The classic example is the thyroid axis: TRH → TSH → thyroid hormone → suppression of TRH and TSH. The adrenal axis works the same way: CRH → ACTH → cortisol → suppression of CRH and ACTH.

Here is the clinical consequence, and it is worth reading twice. The loop cannot tell the difference between hormone made by the patient and hormone you injected. When you give prednisone daily for two months, the patient's hypothalamus sees plenty of steroid, stops sending CRH, the pituitary stops sending ACTH, and the adrenal cortex — unused, unstimulated — atrophies. Stop the prednisone abruptly and there is no cortisol from either source: the drug is gone and the gland cannot answer. That is adrenal crisis, and it is the reason you will hear "never stop a steroid suddenly" for the rest of your career.
