The same logic explains why replacement doses are titrated against the pituitary's signal — a TSH level is really the pituitary's opinion of whether your levothyroxine dose is right.

Drugs that act at the pituitary

The pituitary is two glands in one coat. The anterior pituitary manufactures its own hormones — growth hormone, TSH, ACTH, prolactin, FSH, LH. The posterior pituitary manufactures nothing; it is a storage tank for two hormones made in the hypothalamus, antidiuretic hormone (ADH, vasopressin) and oxytocin. Pituitary drugs either replace a missing signal or shut down an excessive one.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Growth hormonesomatropinReplaces GH in deficiency; promotes linear growth in childrenSubcutaneous only; rotate sites. Monitor growth velocity and blood glucose — GH is hyperglycemic. Report limp or hip/knee pain (slipped epiphysis).
Somatostatin analogoctreotideInhibits GH release; treats acromegaly, carcinoid, variceal bleedingMonitor glucose (can swing either way), watch for gallstones, bradycardia, steatorrhea/cramping. Check IGF-1 for response.
Dopamine agonistcabergoline, bromocriptineDopamine inhibits prolactin, so these lower prolactin and shrink prolactinomasOrthostatic hypotension — first dose at bedtime, rise slowly. Nausea; take with food. Watch for compulsive behaviors.
ADH replacementdesmopressin (DDAVP)Synthetic ADH; treats diabetes insipidus, nocturnal enuresis, mild hemophilia A/von WillebrandWater intoxication and hyponatremia. Monitor daily weight, I&O, urine specific gravity, serum sodium. Report headache, confusion, seizure.
VasopressinvasopressinADH plus potent vasoconstriction; used in DI and in shockIV in critical care; monitor BP, sodium, and the site — extravasation causes tissue necrosis.
OxytocicoxytocinStimulates uterine contraction and milk let-downHigh-alert drug. Always on a pump, always titrated; monitor contraction pattern and fetal heart rate. Antidiuretic at high doses — watch sodium.

Notice the pattern hiding in that table: diabetes insipidus and SIADH are mirror images, and so are their drugs. In diabetes insipidus there is too little ADH, so the kidney cannot hold water — the patient pours out liters of pale, dilute urine, the specific gravity is low, and the serum sodium climbs. You give desmopressin. In SIADH there is too much ADH, water is retained, urine is scant and concentrated, and the sodium falls. Give too much desmopressin and you have simply converted the first problem into the second — which is why the monitoring for desmopressin is not the urine output alone but the sodium.

Weigh the patient. On any drug that moves water — desmopressin, vasopressin, steroids — the daily weight is a more honest fluid record than the I&O sheet, because nobody forgets to weigh and everybody forgets to chart a cup of ice chips. One kilogram of weight change is one liter of water. A patient on desmopressin who gains two kilograms overnight and complains of a headache is telling you the sodium is falling before the lab does.
Interactive Turn the exogenous hormone dose and duration up on four hypothalamic–pituitary axes and watch the tropic signal fall and the gland atrophy — why chronic steroids must be tapered, why a suppressed TSH means too much levothyroxine, why testosterone causes infertility, and why somatropin needs no taper at all. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Hormones act at microgram concentrations — decimal errors are catastrophic; peptide hormones like insulin are digested, so they cannot be oral.
  • Negative feedback cannot distinguish the patient's hormone from yours, so exogenous hormone suppresses and atrophies the patient's own gland.
  • The pituitary drugs either replace (somatropin, desmopressin) or suppress (octreotide, cabergoline).
  • Desmopressin's danger is water intoxication — monitor serum sodium, daily weight, and neuro status, not just urine output.

Section 2Thyroid drugs

Levothyroxine: replacing what is missing

Thyroid hormone sets the body's metabolic idle speed. Too little and everything slows — the patient is cold, constipated, bradycardic, gaining weight, thinking through fog. Levothyroxine (T4) is the synthetic replacement, and it is one of the most-prescribed drugs in the country. It is chemically identical to the T4 the thyroid makes, and the body converts it to the more active T3 at the tissues, which is exactly why T4 is preferred over giving T3 directly: the patient's own tissues decide how much active hormone they need. Levothyroxine has a long half-life (about 7 days), so it is a once-daily drug, effects take weeks to appear, and a single missed dose is not an emergency.

It is also a narrow therapeutic index drug, which drives everything about how it is taken. Give it on an empty stomach with water, 30–60 minutes before breakfast, at the same time every day. Food — especially soy, walnuts, high-fiber meals, and coffee — blunts absorption. Calcium, iron, multivitamins, antacids, sucralfate, and cholestyramine bind it in the gut; separate those by at least 4 hours. Because absorption is so finicky, patients should stay on the same product and not switch brands casually. Dose is titrated against the TSH, rechecked about 6–8 weeks after any change — sooner is just measuring noise, because the hormone has not reached steady state.

Teach the patient what over-replacement feels like, because it looks exactly like hyperthyroidism: tachycardia, palpitations, nervousness, insomnia, heat intolerance, tremor, unintended weight loss. In older adults, an excessive dose brings two real hazards — atrial fibrillation and, over years, osteoporosis. This is why an older patient, or anyone with coronary artery disease, is started on a deliberately low dose and increased slowly: a suddenly faster heart in an already-narrowed coronary artery can precipitate angina or infarction. Two more teaching points that come up constantly: levothyroxine is lifelong — feeling better is evidence it is working, not permission to stop — and requirements increase during pregnancy, so a pregnant patient needs her dose and her TSH revisited early.

Antithyroid agents: turning the gland down

Hyperthyroidism is the opposite picture — racing heart, weight loss despite eating, heat intolerance, tremor, anxiety, diarrhea. The thionamides, methimazole and propylthiouracil (PTU), block the enzyme that builds thyroid hormone. They do not touch hormone already stored in the gland, so the patient does not improve for several weeks — a fact worth telling them up front, or they will decide the drug does not work and quit.

Methimazole is first-line: it is more potent, dosed once daily, and better tolerated. PTU is reserved for three situations — the first trimester of pregnancy (methimazole carries a teratogenic risk in early pregnancy), thyroid storm (PTU has the extra benefit of blocking peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion), and patients who cannot take methimazole — because PTU carries a boxed warning for severe hepatotoxicity, including liver failure. Both drugs share the effect you must burn into memory: agranulocytosis. If a patient on a thionamide reports a sore throat, fever, or mouth ulcers, that is not a cold. Hold the drug, notify the provider, and get a CBC with differential.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Thyroid replacementlevothyroxine (T4)Restores metabolic rate; converted to T3 peripherallyEmpty stomach, 30–60 min before breakfast; separate calcium/iron/antacids by 4 h. Titrate to TSH q6–8 wk. Report chest pain, palpitations, weight loss. Lifelong.
T3 replacementliothyronine (T3)Faster onset, shorter half-life; myxedema coma, selected casesMore cardiac stimulation than T4 — monitor HR and rhythm closely.
ThionamidemethimazoleBlocks hormone synthesis; first-line for hyperthyroidismAgranulocytosis — report sore throat/fever at once. Avoid in first trimester. Effect takes weeks.
Thionamidepropylthiouracil (PTU)Blocks synthesis and peripheral T4→T3 conversionBoxed warning: hepatotoxicity. Report jaundice, dark urine, RUQ pain, anorexia. Reserved for first trimester and thyroid storm.
Iodine solutionpotassium iodide (SSKI, Lugol's)High-dose iodine transiently shuts down hormone release; shrinks/firms gland pre-opGive 1 hour after the thionamide. Dilute in juice, use a straw (stains teeth). Metallic taste. Short-term use only.
Radioactive iodineiodine-131Destroys thyroid tissue permanentlyContraindicated in pregnancy/lactation. Radiation precautions; expect eventual hypothyroidism and lifelong levothyroxine.
Beta blockerpropranololDoes not treat the thyroid — blocks the adrenergic symptomsSymptom relief while thionamides take effect. Monitor HR and BP; caution in asthma.
Sore throat is a lab test. A patient on methimazole or PTU who calls about a sore throat and fever is describing agranulocytosis until a CBC proves otherwise. It is the one complaint on these drugs that never gets a "push fluids and rest" answer — and the reason you teach it on day one, before the patient has any reason to think a sore throat is worth a phone call.
Interactive Pick a patient's thyroid state and a drug, then watch the labs, vital signs, and body change — why levothyroxine replaces and thionamides block, and what happens when you give the wrong one. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Levothyroxine: empty stomach 30–60 min before breakfast, separate binders by 4 h, titrate to TSH every 6–8 weeks, lifelong, start low in older/cardiac patients.
  • Over-replacement mimics hyperthyroidism — tachycardia, weight loss, insomnia; long term it risks atrial fibrillation and osteoporosis.
  • Methimazole is first-line; PTU is for the first trimester and thyroid storm and carries a hepatotoxicity boxed warning.
  • Both thionamides can cause agranulocytosis — sore throat, fever, or mouth ulcers means hold the drug and draw a CBC.

Section 3Corticosteroids

Two jobs in one gland

The adrenal cortex makes two kinds of steroid that matter here. Glucocorticoids — cortisol is the natural one — raise blood glucose, suppress inflammation, dampen the immune system, and help the body survive stress. Mineralocorticoids — aldosterone is the natural one — make the kidney retain sodium and water and excrete potassium. Every corticosteroid drug you give is a blend of those two activities, and the blend is the whole reason there are so many of them.

Hydrocortisone is essentially cortisol, with both activities, which makes it the drug of choice when you are replacing a missing adrenal gland. Prednisone and methylprednisolone are mostly glucocorticoid with a little mineralocorticoid — the everyday anti-inflammatories. Dexamethasone is nearly pure glucocorticoid, very potent, long-acting, and carries almost no salt-retaining effect, which is why it is chosen for cerebral edema and for situations where fluid retention would be a problem. Fludrocortisone is the mirror image: chosen for its mineralocorticoid punch, to replace aldosterone. One more thing about prednisone worth knowing: it is a prodrug, activated by the liver into prednisolone, so a patient in liver failure may be given prednisolone directly.

AgentEquivalent doseGlucocorticoid / mineralocorticoidTypical use & nursing alert
hydrocortisone20 mgBoth, roughly balancedAdrenal insufficiency replacement; IV stress dosing. Short-acting.
prednisone5 mgMostly gluco, some mineraloOral workhorse (asthma, RA, IBD). Prodrug — needs a working liver. Give with food.
methylprednisolone4 mgGluco > mineraloIV equivalent of prednisone; dose packs taper by design — teach the schedule.
dexamethasone0.75 mgAlmost pure gluco; long-actingMost potent; cerebral edema, croup, antiemetic. Little salt retention. Big glucose spikes.
fludrocortisone—Chosen for mineraloAddison's disease. Monitor BP, weight, edema, and potassium — it drives K+ out.

The adverse effects: predictable, and all one story

Every classic steroid side effect is just cortisol's normal job, turned up too far and left on too long. Cortisol raises glucose → steroids cause hyperglycemia, and a diabetic patient started on prednisone will need more insulin, not less. Cortisol suppresses immunity → steroids bring infection risk, and worse, they mask the signs: they blunt fever and inflammation, so a serious infection can be well advanced before it announces itself. Cortisol is catabolic → long-term use brings muscle wasting, thin fragile skin, easy bruising, poor wound healing, and osteoporosis. Redistributed fat gives the cushingoid look: moon face, buffalo hump, truncal obesity on thin limbs. Add mood changes (euphoria, agitation, insomnia, occasionally frank psychosis), GI irritation and ulcer risk (worse with concurrent NSAIDs), cataracts and glaucoma, growth suppression in children, and — from the mineralocorticoid side — fluid retention, hypertension, and hypokalemia.

The nursing translation is short and concrete: check blood glucose even in patients who are not diabetic, take daily weights and blood pressures, monitor potassium, give oral doses with food, watch for any infection with a low index of suspicion, and teach the patient to avoid crowds and sick contacts and to report any fever. Live vaccines are generally avoided in patients on immunosuppressive doses. Doses are usually given in the morning, mimicking the body's natural cortisol peak — which both reduces insomnia and disturbs the axis a little less. And every patient on chronic steroids should carry medical identification.

Tapering, and the crisis that follows if you don't

Return to the feedback loop from Section 1. After roughly two to three weeks of systemic steroid at a meaningful dose, the HPA axis is suppressed and the adrenal cortex begins to atrophy. It cannot resume production on demand. So a course of more than about two weeks is tapered — the dose stepped down gradually over days to weeks — giving the hypothalamus, pituitary, and adrenal time to wake up in sequence. Stop abruptly and the patient falls into acute adrenal insufficiency (adrenal crisis): hypotension, shock, weakness, nausea and vomiting, abdominal pain, hypoglycemia, hyponatremia with hyperkalemia. It is a genuine emergency treated with IV hydrocortisone and fluids.

The same physiology explains stress dosing. A healthy adrenal gland pumps out extra cortisol during surgery, trauma, or serious infection; a suppressed one cannot. So patients on chronic steroids need increased doses during physiologic stress — and this is exactly when a well-meaning patient with a stomach flu decides to skip their pills because they feel too sick to eat. Teach the reverse: sick days are when the dose matters most, and if they cannot keep it down, they need to be seen.

"They feel better, so we stopped it." The most dangerous thing about a steroid taper is that it makes no sense to the patient — the symptoms are gone and the pills are still going. Say the mechanism out loud: this medicine put your own adrenal glands to sleep, and we have to wake them up slowly. A patient who understands that the taper is for the gland, not the disease, finishes it.
Interactive Tap an 11-site body map of long-term corticosteroid adverse effects, then drag a taper from abrupt stop to 12 weeks to watch total glucocorticoid fall into — or clear — the adrenal crisis zone. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Glucocorticoids (cortisol/prednisone) raise glucose and suppress inflammation; mineralocorticoids (aldosterone/fludrocortisone) retain sodium and water and waste potassium.
  • Potency ladder: hydrocortisone 20 mg = prednisone 5 mg = methylprednisolone 4 mg = dexamethasone 0.75 mg (most potent, least salt-retaining).
  • Adverse effects are cortisol's job overdone: hyperglycemia, infection risk with masked signs, osteoporosis, cushingoid changes, mood swings, GI ulcer, cataracts, fluid retention, hypokalemia.
  • Never stop abruptly after >2–3 weeks — taper, and increase the dose during illness, surgery, or trauma to prevent adrenal crisis.

Section 4Insulins

The highest of the high-alert drugs

Insulin is the hormone that lets glucose out of the blood and into cells, and it is the drug that appears on every "high-alert" list in every hospital in the country — not because it is unpredictable, but because the consequences of a wrong dose are immediate and can be fatal. The safety practices are non-negotiable and worth stating plainly. Use an insulin syringe (U-100) or an insulin pen — never a tuberculin or general-purpose syringe. Never write or accept the abbreviation "U" for units; a hand-written "10U" reads as "100" often enough that the abbreviation is banned outright. Most facilities require an independent double-check by a second nurse before administration. And because insulin names look and sound alike — glargine, glulisine, lispro, degludec, detemir — read the vial label, not the shelf position.

Insulin is given subcutaneously for routine use; the abdomen absorbs fastest and most predictably, then the arm, thigh, and buttock. Rotate within one anatomic area rather than hopping between areas, so absorption stays consistent, and space injections about an inch apart to prevent lipohypertrophy — the rubbery fatty lumps that form in an overused site and absorb insulin erratically. Do not massage the site, and do not exercise the injected limb right away; both speed absorption and can drop the glucose faster than expected. Unopened vials live in the refrigerator; the in-use vial or pen can stay at room temperature and is generally discarded after 28 days (check the specific product). Never give cold insulin from the fridge if you can help it — it stings.

Onset, peak, duration: the numbers that prevent hypoglycemia

Every insulin is the same molecule; what differs is how fast it is absorbed out of the subcutaneous tissue. The peak is the number that matters clinically, because the peak is when the patient will go low. Learn the peaks and you can predict hypoglycemia instead of discovering it.

ClassPrototypeOnset / peak / durationNursing alert
Rapid-actinglispro, aspart, glulisine~15 min / 1–2 h / 3–5 hGive with the meal in front of the patient (within 15 min). If the tray does not come, the patient goes low.
Short-actingregular insulin~30 min / 2–3 h / 5–8 hGive 30 min before the meal. The only insulin given IV — DKA drips, hyperkalemia. Clear.
IntermediateNPH (isophane)1–2 h / 4–12 h / 14–24 hThe only cloudy insulin — roll gently, never shake. Broad peak means a real risk of nocturnal hypoglycemia. Can be mixed with regular.
Long-actingglargine, detemir1–2 h / no true peak / ~24 hBasal coverage, usually once daily — detemir's duration is dose-dependent and is often given twice daily. Never mix with any other insulin and never give IV. Clear — do not mistake for rapid-acting.
Ultra-longdegludec1 h / no peak / >42 hVery flat and forgiving; do not mix. Steady state takes ~3 days.
Premixed70/30 (NPH/regular), 75/25Combines both profilesCloudy — roll to resuspend. Convenience at the cost of flexibility; meals must be on time.

When two insulins are drawn into one syringe — classically NPH plus regular — the sequence is fixed: inject air into the cloudy (NPH) vial, inject air into the clear (regular) vial, withdraw the clear first, then the cloudy. Clear before cloudy — or, as generations of students have said it, regular before NPH. The reason is contamination: a trace of clear regular insulin carried into the NPH vial is harmless, but a trace of NPH's protamine carried into the regular vial would slow the action of your fast insulin, and you would never know it happened.

Hypoglycemia

A blood glucose below 70 mg/dL is hypoglycemia and it is the acute danger of every insulin. The early signs are adrenergic — the body's alarm system firing: shaky, sweaty, tachycardic, anxious, hungry, pale. If it drops further, the brain runs out of fuel and the signs turn neuroglycopenic: confusion, slurred speech, blurred vision, odd behavior, seizure, coma. That second set is often mistaken for intoxication or a stroke, and an unresponsive diabetic patient gets a fingerstick before anything else. Note that a patient on a beta blocker may lose the adrenergic warning entirely — the tremor and tachycardia are blocked — and will present with confusion as the first sign. Diaphoresis usually still comes through.

Treatment is the rule of 15: if the patient is awake and can swallow, give 15 grams of fast-acting carbohydrate — 4 ounces of juice or regular soda, 3–4 glucose tablets, a tablespoon of honey — wait 15 minutes, and recheck. Repeat until above 70, then give a snack with protein and a complex carbohydrate if the next meal is more than an hour away, so the glucose does not simply fall again. If the patient cannot safely swallow, nothing goes in the mouth: give IV dextrose 50% if there is access, or glucagon (IM, SC, or intranasal) if there is not.

Match the insulin to the tray, not to the clock. The single most common cause of inpatient hypoglycemia is rapid-acting insulin given for a meal that never arrived — the patient went to imaging, the tray was held, the order was NPO. Look at the food before you draw up lispro. If the meal is uncertain, insulin waits; the glucose can be brought down later, but a peak with no carbohydrate underneath it cannot be taken back.
Interactive Plot any insulin's onset, peak, and duration across 24 hours — drag the dose time and watch the hypoglycemia risk window move against the meal tray. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Insulin is a high-alert drug: insulin syringe or pen only, never abbreviate "units," independent double-check, read the label — the names look alike.
  • The peak predicts the hypoglycemia. Rapid ~1–2 h, regular 2–3 h, NPH 4–12 h (nocturnal lows), glargine/detemir/degludec have no true peak.
  • Regular is the only IV insulin; NPH is the only cloudy one; long-acting insulins are never mixed. Draw clear before cloudy.
  • Hypoglycemia <70 mg/dL → rule of 15 if awake; D50 IV or glucagon if not. Beta blockers hide the early adrenergic warning signs.

Section 5Oral antidiabetics and glucagon

Metformin: the one to know cold

Type 2 diabetes is a problem of resistance — there is insulin, but the tissues have stopped listening, and the liver keeps pouring out glucose it does not need to. Metformin, a biguanide, is first-line therapy for almost every newly diagnosed adult, and it is worth knowing better than any other oral agent. It works in three ways: it decreases hepatic glucose production (its main effect), it decreases intestinal glucose absorption, and it increases the sensitivity of muscle and fat to the insulin already present.

Read that list again and notice what is absent: metformin does not squeeze more insulin out of the pancreas. That is why metformin alone does not cause hypoglycemia — a point tested relentlessly and, more importantly, a point that changes how you teach. It is also weight-neutral to modestly weight-reducing, which is a genuine advantage. Its everyday problem is the gut: nausea, diarrhea, cramping, metallic taste, largely solved by taking it with food, starting low, and using the extended-release form. Long-term use can cause vitamin B12 deficiency, so B12 is checked periodically — particularly if a patient develops anemia or neuropathy.

The rare but serious effect is lactic acidosis, which carries a boxed warning. It happens when metformin accumulates, so the risk sits with anything that impairs clearance or oxygenation: renal impairment (metformin is contraindicated at eGFR <30), acute heart failure, hypoxia, sepsis, dehydration, liver disease, and heavy alcohol use. Teach the signs, because they are vague and easy to dismiss: unusual muscle aches, deep or labored breathing, unusual sleepiness, abdominal pain, feeling generally unwell. This is also the reason metformin is held before and after iodinated contrast studies — contrast can injure the kidney, the kidney is the exit route, and the drug is typically stopped at the time of the study and resumed 48 hours later once renal function is confirmed acceptable.

The other classes, and the one alert that defines each

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Biguanidemetformin↓ hepatic glucose output; ↑ insulin sensitivityNo hypoglycemia alone. Take with food. Hold for contrast; avoid eGFR <30. Watch B12; teach lactic acidosis signs.
Sulfonylureaglipizide, glimepiride, glyburideStimulate beta cells to release insulinHypoglycemia — the big one, especially glyburide in older adults. Take with breakfast. Weight gain; sulfa allergy caution; alcohol may cause flushing.
Meglitiniderepaglinide, nateglinideShort, fast insulin release for the mealTake with each meal — skip the meal, skip the dose. Hypoglycemia risk.
Thiazolidinedionepioglitazone↑ insulin sensitivity at muscle/fatBoxed warning: heart failure. Fluid retention — report weight gain, edema, dyspnea. Monitor LFTs. Slow onset (weeks).
DPP-4 inhibitorsitagliptinProlongs the body's own incretins → glucose-dependent insulin releaseWell tolerated, weight-neutral. Report severe persistent abdominal pain (pancreatitis); joint pain.
GLP-1 agonistsemaglutide, liraglutide, dulaglutideIncretin mimic: ↑ insulin, ↓ glucagon, slows gastric emptying, ↓ appetiteInjectable (some oral). Nausea is dose-limiting; significant weight loss. Boxed warning: thyroid C-cell tumors — avoid with personal/family history of medullary thyroid cancer or MEN 2. Pancreatitis; delayed gastric emptying.
SGLT2 inhibitorempagliflozin, dapagliflozinBlocks renal glucose reabsorption → glucose out in the urineProven heart-failure and kidney benefit. Genital mycotic infections and UTIs — teach hygiene, encourage fluids. Volume depletion/orthostasis. Euglycemic DKA — ketoacidosis with a near-normal glucose.
Alpha-glucosidase inhibitoracarbose, miglitolSlows carbohydrate digestion → blunts the post-meal spikeTake with the first bite. Flatulence, diarrhea. If hypoglycemic on a combination, treat with glucose tablets, not table sugar or juice — sucrose cannot be broken down.

A few threads run through that table. The agents that push the pancreas — sulfonylureas and meglitinides — are the oral drugs that cause hypoglycemia; the rest are much safer alone, though any of them can contribute when combined with insulin. Two classes now get prescribed as much for the heart and kidney as for the sugar: SGLT2 inhibitors and GLP-1 agonists both showed cardiovascular and renal benefit in outcome trials, which is why you will find them on the medication list of patients whose A1C is nearly at goal. And the euglycemic DKA of the SGLT2 drugs deserves a moment: because the drug dumps glucose into the urine, a patient can be in genuine ketoacidosis — nauseated, breathing deeply, acidotic — with a glucose of 180. A normal fingerstick does not rule it out.

Glucagon: the rescue

Glucagon is insulin's opposite number, the other hormone of the islets. When glucose falls, alpha cells release it, and it tells the liver to break down stored glycogen into glucose and release it into the blood. As a drug it is the rescue for severe hypoglycemia in a patient who cannot safely swallow and has no IV access — a seizing patient, an unresponsive patient, a child at home. It is given IM, subcutaneously, or intranasally, and it works in roughly 10–15 minutes.

Three things about glucagon must be taught to the family, not just charted. First, it works by emptying the liver's glycogen stores — so it fails or works poorly in a patient with no stores: chronic alcohol use, starvation, prolonged fasting, advanced liver disease. Those patients need IV dextrose. Second, vomiting is common after glucagon, so turn the patient onto their side before it takes effect to protect the airway. Third, the effect is temporary — the liver's stores are finite and the insulin that caused the low is often still working. As soon as the patient is awake and can swallow, they need oral carbohydrate, and they still need to be evaluated. Rescuing someone with glucagon and leaving is how a patient goes low a second time an hour later.

Ask who else lives there. A glucagon kit in a drawer that nobody can assemble is decoration. The person who will actually use it is a spouse, a parent, a roommate — and they will be doing it while frightened, in the dark, with someone seizing. Teach them, have them demonstrate, and point out the intranasal form, which requires nothing but a puff into a nostril and works even if the patient is not breathing through their nose.
Interactive Tap each oral antidiabetic class to light up the organ it works on — liver, pancreas, gut, kidney, or muscle — then step through the glucagon rescue for severe hypoglycemia. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Metformin is first-line, lowers hepatic glucose output, does not cause hypoglycemia alone, is taken with food, held for iodinated contrast, avoided at eGFR <30, and carries a lactic acidosis boxed warning; monitor B12.
  • Sulfonylureas and meglitinides squeeze the pancreas — they are the oral agents that cause hypoglycemia; skip the meal, skip the meglitinide.
  • Class-defining alerts: pioglitazone → heart failure; GLP-1 agonists → nausea, weight loss, thyroid C-cell boxed warning; SGLT2 inhibitors → genital infections and euglycemic DKA; acarbose → treat lows with glucose only.
  • Glucagon mobilizes hepatic glycogen for severe hypoglycemia without IV access; it causes vomiting (turn the patient), is temporary (follow with oral carbs), and fails without glycogen stores.

The chapter in one breath

  • Negative feedback is the master idea: hormone given from outside suppresses and atrophies the patient's own gland, which is why steroids are tapered and why a TSH tells you if a levothyroxine dose is right.
  • Peptide hormones are digested — insulin can never be a pill; lipid-soluble hormones (thyroid, steroids) act slowly on genes and can be swallowed.
  • Pituitary agents replace (somatropin, desmopressin) or suppress (octreotide, cabergoline); desmopressin's hazard is water intoxication and hyponatremia.
  • Levothyroxine: empty stomach 30–60 min before breakfast, binders separated by 4 hours, TSH every 6–8 weeks, lifelong, start low in older and cardiac patients.
  • Methimazole (first-line) and PTU (first trimester, thyroid storm, hepatotoxicity warning) both risk agranulocytosis — sore throat and fever means hold and draw a CBC.
  • Corticosteroid adverse effects are cortisol overdone: hyperglycemia, infection with masked signs, osteoporosis, cushingoid changes, mood, ulcers, fluid retention — and abrupt withdrawal after >2–3 weeks causes adrenal crisis.
  • Insulin is high-alert: know the peaks, because the peak is when the patient goes low; regular is the only IV insulin, NPH the only cloudy one, long-acting are never mixed, and hypoglycemia <70 gets the rule of 15 or, if unable to swallow, D50 or glucagon.
  • Metformin leads oral therapy and causes no hypoglycemia alone; each other class is defined by one alert — sulfonylurea lows, pioglitazone heart failure, GLP-1 nausea and C-cell warning, SGLT2 euglycemic DKA, acarbose needing pure glucose to treat a low.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adrenal crisis
Acute, life-threatening cortisol deficiency — hypotension, shock, weakness, vomiting, hypoglycemia — classically triggered by abruptly stopping a chronic steroid or by physiologic stress in a patient with a suppressed axis. Treated with IV hydrocortisone and fluids.
Agranulocytosis
A dangerous fall in neutrophils, leaving the patient defenseless against infection; the reason a sore throat, fever, or mouth ulcer on methimazole or PTU is an emergency, not a cold.
Antidiuretic hormone (ADH, vasopressin)
The posterior pituitary hormone that makes the kidney conserve water. Too little causes diabetes insipidus; too much causes SIADH.
Basal insulin
Long-acting, peakless insulin (glargine, detemir, degludec) that covers the body's constant background need between meals and overnight.
Biguanide
The class containing metformin; lowers hepatic glucose production and improves insulin sensitivity without stimulating the pancreas.
Duration
How long an insulin continues to lower glucose after it is given.
Euglycemic DKA
Diabetic ketoacidosis occurring with a near-normal blood glucose, seen with SGLT2 inhibitors because the drug excretes glucose in the urine; a normal fingerstick does not rule it out.
Glucagon
The pancreatic alpha-cell hormone that mobilizes hepatic glycogen to raise blood glucose; used as rescue for severe hypoglycemia when the patient cannot swallow.
Glucocorticoid
A steroid whose main actions are raising blood glucose and suppressing inflammation and immunity; cortisol is the natural one, prednisone and dexamethasone the common drugs.
High-alert medication
A drug that carries a heightened risk of significant patient harm when used in error — insulin is the archetype — and therefore requires extra safeguards such as an independent double-check.
Hypoglycemia
Blood glucose below 70 mg/dL; early adrenergic signs (shaky, sweaty, tachycardic, hungry) give way to neuroglycopenic ones (confusion, slurred speech, seizure, coma).
Incretin
A gut hormone (chiefly GLP-1) released with a meal that stimulates insulin, suppresses glucagon, and slows gastric emptying; mimicked by GLP-1 agonists and prolonged by DPP-4 inhibitors.
Lactic acidosis
The rare, serious metformin toxicity that occurs when the drug accumulates; suspect it with muscle aches, deep or labored breathing, unusual sleepiness, and abdominal pain.
Lipohypertrophy
Rubbery fatty thickening at an overused injection site that absorbs insulin unpredictably; prevented by rotating within an anatomic area.
Mineralocorticoid
A steroid whose main action is sodium and water retention with potassium loss; aldosterone is the natural one, fludrocortisone the drug.
Narrow therapeutic index
A drug whose effective dose and toxic dose sit uncomfortably close together, demanding precise dosing and monitoring — levothyroxine and insulin both qualify.
Negative feedback
The thermostat arrangement in which a hormone shuts off the signals that ordered it; the loop cannot tell exogenous hormone from the patient's own.
Onset
How long after administration an insulin begins to lower blood glucose.
Peak
When an insulin exerts its maximum effect — and therefore when hypoglycemia is most likely.
Rule of 15
Treatment of hypoglycemia in a patient who can swallow: 15 grams of fast-acting carbohydrate, wait 15 minutes, recheck, repeat as needed.
Taper
The gradual stepwise dose reduction used to end corticosteroid therapy, allowing the suppressed HPA axis and atrophied adrenal cortex to resume production.
Thionamide
The antithyroid class — methimazole and propylthiouracil — that blocks thyroid hormone synthesis; effects take weeks because stored hormone is unaffected.

Check yourself

1. A patient asks why insulin can't be made into a pill "like all my other medicines." Answer them.
Insulin is a protein (a peptide hormone), and digestive enzymes in the stomach and small intestine would break it apart before it ever reached the blood. Lipid-soluble hormones like thyroid hormone and steroids survive the gut and can be swallowed; water-soluble peptides such as insulin, growth hormone, and glucagon must be injected or given by another route that bypasses digestion.
2. Explain, using negative feedback, why a patient who has taken prednisone daily for two months cannot simply stop it.
The circulating prednisone suppressed CRH and ACTH, so the adrenal cortex went unstimulated and atrophied — stopping abruptly leaves the patient with no cortisol from the drug and none from the gland, causing adrenal crisis. The dose is tapered so the hypothalamus, pituitary, and adrenal cortex can wake back up in sequence. The same reasoning explains stress dosing: a suppressed gland cannot surge cortisol during surgery or serious illness, so the dose is increased then.
3. A patient on desmopressin for diabetes insipidus gains 2 kg overnight and reports a headache and nausea. What is happening, and what do you check?
Water intoxication from too much ADH effect — retained water is diluting the serum sodium (hyponatremia). Check the serum sodium, neuro status, daily weight, I&O, and urine specific gravity, and notify the provider. Headache, confusion, and seizure are the danger signs. Essentially the patient has been converted from diabetes insipidus into SIADH.
4. Write out the levothyroxine teaching you would give at discharge — timing, interactions, monitoring, and duration.
Take it once daily on an empty stomach with water, 30–60 minutes before breakfast, at the same time each day; separate calcium, iron, multivitamins, and antacids by at least 4 hours; expect TSH to be rechecked in 6–8 weeks; and understand it is lifelong. Report chest pain, palpitations, tremor, insomnia, or unintended weight loss — those suggest the dose is too high. Feeling better means the drug is working, not that it can be stopped. Requirements rise in pregnancy.
5. An older adult with coronary artery disease is starting levothyroxine. Why is the initial dose deliberately low?
Thyroid hormone increases heart rate and myocardial oxygen demand; raising it quickly in a narrowed coronary circulation can precipitate angina, arrhythmia, or myocardial infarction. Start low, increase slowly, and monitor heart rate, rhythm, and any chest pain. Long-term over-replacement also risks atrial fibrillation and osteoporosis.
6. A patient started on methimazole three days ago calls with a sore throat and a fever of 101°F. What is your response and why?
Assume agranulocytosis: hold the dose, notify the provider immediately, and arrange a CBC with differential. Thionamides can drop the neutrophil count precipitously, and a sore throat with fever is the classic presentation of an infection the patient can no longer fight. This is taught at the very first dose, because otherwise a patient would never think a sore throat is worth a call.
7. Why is PTU chosen over methimazole in the first trimester of pregnancy and in thyroid storm, despite its boxed warning?
Methimazole carries a teratogenic risk in early pregnancy, and PTU has the added benefit of blocking peripheral conversion of T4 to T3 — useful when hormone levels must drop fast, as in thyroid storm. Its boxed warning is severe hepatotoxicity, so teach the patient to report jaundice, dark urine, right upper quadrant pain, or anorexia, and reserve the drug for those specific situations.
8. Rank hydrocortisone, prednisone, methylprednisolone, and dexamethasone by potency, and say which you would choose for cerebral edema and why.
Roughly equivalent doses: hydrocortisone 20 mg = prednisone 5 mg = methylprednisolone 4 mg = dexamethasone 0.75 mg, so dexamethasone is the most potent. Choose dexamethasone for cerebral edema. It is nearly pure glucocorticoid with almost no mineralocorticoid activity, so it delivers a powerful anti-inflammatory effect without causing the sodium and water retention that would worsen swelling.
9. A patient on chronic prednisone has a normal temperature but reports feeling "off" and has a small red area on the leg. Why is this concerning?
Glucocorticoids both increase infection risk and mask its signs — they blunt fever and the inflammatory response, so a serious infection can be well established before it looks impressive. Assess with a low index of suspicion rather than waiting for classic signs. Teach the patient to avoid sick contacts, report any fever, carry medical identification, and expect that live vaccines will generally be avoided.
10. It is 0730. You have an order for insulin lispro before breakfast, but the patient has just been made NPO for a procedure. What do you do, and why does the class matter?
Hold the lispro and notify the provider. Lispro is rapid-acting — onset ~15 minutes, peak in 1–2 hours — and giving it with no meal underneath it guarantees hypoglycemia at the peak. Mealtime insulin is matched to the food actually in front of the patient, not to the clock. Basal insulin (glargine, detemir, degludec) is a different conversation, since it covers background need rather than a meal.
11. You are drawing up NPH and regular insulin in one syringe. State the order and explain the reason. Which insulins may never be mixed, and which may be given IV?
Air into the cloudy NPH vial, air into the clear regular vial, then withdraw the clear regular first and the cloudy NPH second — "clear before cloudy," or "regular before NPH." Long-acting insulins (glargine, detemir, degludec) are never mixed with anything, and regular insulin is the only insulin given IV. The order prevents protamine from the NPH vial contaminating the regular vial, which would slow your fast insulin's action invisibly. NPH is the only cloudy insulin and must be gently rolled, never shaken.
12. A patient on metformin is scheduled for a CT with iodinated contrast. What happens to the metformin, and what is the underlying reason? Would you expect metformin alone to cause hypoglycemia?
Metformin is held at the time of the study and typically resumed about 48 hours later once renal function is confirmed acceptable, because contrast can impair the kidney — metformin's only exit route — and an accumulating drug can cause lactic acidosis. No: metformin alone does not cause hypoglycemia. It lowers hepatic glucose output and improves insulin sensitivity but does not stimulate the beta cells to release insulin. Teach the lactic acidosis signs — muscle aches, deep or labored breathing, unusual sleepiness, abdominal pain — and avoid the drug at eGFR <30 or with heavy alcohol use.
Pharmacology · Chapter 9

Cardiovascular Drugs I

Blood has one job that is nearly impossible: stay liquid everywhere, and turn solid in exactly one place, at exactly the right moment. The drugs in this chapter live on both sides of that line — some thin the blood, some help it clot, some rebuild it, and some quietly lower the pressure that is grinding down a patient's arteries year after year. Almost every one of them is a high-alert medication, meaning an error does not produce a rash, it produces a hemorrhage or a stroke. This chapter walks the clotting cascade, the anticoagulants and antiplatelets that interrupt it, the antidotes that reverse them, the agents that rebuild blood, and the antihypertensives you will hang, titrate, hold, and teach more than any other class in your career.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe hemostasis — vasoconstriction, the platelet plug, and the clotting cascade — and locate where each anticoagulant acts.
  • Compare heparin, low–molecular-weight heparin, warfarin, and the DOACs by mechanism, monitoring test, and antidote.
  • Explain how aspirin and clopidogrel differ from anticoagulants, and why dual antiplatelet therapy must not be stopped casually.
  • State the nursing priorities for alteplase, including time windows, contraindications, and bleeding surveillance.
  • Describe erythropoiesis-stimulating agents, filgrastim, and the major blood products, with their key warnings and transfusion safety steps.
  • Explain how cardiac output, systemic vascular resistance, baroreceptors, and the RAAS set arterial blood pressure.
  • Identify the prototype, key effect, and signature adverse effect of each major antihypertensive class.
  • Assess for orthostatic hypotension and build a realistic adherence plan with a patient who feels perfectly fine.

Section 1Hemostasis and anticoagulants

How the body builds a clot

Cut a vessel and three things happen in sequence. First, the vessel wall clamps down — vasoconstriction — narrowing the hole. Second, platelets stick to the exposed collagen, activate, change shape, and glue themselves to one another into a soft, temporary platelet plug. That plug will stop a paper cut, but it is fragile; a cough or a blood pressure spike blows it off. So third, the body reinforces it with the coagulation cascade, a chain of plasma proteins that ends by weaving a mesh of fibrin through the platelet plug and locking it in place.

The cascade has two on-ramps and one shared exit. The extrinsic pathway is the fast one: damaged tissue releases tissue factor, which activates factor VII. The intrinsic pathway is the slower one, triggered by contact with a damaged surface inside the vessel. Both funnel into the common pathway, where factor X becomes factor Xa, Xa converts prothrombin (factor II) into thrombin, and thrombin converts fibrinogen into fibrin. Thrombin is the engine of the whole system, and factor Xa is the gate just upstream of it — which is exactly why so many drugs aim at those two proteins.

Two lab tests map onto those two on-ramps, and you will read them for the rest of your career. PT/INR follows the extrinsic and common pathways — it is the warfarin test. aPTT follows the intrinsic and common pathways — it is the heparin test. A memory hook that works: PeT the extrinsic dog (PT → extrinsic → warfarin); the PTT is for the intrinsic tubing (aPTT → intrinsic → heparin).

One more idea before the drugs: nothing here dissolves a clot that already exists. Anticoagulants prevent clots from forming and from growing. They do not melt the clot in the patient's leg — the body's own fibrinolytic system does that slowly over weeks. When a patient asks, "Is this shot breaking up my clot?", the honest answer is that it is holding the line while the body cleans up.

Heparins — fast, injectable, reversible

Unfractionated heparin (UFH) works indirectly: it binds antithrombin and makes it roughly a thousand times better at inactivating thrombin and factor Xa. Because it does not touch the liver's factor production, it works within minutes — IV heparin is essentially instant, which is why it is the drug of choice when you need anticoagulation now and might need to shut it off just as fast. Its half-life is short (about 60–90 minutes), so stopping the drip is itself a partial reversal. Monitor with aPTT (goal typically 1.5–2.5 times control) or an anti-Xa level, per your facility's protocol and nomogram. The antidote is protamine sulfate.

The complication that defines heparin is heparin-induced thrombocytopenia (HIT). It is counterintuitive and it kills people: an antibody response, typically 5–10 days in, drops the platelet count by 50% or more and paradoxically causes clotting, not bleeding. A falling platelet count on heparin is never something to watch overnight. Heparin is stopped — all of it, including flushes and coated catheters — and a non-heparin anticoagulant such as argatroban is started. Baseline and serial platelet counts are a nursing responsibility, not a lab formality.

Low–molecular-weight heparin (LMWH) — prototype enoxaparin (Lovenox) — is heparin chopped into shorter chains that inhibit factor Xa much more than thrombin. That gives a predictable, weight-based subcutaneous dose, no routine aPTT, a longer half-life, and a lower HIT risk, which is what let anticoagulation move out of the hospital. Give it deep subcutaneously in the abdominal "love handle," at least 2 inches from the umbilicus, and do not expel the air bubble in the prefilled syringe — that bubble clears the drug from the needle track and reduces bruising. Do not aspirate and do not massage the site. LMWH is cleared renally, so it accumulates dangerously in kidney impairment; it also carries a boxed warning about spinal/epidural hematoma with neuraxial anesthesia or spinal puncture — a warning shared by fondaparinux and the DOACs, so timing around an epidural is always a team decision. Fondaparinux is a synthetic indirect factor Xa inhibitor given subcutaneously — like heparin it works through antithrombin, which is why it is not a DOAC and why protamine does not reverse it. It has essentially no reliable reversal agent, which is a real consideration when choosing it.

Warfarin — slow, oral, and endlessly interactive

Warfarin (Coumadin) blocks vitamin K epoxide reductase, the enzyme that recycles vitamin K in the liver. Without recycled vitamin K, the liver cannot finish building the vitamin K–dependent factors — II, VII, IX, and X (the "1972" factors), plus the natural anticoagulants protein C and protein S. Notice what that means: warfarin does nothing to the factors already circulating. It takes 3–5 days for the existing factors to decay before the patient is truly anticoagulated, which is why a patient with an acute clot is bridged with heparin or LMWH until the INR is therapeutic on two consecutive days.

Monitor with PT/INR. The usual goal is an INR of 2.0–3.0; a mechanical mitral valve or certain higher-risk indications may target 2.5–3.5. The antidote is vitamin K (phytonadione), and for serious or life-threatening bleeding, 4-factor prothrombin complex concentrate (PCC), which replaces the missing factors in minutes rather than hours. Fresh frozen plasma is an older alternative when PCC is unavailable.

Warfarin is the great interaction magnet — it is highly protein-bound and metabolized by CYP450, so antibiotics, amiodarone, azole antifungals, NSAIDs, herbals, alcohol, and even a change in appetite can move the INR. The teaching point students most often get backwards: patients are not told to avoid vitamin K foods. They are told to be consistent. A patient who eats a salad every day is easy to dose; a patient who eats no greens for a month and then goes on a kale kick will swing from bleeding to clotting. Warfarin is also a teratogen — contraindicated in pregnancy (heparin/LMWH do not cross the placenta and are used instead).

DOACs — the modern default

Direct oral anticoagulants skip the liver-synthesis detour and block a clotting protein directly. Dabigatran (Pradaxa) is a direct thrombin inhibitor; apixaban (Eliquis), rivaroxaban (Xarelto), and edoxaban are direct factor Xa inhibitors. The "-xaban" suffix literally names the target: Xa ban. They have a fast onset, few food interactions, fixed dosing, and no routine INR monitoring — which patients love and which quietly removes the safety net that regular lab visits used to provide.

Reversal exists now: idarucizumab (Praxbind) for dabigatran, and andexanet alfa (Andexxa) for apixaban and rivaroxaban. Nursing specifics matter here. Dabigatran must stay in its original bottle or blister — it absorbs moisture and degrades, and the opened bottle is discarded after 4 months; capsules are never opened or crushed (doing so hugely increases absorption). Rivaroxaban's treatment doses are taken with food for absorption. DOACs are renally cleared to varying degrees and need dose adjustment or avoidance in kidney disease, and they are not used for mechanical heart valves or moderate-to-severe mitral stenosis — those patients still get warfarin. Missing doses matters more than it does with warfarin, because the short half-life means an unanticoagulated patient within a day — which is exactly why every DOAC carries a boxed warning that premature discontinuation raises the risk of thrombotic events.

ClassPrototypeKey effectMonitorAntidoteNursing alert
Unfractionated heparinheparin (IV/SC)Potentiates antithrombin → blocks thrombin and XaaPTT or anti-Xa; plateletsProtamine sulfateWatch for HIT — falling platelets means stop, don't wait. High-alert; independent double check.
LMWHenoxaparinMostly anti-Xa; predictable SC dosingNone routine; anti-Xa if obese/renal/pregnantProtamine (partial only)Deep SC in abdomen, don't expel the bubble, don't massage. Renal dosing. Spinal hematoma warning.
Indirect Xa inhibitorfondaparinuxSelective Xa inhibition via antithrombin (not a DOAC)None routine; anti-Xa if neededNone reliable — protamine does not workAvoid in significant renal impairment; no reversal is a real risk. Spinal hematoma warning.

"Protamine for heparin, vitamin K for warfarin" is a two-second recall that saves lives. Chain it: heparin is fast and IV, so its antidote is fast and IV (protamine, minutes). Warfarin is slow and oral and works through vitamin K, so its antidote is vitamin K — and because you're waiting on the liver to rebuild factors, vitamin K takes hours, which is why an actively bleeding patient also gets PCC to replace factors right now.
Interactive Tap heparin, warfarin, or a DOAC to see exactly which clotting factors it blocks — then tap any factor to learn its job, its lab test, and its antidote. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Hemostasis = vasoconstriction → platelet plug → fibrin mesh; thrombin and factor Xa are the drug targets.
  • aPTT → heparin (intrinsic); PT/INR → warfarin (extrinsic). Antidotes: protamine and vitamin K.
  • Anticoagulants prevent clot formation and extension — they do not dissolve an existing clot.
  • HIT causes thrombosis, not bleeding — a 50% platelet drop on heparin means stop heparin entirely.
  • Warfarin needs 3–5 days and bridging; DOACs need no routine monitoring but need renal dosing and adherence.

Section 2Antiplatelets and thrombolytics

Two kinds of clot, two kinds of drug

Here is the distinction that makes this section click. Clots in fast-moving arterial blood are built mostly of platelets — they form on a ruptured atherosclerotic plaque and cause heart attacks and most strokes. Clots in slow-moving venous blood are built mostly of fibrin — they form in a leg vein or a fibrillating atrium and cause DVT, PE, and cardioembolic stroke. That is why a patient with a coronary stent goes home on aspirin and clopidogrel while a patient with atrial fibrillation goes home on apixaban. Different clot, different drug. Giving the wrong one is not a small miss.

Aspirin and the P2Y12 inhibitors

Aspirin irreversibly acetylates cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) inside the platelet, shutting off thromboxane A2, the signal platelets use to recruit and aggregate one another. "Irreversibly" is the whole story: a platelet has no nucleus, so it cannot make new COX. That single dose disables that platelet for its entire 7–10 day lifespan, and the effect only wears off as the marrow releases fresh platelets. Practical consequences: a low dose (81 mg) is enough for prevention, aspirin is typically held about 7 days before elective surgery, and a patient who took aspirin this morning is still "aspirinized" next week. In suspected MI, the patient chews a non-enteric-coated tablet — 162–325 mg, commonly four 81 mg tablets or one 325 mg tablet — because chewing gets it absorbed in minutes when minutes matter, and an enteric coating defeats the whole point.

Aspirin's costs are GI: dyspepsia, ulcers, and GI bleeding, plus tinnitus as an early sign of salicylate toxicity. It is contraindicated in children and teens with viral illness because of Reye syndrome, and it is avoided in aspirin-exacerbated respiratory disease (the asthma/nasal polyp/aspirin triad).

Clopidogrel (Plavix) blocks the platelet's P2Y12 ADP receptor — a different aggregation signal, which is why aspirin plus clopidogrel is genuinely additive and is called dual antiplatelet therapy (DAPT). Clopidogrel is a prodrug that must be activated by CYP2C19; patients who are poor metabolizers, or who take a strong CYP2C19 inhibitor like omeprazole, get less protection (pantoprazole is the safer PPI pairing). Prasugrel is more potent but is contraindicated after prior stroke/TIA because of intracranial bleeding; ticagrelor is reversible, dosed twice daily, and famously causes dyspnea that is benign but alarming to patients — and its aspirin partner must stay at a low dose. The glycoprotein IIb/IIIa inhibitors (eptifibatide, tirofiban) block the final common step of aggregation and are IV, cath-lab drugs.

The most important DAPT teaching in nursing: a patient with a recent coronary stent must not stop DAPT on their own. Stopping early can thrombose the stent and cause a massive MI. If a dentist or surgeon wants it held, that decision goes back to cardiology — it is not a bedside call, and it is not the patient's call.

Thrombolytics — the clot-busters

Thrombolytics are the one group here that actually dissolves a clot. Alteplase (tPA) and tenecteplase (TNK) are tissue plasminogen activators: they convert plasminogen into plasmin, the enzyme that digests fibrin. That is fantastic when the fibrin is blocking a cerebral artery and catastrophic when the fibrin is the scab holding a recent surgical site together — and the drug cannot tell the difference. Every contraindication follows from that one fact.

Time is the drug. For acute ischemic stroke, IV alteplase is given within 3 hours of last known well, extended to 4.5 hours in selected patients; a noncontrast head CT must first rule out hemorrhage, because giving a thrombolytic into a bleeding brain is fatal. For STEMI, thrombolysis is the fallback when primary PCI cannot be delivered in time, ideally within 30 minutes of arrival. Absolute contraindications cluster around bleeding you cannot compress: any prior intracranial hemorrhage, known cerebral vascular malformation or malignancy, ischemic stroke within 3 months, active internal bleeding, suspected aortic dissection, significant closed head trauma within 3 months.

Nursing during and after a thrombolytic is essentially a bleeding watch with a neuro exam stapled to it. Establish IV access, draw labs, and place any lines before the infusion. Then leave the patient alone: no arterial punctures, no IM injections, no indwelling catheter or NG placement unless truly necessary, hold pressure far longer than usual. Frequent neuro checks and blood pressure — BP is kept below the ordered threshold because hypertension on tPA drives hemorrhagic conversion. A sudden headache, vomiting, altered mental status, or a new neuro deficit means stop the infusion and call. Also keep bleeding precautions in mind: soft toothbrush, electric razor, no rectal temperatures or suppositories, fall precautions.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
COX-1 inhibitoraspirinIrreversibly blocks thromboxane A2 for the platelet's 7–10 day lifeChew 162–325 mg non-enteric for suspected MI. GI bleed, tinnitus. No aspirin in children with viral illness (Reye). Hold ~7 days pre-op.
P2Y12 inhibitorclopidogrelBlocks ADP-mediated aggregation; prodrug via CYP2C19Pair with pantoprazole, not omeprazole. Never stop DAPT after a stent without cardiology.
P2Y12 (others)prasugrel, ticagrelorMore potent/reversible ADP blockadePrasugrel contraindicated with prior stroke/TIA. Ticagrelor → dyspnea, BID dosing, low-dose ASA only.

Other antiplateletcilostazol, dipyridamolePDE inhibition → antiplatelet + vasodilationCilostazol for claudication; contraindicated in heart failure. Headache is common.
Thrombolyticalteplase, tenecteplasePlasminogen → plasmin → digests existing fibrinHead CT first. Stroke window 3–4.5 h. Lines/labs before infusing. No punctures. Neuro + BP checks. Sudden headache = stop.
Antiplatelet, anticoagulant, thrombolytic — three verbs, not synonyms. Antiplatelets keep platelets from sticking. Anticoagulants keep fibrin from forming. Thrombolytics dissolve fibrin that is already there. If a patient or a family member says "blood thinner," find out which one you're actually talking about before you answer — the monitoring, the antidote, and the risk are completely different.
Interactive Step an artery through platelet adhesion, activation, aggregation, fibrin mesh, and occlusion — then tap aspirin, a P2Y12 inhibitor, a PDE inhibitor, a GP IIb/IIIa inhibitor, or a thrombolytic to watch exactly where each one cuts into the forming clot, and what that means at the bedside. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Arterial clots are platelet-rich (antiplatelets); venous clots are fibrin-rich (anticoagulants).
  • Aspirin's COX-1 block is irreversible — one dose lasts that platelet's 7–10 day lifespan.
  • Clopidogrel is a CYP2C19 prodrug; pair with pantoprazole, and never stop DAPT after a stent without cardiology.
  • Alteplase actually dissolves fibrin: CT first, tight time window, lines before infusion, obsessive neuro and bleeding checks.

Section 3Hematopoietic agents and blood products

Pushing the marrow

Every blood cell descends from one hematopoietic stem cell in the bone marrow, and each branch of that family tree has its own growth-factor signal. Drug companies made recombinant copies of those signals, so we can now push a specific line: red cells with erythropoietin, neutrophils with filgrastim, platelets with romiplostim. These are not transfusions — they are instructions. They take days to weeks to work, and they only work if the marrow is capable of responding and has the raw materials on hand.

Erythropoiesis-stimulating agents (ESAs) — epoetin alfa (Epogen, Procrit) and the longer-acting darbepoetin alfa (Aranesp) — replace the erythropoietin a failing kidney can no longer make. They are used mainly in anemia of chronic kidney disease and in chemotherapy-induced anemia. Their boxed warning is one you must understand rather than memorize: pushing hemoglobin too high or too fast increases death, MI, stroke, venous thromboembolism, and tumor progression. Thicker blood clots. So we do not normalize hemoglobin — we use the lowest dose needed to avoid transfusion, and we hold or reduce for a hemoglobin above roughly 11 g/dL or a rise of more than about 1 g/dL in 2 weeks. ESAs also raise blood pressure, so BP is checked before dosing. And ESAs fail without iron: you can shout at the marrow all day, but if there is no iron, ferritin, or transferrin saturation to work with, no hemoglobin gets built. Assess iron stores first. Do not shake the vial — it is a protein and shaking denatures it.

Filgrastim (Neupogen) is granulocyte colony-stimulating factor (G-CSF); it drives the marrow to pump out neutrophils, shortening the neutropenic window after chemotherapy and reducing febrile neutropenia. Pegfilgrastim (Neulasta) is the pegylated once-per-cycle version. Timing is the classic exam point: give it at least 24 hours after the chemotherapy dose, not with it — chemotherapy kills dividing cells, and you have just told the marrow to divide. The signature adverse effect is bone pain, often deep in the sternum and pelvis, and it is a sign the drug is working; it usually responds to acetaminophen or an NSAID (if not contraindicated). Watch for the rare but serious splenic rupture (left upper quadrant or shoulder-tip pain) and ARDS. The nursing outcome is measured with the absolute neutrophil count (ANC), not the white count. Sargramostim (GM-CSF) is broader; romiplostim and eltrombopag are thrombopoietin agonists for immune thrombocytopenia.

Blood products — when you cannot wait for the marrow

Growth factors take days. When a patient is bleeding now, you give them the cells. Each product answers a different question, and matching the product to the deficit is the point. Packed red blood cells carry oxygen. Platelets plug. Fresh frozen plasma supplies all the clotting factors. Cryoprecipitate is the concentrated fibrinogen fraction. Albumin holds volume in the vascular space and carries no clotting or oxygen benefit at all.

Transfusion safety is one of the most protocolized things a nurse does, and for good reason: the deaths come from human identification errors, not from the biology. The bones of it, always following your facility's policy: verify the order and consent; type and crossmatch; a large-bore IV (18–20 g for red cells); only 0.9% normal saline through the Y-tubing, because dextrose lyses cells and lactated Ringer's contains calcium that can clot the line; a two-person verification at the bedside comparing the patient's ID band, the unit, and the paperwork; baseline vital signs. Then start slowly and stay with the patient for the first 15 minutes, because that is when a fatal acute hemolytic reaction announces itself. Recheck vitals per policy, complete each unit within 4 hours, and never add medication to blood.

Learn the reactions by their fingerprints. Acute hemolytic (ABO mismatch): within minutes — fever, chills, flank or back pain, dark urine, hypotension, a sense of impending doom. Febrile nonhemolytic: the most common — a fever rise with chills, from donor leukocytes. Allergic: hives and itching (mild) up to anaphylaxis (more likely in IgA deficiency). TACO (circulatory overload): dyspnea, crackles, JVD, hypertension — too much volume too fast, classically in the small, elderly, or heart-failure patient. TRALI: acute lung injury within 6 hours with dyspnea, hypoxia, and hypotension — and it is a leading cause of transfusion death. TACO and TRALI both drown the patient; the blood pressure is what separates them at the bedside.

The first action for any suspected reaction never changes: stop the transfusion, keep the IV open with normal saline using new tubing, assess and take vitals, and notify the provider and the blood bank. Send the unit, tubing, and the required samples back. Stopping first is not optional — every additional milliliter of an incompatible unit is more hemolysis.

Product / agentPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
ESAepoetin alfa, darbepoetinStimulates red cell production (days–weeks)Boxed warning: death, MI, stroke, VTE, tumor progression if Hgb pushed too high. Hold if Hgb > ~11. Check iron and BP. Don't shake the vial.
G-CSFfilgrastim, pegfilgrastimRaises neutrophils → shortens neutropeniaGive ≥ 24 h after chemo. Bone pain = working. Follow the ANC. Splenic rupture is rare but real.

TACO is hypertensive; TRALI is hypotensive. Both patients are suddenly short of breath during or just after a transfusion, and the instinct is to reach for a diuretic. Check the blood pressure first: a high BP with crackles and JVD is overload (slow or stop, sit them up, diuretic per order); a low BP with hypoxia and clear volume status is TRALI (stop, oxygen, support — a diuretic here makes it worse). And with either one, the very first move is still the same: stop the transfusion.
Interactive Tap a growth factor to watch its marrow line switch on and fill the bloodstream, or tap a blood component to see its compatibility, tubing, rate, and what one unit actually gives. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Growth factors instruct the marrow and take days; blood products replace cells now.
  • ESAs carry a boxed warning — don't normalize hemoglobin, check iron and BP, hold if Hgb > ~11 g/dL.
  • Filgrastim goes at least 24 h after chemo; bone pain means it's working; follow the ANC.
  • Transfusion: NS only, two-person ID check, stay 15 minutes, 4-hour limit, and stop first for any reaction.
  • TACO = hypertensive overload; TRALI = hypotensive lung injury.

Section 4Blood pressure and antihypertensives

What actually sets the pressure

Every antihypertensive drug in existence works by moving one of two numbers in a single equation:

Blood pressure = cardiac output × systemic vascular resistance. And cardiac output is itself heart rate × stroke volume, where stroke volume depends on preload (the volume coming back), contractility (squeeze), and afterload (what the ventricle pushes against). So there are exactly four levers: drain the tank (diuretics reduce volume → preload), slow and soften the pump (beta-blockers reduce rate and contractility), widen the pipes (vasodilators, CCBs, ACE inhibitors, ARBs reduce resistance), or cut the hormonal signal telling the body to do all of the above (RAAS blockers). Once you see a drug's lever, you can predict both its benefit and its adverse effects without memorizing them separately.

The body defends its pressure with two systems you will fight constantly. The baroreceptor reflex is the fast one: stretch receptors in the carotid sinus and aortic arch sense a pressure drop within seconds and fire the sympathetic system — heart rate up, vessels constricted. This is the reflex that catches you when you stand up, and it is the reflex that produces reflex tachycardia when a vasodilator drops pressure abruptly. The RAAS is the slow one: the kidney senses low perfusion and releases renin, renin converts angiotensinogen to angiotensin I, ACE (largely in the lung) converts it to angiotensin II — a ferocious vasoconstrictor — and angiotensin II triggers aldosterone, which retains sodium and water, and ADH, which retains water. Pressure up. Blocking this loop at any point is the backbone of modern antihypertensive therapy.

Why bother? Because hypertension does its damage silently for decades and then presents as a stroke, a myocardial infarction, heart failure, kidney failure, or blindness. The patient feels nothing. That single fact — that the disease is invisible and the treatment has side effects the patient can feel — is the whole clinical problem, and it is the subject of the next section.

The classes, and what each one costs

Thiazide diuretics (hydrochlorothiazide, chlorthalidone) block the sodium-chloride symporter in the distal convoluted tubule; less sodium means less water, less volume, less preload, and over time some vasodilation. They are cheap, oral, first-line, and their price is metabolic: hypokalemia above all, plus hyponatremia, hypercalcemia, hyperuricemia (gout flares), hyperglycemia, and hyperlipidemia. Loop diuretics (furosemide) are stronger, work at the thick ascending limb of the loop of Henle, keep working in kidney impairment, and are the drug of volume overload and heart failure rather than routine hypertension; they waste potassium and calcium and can cause ototoxicity, especially when pushed IV too fast. Potassium-sparing agents (spironolactone, an aldosterone antagonist) hold potassium back — useful, and the reason spironolactone's main danger is hyperkalemia, along with gynecomastia. Give all diuretics in the morning so the patient is not up all night.

ACE inhibitors (lisinopril — the "-pril" family) block conversion of angiotensin I to II. Less angiotensin II means vasodilation and less aldosterone, and they are protective in heart failure, in diabetic kidney disease, and after MI. ACE also degrades bradykinin, so blocking it lets bradykinin build up — which is why 10–20% of patients get the maddening dry, hacking cough, and why a small number get angioedema, a swelling of the lips, tongue, and airway that is a true emergency. Because less aldosterone means less potassium excretion, expect hyperkalemia, and teach patients to avoid potassium-based salt substitutes. First-dose hypotension is real. ACE inhibitors are contraindicated in pregnancy — fetal renal injury and death.

ARBs (losartan — the "-sartan" family) block the angiotensin II receptor instead of the enzyme. Same downstream benefit, but bradykinin is untouched, so no cough — which is exactly why patients get switched from a "-pril" to a "-sartan." They still cause hyperkalemia and are still contraindicated in pregnancy. ACE inhibitors and ARBs are not combined.

Calcium channel blockers split into two personalities. The dihydropyridines (amlodipine — the "-dipine" family) act on vascular smooth muscle: pure vasodilation, so expect peripheral edema, flushing, headache, and reflex tachycardia. The non-dihydropyridines (diltiazem, verapamil) act on the heart too: they slow conduction and reduce contractility, so expect bradycardia, heart block, worsened heart failure, and verapamil's notorious constipation. Grapefruit juice raises levels of several of these.

Beta-blockers (metoprolol, atenolol — the "-olol" family) blunt sympathetic drive: heart rate down, contractility down, renin release down. Cardioselective agents (metoprolol, atenolol, bisoprolol) prefer beta-1 in the heart; nonselective agents (propranolol) also hit beta-2 and can bronchoconstrict in asthma. Watch for bradycardia, fatigue, and the sneaky one: beta-blockers mask the tachycardia and tremor of hypoglycemia, so a diabetic patient may lose their early warning (sweating still gets through). Never stop a beta-blocker abruptly — rebound tachycardia, hypertension, angina, even MI. Alpha-1 blockers (doxazosin — "-osin") relax vessels and the prostate, and cause a dramatic first-dose orthostatic syncope, so the first dose is given at bedtime. Central alpha-2 agonists (clonidine) reduce central sympathetic outflow, cause sedation and dry mouth, and cause severe rebound hypertension if stopped suddenly — patch and pill both. Direct vasodilators (hydralazine, minoxidil) relax arterioles directly and provoke reflex tachycardia and fluid retention, so they are usually paired with a beta-blocker and a diuretic. Sodium nitroprusside is the ICU emergency drip: near-instant, titrated to an arterial line, light-protected, and it can accumulate as cyanide/thiocyanate with prolonged high-dose use.
