Let the suffix do the work. −pril = ACE inhibitor (and a cough). −sartan = ARB (no cough). −dipine = dihydropyridine CCB (swollen ankles). −olol = beta-blocker (slow pulse, never stop abruptly). −osin = alpha-1 blocker (don't stand up fast). Learning the suffix means that the first time you meet olmesartan or nebivolol on a med list, you already know the class, the monitoring, and the teaching — before you've looked anything up.
Interactive Tap an ACE inhibitor, ARB, calcium channel blocker, beta blocker, or thiazide to watch it cut the blood-pressure circuit — or tap the heart, arterioles, kidney, or RAAS to see which drugs work there. Open full ↑
Key points
  • BP = CO × SVR; every antihypertensive drains volume, slows the pump, widens the pipes, or blocks the RAAS.
  • The baroreceptor reflex defends pressure in seconds (reflex tachycardia); the RAAS defends it over hours.
  • ACE inhibitors → cough, angioedema, hyperkalemia; ARBs → same benefit, no cough; both are contraindicated in pregnancy.
  • Dihydropyridine CCBs → edema; non-dihydropyridines → bradycardia and constipation.
  • Beta-blockers mask hypoglycemia and must never be stopped abruptly; clonidine causes rebound hypertension.

Section 5Antihypertensive nursing care

The number you write down is a nursing intervention

An antihypertensive is titrated against a number that you produce. If the number is wrong, the dose is wrong — and a bad technique can move a reading by 10 to 30 mmHg, which is more than most single drugs do. So the technique is not a formality:

  • Cuff size is the biggest error. A cuff that is too small reads falsely high; too large reads falsely low. The bladder should encircle about 80% of the arm.
  • Patient seated 5 minutes, back supported, feet flat on the floor (crossed legs raise the reading), arm bare and supported at heart level (a dangling arm reads high), and no talking during the reading.
  • No caffeine, smoking, or exercise for 30 minutes; bladder empty.
  • Take two readings a minute or more apart and average them. Check both arms at the initial visit and use the higher arm thereafter.
  • Know your hold parameters before you pour the pill — and if there is no parameter for a drug that can bottom out a pressure, that is a question worth asking, not assuming.

Around the drug itself, follow the class-specific labs and vitals: potassium and renal function for ACE inhibitors, ARBs, spironolactone, and diuretics; apical pulse for a full minute before a beta-blocker or a non-dihydropyridine CCB (commonly hold for a pulse under 60 or per order); daily weight at the same time on the same scale in the same clothing for anyone on a diuretic — because a 1 kg change is about a liter of fluid and weight beats a lung exam for catching it early. Assess ankles for the amlodipine patient, ask about cough for the lisinopril patient, and look at the tongue and lips of anyone new on an ACE inhibitor.

Orthostatic hypotension: the side effect that breaks hips

Nearly every drug in the last section can drop a patient's pressure when they stand up. Orthostatic hypotension is a fall of ≥ 20 mmHg systolic or ≥ 10 mmHg diastolic within 3 minutes of standing, and it is not an academic definition — it is the mechanism behind the 3 a.m. fall, the fractured hip, the head bleed on the patient who is also anticoagulated. The physiology is simple: standing dumps about half a liter of blood into the legs, and the baroreceptor reflex normally corrects it in seconds. Beta-blockers blunt the heart-rate half of that reflex, alpha-blockers blunt the vasoconstriction half, and diuretics remove the volume the reflex needs to work with. The patient's own compensation has been pharmacologically disabled.

Measure it properly: BP and pulse supine after a few minutes of rest, then sitting, then standing at 1 minute and again at 3 minutes — and stay with the patient with a gait belt or a chair behind them, because the point of the test is to reproduce a near-faint. Ask about the symptom, not just the number: lightheadedness, gray or tunnel vision, a "wave" on standing.

What you teach is what protects them. Change positions slowly and use the three-stage rise: lie, then sit, then dangle the legs for a full minute, then stand with support. Pump the ankles before rising. Rise from a hot shower or a big meal with extra care — both pool blood away from the brain. First doses and dose increases — especially alpha-blockers — go at bedtime. Call for help before getting up at night; put the light on. Adequate fluid unless restricted, no alcohol on top of the drug, and compression stockings if ordered. And keep the environment honest: bed low, call light in reach, path clear, non-skid footwear.

Adherence: the hardest part of the whole chapter

Roughly half of patients on long-term antihypertensives are not taking them as prescribed a year in, and it is not because they are careless. Look at it from their side: the disease has no symptoms, and the drug has plenty. Before you were treated you felt fine; now you are dizzy, tired, coughing, urinating all day, swollen at the ankles, and possibly having sexual side effects nobody warned you about — all to prevent a stroke you cannot imagine having. Stopping is a rational response to that math. Your job is to change the math, not to lecture.

What actually helps: name the trade honestly ("this doesn't make you feel better — it keeps you from having a stroke"); predict the side effects out loud so that when they arrive they feel expected rather than like a betrayal; teach that a side effect is a reason to call, not to quit (a cough becomes an ARB; edema becomes a lower dose); pair the dose with something they already do every day; hand them a home BP log, because a patient with numbers becomes a participant instead of a subject; simplify to once-daily or combination pills where possible; and ask about cost, plainly, because the most common reason a prescription is not taken is that it was never filled.

And teach the non-negotiables. Never stop a beta-blocker or clonidine abruptly — rebound hypertension, tachycardia, angina, MI, and with clonidine a hypertensive crisis. No potassium salt substitutes on an ACE inhibitor, ARB, or spironolactone. Beware over-the-counter NSAIDs (they blunt antihypertensives and stress the kidney) and decongestants like pseudoephedrine (they raise pressure). Grapefruit juice with certain CCBs. Get up slowly, always. Report a persistent cough, any facial or tongue swelling (go to the ED), a pulse under 60, weight gain over 2–3 lb in a day or 5 lb in a week, or fainting. If pregnancy is possible, discuss it before starting an ACE inhibitor or an ARB. And do not stop taking it because the numbers look good — the numbers look good because of it.

If the patient is on…Assess / monitorTeach
Thiazide or loop diureticK, Na, renal function, daily weight, I&O, orthostaticsTake in the morning; potassium-rich foods; report muscle cramps, weakness, palpitations; rise slowly.
SpironolactoneK (hyperkalemia), renal functionNo salt substitutes or K supplements; report breast tenderness.
ACE inhibitor / ARBK, creatinine, BP, cough, lips & tongueCough → call (switchable). Swelling of face/tongue → ED now. No salt substitutes. Not in pregnancy.
Amlodipine (dihydropyridine)Ankle/pedal edema, BP, headacheElevate legs; report worsening swelling; avoid grapefruit juice.
Diltiazem / verapamilApical pulse 1 full minute, BP, HF signs, bowel patternReport pulse < 60 or dizziness; fiber and fluid for constipation.
Beta-blockerApical pulse, BP, glucose in diabetics, wheezeNever stop abruptly. Hypoglycemia may feel different — check the meter, don't trust the tremor.
Doxazosin / clonidineOrthostatic BP, sedation, fall riskFirst dose at bedtime; dangle before standing; never stop clonidine abruptly; rotate patch sites.
Ask the question that gets a real answer. "Are you taking your blood pressure medicine?" gets you "yes" every time — it's a test, and the patient knows the right answer. Try instead: "Most people miss doses sometimes. How many days last week do you think you missed?" or "What gets in the way of taking it?" A non-judgmental, assume-imperfection question is the single cheapest adherence intervention you own, and it routinely uncovers the real issue — the copay, the dizziness, the every-morning bathroom problem — that a lecture never would.
Interactive Walk a patient from lying to standing on seven different antihypertensives and watch the cuff, the pulse, and the patient — why alpha blockers drop the pressure, why a beta-blocked pulse lies, and what the nurse monitors and teaches for each. Open full ↑
Key points
  • An accurate BP is an intervention: right cuff, 5 minutes of rest, feet flat, arm at heart level, no talking, average two readings.
  • Orthostatic hypotension = ≥ 20 systolic or ≥ 10 diastolic drop within 3 minutes of standing — measure supine, sitting, standing at 1 and 3 min, and guard the patient.
  • Teach the slow three-stage rise with a full minute of dangling; first doses of alpha-blockers at bedtime.
  • Non-adherence is rational when the disease is silent and the drug is not — predict side effects, teach "call, don't quit," and use a home BP log.
  • Never abruptly stop a beta-blocker or clonidine; avoid potassium salt substitutes, NSAIDs, and decongestants.

The chapter in one breath

  • Hemostasis is vasoconstriction → platelet plug → fibrin mesh; nearly every drug here aims at thrombin or factor Xa, and nearly every one is high-alert.
  • aPTT tracks heparin (antidote protamine); PT/INR tracks warfarin (antidote vitamin K, plus PCC for major bleeding); DOACs need no routine monitoring but do need renal dosing, adherence, and their own reversal agents.
  • A falling platelet count on heparin is HIT — it clots rather than bleeds, and heparin comes off completely.
  • Arterial clots are platelet-rich (aspirin, clopidogrel, DAPT after a stent — never stopped casually); venous clots are fibrin-rich (anticoagulants); only thrombolytics dissolve fibrin already formed, on a brutal clock and with a long list of contraindications.
  • ESAs and filgrastim instruct the marrow over days — don't normalize hemoglobin, check iron and BP, and give G-CSF at least 24 hours after chemo; blood products replace cells now, under NS-only, two-person-check, stay-15-minutes, 4-hour rules.
  • Any suspected transfusion reaction: stop the transfusion first. TACO is hypertensive overload; TRALI is hypotensive lung injury.
  • BP = CO × SVR; drugs drain the tank, slow the pump, widen the pipes, or block the RAAS — and the suffix (-pril, -sartan, -dipine, -olol, -osin) tells you the class, the adverse effect, and the teaching.
  • The nursing work of hypertension is an accurate cuff, an orthostatic assessment, and an honest adherence conversation with a patient whose disease has never once made them feel sick.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Absolute neutrophil count (ANC)
The number of infection-fighting neutrophils in circulation; the value that tells you whether filgrastim is working and whether a patient is neutropenic.
Afterload
The resistance the ventricle must push against to eject blood; lowered by vasodilators, CCBs, ACE inhibitors, and ARBs.
Angioedema
Rapid swelling of the lips, tongue, face, or airway; an airway emergency and an absolute reason to stop an ACE inhibitor permanently.
Anticoagulant
A drug that prevents fibrin clots from forming or extending — it does not dissolve an existing clot.
Antiplatelet
A drug that keeps platelets from aggregating; the treatment for platelet-rich arterial clots.
aPTT
Activated partial thromboplastin time — the test that follows the intrinsic and common pathways, used to monitor unfractionated heparin.
Baroreceptor reflex
The second-to-second reflex from the carotid sinus and aortic arch that restores blood pressure by raising heart rate and constricting vessels; blunted by many antihypertensives.
Cardiac output
Heart rate × stroke volume; one of the two factors that, with systemic vascular resistance, determines blood pressure.
DAPT (dual antiplatelet therapy)
Aspirin plus a P2Y12 inhibitor, standard after coronary stenting; stopping it early risks stent thrombosis.
DOAC
Direct oral anticoagulant — dabigatran (thrombin) or the "-xaban" agents (factor Xa); no routine monitoring, renally dosed, with specific reversal agents.
Fibrinolysis
Breakdown of a fibrin clot by plasmin; the process thrombolytics accelerate.
Hemostasis
The body's stepwise stopping of bleeding: vasoconstriction, platelet plug, then a reinforcing fibrin mesh.
Heparin-induced thrombocytopenia (HIT)
An immune reaction to heparin, usually 5–10 days in, that drops platelets by ≥ 50% and paradoxically causes thrombosis; all heparin must stop.
High-alert medication
A drug that carries a heightened risk of significant harm when used in error — anticoagulants, thrombolytics, and IV antihypertensives all qualify.
INR
International normalized ratio — the standardized PT used to dose warfarin; usual target 2.0–3.0.
Orthostatic hypotension
A drop of ≥ 20 mmHg systolic or ≥ 10 mmHg diastolic within 3 minutes of standing; the mechanism behind medication-related falls.
Preload
The volume of blood returning to and stretching the ventricle before contraction; reduced by diuretics and venodilators.
RAAS
Renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system — the hormonal loop (renin → angiotensin II → aldosterone) that raises blood pressure and that most first-line antihypertensives interrupt.
Systemic vascular resistance (SVR)
The resistance of the arterioles to blood flow; the "width of the pipes" half of the blood pressure equation.
TACO
Transfusion-associated circulatory overload — dyspnea, crackles, JVD, and hypertension from too much volume too fast.
Thrombolytic
A drug such as alteplase that converts plasminogen to plasmin and dissolves fibrin already formed; the highest-bleeding-risk drug class in this chapter.
TRALI
Transfusion-related acute lung injury — dyspnea and hypoxia with hypotension within 6 hours of transfusion; a leading cause of transfusion death.

Check yourself

1. Your patient is started on IV heparin and warfarin on the same day for a new DVT. The patient asks why they need two blood thinners. How do you explain it?
Warfarin takes 3–5 days to work, so heparin covers the gap — this is called bridging. Warfarin blocks the synthesis of factors II, VII, IX, and X but does nothing to the factors already circulating; the patient is not truly anticoagulated until those decay. Heparin works in minutes. The heparin is stopped once the INR is therapeutic (usually 2–3) on two consecutive days.
2. Match the monitoring test and the antidote to each drug: heparin, warfarin, dabigatran, apixaban.
Heparin → aPTT (or anti-Xa) → protamine sulfate. Warfarin → PT/INR → vitamin K (plus 4-factor PCC for major bleeding). Dabigatran → no routine test → idarucizumab. Apixaban → no routine test → andexanet alfa. Remember that "no routine monitoring" is not the same as "no monitoring" — DOACs still require renal function checks.
3. A patient on day 7 of heparin has a platelet count that has fallen from 250,000 to 110,000. What is happening, what is the danger, and what is the action?
Heparin-induced thrombocytopenia (HIT) — and the danger is clotting, not bleeding. A ≥ 50% platelet drop at day 5–10 on heparin is HIT until proven otherwise. Stop all heparin, including flushes and heparin-coated catheters, notify the provider, and expect a non-heparin anticoagulant such as argatroban. Do not "watch it overnight."
4. A patient on warfarin says her neighbor told her to stop eating all green vegetables. What do you teach?
Not avoidance — consistency. Vitamin K opposes warfarin, so the dose is titrated to her usual intake. Eating greens every day is fine; swinging from none to a lot is what destabilizes the INR and causes bleeding or clotting. Teach a steady pattern, plus reporting any new medication, antibiotic, herbal, or alcohol change.
5. You are teaching a patient to self-administer enoxaparin at home. Name four technique points.
Deep subcutaneous in the abdominal fat at least 2 inches from the umbilicus; do not expel the air bubble in the prefilled syringe; do not aspirate; do not massage the site; rotate sides. The air bubble clears the drug from the needle track and reduces bruising. Also teach bleeding precautions and to report unusual bruising, dark stools, or back pain (spinal hematoma risk with neuraxial procedures).
6. Why does one 81 mg aspirin dose affect a platelet for 7–10 days, and what are the two practical consequences?
Aspirin blocks COX-1 irreversibly, and a platelet has no nucleus, so it can never make new COX — the block lasts the platelet's whole 7–10 day lifespan. Consequences: a low daily dose is enough for prevention, and aspirin is typically held about 7 days before elective surgery because effect only fades as fresh platelets are released.
7. A patient 2 months post coronary stent wants to stop clopidogrel before a dental extraction "for just a week." What is your response and why?
Do not stop it — this decision goes back to cardiology. Interrupting dual antiplatelet therapy early can cause stent thrombosis and a large, sometimes fatal MI. Most dental procedures can be done on DAPT with local hemostatic measures. This is never a patient-alone or bedside decision.
8. Before alteplase for acute ischemic stroke, name the mandatory imaging step, the time window, and three things you do before the infusion starts.
A noncontrast head CT must rule out hemorrhage; the window is 3 hours from last known well (up to 4.5 in selected patients). Before infusing: establish IV access, draw all labs, and place any needed lines/catheters — because afterward there are no arterial punctures, no IM injections, and no non-essential invasive procedures. During and after: frequent neuro checks and BP control; sudden headache, vomiting, or a new deficit means stop the infusion and call.
9. A patient with CKD on epoetin alfa has a hemoglobin that rose from 9.2 to 11.4 g/dL in 10 days. What is your concern and what do you do?
Too high, too fast — this is the boxed warning territory of increased MI, stroke, VTE, and death. Hold or reduce the dose per protocol and notify the provider. Goal is the lowest dose that avoids transfusion, not a normal hemoglobin. Also check iron stores (ESAs fail without iron) and blood pressure before dosing, and never shake the vial.
10. Ten minutes into a unit of PRBCs your patient has a fever, chills, flank pain, and a falling blood pressure. What do you do first, and what is your suspicion?
Stop the transfusion immediately — suspect an acute hemolytic (ABO incompatibility) reaction. Then keep the line open with normal saline using new tubing, assess and take vitals, notify the provider and blood bank, and return the unit, tubing, and required samples. Stopping is always the first action; every extra milliliter is more hemolysis. This is why you stay with the patient for the first 15 minutes.
11. Two patients become dyspneic during transfusion. One is hypertensive with crackles and JVD; one is hypotensive and hypoxic with clear volume status. Name each and explain why the distinction matters.
Hypertensive with crackles and JVD = TACO (circulatory overload). Hypotensive with hypoxia = TRALI (transfusion-related acute lung injury). It matters because the treatments diverge: TACO is slowed/stopped, the patient is sat upright, and a diuretic is given per order; TRALI needs oxygen and supportive care, and a diuretic would worsen it. Both start the same way — stop the transfusion.
12. Your patient on lisinopril and hydrochlorothiazide reports a nagging dry cough, ankle swelling since amlodipine was added, and admits she "takes a break on weekends because it makes me pee." Address each issue.
Cough = bradykinin from the ACE inhibitor — report it; she can likely be switched to an ARB ("-sartan"), which has the same benefit without the cough. Ankle edema = expected dihydropyridine vasodilation — not heart failure by itself; elevate legs, report worsening, and the dose can be adjusted. The weekend gaps = the real problem. Move the diuretic to the morning so she isn't up at night, and use a non-judgmental question ("how many days last week did you miss?") to find the true barrier. Teach that a side effect is a reason to call, not to quit — and that she feels fine because of the medicine, not despite it.
Pharmacology · Chapter 10

Cardiovascular Drugs II

The first cardiovascular chapter was about pressure and volume. This one is about the heart muscle itself — the plaque that starves it, the pain that warns you, the pump that fails, and the electricity that keeps it honest. These are the drugs that live on your high-alert list: digoxin with its razor-thin therapeutic window, nitroglycerin that can drop a blood pressure through the floor, amiodarone that quietly scars a lung over months, and statins that most of your patients will take for the rest of their lives. What makes a nurse valuable here is not memorizing every drug — it is knowing which number to check before you push, which symptom means stop, and which sentence, said clearly at discharge, keeps a patient out of the emergency department. That is what this chapter teaches.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain how statins lower LDL, and describe the muscle and liver monitoring a nurse performs.
  • Compare the non-statin lipid agents — ezetimibe, PCSK9 inhibitors, fibrates, niacin, bile acid sequestrants — and their signature adverse effects.
  • Teach a patient to use sublingual nitroglycerin safely, including storage, dosing intervals, and when to call 911.
  • Identify the drugs in the acute coronary syndrome bundle and the nursing priorities for each.
  • Describe the four pillars of heart failure therapy and the monitoring each one demands.
  • Recognize digoxin toxicity early, name its antidote, and explain why potassium is the drug's hidden partner.
  • Classify antiarrhythmics by Vaughan Williams class and state what each class does to the cardiac action potential.
  • Perform and document an apical pulse, and state the parameters that mean hold the dose and call.

Section 1Dyslipidemia drugs

Why we chase a number the patient cannot feel

Dyslipidemia is the quietest diagnosis in the chart. Nobody feels a high LDL. There is no pain, no shortness of breath, no reason to take a pill every night for thirty years — and that is exactly the nursing problem. Low-density lipoprotein (LDL) is the particle that carries cholesterol into the arterial wall, where it oxidizes, recruits inflammatory cells, and builds the plaque you saw in the figure above. High-density lipoprotein (HDL) carries cholesterol back out toward the liver. Triglycerides are a separate problem: at very high levels (roughly above 500 mg/dL) they stop being a cardiac issue and become a pancreatitis issue.

Modern lipid management is not really about hitting a magic number. It is about risk: how likely is this person to have a heart attack or stroke in the next ten years, and how hard should we push? A patient who has already had a myocardial infarction gets high-intensity therapy without much debate. A healthy 45-year-old with a borderline panel gets a conversation. Your job is to make sure that conversation includes the truth: this drug does nothing you will notice, and it works only if you keep taking it.

Statins: the backbone

The HMG-CoA reductase inhibitors — every drug ending in -statin — block the rate-limiting enzyme the liver uses to make cholesterol. Starved of homemade cholesterol, the liver cell puts more LDL receptors on its surface and pulls LDL out of the blood. That is the whole mechanism, and it is remarkably effective: atorvastatin 40–80 mg or rosuvastatin 20–40 mg (the two high-intensity regimens) drop LDL by roughly half. Statins also stabilize existing plaque and damp vascular inflammation, which is why they are started in the hospital during an acute coronary syndrome even when the LDL looks acceptable.

Timing depends on half-life, and students get this backwards constantly. The liver makes most of its cholesterol overnight, so the short-acting statins — simvastatin, lovastatin, fluvastatin — are given in the evening. The long-acting ones — atorvastatin, rosuvastatin, pitavastatin — have half-lives long enough that they can be taken at any consistent hour, which is a gift for adherence.

Statins are metabolized largely by CYP3A4, which is why grapefruit juice matters. Grapefruit inhibits intestinal CYP3A4 and raises drug levels — the effect is real for simvastatin and lovastatin (avoid entirely), modest for atorvastatin (an occasional glass is usually tolerated), and essentially absent for pravastatin, rosuvastatin, and pitavastatin. If a patient loves grapefruit and refuses to give it up, that is a reason to switch statins, not a reason to lose the patient.

Myopathy: the thing you are actually watching for

Muscle complaints are the reason statins get stopped. They sit on a spectrum, and knowing where a patient falls on it is a genuinely useful nursing skill.

Myalgia is muscle aching or weakness with a normal creatine kinase — common, uncomfortable, not dangerous. Myopathy or myositis means symptoms plus a rising creatine kinase (CK). Rhabdomyolysis is the emergency: CK typically more than ten times the upper limit of normal, muscle protein spilling into the blood, and myoglobin clogging the renal tubules into acute kidney injury. The teaching sentence is short and specific: "If your muscles hurt badly, feel weak, and your urine turns the color of cola or tea, stop the drug and call today." Dark urine is the line between an annoyance and a hospitalization.

What do you actually monitor? Less than students expect. Check a lipid panel at baseline and again about 4–12 weeks after starting or changing a dose, then periodically. Check liver enzymes (ALT) at baseline — routine repeat LFTs are no longer recommended, only checked if symptoms of hepatotoxicity appear. Do not draw a routine CK either; get a baseline CK only in high-risk patients (older adults, kidney disease, hypothyroidism, multiple interacting drugs), and draw one whenever a patient reports muscle symptoms. Risk climbs with age, small body frame, renal impairment, untreated hypothyroidism, heavy alcohol use, and CYP3A4 inhibitors like clarithromycin, some azole antifungals, and certain HIV protease inhibitors.

Statins are also traditionally stopped in pregnancy and during breastfeeding. The labeling has been softened to allow individualized use in a narrow group of very-high-risk patients, but the practical bedside expectation remains: a patient who becomes pregnant or is planning pregnancy stops the statin and calls the prescriber. Always ask about pregnancy plans in patients of childbearing potential before discharge.

When a statin is not enough

Statins do the heavy lifting; everything else is an add-on for patients who cannot reach goal, cannot tolerate statins, or have a triglyceride problem instead of an LDL problem.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor (statin)atorvastatin, rosuvastatinLDL down 30–55%; stabilizes plaqueMuscle pain plus dark urine = rhabdomyolysis. Draw CK if symptoms. Short-acting statins at night; avoid grapefruit with simvastatin/lovastatin
Cholesterol absorption inhibitorezetimibeBlocks NPC1L1 in gut; adds another 15–25% LDL dropVery well tolerated; myopathy risk rises slightly when paired with a statin. Give 2 h before or 4 h after a bile acid sequestrant
PCSK9 inhibitor (monoclonal antibody)alirocumab, evolocumabFrees LDL receptors for recycling; LDL down another ~50%Subcutaneous every 2–4 weeks. Injection-site reactions; teach self-injection and refrigerated storage (warm to room temperature first)
Small interfering RNAinclisiranSilences PCSK9 synthesis in the liverSubcutaneous, given in the office — then only twice a year. A powerful option for the nonadherent patient
ATP-citrate lyase inhibitorbempedoic acidLDL down ~15–25%; prodrug activated in liver, not muscleUseful in statin intolerance. Watch uric acid (gout flares) and rare tendon rupture
Fibratefenofibrate, gemfibrozilBest triglyceride reduction (~40–50%); modest HDL riseNever combine gemfibrozil with a statin — severe myopathy. Fenofibrate is the safer partner. Gallstones; monitor renal function
Nicotinic acid (niacin)niacin (extended release)Raises HDL, lowers TG and LDLFlushing is near-universal: aspirin 325 mg 30 min before, take with food, avoid alcohol and hot drinks. Also hyperglycemia, hyperuricemia, hepatotoxicity
Bile acid sequestrantcholestyramine, colesevelamBinds bile acids in the gut; liver burns cholesterol to replace themConstipation, bloating. Binds other drugs — give other medications 1 h before or 4 h after. Can raise triglycerides
Omega-3 fatty acidicosapent ethylLowers triglycerides; reduces events in select patientsBleeding risk with anticoagulants; small increase in atrial fibrillation. Fishy aftertaste and burping drive nonadherence
Muscle pain is a conversation, not an automatic stop. Most statin-associated muscle aching is real but benign, and a patient who quits the drug over it loses years of protection. The nurse's move is to take the complaint seriously, draw a CK, ask about the urine color, and get the prescriber to consider a lower dose, a different statin, or alternate-day rosuvastatin — instead of letting the patient silently stop and tell nobody. The only statin that works is the one the patient will actually take.
Interactive Run a statin, ezetimibe, PCSK9 inhibitor, bile acid sequestrant, fibrate, niacin, or omega-3 on one patient's lipid panel and watch exactly which of the four numbers it moves — then tap any row to see who moves it best. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Statins inhibit HMG-CoA reductase, forcing the liver to pull LDL out of the blood; they also stabilize plaque.
  • Give short-acting statins (simvastatin, lovastatin) in the evening; atorvastatin and rosuvastatin any consistent time.
  • Monitor lipids at baseline and 4–12 weeks; ALT at baseline only; draw CK when muscle symptoms appear, not routinely.
  • Muscle pain plus cola-colored urine equals rhabdomyolysis — hold the drug and report the same day.

Section 2Angina and acute coronary syndrome

Supply versus demand, and nothing else

Every antianginal drug ever invented does one of two things: it increases the oxygen supply reaching the myocardium, or it lowers the myocardial oxygen demand. That is the entire logic of this section, and if you hold onto it, the drug list organizes itself.

Demand rises with heart rate, contractility, and wall tension (which reflects preload and afterload). So a drug that slows the heart, softens the squeeze, or drains the ventricle before it contracts will lower demand. Supply depends on coronary flow, which happens mostly during diastole — another reason slowing the heart helps, since a slower heart spends proportionally more time in diastole.

Stable angina is predictable: it comes on with exertion or emotion and goes away with rest or nitroglycerin, because a fixed plaque cannot pass enough blood when demand climbs. Unstable angina is new, worsening, or occurs at rest — the plaque has ruptured and a clot is forming. Vasospastic (Prinzmetal) angina is coronary spasm in an artery that may be nearly normal, classically at rest and in the early morning. Acute coronary syndrome (ACS) is the umbrella over unstable angina, NSTEMI, and STEMI — and the distinction between them lives in the troponin and the ST segment, not in how loudly the patient complains.

Nitrates: the drug your patient carries in a pocket

Nitrates are converted to nitric oxide in vascular smooth muscle, which relaxes it. At usual doses the effect is mostly venodilation: blood pools in the veins, less returns to the heart, preload falls, the ventricle wall is under less tension, and demand drops. At higher doses arteries dilate too, lowering afterload, and the coronary arteries themselves relax — which is why nitrates are excellent for vasospastic angina.

Sublingual nitroglycerin teaching is one of the most testable and most genuinely life-saving conversations you will have. Sit or lie down first — this drug drops blood pressure and people faint standing in kitchens. Place one 0.4 mg tablet under the tongue (or one spray onto or under the tongue) and let it dissolve; do not swallow it, do not chew it. A tingling or burning sensation may occur but its absence does not mean the tablet is dead. If the pain is unimproved or worsening 5 minutes after the first dose, call 911 — then continue up to a total of three doses, 5 minutes apart, while help is on the way. That order matters: the old teaching of "three doses, then call" delayed too many people.

Storage sinks a lot of prescriptions. Tablets belong in their original amber glass bottle, tightly capped, away from heat, light, and moisture — not in a pill organizer, not in a pocket against body heat, not in a steamy bathroom cabinet or a hot car. Once the bottle is opened, replace it about every 6 months. The metered spray is more stable and often the better choice for someone who rarely uses it. Expect headache — it is the most common effect, it means the drug is working, and acetaminophen usually handles it. Warn about dizziness and orthostasis.

Other nitrate forms have their own traps. Transdermal patches and long-acting isosorbide mononitrate/dinitrate cause tolerance if given around the clock, so the regimen builds in a nitrate-free interval of 10–12 hours — usually the patch goes on in the morning and comes off in the evening. Remove the old patch before applying a new one, rotate sites, and wear gloves so you do not dose yourself with a headache. Intravenous nitroglycerin is titrated to pain and blood pressure and traditionally requires non-PVC (polyethylene or glass) tubing, because the drug adsorbs to standard PVC.

Ask about erectile dysfunction drugs before any nitrate. Every time. Combining a nitrate with a PDE5 inhibitor — sildenafil, vardenafil (within 24 hours) or tadalafil (within 48 hours) — can cause profound, refractory hypotension and death. Patients will not volunteer this. Ask it plainly and privately, in the ambulance bay and on the unit: "Have you taken any medication for erections in the last two days?" The same caution applies to riociguat. Also withhold nitrates in suspected right ventricular infarction (often with an inferior MI) — that ventricle is preload-dependent, and dropping preload can crash the pressure.

The rest of the antianginal cabinet

Beta blockers (metoprolol, atenolol, bisoprolol) are first-line for chronic stable angina and reduce mortality after MI: they cut heart rate and contractility, so demand falls and diastolic filling time lengthens. Hold and reassess for bradycardia, hypotension, or acute decompensated heart failure, and never stop them abruptly — rebound tachycardia and angina can trigger an infarct. One classic exception: avoid beta blockers in cocaine-associated chest pain, where unopposed alpha stimulation can worsen coronary spasm; benzodiazepines and nitrates are used instead.

Calcium channel blockers dilate coronary and systemic arteries. Amlodipine and other dihydropyridines mainly vasodilate (ankle edema, reflex tachycardia, flushing). Verapamil and diltiazem also slow the heart and reduce contractility, which makes them useful when a beta blocker is not tolerated — but combining them with a beta blocker risks serious bradycardia and heart block. CCBs are the drugs of choice for vasospastic angina. Ranolazine is a later-line option for chronic angina that inhibits the late sodium current; it barely touches heart rate or blood pressure, which is its selling point, but it prolongs the QT interval and is never used for an acute attack.

The ACS bundle

When a patient with crushing chest pain hits the door, a familiar set of drugs goes in fast. Older courses teach the mnemonic MONA (morphine, oxygen, nitrates, aspirin) — keep the letters, but update the practice: morphine has been deprioritized (it may blunt antiplatelet absorption and can mask ongoing ischemia), and oxygen is given only if the SpO2 is below about 90%, because routine oxygen in a non-hypoxic patient offers no benefit and may cause harm. Aspirin remains the one drug that saves lives in the first minutes.

RolePrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Antiplatelet (immediate)aspirin 162–325 mgIrreversible COX-1 blockade; stops thromboxane A2Chewed, not swallowed — faster absorption. Non-enteric-coated. Give first, ask questions second
P2Y12 inhibitorclopidogrel, ticagrelor, prasugrelBlocks ADP-mediated platelet aggregationTicagrelor causes dyspnea (not heart failure) and requires aspirin ≤100 mg/day. Prasugrel is contraindicated with prior stroke/TIA. Hold before CABG
Anticoagulantheparin, enoxaparin, bivalirudinStops clot propagationHigh-alert. Monitor aPTT (heparin), platelets for HIT, bleeding at all sites. Antidote for heparin: protamine sulfate
Nitratenitroglycerin SL, then IVLowers preload; relieves pain and spasmAsk about PDE5 inhibitors. Hold for SBP <90 or RV infarction. Continuous BP monitoring on the drip
Beta blockermetoprololLowers demand; reduces mortality and reinfarctionStart within 24 h if stable. Hold for bradycardia, hypotension, decompensated HF, cocaine use
High-intensity statinatorvastatin 80 mgStabilizes plaque; reduces recurrent eventsStarted in hospital regardless of the LDL number. Teach that this is a lifetime drug

Fibrinolyticalteplase, tenecteplaseDissolves the clot when PCI is unavailableScreen hard for bleeding contraindications. Door-to-needle ≤30 min; PCI door-to-balloon ≤90 min
