Interactive Build the acute coronary syndrome bundle one order at a time and watch each drug move myocardial oxygen supply or demand — why nitrates cut preload, why aspirin and reperfusion save the artery, why oxygen does nothing at a saturation of 96%, and why nitrates plus a PDE-5 inhibitor must never meet. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Every antianginal either raises supply or lowers demand; nitrates work mainly by dropping preload.
  • Sublingual nitroglycerin: sit down, one dose, call 911 if unimproved at 5 minutes, up to 3 doses 5 minutes apart. Amber glass, replace every 6 months. Headache is expected.
  • Nitrates plus PDE5 inhibitors can kill — ask about sildenafil (24 h) and tadalafil (48 h) before every dose.
  • ACS: chewed aspirin immediately, P2Y12 inhibitor, anticoagulant, beta blocker, high-intensity statin; oxygen only if SpO2 <90%; morphine deprioritized.

Section 3Heart failure drugs

What a failing heart is actually doing

In heart failure with reduced ejection fraction (HFrEF), the ventricle cannot eject enough blood, and the body — sensing what looks like low volume — does exactly the wrong thing. The sympathetic nervous system fires, raising heart rate and afterload. The renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system (RAAS) switches on, retaining sodium and water and driving vasoconstriction. Over months, angiotensin II and aldosterone remodel the ventricle into a dilated, fibrotic, poorly functioning bag. The compensation becomes the disease.

That is why modern heart failure therapy looks strange at first glance: the cornerstone drugs are ones that slow the heart and block the very hormones the body is producing. We are not helping the heart work harder. We are calling off the neurohormonal siege. And the payoff is not just symptom relief — these are mortality drugs, which is why titrating them to target doses matters more than almost anything else on the outpatient side.

The four pillars

Guideline-directed medical therapy for HFrEF now rests on four drug classes, all started early and titrated up as tolerated. Students should be able to name all four cold.

PillarPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
ARNI (or ACE inhibitor / ARB)sacubitril/valsartan; lisinopril; losartanBlocks RAAS; ARNI also blocks neprilysin, preserving natriuretic peptides36-hour washout between an ACE inhibitor and an ARNI — overlapping causes angioedema. Never give both. Monitor BP, K+, creatinine. Fetal toxicity — contraindicated in pregnancy
Beta blocker (evidence-based only)carvedilol, metoprolol succinate, bisoprololBlocks sympathetic overdrive; reverses remodeling; lowers mortalityStart low, go slow. Symptoms may worsen for weeks before improving — warn the patient or they will quit. Do not start during acute decompensation. Metoprolol tartrate is not a heart failure drug
Mineralocorticoid receptor antagonistspironolactone, eplerenoneBlocks aldosterone; antifibrotic; potassium-sparingHyperkalemia — check K+ and creatinine about 1 week and 1 month after start/titration. Spironolactone causes gynecomastia; eplerenone rarely does. No salt substitutes
SGLT2 inhibitordapagliflozin, empagliflozinImproves outcomes in HF with or without diabetesGenital mycotic infections (teach perineal hygiene), volume depletion, rare euglycemic DKA. Hold with acute illness or prolonged fasting

Around the pillars sit useful extras. Hydralazine plus isosorbide dinitrate is added for patients who remain symptomatic (with proven benefit in self-identified Black patients) and is a fallback when ACE inhibitors and ARBs cannot be used because of renal function or hyperkalemia. Ivabradine slows the SA node's funny current for patients in sinus rhythm with a heart rate still at or above 70 despite a maximally tolerated beta blocker; it causes luminous visual phenomena (phosphenes) that alarm patients unless you warn them. Vericiguat is an option after a worsening event.

The ACE inhibitor profile deserves its own list, because it recurs everywhere: a persistent dry, hacking cough from bradykinin accumulation (switch to an ARB — ARBs do not cause it), angioedema (a true emergency; airway first; a lifetime contraindication to the whole class and to ARNI), hyperkalemia, first-dose hypotension (give the first dose at bedtime, hold diuretics if possible), a rise in creatinine, and fetal toxicity.

Diuretics: symptom control, not survival

Diuretics are the drugs patients feel working — and they are also the ones that do not, by themselves, prolong life. They exist to pull water off a congested patient who cannot breathe.

Loop diuretics (furosemide, bumetanide, torsemide) block the Na-K-2Cl transporter in the thick ascending limb and are powerful enough to work even when the GFR is poor. Their costs are electrolytic: hypokalemia, hypomagnesemia, hyponatremia, hypocalcemia, metabolic alkalosis, hyperuricemia, hyperglycemia, and dehydration with orthostatic hypotension. Ototoxicity — tinnitus and hearing loss, usually reversible — is linked to rapid IV administration and high doses, which is why IV furosemide is pushed slowly (commonly no faster than about 20 mg/min) and why loop plus aminoglycoside is a bad pairing. Loops are sulfonamide-derived; ethacrynic acid is the alternative for a true sulfa allergy. Give the morning dose early and the second dose by mid-afternoon so the patient is not up all night.

Thiazides (hydrochlorothiazide, chlorthalidone) are weaker and fail below a GFR of roughly 30. Metolazone is the exception used for diuretic resistance: it is given about 30 minutes before the loop dose, and the combination is potent enough to strip potassium dangerously fast — watch the K+ closely. Potassium-sparing agents (spironolactone, eplerenone, triamterene, amiloride) go the other way and retain potassium.

Digoxin and its narrow window

Digoxin is the oldest drug in this chapter and the one most likely to hurt someone. It inhibits the sodium–potassium ATPase in cardiac myocytes. Sodium builds up inside the cell, the sodium–calcium exchanger runs less efficiently, intracellular calcium rises, and the contraction gets stronger — a positive inotropic effect. Separately, digoxin increases vagal tone at the AV node, slowing conduction and heart rate — a negative chronotropic and negative dromotropic effect. That combination is why it is used both for symptomatic HFrEF (fewer hospitalizations, but no mortality benefit) and for rate control in atrial fibrillation, particularly in a hypotensive patient who cannot tolerate a beta blocker.

The therapeutic range is classically 0.5–2.0 ng/mL, but in heart failure the modern target is a narrower 0.5–0.9 ng/mL — higher levels increase mortality without adding benefit. Draw the level as a trough, at least 6–8 hours after the last dose (12 hours or immediately before the next dose is cleanest); a level drawn too early looks alarmingly high and reflects distribution, not toxicity.

The signs of toxicity are famous because they are exam gold and because they are easy to miss at 3 a.m.

SystemWhat you seeWhy it matters
GastrointestinalAnorexia first, then nausea, vomiting, abdominal painLoss of appetite is the earliest sign — ask about it every visit
VisualBlurred or yellow-green vision, halos around lightsNearly pathognomonic; patients rarely mention it unless asked
NeurologicFatigue, weakness, confusion, deliriumIn older adults, new confusion is often the only sign
CardiacBradycardia, AV block, any arrhythmia (classically bidirectional VT, or atrial tachycardia with block)The lethal endpoint. Digoxin can cause the very rhythms it treats
ElectrolytesHypokalemia, hypomagnesemia, hypercalcemiaEach one potentiates toxicity at a normal drug level

That last row is the concept students most often miss. Potassium and digoxin compete for the same binding site on the Na+/K+ ATPase. When potassium falls — and what drug does your heart failure patient also take? A loop diuretic — digoxin binds more avidly and the patient can become toxic with a "therapeutic" level on the lab report. A normal digoxin level does not rule out digoxin toxicity. Interacting drugs raise levels too: amiodarone, verapamil, quinidine, and clarithromycin all push digoxin up, and the dose is typically cut roughly in half when amiodarone is added.

Treatment of significant toxicity is digoxin immune Fab (DigiFab) — antibody fragments that bind digoxin and pull it out of tissue — used for life-threatening arrhythmia, severe hyperkalemia from acute overdose, or hemodynamic instability. After Fab is given, total serum digoxin levels become uninterpretable (they measure bound drug), so treat the patient, not the number. Correct hypokalemia and hypomagnesemia; in acute overdose with hyperkalemia, calcium is generally avoided.

"Grandma isn't eating and the TV looks funny." That sentence, from a family member on the phone, is digoxin toxicity until proven otherwise. Anorexia comes first, the yellow-green visual change is the giveaway, and confusion in an older adult may be the only thing anyone notices. Take an apical pulse for a full minute, hold the dose if it is under 60, draw a level and a potassium, and call. Do not wait for the vomiting.
Interactive Tap each heart failure drug class to see which of the four moves it makes — drain preload, drop afterload, slow the rate, or squeeze harder — then drag the potassium and digoxin sliders to watch K+ and digoxin compete for the same site on the Na+/K+-ATPase, turning a normal-looking level toxic. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Heart failure therapy blocks the body's own compensation: the four pillars are ARNI/ACEi/ARB, an evidence-based beta blocker, an MRA, and an SGLT2 inhibitor.
  • Allow a 36-hour washout from an ACE inhibitor before an ARNI; ACE inhibitor cough means switch to an ARB; angioedema means stop the class forever.
  • Loop diuretics relieve congestion but waste potassium and magnesium and are ototoxic if pushed fast; MRAs and ACE inhibitors retain potassium — know which way each drug pulls.
  • Digoxin: target 0.5–0.9 ng/mL in HF, trough drawn 6–8+ h post-dose; anorexia is the first sign; hypokalemia potentiates toxicity; antidote is digoxin immune Fab.

Section 4Antiarrhythmics

The five phases, and why anyone should care

Antiarrhythmics are frightening to learn because the classification looks arbitrary. It is not. Each class is named for the ion channel it blocks, and each channel owns a phase of the action potential. Learn the phases once and the classes become obvious.

In a working ventricular myocyte: Phase 0 is the rapid upstroke — sodium rushes in and the cell depolarizes. Phase 1 is a brief notch as potassium leaks out. Phase 2 is the long plateau unique to cardiac muscle — calcium enters and drives contraction, and this is what keeps the heart from tetanizing. Phase 3 is repolarization as potassium floods out. Phase 4 is the resting membrane potential — except in pacemaker cells (SA and AV nodes), where phase 4 is a slow spontaneous drift upward (the "funny current") that sets the heart rate, and where phase 0 is driven by calcium, not sodium. That last detail explains why calcium channel blockers act on nodes while sodium channel blockers act on the working muscle.

The Vaughan Williams classes

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
IA — Na+ block (moderate)procainamide, quinidine, disopyramideSlows phase 0; prolongs repolarization → widened QRS and longer QTProcainamide: drug-induced lupus (arthralgia, butterfly rash — check ANA), agranulocytosis, hypotension on rapid IV. Quinidine: cinchonism (tinnitus, headache, vertigo), diarrhea, raises digoxin. Watch for torsades
IB — Na+ block (weak)lidocaine, mexiletineSlows phase 0 slightly; shortens repolarization; targets ischemic ventricular tissueVentricular arrhythmias only — useless in atrial fibrillation. Toxicity is neurologic: perioral numbness, tinnitus, confusion, twitching, seizures. Never use the lidocaine with epinephrine formulation IV
IC — Na+ block (strong)flecainide, propafenoneMarkedly slows phase 0; minimal effect on repolarizationContraindicated with structural heart disease or prior MI — increases mortality. Used in structurally normal hearts, sometimes as a "pill in the pocket" for AF
II — beta blockersmetoprolol, esmolol, atenololBlunt sympathetic drive; slow phase 4 in the SA node and slow AV conductionRate control workhorses. Hold for bradycardia or hypotension; caution in asthma (use cardioselective); mask hypoglycemia symptoms in diabetes; never stop abruptly
III — K+ blockamiodarone, sotalol, dofetilide, ibutilide, dronedaroneProlong phase 3 repolarization → longer refractory period and longer QTQT prolongation and torsades. Dofetilide and sotalol require inpatient telemetry initiation. Sotalol is also a beta blocker. See amiodarone below
IV — non-dihydropyridine Ca2+ blockverapamil, diltiazemSlow phase 0 in nodal tissue; slow AV conduction and rateBradycardia, heart block, hypotension, constipation (verapamil). Avoid in HFrEF (negative inotropes) and use caution with a beta blocker
Unclassifiedadenosine, digoxin, magnesium, atropineAdenosine transiently blocks the AV node; magnesium treats torsades; atropine treats symptomatic bradycardiaSee adenosine below. Magnesium is the answer for torsades de pointes. Atropine 1 mg IV for unstable bradycardia

Two drugs that deserve their own paragraph

Amiodarone is the most useful and most toxic antiarrhythmic in the building. It blocks potassium channels (class III) but also has sodium-blocking, beta-blocking, and calcium-blocking activity — it is all four classes in one molecule, which is why it works on almost anything. It is also lipophilic and stored in fat, giving it a half-life of roughly 40–60 days: side effects can appear months after starting and persist months after stopping. Monitoring is a systems tour — pulmonary fibrosis (the feared one; baseline chest X-ray and pulmonary function, then investigate any new dry cough or dyspnea), thyroid dysfunction in either direction because the molecule is loaded with iodine (baseline and periodic TSH), hepatotoxicity (LFTs), corneal microdeposits (usually benign; annual eye exam; report visual halos), and blue-gray skin discoloration with severe photosensitivity (sunscreen and covering clothing — a real quality-of-life issue patients are not warned about often enough). It raises digoxin and warfarin levels, so both doses come down when amiodarone goes up. IV amiodarone can cause hypotension and phlebitis — use a central line or large vein and an inline filter for continuous infusions.

Adenosine is the drug that makes new nurses gasp. Given for stable SVT, it briefly blocks the AV node and the monitor shows a few seconds of asystole before sinus rhythm resumes. Its half-life is under 10 seconds, so it must be given as a rapid IV push — 6 mg, immediately followed by a 20 mL saline flush, ideally through a proximal large-bore line with the arm elevated (a two-syringe or stopcock technique). Warn the patient first: they will feel chest pressure, flushing, and a sense of doom for a few seconds. Have the defibrillator nearby and a rhythm strip running — the strip is diagnostic even when the drug does not convert.

Every antiarrhythmic is also a proarrhythmic. These drugs manipulate the same channels that cause arrhythmias, so any of them can create a worse rhythm than the one you are treating. That is the whole story of the CAST trial, where flecainide suppressed PVCs after MI beautifully and killed people anyway. Practically: watch the QTc. A QTc above 500 ms, or an increase of more than 60 ms from baseline, is a call to the prescriber — and check the potassium and magnesium while you are at it, because low levels make torsades far more likely.
Interactive Tap a Vaughan Williams class — I, II, III, IV, plus adenosine and digoxin — and watch it reshape the cardiac action potential, with the ventricular myocyte and the SA/AV nodal curve side by side so you can see which tissue each drug can actually reach, which ECG interval stretches, and what to monitor. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Vaughan Williams: I = sodium (phase 0), II = beta blockers (phase 4/AV node), III = potassium (phase 3, QT), IV = calcium (nodal phase 0).
  • IA prolongs repolarization (procainamide → lupus-like syndrome), IB shortens it and is ventricular-only (lidocaine → seizures), IC is contraindicated in structural heart disease.
  • Amiodarone works on everything and damages everything: lungs, thyroid, liver, cornea, skin. Half-life 40–60 days; cut digoxin and warfarin doses.
  • Adenosine is a rapid push with a 20 mL flush — warn the patient, expect a brief asystolic pause. Magnesium is the answer for torsades. Watch the QTc on every class III drug.

Section 5Cardiovascular monitoring and nursing care

The apical pulse: a 60-second skill that prevents harm

Before digoxin and before beta blockers and non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blockers, you take an apical pulse. Not a radial pulse, not the monitor's number, and not for fifteen seconds times four. Place the diaphragm at the fifth intercostal space, midclavicular line — the point of maximal impulse — and count for a full minute. The full minute is not ritual: these are exactly the patients with irregular rhythms and a pulse deficit, where weak beats generate a heart sound but no radial pulse, and a 15-second sample can be wrong by 20 beats.

Then apply the parameter. Hold digoxin and notify the prescriber if the apical rate is below 60 in an adult (below 70 in a child, below 90–110 in an infant), or if the rate is above 100, or if the rhythm has newly changed. Document the rate, the rhythm, what you held, and who you called. If a patient is discharged on digoxin, they take their own radial pulse for a full minute before every dose — watch them do it, do not just tell them.

Levels, potassium, and the numbers that matter

What you checkNormal / targetWhen to checkNursing alert
Apical pulse60–100 bpmBefore every dose of digoxin, beta blocker, verapamil, diltiazemHold and call if <60 (adult). Full minute, fifth ICS midclavicular
Digoxin level0.5–2.0 ng/mL overall; 0.5–0.9 in heart failureTrough — 6–8+ h after the dose, or just before the next oneA normal level does not rule out toxicity if K+ is low. Antidote: digoxin immune Fab
Potassium3.5–5.0 mEq/LBaseline, then with every diuretic, ACEi/ARB/ARNI, or MRA change; ~1 week and 1 month after an MRA startLow K+ → digoxin toxicity and torsades. High K+ → bradycardia and arrest. Both directions kill
Magnesium1.5–2.5 mg/dLWith loop diuretics; with any QT-prolonging drug; in refractory hypokalemiaYou cannot correct potassium until magnesium is corrected. Magnesium is the treatment for torsades
Serum creatinine / eGFRBaseline-dependentBefore and after starting ACEi/ARB/ARNI/MRA/SGLT2i; with diuretic changesDigoxin is renally cleared — falling GFR means rising digoxin. A modest early creatinine rise on an ACE inhibitor is often acceptable
Blood pressure (with orthostatics)IndividualizedBefore nitrates, ACEi/ARNI first doses, and any titrationHold nitrates for SBP <90. A drop of ≥20 systolic or ≥10 diastolic on standing is orthostatic hypotension — a fall risk
QTc interval<450 ms (men), <460 ms (women)Baseline and with class IA/III drugs, ranolazine, and dose changesQTc >500 ms or a rise >60 ms → call. Check K+ and Mg2+ at the same time
Daily weightPatient's dry weightEvery morning at home in heart failure2–3 lb in a day or 5 lb in a week → call the clinic. Weight beats edema as an early warning
Lipid panelRisk-based LDL goalBaseline; 4–12 weeks after a change; then periodicallyFasting is often unnecessary now. Adherence, not the number, is the usual reason a panel disappoints
Creatine kinase (CK)Lab-specificOnly when a statin patient reports muscle symptoms (plus baseline in high-risk patients)CK >10× ULN with symptoms and dark urine = rhabdomyolysis. Hold the statin

The potassium row is the one to internalize, because your heart failure patient is often on drugs pulling potassium in both directions at once: a loop diuretic dumping it and an ACE inhibitor plus spironolactone holding it. The net result is unpredictable in any individual, which is why we measure rather than assume. Two teaching points fall out of this. First, salt substitutes are potassium chloride — a patient dutifully avoiding sodium with a shaker of "lite salt" while taking lisinopril and spironolactone can arrive hyperkalemic and bradycardic. Ask specifically. Second, potassium-rich foods (bananas, oranges, potatoes, tomatoes, spinach, avocado) are advice for the patient on a loop diuretic alone, and can be exactly wrong for the patient on an MRA.

What you say before they walk out the door

Cardiovascular drugs fail at home, not in the hospital. These are asymptomatic conditions treated with drugs that cause symptoms — and the patient will do the arithmetic. Discharge teaching is the intervention.

Drug or classWhat the patient must know
StatinTake it forever; it works invisibly. Report muscle pain, weakness, and especially dark urine. Evening dosing for simvastatin/lovastatin. Grapefruit warning where it applies. Stop and call if pregnancy is possible
Sublingual nitroglycerinSit down. One dose under the tongue; if not better in 5 minutes, call 911, then up to 3 doses total, 5 minutes apart. Amber glass bottle, cool and dark, replace every 6 months. Headache is normal. No erectile dysfunction drugs
Nitroglycerin patchApply in the morning, remove in the evening for a 10–12 h nitrate-free interval. Remove the old patch first, rotate sites, wash hands
Beta blockerNever stop suddenly — rebound angina, tachycardia, or infarct. Check your pulse before doses. Rise slowly. In diabetes, it can mask the shakiness of hypoglycemia — check glucose, do not rely on feel
ACE inhibitor / ARB / ARNIA dry nagging cough is worth reporting (it is fixable). Any swelling of the lips, tongue, or throat is a 911 emergency. No salt substitutes. Not in pregnancy — call the day you find out
Loop diureticMorning doses so you can sleep. Weigh yourself daily, same time, same scale, after voiding, before breakfast. 2–3 lb in a day or 5 lb in a week → call. Report ringing in the ears, cramps, or dizziness
Spironolactone / eplerenoneNo salt substitutes, no potassium supplements unless told. Report breast tenderness or enlargement (there is an alternative). Keep the lab appointments — they are how we catch high potassium before it hurts you
DigoxinTake your pulse for a full minute before every dose; hold and call if under 60. Report loss of appetite, nausea, blurred or yellow-green vision, halos, or new confusion. Never double a missed dose. Keep potassium labs
AmiodaroneReport any new cough or shortness of breath right away. Sunscreen and covering clothes — you will burn badly. Keep the thyroid, liver, and eye follow-ups. Tell every prescriber you take it, because it changes other drug levels
Antiplatelets / anticoagulantsSoft toothbrush, electric razor, no contact sports. Report black tarry stools, blood in urine, unusual bruising, or a headache after a fall. Never stop a P2Y12 inhibitor after a stent without calling the cardiologist — the stent can clot

One more layer sits underneath all of it: several drugs in this chapter are formally high-alert medications — IV anticoagulants, IV antiarrhythmics, IV inotropes, and digoxin among them. High-alert does not mean used more often incorrectly; it means when an error happens, the harm is catastrophic. That is what justifies the independent double-check, the smart pump limits, and the habit of saying the dose out loud with another nurse. Use them. Nobody has ever been disciplined for verifying a drip.

The most useful cardiac assessment tool costs twenty dollars and lives in your pocket. A full-minute apical pulse, a scale, and a real conversation about symptoms catch more heart failure decompensation and more digoxin toxicity than any monitor. Ask the three questions every single time: Are you eating? Are you weighing? Has anything looked strange — halos, yellow, blurry? Patients answer honestly when you ask specifically.
Interactive Set the apical pulse, digoxin level, potassium, and the patient's complaints on a live bedside chart, and see whether the nurse gives the dose, reports the finding, holds and notifies, or escalates for toxicity. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Take an apical pulse for a full minute at the fifth ICS midclavicular line before digoxin, beta blockers, verapamil, and diltiazem; hold and call if <60.
  • Draw digoxin levels as a trough ≥6–8 h after the dose; target 0.5–0.9 ng/mL in heart failure. A normal level does not exclude toxicity when potassium is low.
  • Potassium is the master variable — loops dump it, MRAs and ACE inhibitors hold it, salt substitutes are potassium chloride, and both extremes are lethal.
  • Teach daily weights (2–3 lb/day or 5 lb/week means call), never stopping a beta blocker abruptly, and never combining nitrates with PDE5 inhibitors.

The chapter in one breath

  • Statins block HMG-CoA reductase and pull LDL out of the blood; watch for myopathy, draw a CK only when muscles hurt, and treat muscle pain plus dark urine as rhabdomyolysis until proven otherwise.
  • Non-statin agents fill gaps: ezetimibe (absorption), PCSK9 inhibitors and inclisiran (receptor recycling), bempedoic acid (statin intolerance), fibrates and icosapent ethyl (triglycerides), niacin (flushing — pretreat with aspirin), sequestrants (separate other drugs by 1–4 hours).
  • Antianginals either raise supply or lower demand; nitrates drop preload, cause headache, and are lethal with PDE5 inhibitors — sit down, one dose, call 911 at 5 minutes, three doses maximum, amber glass, replace every 6 months.
  • The ACS bundle is chewed aspirin, a P2Y12 inhibitor, an anticoagulant, a beta blocker, a high-intensity statin, and an ACE inhibitor; oxygen only if SpO2 <90%, morphine deprioritized, reperfusion within 30 (needle) or 90 (balloon) minutes.
  • Heart failure therapy calls off the body's own neurohormonal siege: the four pillars are ARNI/ACEi/ARB, an evidence-based beta blocker, an MRA, and an SGLT2 inhibitor; diuretics relieve symptoms but do not prolong life.
  • Digoxin is a positive inotrope and negative chronotrope with a razor-thin window: anorexia first, then nausea, yellow-green vision, confusion, and arrhythmia; hypokalemia potentiates it; the antidote is digoxin immune Fab.
  • Antiarrhythmics are named for the channel they block — I sodium, II beta, III potassium, IV calcium — and every one of them is also proarrhythmic; amiodarone works everywhere and damages lungs, thyroid, liver, cornea, and skin.
  • The nursing core is small and it holds: full-minute apical pulse, trough digoxin levels, potassium and magnesium, daily weights, QTc, and the discharge sentences that keep the patient alive at home.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Acute coronary syndrome (ACS)
The umbrella term for unstable angina, NSTEMI, and STEMI — all caused by plaque rupture and thrombus in a coronary artery.
Afterload
The resistance the ventricle must overcome to eject blood; lowering it reduces cardiac workload.
Angioedema
Rapid swelling of the lips, tongue, or airway; a rare but life-threatening reaction to ACE inhibitors and ARNIs, and a permanent contraindication to the class.
Apical pulse
The heart rate counted with a stethoscope at the fifth intercostal space, midclavicular line, for a full minute — required before digoxin and rate-slowing drugs.
Chronotropic effect
An effect on heart rate; digoxin and beta blockers are negative chronotropes.
Cinchonism
Quinidine toxicity: tinnitus, headache, vertigo, and visual disturbance.
Creatine kinase (CK)
A muscle enzyme that rises with muscle injury; drawn when a statin patient reports muscle symptoms.
Digoxin immune Fab
Antibody fragments that bind and inactivate digoxin; the antidote for life-threatening digoxin toxicity.
Guideline-directed medical therapy (GDMT)
The evidence-based drug regimen for heart failure, built on four pillars and titrated to target doses.
High-alert medication
A drug that causes catastrophic harm when given in error — digoxin, heparin, IV antiarrhythmics — warranting an independent double-check.
Inotropic effect
An effect on the force of cardiac contraction; digoxin is a positive inotrope.
Low-density lipoprotein (LDL)
The particle that delivers cholesterol into the arterial wall; the primary target of lipid therapy.
Myopathy
Muscle pain or weakness accompanied by a rising CK; the middle of the statin muscle spectrum, between benign myalgia and rhabdomyolysis.
Nitrate tolerance
Loss of nitrate effect from continuous exposure; prevented by a daily nitrate-free interval of 10–12 hours.
Preload
The volume stretching the ventricle at end-diastole; nitrates and diuretics reduce it.
Proarrhythmia
A new or worsened arrhythmia caused by an antiarrhythmic drug — a risk of every drug in the class.
Pulse deficit
The difference between the apical and radial rates, caused by beats too weak to reach the periphery.
QT interval (QTc)
The ECG measure of ventricular repolarization; prolongation predisposes to torsades de pointes.
Rhabdomyolysis
Severe muscle breakdown with markedly elevated CK and myoglobinuria (dark, cola-colored urine), risking acute kidney injury.
Torsades de pointes
A polymorphic ventricular tachycardia arising from a prolonged QT; treated with intravenous magnesium.
Vasospastic (Prinzmetal) angina
Chest pain from coronary artery spasm, often at rest; treated with nitrates and calcium channel blockers, not beta blockers.
Vaughan Williams classification
The system that sorts antiarrhythmics by the ion channel they block: I sodium, II beta-adrenergic, III potassium, IV calcium.

Check yourself

1. A patient starting simvastatin asks why it has to be taken at bedtime when their neighbor takes atorvastatin with breakfast. What do you say?
Because simvastatin is short-acting and the liver makes most of its cholesterol overnight; atorvastatin has a long half-life and works whenever it is taken. The same is true of lovastatin and fluvastatin (evening) versus rosuvastatin and pitavastatin (any consistent time). Consistency matters more than the clock for the long-acting agents.
2. A patient on rosuvastatin reports two weeks of aching thighs and says his urine "looks like Coke." What is your priority action and why?
Hold the statin, draw a CK and renal function, and notify the prescriber immediately — this is rhabdomyolysis until proven otherwise. Dark urine is myoglobin, which clogs renal tubules and causes acute kidney injury. Muscle ache with a normal CK is benign myalgia; muscle ache with dark urine is an emergency.
3. Why is gemfibrozil a dangerous partner for a statin, and what is the safer alternative in the same class?
Gemfibrozil markedly raises statin levels and the combination carries a high risk of severe myopathy and rhabdomyolysis; fenofibrate is the safer fibrate to combine. If a patient needs both LDL and triglyceride lowering, fenofibrate is the standard choice.
4. A patient on niacin calls, alarmed by intense facial flushing 30 minutes after each dose. What do you teach?
Flushing is a prostaglandin-mediated effect that is uncomfortable but not dangerous; take aspirin 325 mg about 30 minutes before the dose, take niacin with food, and avoid alcohol and hot beverages around dosing. It usually diminishes over weeks. Also monitor glucose, uric acid, and liver enzymes on niacin.
5. Your patient with chest pain has taken tadalafil yesterday evening. The provider orders sublingual nitroglycerin. What do you do?
Do not give it — hold the dose and notify the provider immediately. Tadalafil and nitrates must be separated by 48 hours (24 hours for sildenafil and vardenafil); together they cause profound, refractory hypotension that can be fatal. Ask every chest-pain patient about erectile dysfunction drugs, privately and specifically.
6. Teach a patient the correct home use of sublingual nitroglycerin, including when to call 911.
Sit or lie down, place one tablet under the tongue and let it dissolve; if the pain is unimproved or worsening 5 minutes later, call 911, then take up to two more doses 5 minutes apart while waiting. Store in the original amber glass bottle, cool and dark, and replace about every 6 months. Headache is expected and normal.
7. Why does a nitroglycerin patch come off at night, and what happens if it does not?
To provide a nitrate-free interval of 10–12 hours, which prevents nitrate tolerance. Continuous exposure causes the vasculature to stop responding, and the patient loses the drug's benefit entirely. Remove the old patch before applying a new one and rotate sites.
8. A patient arrives with crushing chest pain and an SpO2 of 96% on room air. Which of the classic "MONA" interventions is now questioned, and what has replaced the old routine?
Oxygen is withheld unless the SpO2 is below about 90%, and morphine is deprioritized to pain refractory to nitrates. Routine oxygen in a non-hypoxic patient provides no benefit and may harm; morphine may blunt antiplatelet absorption and mask ongoing ischemia. Chewed, non-enteric-coated aspirin remains the immediate priority.
9. Name the four pillars of guideline-directed therapy for heart failure with reduced ejection fraction, with a prototype for each.
An ARNI (sacubitril/valsartan) or ACE inhibitor/ARB; an evidence-based beta blocker (carvedilol, metoprolol succinate, bisoprolol); a mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist (spironolactone, eplerenone); and an SGLT2 inhibitor (dapagliflozin, empagliflozin). All four lower mortality. Remember the 36-hour washout between an ACE inhibitor and an ARNI, and that metoprolol tartrate is not the heart failure formulation.
10. An 82-year-old on digoxin and furosemide says she has no appetite and the television "looks yellowish." Her digoxin level is 1.4 ng/mL. Is she toxic?
Very possibly yes — a level inside the reference range does not rule out toxicity, especially when potassium is low. Digoxin and potassium compete for the same site on the Na+/K+ ATPase, so the furosemide-induced hypokalemia lets digoxin bind more avidly. Anorexia is the earliest sign and yellow-green vision is nearly diagnostic. Hold the dose, take a full-minute apical pulse, draw a potassium and magnesium, and call. The antidote for severe toxicity is digoxin immune Fab.
11. Match each Vaughan Williams class to the channel it blocks and its effect on the action potential.
Class I blocks sodium and slows phase 0 (IA prolongs repolarization, IB shortens it, IC has little effect on it); class II are beta blockers, slowing phase 4 in the SA node and AV conduction; class III blocks potassium, prolonging phase 3 repolarization and the QT; class IV blocks calcium, slowing phase 0 in nodal tissue. Amiodarone is nominally class III but has properties of all four, which is why it works on nearly any rhythm.
12. Your patient is about to receive adenosine for stable SVT. What do you do before, during, and after the push?
Before: warn the patient about a few seconds of chest pressure, flushing, and a sense of doom; ensure a proximal large-bore IV, continuous ECG with a strip running, and the defibrillator nearby. During: 6 mg as a rapid IV push immediately followed by a 20 mL saline flush with the arm elevated. After: watch for the brief asystolic pause and conversion, and keep the rhythm strip — it is diagnostic even if the drug does not convert. Adenosine's half-life is under 10 seconds, which is why the push must be fast and the flush immediate.
Pharmacology · Chapter 11

Diuretics, IV Fluids & Poisoning

Almost everything in this chapter comes down to water and the particles dissolved in it. Diuretics pull water out of a patient by tricking one specific stretch of the kidney tubule; IV fluids push water back in, and where that water lands depends entirely on the tonicity printed on the bag. Electrolyte replacement sits in the middle — the corrections that fix a dangerous lab value can, given too fast, stop a heart. And when the wrong substance gets into a patient by accident or on purpose, a small handful of antidotes can reverse what would otherwise be fatal. These are among the highest-alert drugs a nurse handles, and the nurse's monitoring is usually what makes them safe.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Trace filtrate through the nephron and name the segment each diuretic class blocks.
  • Compare loop, thiazide, potassium-sparing, osmotic, and carbonic anhydrase inhibitor diuretics by prototype, potency, and electrolyte fallout.
  • Anticipate and monitor the characteristic adverse effects of each diuretic class, especially hypokalemia and hyperkalemia.
  • Classify an IV solution as isotonic, hypotonic, or hypertonic and predict which fluid compartment it will fill.
  • Select an appropriate IV fluid for common bedside scenarios and name the danger of each type.
  • Replace potassium, calcium, and magnesium safely, including the rules that must never be broken.
  • Match common poisonings to their antidotes and describe the nurse's role in each reversal.
  • Explain why diuretics, concentrated electrolytes, and reversal agents are treated as high-alert medications.

Section 1The nephron & where diuretics act

One million tiny filters

Each kidney holds roughly a million nephrons, and each nephron is a factory that does the same four jobs: filtration, reabsorption, secretion, and excretion. Blood arrives at the glomerulus, a tuft of capillaries wedged inside Bowman's capsule, and pressure squeezes water and small solutes out into the tubule. That filtrate is enormous — about 180 liters a day. Since nobody urinates 180 liters, the obvious conclusion is the important one: the nephron's real work is not making urine, it is taking almost all of it back. Roughly 99 percent of the filtered water is reabsorbed. Urine is the leftover 1 percent.

This is the single insight that makes diuretics make sense. A diuretic does not squeeze more fluid out of the blood. It interferes with reabsorption. Water follows sodium, so any drug that blocks sodium from being pulled back out of the tubule leaves that sodium in the filtrate, and the water stays with it and leaves as urine. Every diuretic in this chapter is a variation on that one theme: block sodium reabsorption somewhere, and water comes along.

The segments, in order

Filtrate leaves Bowman's capsule and enters the proximal convoluted tubule (PCT), the workhorse, where about 65 percent of filtered sodium and water — plus glucose, amino acids, and bicarbonate — are reclaimed. Next comes the loop of Henle, which dives into the salty medulla; its thick ascending limb pumps out another 20–25 percent of the sodium using the Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporter. Then the distal convoluted tubule (DCT) reclaims perhaps 5–10 percent through a sodium-chloride cotransporter. Finally the collecting duct fine-tunes the last 2–3 percent under hormonal control: aldosterone tells it to keep sodium and dump potassium, and antidiuretic hormone (ADH) tells it to keep water by inserting aquaporins.

The percentages tell you the potency ranking before you learn a single drug name. Block the loop, where a quarter of the sodium is handled, and you get a powerful diuresis. Block the DCT, where under a tenth is handled, and you get a moderate one. Block the collecting duct, which is only fine-tuning, and you get a weak diuresis — but exquisite control over potassium.

SegmentSodium reclaimedTransporter / controlDiuretic that acts here
Proximal tubule~65%Na⁺/H⁺ exchange; carbonic anhydrase; osmotic gradientCarbonic anhydrase inhibitors; osmotic diuretics
Thick ascending limb~20–25%Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporterLoop diuretics — most potent
Distal convoluted tubule~5–10%Na⁺-Cl⁻ cotransporterThiazides — moderate
Collecting duct~2–3%Aldosterone-driven Na⁺ channels (ENaC); ADH/aquaporinsPotassium-sparing — weak, but K⁺-protective

Why potassium is always in the conversation

Look at the collecting duct again. Aldosterone reabsorbs sodium there and trades it for potassium, which goes out into the urine. Now imagine a loop or thiazide diuretic upstream, dumping a flood of sodium into the collecting duct. More sodium arriving means more sodium–potassium trading, which means more potassium lost. That is why loop and thiazide diuretics cause hypokalemia — it is not a random side effect, it is arithmetic. And drugs that block that trade at the collecting duct do the reverse: they hold potassium in, which is why they are called potassium-sparing and why their signature danger is hyperkalemia.

Say the segment out loud. If you can name the segment a diuretic blocks, you can derive its potency, its electrolyte losses, and its monitoring plan without memorizing a list. Loop = the ascending limb = big sodium = big diuresis = big potassium loss. Everything else follows.
Interactive Tap a nephron segment or a diuretic class to see where each drug blocks sodium reabsorption — with its potency ceiling, potassium and calcium effects, and what the nurse monitors. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The nephron filters ~180 L/day and reabsorbs about 99% of it; urine is the remainder.
  • Diuretics work by blocking sodium reabsorption — water follows sodium out.
  • Potency tracks the segment: loop > thiazide > potassium-sparing, because that is the order of how much sodium each segment handles.
  • The collecting duct trades sodium for potassium under aldosterone — the reason most diuretics wreck potassium in one direction or the other.

Section 2Diuretic classes

Loop diuretics — the fire hose

Furosemide is the prototype, joined by bumetanide, torsemide, and ethacrynic acid. They block the Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporter in the thick ascending limb, and the result is the most powerful diuresis in pharmacology — up to 20–25 percent of filtered sodium out the door. IV furosemide begins working within minutes, which is exactly what you want in acute pulmonary edema, when a patient is drowning in their own fluid and cannot wait. Loops also keep working when kidney function is poor, unlike thiazides, so they are the diuretic of chronic kidney disease. Their classic uses: heart failure with fluid overload, pulmonary edema, edema of renal or hepatic origin.

The trade-off is that a fire hose flushes out everything. Loops waste potassium, magnesium, calcium, sodium, and chloride and can cause dehydration, orthostatic hypotension, and metabolic alkalosis. They are also ototoxic — tinnitus and hearing loss, made far more likely by rapid IV push or by pairing with an aminoglycoside. Push IV furosemide slowly, no faster than about 20 mg/minute in an adult, and slower still in the elderly. Because furosemide is a sulfonamide derivative, ask about sulfa allergy; ethacrynic acid is the non-sulfa alternative.

Thiazides — the everyday drug

Hydrochlorothiazide is the prototype; chlorthalidone (longer-acting, generally preferred for blood pressure) and metolazone round out the group. They block the Na⁺-Cl⁻ cotransporter in the distal tubule — a smaller job, so a gentler diuresis, but the mild sustained vasodilation that comes with it makes thiazides a cornerstone of hypertension therapy. They are also, delightfully, the only diuretic that retains calcium, which makes them useful in calcium-based kidney stones and mildly protective of bone. Note the ceiling: thiazides lose effectiveness once the glomerular filtration rate falls below roughly 30 mL/min. That is when the prescriber switches to a loop. Metolazone is the exception worth knowing — it keeps working at a low GFR, which is why it is deliberately added to a loop diuretic when a patient stops responding to the loop alone. That combination is potent enough to be dangerous: expect a brisk diuresis and watch potassium and volume status closely.

Teach patients the metabolic mischief: thiazides raise glucose, uric acid (watch for gout flares), lipids, and calcium, while lowering potassium, sodium, and magnesium. Thiazide-induced hyponatremia is a real and underappreciated cause of confusion and falls in older adults.

Potassium-sparing — the counterweight

Two mechanisms, one result. Spironolactone and eplerenone are aldosterone antagonists: they block aldosterone's receptor in the collecting duct. Amiloride and triamterene block the sodium channel directly without touching the receptor. Either way, sodium leaves and potassium stays. The diuresis is weak, so these are rarely used alone for fluid — they are used with a loop or thiazide to blunt potassium loss, and, in the case of spironolactone and eplerenone, because they independently reduce mortality in heart failure with reduced ejection fraction by blocking aldosterone-driven cardiac remodeling. Spironolactone is also standard for the ascites of cirrhosis.

The danger is hyperkalemia — and it is a lethal one. Teach patients to avoid salt substitutes, which are largely potassium chloride, and never to add a potassium supplement without being told to. Combining a potassium-sparing diuretic with an ACE inhibitor or ARB stacks the risk. Spironolactone is also non-selective at steroid receptors, so it causes gynecomastia, breast tenderness, and menstrual irregularity; eplerenone is selective and largely avoids this.

Osmotic and carbonic anhydrase inhibitors — the specialists

Mannitol is an osmotic diuretic: a sugar alcohol that is filtered but not reabsorbed, so it drags water with it osmotically all the way down the tubule. Its real job is usually not the urine — it is pulling water out of swollen tissue. Mannitol is used for increased intracranial pressure, cerebral edema, and acutely raised intraocular pressure. Because it expands plasma volume before it diureses, it can precipitate heart failure and pulmonary edema in a vulnerable patient. Mannitol crystallizes in cold solution, so always use an in-line filter and inspect the bag; warm it if crystals are present.

Acetazolamide blocks carbonic anhydrase in the proximal tubule, dumping bicarbonate. Weak as a diuretic, but useful in glaucoma, altitude sickness, and to alkalinize urine. It predictably causes a metabolic acidosis — which is sometimes the point.

The scale is the most honest monitor you have. One liter of retained fluid weighs about one kilogram (2.2 lb). Daily weight — same scale, same time, same clothing, after voiding — detects fluid shifts long before crackles or edema appear. Teach heart-failure patients to call for a gain of 2–3 lb in a day or 5 lb in a week. That one habit prevents readmissions.
Interactive Tap a nephron site to compare the four diuretic classes — loop, thiazide, potassium-sparing, and osmotic — by site of action, potency, and effect on serum potassium. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Loops (furosemide) are the most potent, work in renal failure, and are ototoxic if pushed fast.
  • Thiazides (hydrochlorothiazide) are first-line for hypertension, spare calcium, and fail below a GFR of ~30.
  • Potassium-sparing agents cause hyperkalemia; spironolactone adds a survival benefit in heart failure and causes gynecomastia.
  • Mannitol treats increased ICP, needs an in-line filter, and can transiently worsen heart failure.
  • Loops and thiazides drop potassium; potassium-sparing agents raise it — and they are frequently paired for exactly that reason.

Section 3IV fluids

Tonicity is a comparison, not a property

Tonicity describes how a solution's solute concentration compares to that inside the patient's cells. Normal serum osmolality is roughly 275–295 mOsm/kg, and that is the reference point for everything below. Water always moves toward the higher concentration of solute, so the tonicity of the bag decides which way water travels once it is in the vein. That is the whole lesson. An isotonic fluid matches serum, so no net water movement occurs and the fluid simply stays where you put it — expanding the extracellular space. A hypotonic fluid is more dilute than serum, so water leaves the vessel and moves into the cells, swelling them. A hypertonic fluid is more concentrated, so it pulls water out of the cells and into the vessel.

Remember it physically: isotonic stays, hypotonic enters the cell, hypertonic exits the cell. Now attach the danger to each. Too much isotonic fluid overloads the circulation → pulmonary edema. Too much hypotonic fluid swells cells → cerebral edema. Too much hypertonic fluid drags water into an already-full vasculature → fluid overload and shriveled cells.

The bags you will actually hang

0.9% sodium chloride (normal saline) and lactated Ringer's (LR) are the isotonic workhorses: resuscitation, hypovolemia, hemorrhage, dehydration, surgery, and a keep-vein-open line. Normal saline is the only fluid compatible with blood products. Large volumes of it, though, produce a hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis, because you are pouring in far more chloride than plasma normally carries. Lactated Ringer's is more physiologic — it carries potassium, calcium, and lactate that the liver converts to bicarbonate — but avoid it in liver failure (lactate is not cleared) and in significant hyperkalemia, and never run it in the same line as blood, because its calcium can trigger clotting in the tubing. D5W is a special case: isotonic in the bag, but the dextrose is metabolized within minutes and what remains is free water, so it behaves as hypotonic once infused. Do not use it for resuscitation, and do not use it in a patient at risk of cerebral edema.

0.45% sodium chloride (half-normal saline) is the everyday hypotonic fluid, used for cellular dehydration and as maintenance in a patient with hypernatremia or diabetic ketoacidosis after initial resuscitation. Hypotonic fluids are contraindicated in head injury, stroke, and any raised ICP — the last thing a swollen brain needs is more water inside its cells. 3% sodium chloride is the hypertonic fluid you will see in an ICU for symptomatic hyponatremia or cerebral edema; it is a high-alert drug, usually needs a central line and a pump, and demands frequent sodium checks. Correcting sodium too fast is its own catastrophe — generally not more than 8–10 mEq/L in 24 hours — because rapid correction of chronic hyponatremia can cause osmotic demyelination syndrome, a devastating and irreversible brain injury. D5½NS and D5LR are hypertonic maintenance fluids that provide some calories.

FluidTonicityWhere the water goes / when to useNursing alert
0.9% NaCl (normal saline)IsotonicStays extracellular; resuscitation, hypovolemia, blood administration, KVOOnly fluid compatible with blood products; large volumes → hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis; overload risk in HF/renal disease
Lactated Ringer'sIsotonicStays extracellular; surgery, burns, trauma, GI losses; lactate → bicarbonateAvoid in liver failure and hyperkalemia; never with blood (calcium clots the line)
D5WIsotonic in the bag, hypotonic in the bodyDextrose burns off → free water; free-water replacement, drug diluent, hypernatremiaNot for resuscitation; contraindicated in raised ICP; monitor glucose
0.45% NaCl (½NS)HypotonicWater enters cells; cellular dehydration, hypernatremia, DKA maintenanceCerebral edema risk — contraindicated in head injury/stroke/raised ICP; can cause hypotension & vascular collapse
3% NaClHypertonicPulls water out of cells; symptomatic hyponatremia, cerebral edemaHigh-alert; pump + frequent Na⁺; correct ≤8–10 mEq/L per 24 h to avoid osmotic demyelination; watch for overload
D5½NS / D5LR / D5NSHypertonicMaintenance with calories; post-op fluidFluid overload; monitor glucose; not for a patient already fluid-overloaded
Assess the lungs before you blame the IV pump. The earliest signs of fluid overload are the ones you gather with your own senses: new crackles at the bases, a bounding pulse, jugular vein distension, a cough that turns frothy, and a rising respiratory rate. If you see them, slow the infusion, sit the patient upright, apply oxygen, and notify the prescriber. Do not wait for the chest film to confirm what your stethoscope already told you.
Interactive Switch between the three IV-fluid tonicities and watch a red blood cell stay the same, swell, or shrink — with example fluids, clinical uses, and the nurse's monitoring for each. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Isotonic stays, hypotonic enters the cell, hypertonic exits the cell — every fluid decision starts here.
  • Normal saline is the only fluid to hang with blood; lactated Ringer's is avoided in liver failure and never runs with blood.
  • D5W is isotonic in the bag but functionally hypotonic in the patient — never for resuscitation, never in raised ICP.
  • Hypotonic fluids risk cerebral edema; hypertonic fluids risk fluid overload and, if sodium is corrected faster than ~8–10 mEq/L/day, osmotic demyelination.

Section 4Electrolyte replacement

Potassium — the one that will kill you fastest

Normal serum potassium is 3.5–5.0 mEq/L, and that narrow window is not an accident: almost all the body's potassium lives inside cells, so the small amount in serum is a razor-thin margin between a beating heart and a stopped one. Both directions are dangerous. Hypokalemia flattens T waves, produces U waves, and invites ventricular dysrhythmias — and it dramatically potentiates digoxin toxicity, so a patient on both a loop diuretic and digoxin deserves a hard look at every potassium result. Hyperkalemia gives peaked T waves, a widening QRS, and eventually cardiac arrest.

The replacement rules exist because people have died from breaking them, and they are worth memorizing exactly:

  • Never give potassium IV push. Never. Not ever. IV push potassium causes fatal cardiac arrest. It is always diluted and always infused.
  • Always on a pump, never by gravity, and never as a bolus.
  • Peripheral infusion generally does not exceed 10 mEq/hour; higher rates require central access and continuous cardiac monitoring per institutional policy.
  • Never exceed the facility's maximum concentration — commonly 10 mEq per 100 mL peripherally.
  • Verify urine output (a common threshold is at least 30 mL/hour) before infusing — potassium is renally excreted, and giving it to an anuric patient stacks it up.
  • Potassium burns. Phlebitis and infiltration are common; assess the site frequently and stop for pain.
  • Oral potassium irritates the GI tract — give with food and a full glass of water, and teach that extended-release tablets are not crushed.

For hyperkalemia, the order of operations matters. IV calcium gluconate comes first — it does not lower potassium at all, it stabilizes the cardiac membrane so the heart survives the next few minutes. Then insulin with dextrose (and sometimes albuterol or sodium bicarbonate) shifts potassium into cells temporarily. Only then do you actually remove it, with a binder such as sodium zirconium cyclosilicate or patiromer, a loop diuretic, or dialysis. Stabilize, shift, eliminate.

Calcium — the muscle and the vein

Normal total serum calcium is roughly 8.5–10.5 mg/dL, and remember that about half of it is bound to albumin — a low albumin makes total calcium look falsely low, so check an ionized calcium when it matters. Hypocalcemia produces the classic neuromuscular irritability: tingling around the mouth and fingertips, muscle cramps, Trousseau's sign (carpal spasm when the blood-pressure cuff inflates) and Chvostek's sign (facial twitch when the cheek is tapped), and, at the extreme, laryngospasm and tetany. Hypercalcemia does the opposite — the muscles and mind go slack: weakness, lethargy, constipation, kidney stones, confusion.

Two IV forms exist and they are not interchangeable. Calcium chloride carries about three times the elemental calcium of calcium gluconate and is far more damaging if it extravasates, so gluconate is preferred peripherally and chloride is generally reserved for central access in an emergency. Either way, push slowly — a fast push causes bradycardia and hypotension — and keep the patient on a cardiac monitor. Do not mix calcium with sodium bicarbonate or phosphate, which precipitate in the line. Calcium also potentiates digoxin and can precipitate toxicity. Teach oral calcium with vitamin D for absorption, spaced away from certain antibiotics it chelates.

Magnesium — check the reflexes

Normal magnesium runs about 1.5–2.5 mEq/L. It is quietly essential: magnesium is a cofactor for hundreds of enzymes, and — critically for the bedside — you cannot correct a potassium until you have corrected the magnesium. Refractory hypokalemia is almost always undiagnosed hypomagnesemia, because low magnesium drives renal potassium wasting no matter how much potassium you give. Hypomagnesemia causes hyperreflexia, tremor, positive Chvostek and Trousseau signs, and torsades de pointes — for which IV magnesium is itself the treatment. It is common in chronic alcohol use, malnutrition, and loop diuretic therapy.

Hypermagnesemia is the mirror image and is the risk you manage during any magnesium infusion. Watch the sequence: loss of deep tendon reflexes comes first, then respiratory depression, then hypotension and bradycardia, then cardiac arrest. That is why the reflex check is not busywork — a diminished patellar reflex is the early warning that buys you time. During IV magnesium (for eclampsia, for example) monitor reflexes, respiratory rate, blood pressure, and urine output, and keep calcium gluconate at the bedside as the antidote.

ElectrolyteNormal rangeDeficiency & excessNursing alert
Potassium3.5–5.0 mEq/LLow: flat T waves, U waves, dysrhythmias, digoxin toxicity. High: peaked T waves, wide QRS, arrestNEVER IV push; dilute, pump, ≤10 mEq/h peripherally; confirm urine output; watch the site (burns); do not crush ER tablets
Calcium8.5–10.5 mg/dL totalLow: perioral tingling, cramps, Trousseau & Chvostek, tetany, laryngospasm. High: weakness, lethargy, constipation, stonesGluconate peripherally, chloride centrally; push slowly on a monitor; never mix with bicarbonate or phosphate; potentiates digoxin; check ionized Ca if albumin is low
Magnesium1.5–2.5 mEq/LLow: hyperreflexia, tremor, torsades, refractory hypokalemia. High: reflexes lost → respiratory depression → arrestCheck deep tendon reflexes — first sign of toxicity; monitor RR, BP, urine output; calcium gluconate is the antidote; fix Mg before you can fix K⁺
Potassium chloride is a high-alert medication for one reason: people die when it is given wrong. Concentrated potassium is not stocked on the unit for exactly that reason. If you ever find yourself holding a vial of concentrated KCl and a syringe, stop — something upstream has already gone wrong. Independent double-check, pump, dilution, monitoring. Every time.
Interactive Tap potassium, calcium, or magnesium to see how each low electrolyte is replaced and the one hard IV-safety rule, monitoring value, and nursing action for each. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Potassium is never given IV push — always diluted, always on a pump, generally ≤10 mEq/h peripherally, and only with adequate urine output.
  • Hyperkalemia is treated stabilize (calcium) → shift (insulin + dextrose) → eliminate (binders, diuretic, dialysis).
  • Calcium gluconate is safer peripherally than calcium chloride; push slowly, never with bicarbonate or phosphate.
  • Correct magnesium before potassium; during magnesium infusion, loss of deep tendon reflexes is the first warning and calcium gluconate is the antidote.

Section 5Poisoning & antidotes

The patient comes before the poison

The first instinct in a poisoning is to hunt for the antidote. Resist it. Most poisonings have no antidote at all, and the patients who survive do so because of supportive care: airway, breathing, circulation, glucose, and time. So the sequence is ABCs first, decontamination second, antidote third if one exists, and enhanced elimination last. Along the way, call Poison Control — in the United States, 1-800-222-1222 — early rather than late. They do this all day.

For decontamination, activated charcoal is the mainstay, and it works by adsorbing the poison in the gut before it is absorbed. It is most useful within about an hour of ingestion, and it is contraindicated in a patient with a depressed level of consciousness or an unprotected airway, because aspirated charcoal causes a severe pneumonitis. It also does not bind everything: the classic misses are iron, lithium, alcohols, and the strong acids and alkalis — and corrosives should never be given charcoal anyway, since it obscures endoscopy. Do not induce vomiting. Syrup of ipecac is obsolete; it causes harm and does not improve outcomes.

The antidotes worth knowing cold

Learn these as pairs, because they are the ones that show up on exams and in real emergencies. Acetaminophen overdose is the leading cause of acute liver failure in the United States, and it is deceptively quiet — the patient may look fine for a day while a toxic metabolite is destroying hepatocytes. Acetylcysteine replenishes glutathione and is dramatically effective when started early, ideally within 8–10 hours; use the Rumack-Matthew nomogram to decide after a single acute ingestion. Warn patients that the oral form smells like rotten eggs; the IV form can cause an anaphylactoid reaction.

Opioid overdose is diagnosed at the bedside by the triad of respiratory depression, pinpoint pupils, and a depressed level of consciousness. Naloxone reverses it within minutes — but its duration is shorter than most opioids, so the patient can re-sedate, and repeat doses or an infusion may be needed. Never leave a reversed patient unmonitored. In an opioid-dependent patient, naloxone precipitates acute withdrawal, which is miserable but not fatal; respiratory arrest is. Benzodiazepine overdose has an antidote too, flumazenil, but it is used cautiously and rarely: in a chronic benzodiazepine user or a mixed overdose it can precipitate intractable seizures.

The anticoagulants each have a partner. Warfarin is reversed by vitamin K (phytonadione), and, when the bleeding is life-threatening, by prothrombin complex concentrate. Heparin is reversed by protamine sulfate. Dabigatran has idarucizumab; the factor Xa inhibitors apixaban and rivaroxaban have andexanet alfa. Digoxin toxicity — nausea, visual halos or yellow-green vision, bradycardia, confusion — is treated with digoxin immune Fab, and remember that hypokalemia makes digoxin toxicity far more likely, which loops right back to Section 2. Organophosphate or cholinergic poisoning presents as the SLUDGE picture (salivation, lacrimation, urination, defecation, GI upset, emesis) and is treated with atropine plus pralidoxime. And iron, a top cause of pediatric poisoning deaths, is chelated with deferoxamine.

Poison / drugAntidoteKey toxic signsNursing alert
AcetaminophenAcetylcysteineQuiet early phase, then RUQ pain, rising AST/ALT, hepatic failureMost effective within 8–10 h; oral form smells of sulfur (dilute, use a straw); IV can cause anaphylactoid reaction; use the Rumack-Matthew nomogram
OpioidsNaloxoneRespiratory depression + pinpoint pupils + ↓LOCShorter duration than the opioid — re-sedation risk, repeat doses/infusion; precipitates withdrawal in dependence; keep monitoring
BenzodiazepinesFlumazenilSedation, ataxia, slurred speech; respiratory depression if mixedUse cautiously — may precipitate seizures in chronic users or mixed (e.g., TCA) overdose
WarfarinVitamin K (phytonadione)Bleeding, elevated INR, bruising, hematuriaOnset takes hours — PCC/FFP for life-threatening bleed; monitor INR; teach consistent vitamin K intake
HeparinProtamine sulfateBleeding, elevated aPTT, thrombocytopenia (HIT)Give slowly — rapid administration causes hypotension & anaphylaxis; only partially reverses LMWH
DigoxinDigoxin immune FabNausea, anorexia, yellow-green/halo vision, bradycardia, confusionHold for apical pulse <60; hypokalemia potentiates toxicity; narrow therapeutic range (0.5–2 ng/mL)
Organophosphates / cholinergicsAtropine + pralidoximeSLUDGE, bronchorrhea, bradycardia, miosisDecontaminate the skin — protect yourself with PPE; large repeated atropine doses may be needed
