Read the whole label, not the brand. Multi-symptom cold products stack four drugs into one caplet, and the acetaminophen hiding in them is the reason patients cross the 4 g daily ceiling without ever believing they took a single extra dose of a pain reliever — because on the box it was a cold remedy, not acetaminophen. Before you recommend anything from that aisle, add up the acetaminophen across every product the patient is taking, check the decongestant against their blood pressure, and ask whether the cough they want silenced is one they actually need.
Interactive A tap-to-explore symptom map that matches antihistamines, decongestants, and antitussives to the sneezing, congestion, and cough they each relieve, with the key nursing cautions for every class. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Antihistamines block H1 and do not treat anaphylaxis — epinephrine does; they also work best taken before exposure.
  • First-generation agents sedate and are anticholinergic (Beers in elders); second-generation agents (loratadine, cetirizine, fexofenadine) do not.
  • Topical decongestants: 3 days maximum or rebound congestion; oral pseudoephedrine raises BP and HR.
  • Benzonatate is swallowed whole, codeine is boxed-warning contraindicated under 12, and guaifenesin only works with fluids.

Section 5Eye & ear drops

Glaucoma: a plumbing problem

The front of the eye continuously makes a clear fluid, aqueous humor, which nourishes the cornea and lens and then drains out through the angle where the iris meets the cornea. Intraocular pressure (IOP) is simply the balance between how fast fluid is made and how fast it leaves. In glaucoma, drainage cannot keep up, pressure rises, and the optic nerve is slowly strangled — painlessly, from the periphery inward, which is why it is called the silent thief of sight and why patients cannot feel whether their drops are working. In open-angle glaucoma (the common, chronic form) the drain is sluggish; in angle-closure glaucoma the iris physically blocks the angle, pressure spikes, and the patient presents with a red, rock-hard, agonizing eye, a fixed mid-dilated pupil, halos, headache, and vomiting. That is an emergency — vision is lost in hours, not months.

Since IOP is production minus outflow, every drug in the class does one of two things. Prostaglandin analogs — latanoprost, bimatoprost, travoprost — increase uveoscleral outflow, are first-line, and are dosed once daily at bedtime. Warn patients about the cosmetic effects, or they will stop the drug: permanent brown iris pigmentation, darkening of the eyelid skin, and longer, thicker lashes, sometimes in only one eye. Beta blockers — timolol — reduce aqueous production. They are the hidden danger in this chapter: a drop that drains down the tear duct is absorbed systemically without ever passing the liver, and topical timolol has caused bradycardia, hypotension, and life-threatening bronchospasm. Screen for asthma, COPD, heart block, and bradycardia before it is instilled, and teach punctal occlusion. Alpha-2 agonists (brimonidine) both reduce production and improve outflow; they are contraindicated under 2 years of age and used cautiously in older children, because they can cause profound CNS depression, bradycardia, and apnea in the very young. Carbonic anhydrase inhibitors — topical dorzolamide, oral acetazolamide — reduce production; they are sulfonamides (ask about sulfa allergy), and the oral form causes paresthesias, a metallic taste, potassium loss, and metabolic acidosis. Cholinergic miotics (pilocarpine) constrict the pupil to pull the iris out of the angle — still valuable in acute angle closure — and cause dim, blurry vision and brow ache. Netarsudil, a Rho kinase inhibitor, improves outflow and commonly reddens the eye.

ClassPrototypeHow it lowers IOPNursing alert
Prostaglandin analogLatanoprostIncreases outflow (uveoscleral)First-line, once daily at bedtime; permanent iris/lid darkening, lash growth — warn or they quit
Beta blockerTimololDecreases aqueous productionSystemically absorbed: bradycardia, bronchospasm; screen asthma/COPD/heart block; punctal occlusion
Alpha-2 agonistBrimonidineDecreases production + increases outflowDry mouth, drowsiness; avoid in infants — CNS depression, apnea
Carbonic anhydrase inhibitorDorzolamide (topical), acetazolamide (oral)Decreases productionSulfa agents; oral: paresthesias, metallic taste, hypokalemia, metabolic acidosis
Cholinergic mioticPilocarpineMiosis opens the drainage angleDim/blurred vision, brow ache; fall risk at night; used in acute angle closure
Rho kinase inhibitorNetarsudilIncreases trabecular outflowConjunctival redness is common and expected

Getting the drop where it belongs

A drop that lands on a cheek is a missed dose that the chart will record as given. Teach and perform the sequence deliberately. Wash hands and check the label for "ophthalmic." Have the patient sit or lie with the head tilted back and eyes looking up. Pull the lower lid down to form a pouch — the conjunctival sac — and instill one drop into it, holding the bottle about an inch above and never touching the tip to the eye, lashes, or fingers, which contaminates the bottle. Have the patient close the eye gently (not squeeze, which pumps the drug out) and apply punctal occlusion: light pressure on the inner corner near the nose for 1–2 minutes. This one maneuver keeps the drug in the eye and out of the bloodstream, and it is the difference between timolol treating an eye and timolol dropping a heart rate. The eye only holds about one drop's worth of volume, so a second drop of the same drug is waste. When two different drops are due, wait at least 5 minutes between them or the second washes out the first. Give solutions before suspensions, and always put ointments last — ointment blurs vision (so bedtime is ideal) and seals the surface against anything that follows. Contact lenses come out first; each patient gets their own bottle, never shared.

Otic drops follow their own rules, and the first one is absolute: ophthalmic drops may be used in the ear, but otic drops must never go in the eye. Ophthalmic preparations are sterile and formulated for eye tissue; otic ones are not, and will injure a cornea. Beyond that, warm the bottle to about body temperature by rolling it in your hands — cold fluid against the tympanic membrane stimulates the vestibular apparatus and causes dizziness, nausea, and vertigo. Straighten the canal by pulling the pinna up and back for adults and children over about 3, and down and back for infants and younger children, whose canals angle differently. Instill the drops against the side of the canal so air can escape, keep the patient lying on the unaffected side for 3–5 minutes, and if a cotton ball is used, place it loosely at the opening only — never pack it, or it absorbs the dose.

The drugs themselves are a short list. Ofloxacin otic and ciprofloxacin/dexamethasone treat otitis externa and infected tubes; the steroid component calms swelling and pain. Neomycin/polymyxin B/hydrocortisone is effective but ototoxic — it is contraindicated when the tympanic membrane is perforated or tubes are present, because neomycin reaching the middle ear can damage hearing permanently. Assess for perforation, drainage, or tubes before instilling anything, and if you are unsure, stop and ask. Carbamide peroxide softens cerumen by foaming; it is not an antibiotic and must not be used on a draining or perforated ear. And note that topical anesthetic ear drops are no longer an approved option in many settings — systemic analgesia is the safer route for ear pain.

Two fingers, two minutes, one heart rate. Punctal occlusion — gentle pressure at the inner corner of the closed eye for a minute or two — is the most underused skill in this chapter. Nasolacrimal drainage sends drug straight into systemic circulation, bypassing first-pass metabolism entirely, and topical beta blockers have caused symptomatic bradycardia and severe bronchospasm in patients whose "eye drops" were never listed on the medication reconciliation. When a patient on glaucoma drops turns up with a new wheeze or a heart rate in the 40s, look at the eye drops.
Interactive Tap glaucoma drug classes to watch each cut aqueous production or boost outflow and lower eye pressure, then step through correct eye-drop instillation technique. Open full ↑
Key points
  • IOP = production minus outflow; latanoprost (first-line, bedtime) increases outflow, timolol and dorzolamide reduce production.
  • Topical timolol is absorbed systemically — bradycardia and bronchospasm are real; screen for asthma/COPD and teach punctal occlusion.
  • Eye drops go in the conjunctival sac, one drop, tip touches nothing, 5 minutes between different drops, ointments last.
  • Otic: warm the drops; pinna up and back (adult) or down and back (young child); never put otic drops in an eye; neomycin-containing drops are contraindicated with a perforated eardrum.

The chapter in one breath

  • The airway has two drug targets: bronchoconstriction (M3 constricts, beta-2 relaxes) and inflammation — which is why every patient carries two kinds of inhaler.
  • Rescue means albuterol, in minutes; controller means an inhaled corticosteroid, over weeks. Mixing them up is a fatal error, not a vocabulary slip.
  • Albuterol brings tremor, tachycardia, hypokalemia, and hyperglycemia; LABAs are never given alone in asthma; ipratropium/tiotropium block M3 and anchor COPD therapy.
  • Theophylline has almost no margin — 5–15 mcg/mL, nausea and insomnia first, then arrhythmias and seizures; smoking and ciprofloxacin move the level hard.
  • Inhaled corticosteroids demand daily use, rinse-and-spit, and a spacer; montelukast carries a neuropsychiatric boxed warning; technique and peak-flow zones (green/yellow/red) are the teaching.
  • Antihistamines do not treat anaphylaxis; first-generation agents sedate and are Beers-listed; topical decongestants get 3 days before rebound; benzonatate is swallowed whole.
  • Glaucoma drugs either cut aqueous production or open outflow; latanoprost is first-line, and topical timolol can bradycard or bronchospasm a systemic patient.
  • Technique is the drug: conjunctival sac, one drop, punctal occlusion, 5 minutes between drops, ointment last — and warm otic drops with the pinna up and back in adults, down and back in young children.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Airway hyperresponsiveness
The twitchy, easily triggered state of chronically inflamed bronchial smooth muscle; the reason controllers reduce attacks.
Aqueous humor
The clear fluid made in the anterior eye and drained at the angle; its production-versus-outflow balance sets intraocular pressure.
Beta-2 agonist
A drug that stimulates beta-2 receptors on bronchial smooth muscle to produce bronchodilation, e.g., albuterol (short-acting) or salmeterol (long-acting).
Bronchoconstriction
Narrowing of the airway by contraction of bronchial smooth muscle, mediated by acetylcholine at M3 muscarinic receptors.
Bronchodilator
Any drug that relaxes bronchial smooth muscle and widens the airway — beta-2 agonists, antimuscarinics, and methylxanthines.
Conjunctival sac
The pouch formed by pulling down the lower eyelid; the correct target for an eye drop, never the cornea itself.
Controller (maintenance) drug
A scheduled daily drug that reduces airway inflammation over days to weeks, e.g., an inhaled corticosteroid; useless in an acute attack.
Dysphonia
Hoarseness or voice change; a local adverse effect of inhaled corticosteroids, reduced by a spacer and rinse-and-spit.
Expectorant
A drug such as guaifenesin that thins respiratory secretions so a cough can clear them; requires generous fluid intake to work.
Glaucoma
Optic nerve damage associated with elevated intraocular pressure; chronic open-angle disease is painless, while acute angle-closure is a sight-threatening emergency.
Intraocular pressure (IOP)
Pressure inside the eye, determined by aqueous humor production minus outflow; the modifiable target of every glaucoma drug.
LABA
Long-acting beta-2 agonist (salmeterol, formoterol); maintenance only, and never used as monotherapy in asthma.
LAMA
Long-acting muscarinic antagonist (tiotropium, umeclidinium); a once- or twice-daily maintenance bronchodilator and the backbone of COPD therapy.
Methylxanthine
A caffeine-related bronchodilator, chiefly theophylline; narrow therapeutic index with a target level of about 5–15 mcg/mL.
Mucolytic
A drug such as acetylcysteine that chemically breaks up thick mucus by cleaving disulfide bonds.
Ototoxicity
Drug-induced injury to hearing or balance; the reason neomycin-containing otic drops are contraindicated with a perforated eardrum.
Peak expiratory flow
The fastest speed a patient can blow out, measured against personal best: green 80–100%, yellow 50–79%, red below 50%.
Punctal occlusion
Gentle pressure on the inner corner of the closed eye for 1–2 minutes after a drop, keeping drug in the eye and out of systemic circulation.
Rebound congestion (rhinitis medicamentosa)
Worsening nasal congestion caused by topical decongestant use beyond about 3 days.
Rescue (reliever) drug
A fast-acting bronchodilator, prototypically albuterol, used for acute symptoms; it treats the spasm, not the inflammation.
SABA
Short-acting beta-2 agonist (albuterol); onset about 5 minutes, duration 4–6 hours — the rescue inhaler.
Spacer
A holding chamber attached to a metered-dose inhaler that removes the need for perfect timing, increases lung delivery, and reduces oropharyngeal steroid deposition.

Check yourself

1. Name the two distinct drug targets in asthma and match each to the kind of drug that treats it. Why does confusing them endanger patients?
Bronchoconstriction → a rescue bronchodilator (albuterol); airway inflammation → a controller (inhaled corticosteroid). A patient who reaches for the steroid inhaler during an attack gets no relief while the airway keeps closing, and a patient who feels well on albuterol alone may abandon the controller — leaving the inflammation that makes the airway twitchy in the first place completely untreated.
2. A patient in status asthmaticus has had continuous nebulized albuterol for two hours. Which two lab values should you be watching, and why?
Potassium (expect hypokalemia) and glucose (expect hyperglycemia). Beta-2 stimulation drives potassium into cells — the same effect that makes albuterol a useful adjunct in acute hyperkalemia — so watch the potassium and the cardiac monitor. Tremor and tachycardia are expected, not allergic.
3. A prescriber writes salmeterol alone for a newly diagnosed asthma patient. What do you do, and why?
Hold and clarify — a LABA must never be used as monotherapy in asthma. LABA monotherapy was associated with increased asthma-related deaths, so in asthma a LABA is always paired with an inhaled corticosteroid (usually in a single combination inhaler). Salmeterol is also slow in onset, so it is worthless as a rescue drug. (LABA monotherapy is acceptable in COPD.)
4. A COPD patient on theophylline is admitted and, per hospital policy, stops smoking. Three days later he is nauseated, sleepless, and tachycardic. Explain.
Cigarette smoke induces CYP1A2; stopping removes the induction, theophylline clearance falls, and the level climbs into the toxic range on an unchanged dose. Nausea, vomiting, insomnia, and tachycardia are early toxicity — check a level (target ~5–15 mcg/mL) before dysrhythmias or seizures appear. Ciprofloxacin and cimetidine raise levels the same way.
5. A patient using ipratropium by face mask complains of blurred vision and eye pain. What happened, and who is at particular risk?
Anticholinergic mist escaped into the eyes, blocking muscarinic receptors there — causing pupil dilation and blurred vision, and risking an acute attack in narrow-angle glaucoma. Use a mouthpiece when possible, fit the mask well, and keep the mist out of the eyes. Also counsel about dry mouth and, in men with BPH, urinary retention.
6. Your patient says the fluticasone inhaler "doesn't do anything" during an attack, so she stopped it. What do you teach?
She is right that it does nothing in an attack — and that is exactly why she must keep taking it every day. An ICS works by suppressing inflammation at the gene level: days to begin, one to two weeks for full effect, and no acute bronchodilation at all. It is taken daily especially when she feels well, because feeling well is the drug working. The albuterol is for attacks.
7. Why must a patient rinse and spit after an inhaled corticosteroid, and what two problems does it prevent?
Steroid deposited in the mouth suppresses local immunity and irritates the larynx — rinsing prevents oral candidiasis (thrush) and dysphonia (hoarseness). Spit rather than swallow so the leftover drug is not absorbed, and add a spacer, which cuts oropharyngeal deposition substantially.
8. A parent reports that her 9-year-old on montelukast has become agitated, is having nightmares, and seems depressed. What is your concern and action?
Montelukast carries a boxed warning for serious neuropsychiatric events — agitation, depression, sleep disturbance, and suicidal thinking. Report it promptly, assess for safety including suicidal ideation, and expect the prescriber to reconsider the drug. Ask about mood and sleep at every visit for anyone on montelukast, child or adult.
9. Contrast metered-dose inhaler and dry powder inhaler technique in one sentence each, and give the MDI-with-spacer sequence.
MDI: a slow, deep breath timed with the actuation. DPI: a fast, forceful breath, and never exhale into the device (humidity clumps the powder). MDI sequence: shake, breathe all the way out, seal lips on the spacer, press as you begin a slow deep inhalation, hold about 10 seconds, wait about a minute between puffs. If two inhalers are due, bronchodilator first, steroid second.
10. A 78-year-old asks whether diphenhydramine is a good nightly sleep aid. Respond with rationale, and name a better antihistamine choice for her daytime allergies.
No — diphenhydramine is on the Beers criteria as potentially inappropriate in older adults. It is strongly anticholinergic and CNS-penetrating, contributing to confusion, dry mouth, blurred vision, urinary retention, constipation, and falls. For daytime allergies, a second-generation agent — loratadine, cetirizine, or fexofenadine — gives H1 blockade with minimal sedation. (Remind her: an antihistamine never treats anaphylaxis; epinephrine does.)
11. A patient has used oxymetazoline nasal spray daily for three weeks and says he cannot breathe without it. What is happening, and what is the rule?
Rebound congestion (rhinitis medicamentosa) from topical decongestant overuse — the rule is a maximum of 3 days. Beyond that, stopping produces congestion worse than the original, trapping the patient in a cycle of using the spray to treat the spray. The durable fix for allergic rhinitis is an intranasal corticosteroid used daily — aimed away from the septum to avoid epistaxis.
12. Your patient with COPD is newly prescribed timolol eye drops for glaucoma. State your concern, the teaching that reduces it, and the correct sequence for instilling a drop.
Topical timolol is absorbed systemically through the nasolacrimal duct and can cause bronchospasm, bradycardia, and hypotension — a real hazard in COPD or asthma; clarify the order. Teach punctal occlusion: press the inner corner of the closed eye for 1–2 minutes after the drop. Sequence: wash hands, verify "ophthalmic," tilt head back with eyes up, pull the lower lid down to form the conjunctival sac, instill one drop without touching the tip to anything, close the eye gently, occlude the punctum — and wait at least 5 minutes before a different drop, with ointments always last.
Pharmacology · Chapter 13

Gastrointestinal Drugs

The gut is the one organ system your patients will tell you about without being asked. Heartburn, nausea, constipation, diarrhea — these complaints are so ordinary that it is easy to treat them casually, and that is exactly where nurses get into trouble. A proton pump inhibitor given after breakfast does almost nothing. An antidiarrheal given to the wrong patient can turn a survivable infection into toxic megacolon. An antiemetic pushed too fast into a small vein can cost a patient their hand. This chapter walks the gastrointestinal tract from the stomach to the colon, drug class by drug class, and keeps returning to the two questions that matter most at the bedside: when do I give this, and what am I watching for afterward.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain how the parietal cell makes acid and where PPIs, H2 blockers, and antacids each interrupt it.
  • Time acid-suppressing and mucosal-protective drugs correctly, and name the long-term risks of chronic PPI therapy.
  • Describe current Helicobacter pylori eradication regimens and the teaching that makes them succeed.
  • Map the four inputs to the vomiting center and match each antiemetic class to the receptor it blocks.
  • Compare laxative classes by mechanism and onset, and state when a laxative must not be given.
  • Identify the patients in whom antidiarrheals are contraindicated, and explain why.
  • Outline drug therapy for inflammatory bowel disease, including the screening required before a biologic.
  • Prioritize nursing monitoring, teaching, and safety alerts for the major GI drug classes.

Section 1Acid and ulcer drugs

Why the stomach does not digest itself

The stomach lining is a standoff between two forces. On the attack: hydrochloric acid, pumped into the lumen by the parietal cell through an enzyme called the H+/K+-ATPase — the proton pump. Three signals turn that pump on: gastrin from G cells, histamine acting on H2 receptors, and acetylcholine from the vagus nerve acting on M3 receptors. Histamine is the amplifier; block it and the other two lose most of their punch.

On the defense: a layer of mucus, a cushion of bicarbonate underneath it, brisk mucosal blood flow, and prostaglandins that maintain all three. Damage happens when defense fails rather than when acid rises — which is why the two great causes of ulcers are Helicobacter pylori (which burrows into the mucus layer) and NSAIDs (which shut off the prostaglandins that build it). Understanding this explains an otherwise confusing fact: acid suppression heals ulcers, but it does not cure the cause.

Proton pump inhibitors: the strongest tool, and the most misused

Proton pump inhibitors (PPIs) — omeprazole, pantoprazole, esomeprazole, lansoprazole, rabeprazole, dexlansoprazole — shut down the pump itself, and they do it irreversibly. The cell must build new pumps to recover, which is why a once-daily dose suppresses acid for roughly 24 hours even though the drug clears from the blood in a couple of hours. They are the drugs of choice for erosive esophagitis, peptic ulcer disease, H. pylori regimens, NSAID ulcer prevention, and stress-ulcer prophylaxis in the ICU.

Timing is the single most important thing a nurse controls here. A PPI is a prodrug that only activates inside a pump that is actively secreting. If you give it to a fasting patient with dormant pumps, most of the dose is wasted. Give it 30–60 minutes before the first meal of the day — and if a patient tells you they take their omeprazole at bedtime with a snack, you have just found the reason their reflux never got better.

The long-term picture deserves respect. Chronic PPI use is associated with hypomagnesemia, vitamin B12 deficiency, iron malabsorption, increased Clostridioides difficile infection, community-acquired pneumonia, acute interstitial nephritis, and fracture of the hip, wrist, and spine. Stopping abruptly after months of therapy causes rebound acid hypersecretion — symptoms flare, the patient concludes they need the drug forever, and the cycle locks in. Taper instead. One interaction is worth memorizing: omeprazole and esomeprazole inhibit CYP2C19, the enzyme that activates clopidogrel, blunting its antiplatelet effect; pantoprazole is the preferred PPI in a patient on clopidogrel.

H2 blockers, antacids, and mucosal protectants

H2-receptor antagonists — famotidine, cimetidine, nizatidine — block histamine at the parietal cell. They work faster than PPIs but suppress less acid, and tolerance develops within days to weeks, so they are best for intermittent or nocturnal symptoms rather than as a long-term substitute for a PPI. (Ranitidine was withdrawn from the market in 2020 after NDMA contamination was found; do not expect to see it.) Famotidine is renally cleared — in kidney impairment or in an older adult, an unadjusted dose causes confusion, agitation, and delirium. Cimetidine is the outlier: a potent CYP450 inhibitor that raises levels of warfarin, phenytoin, and theophylline, and an antiandrogen that causes gynecomastia and impotence. It is rarely the right choice.

Antacids do not touch the pump — they chemically neutralize acid already present. Onset is minutes, duration is an hour or two, and the choice of cation determines the side effect: magnesium hydroxide causes diarrhea, aluminum hydroxide causes constipation (combination products pair them deliberately), calcium carbonate constipates and can cause milk-alkali syndrome, and sodium bicarbonate delivers a sodium load and systemic alkalosis. In renal failure, magnesium and aluminum accumulate. Antacids also bind other drugs — levothyroxine, tetracyclines, fluoroquinolones, iron — so separate them by 1–2 hours.

Sucralfate is a paste, not a blocker: in acid it polymerizes and sticks to the ulcer crater like a bandage. It needs acid to work and it binds anything nearby, so give it on an empty stomach, 1 hour before meals and at bedtime, and hold other oral drugs for 2 hours. Misoprostol, a prostaglandin E1 analog, replaces what NSAIDs removed and prevents NSAID ulcers — but it stimulates uterine contractions and is contraindicated in pregnancy. Confirm pregnancy status and teach reliable contraception before dispensing it.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
Proton pump inhibitorPantoprazole, omeprazoleIrreversibly blocks the H+/K+-ATPase; strongest acid suppression30–60 min before the first meal. Do not crush delayed-release forms. Watch Mg2+, B12, C. diff. Use pantoprazole with clopidogrel.
H2 blockerFamotidineBlocks histamine H2 on the parietal cell; faster onset, weaker effectReduce dose in renal impairment — confusion and delirium in older adults. Tolerance develops.
AntacidAluminum/magnesium hydroxideNeutralizes existing acid; onset in minutesSeparate from other oral drugs by 1–2 h. Mag = diarrhea, Al = constipation; both accumulate in renal failure.
Mucosal protectantSucralfateForms a physical barrier over the ulcer bedEmpty stomach, 1 h before meals and at bedtime; separate other drugs by 2 h. Contains aluminum.
Prostaglandin analogMisoprostolRestores mucus and bicarbonate; prevents NSAID ulcersContraindicated in pregnancy — abortifacient. Diarrhea and cramping are common.
Bismuth compoundBismuth subsalicylateCoats mucosa, mildly antibacterial against H. pyloriHarmless black stool and black tongue — warn patients. It is a salicylate: avoid in children (Reye syndrome) and in aspirin allergy.

Eradicating H. pylori

H. pylori causes most duodenal ulcers and a large share of gastric ulcers, and it is a recognized risk factor for gastric cancer — so eradication is not optional once it is found. Rising clarithromycin resistance has changed the recipe. Bismuth quadruple therapy — a PPI plus bismuth, tetracycline, and metronidazole for 10–14 days — is now the preferred first-line regimen in most settings, along with rifabutin triple therapy (omeprazole, amoxicillin, rifabutin). The older clarithromycin triple therapy (PPI, amoxicillin, clarithromycin) is reserved for regions with low resistance and patients with no prior macrolide exposure.

These regimens fail mostly for one reason: the patient stops taking them. Four drugs, several times a day, for two weeks, with a metallic taste and dark stools, in someone who feels better by day four. Your teaching is the intervention. Emphasize completing every dose, warn that metronidazole with alcohol causes a disulfiram-like reaction (flushing, vomiting, palpitations), that bismuth blackens the tongue and stool harmlessly, and that a follow-up breath or stool antigen test confirms cure at least 4 weeks after the antibiotics and bismuth finish — with the PPI held for 1–2 weeks before the test, because acid suppression suppresses the organism too and produces a false negative.

The single highest-yield thing you will do with a PPI is move it. Half an hour before breakfast, not with it and not at bedtime. It costs nothing, changes no order, and can be the difference between a drug that works and a drug that does not.
Interactive Tap the acid-making parietal cell to see exactly where proton pump inhibitors, H2 blockers, and antacids each act to lower stomach acid — with the key nursing focus and cautions for every class. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The parietal cell proton pump is the final common path; PPIs block it irreversibly, H2 blockers block its histamine trigger, antacids neutralize acid downstream.
  • PPIs are prodrugs activated by active pumps — give 30–60 minutes before the first meal, and taper rather than stop abruptly.
  • Chronic PPI risks: hypomagnesemia, B12 deficiency, C. difficile, pneumonia, interstitial nephritis, fractures; use pantoprazole with clopidogrel.
  • Bismuth quadruple therapy is first-line for H. pylori; adherence teaching — and the alcohol–metronidazole warning — is the nursing job.

Section 2Antiemetics

Four roads to the vomiting center

Vomiting is a reflex, coordinated by a network in the medulla usually called the vomiting center. It has four separate inputs, and every antiemetic class works by cutting one of those roads. Match the drug to the road and you will almost never pick the wrong agent.

The first road is the chemoreceptor trigger zone (CTZ), sitting in the area postrema on the floor of the fourth ventricle. It lies outside the blood–brain barrier, which makes it a chemical sensor for the bloodstream — it detects opioids, chemotherapy, anesthetics, uremia, and toxins. It is dense in dopamine D2, serotonin 5-HT3, and neurokinin-1 (NK1) receptors. The second road is the gut itself: chemotherapy and radiation damage enterochromaffin cells, which dump serotonin onto 5-HT3 receptors on vagal afferents — a signal that travels from the small bowel to the medulla in minutes. The third road is the vestibular apparatus, rich in histamine H1 and muscarinic receptors, which is why motion sickness responds to antihistamines and scopolamine but not to ondansetron. The fourth road is the cerebral cortex — anxiety, smells, memory — the source of anticipatory nausea, which is why a benzodiazepine is sometimes the right antiemetic.

The classes

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
5-HT3 antagonistOndansetronBlocks serotonin at vagal afferents and CTZ; first-line for chemo and post-op nauseaQT prolongation — check ECG and K+/Mg2+; push IV slowly. Headache and constipation are common. Serotonin syndrome risk with other serotonergic drugs.
NK1 antagonistAprepitantBlocks substance P; covers delayed chemo-induced nauseaCYP3A4 interactions — reduces oral contraceptive efficacy (backup method for 1 month), alters warfarin and dexamethasone dosing.
Dopamine D2 antagonistMetoclopramide, prochlorperazine; promethazine (phenothiazine, mainly H1)Blocks D2 in the CTZ; metoclopramide also speeds gastric emptying. Promethazine's antiemetic effect is largely H1 and antimuscarinic.Extrapyramidal symptoms and, for metoclopramide, a boxed warning for tardive dyskinesia — limit to 12 weeks. Promethazine carries a boxed warning for severe tissue injury and gangrene on extravasation.
Antihistamine (H1)Meclizine, dimenhydrinateBlocks the vestibular road; motion sickness and vertigoSedation, dry mouth, urinary retention; confusion and falls in older adults (Beers criteria). Useless for chemo-induced nausea.
AnticholinergicScopolamine (transdermal)Blocks muscarinic vestibular input; motion sickness prophylaxisApply behind the ear 4 h before travel, change every 72 h, wash hands afterward — drug on the fingers reaches the eye and dilates the pupil.
CorticosteroidDexamethasoneAdjunct that boosts every other antiemetic in chemo regimensHyperglycemia, insomnia, mood change; check glucose in diabetic patients.
CannabinoidDronabinolRefractory chemo-induced nausea; also stimulates appetiteDysphoria, dizziness, sedation — poorly tolerated by older adults. Schedule III.
BenzodiazepineLorazepamBlunts anticipatory nausea driven by the cortexSedation and fall risk; not an antiemetic on its own.

Two special cases are worth knowing cold. In pregnancy, first-line pharmacologic therapy for nausea and vomiting is doxylamine plus pyridoxine (vitamin B6) — among the best-studied drugs in pregnancy — with ondansetron and metoclopramide as later options. And promethazine should be treated with the caution reserved for a high-alert drug: it is contraindicated in children under 2 years because of fatal respiratory depression, and if given IV it must be well diluted and infused slowly into a large, patent vein, because perivascular leak causes tissue necrosis severe enough to require amputation. Deep IM into a large muscle is safer.

What a nurse actually does

Prevention beats rescue every time. Chemotherapy antiemetics are given before the drug that causes the nausea, not after the patient is retching, and the regimen is matched to the emetogenic risk of the chemotherapy — commonly a 5-HT3 antagonist plus dexamethasone plus an NK1 antagonist for highly emetogenic protocols. Beyond dosing, assess what vomiting is doing to the body: loss of gastric contents means loss of hydrogen, chloride, and potassium, producing hypokalemic hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis and dehydration. Track intake and output, daily weight, skin turgor, orthostatic vital signs, and electrolytes. Protect the airway in anyone sedated or with reduced consciousness. And keep asking why — nausea is a symptom, and bowel obstruction, raised intracranial pressure, myocardial infarction, hypercalcemia, and diabetic ketoacidosis all present with vomiting that no antiemetic will fix.

Never push promethazine into a small hand or wrist vein. If a patient reports burning or pain during the infusion, stop immediately — that is the earliest sign of the tissue injury the boxed warning describes, and by the time you can see it, the damage is done.
Interactive Tap each trigger of the emetic reflex to see the receptor it uses and the antiemetic class that blocks it, with the nursing focus for every drug. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Four inputs drive vomiting: the CTZ (D2, 5-HT3, NK1), vagal gut afferents (5-HT3), the vestibular system (H1, muscarinic), and the cortex.
  • Ondansetron is first-line for chemo and post-op nausea — watch QT, potassium, and magnesium; it does nothing for motion sickness.
  • Metoclopramide carries a boxed warning for tardive dyskinesia (limit to 12 weeks); promethazine carries one for gangrene on extravasation.
  • Vomiting causes hypokalemic hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis — treat the fluid and electrolytes, and always look for the underlying cause.

Section 3Laxatives and antidiarrheals

Laxatives: five ways to move stool

Stool consistency comes down to water and transit time. Every laxative either adds water, holds water, or hurries things along before water can be reabsorbed.

Bulk-forming agents — psyllium and methylcellulose (soluble fibers) and calcium polycarbophil (a synthetic water-holding polymer) — absorb water, swell, and stretch the bowel wall, which triggers peristalsis. They are among the safest classes and are appropriate for indefinite daily use, but they take 12–72 hours and they carry one real hazard: taken without enough fluid, they can swell into an esophageal or intestinal obstruction. Teach a full glass of water with every dose, and never give one to a patient who cannot swallow reliably.

Osmotic agents pull water into the lumen. Polyethylene glycol 3350 (PEG) is the workhorse for chronic constipation — well tolerated, non-absorbed, no cramping, and safe long-term. Lactulose is a non-absorbable sugar with a second job: in hepatic encephalopathy, colonic bacteria ferment it to acid, which traps ammonia as ammonium so it is excreted in stool. In that setting the dose is titrated to 2–3 soft stools per day, and a patient who is not stooling is a patient whose ammonia is climbing. Magnesium salts work fast but accumulate dangerously in renal impairment, and sodium phosphate preparations can cause severe electrolyte shifts and acute phosphate nephropathy — avoid them in older adults and anyone with kidney or heart disease.

Stimulant laxatives — senna, bisacodyl — irritate the enteric nerves directly, producing a bowel movement in 6–12 hours orally or 15–60 minutes by suppository. They cramp, and long-term use is associated with melanosis coli. Enteric-coated bisacodyl must not be crushed, and must not be taken within an hour of milk or antacids — raising gastric pH dissolves the coating early and causes gastric irritation. Docusate, a stool softener, is the most-ordered and least-effective laxative in the hospital; the evidence for it as monotherapy is weak, and its real role is paired with senna in opioid-induced constipation. Mineral oil lubricates, but risks lipoid pneumonia if aspirated and blocks absorption of fat-soluble vitamins.

Two newer groups matter. Secretagogues — lubiprostone (a chloride channel activator) and linaclotide/plecanatide (guanylate cyclase-C agonists) — make the intestine secrete fluid; linaclotide and plecanatide carry a boxed warning against use in children under 6 because of fatal dehydration in young animals. And PAMORAs — peripherally acting mu-opioid receptor antagonists such as methylnaltrexone, naloxegol, and naldemedine — are an elegant idea: they block the opioid receptors in the gut that cause constipation while staying out of the brain, so the patient keeps their pain control and gets their bowels back.

ClassPrototypeKey effect / onsetNursing alert
Bulk-formingPsylliumAbsorbs water, adds bulk; 12–72 hFull glass of water with every dose — obstruction risk without it. Safest for long-term use.
OsmoticPolyethylene glycol 3350Draws water into the lumen; 1–3 daysFirst-line for chronic constipation. Lactulose is titrated to 2–3 soft stools/day in hepatic encephalopathy.
Saline osmoticMagnesium hydroxide/citrateRapid osmotic pull; 30 min–6 hAvoid in renal impairment — magnesium toxicity. Sodium phosphate risks phosphate nephropathy.
StimulantSenna, bisacodylStimulates enteric nerves; 6–12 h oralCramping; do not crush enteric-coated bisacodyl or give within 1 h of milk/antacids.
Surfactant (softener)Docusate sodiumLets water enter stool; 1–3 daysWeak as monotherapy; use paired with senna for opioid-induced constipation.
SecretagogueLubiprostone, linaclotideTriggers intestinal fluid secretionNausea (take lubiprostone with food). Linaclotide/plecanatide: boxed warning, contraindicated under age 6.
PAMORAMethylnaltrexone, naloxegolBlocks gut opioid receptors without reversing analgesiaReserved for opioid-induced constipation; contraindicated in known or suspected bowel obstruction (perforation risk).

One rule outranks all of the above: never give a laxative to a patient with undiagnosed abdominal pain, suspected bowel obstruction, or possible appendicitis. Stimulating peristalsis against a blockage or an inflamed appendix invites perforation. Rigid abdomen, absent bowel sounds, vomiting, no flatus — that patient needs an assessment, not a dose.

Antidiarrheals: slowing down, carefully

Loperamide is a mu-opioid agonist that acts on the gut and barely enters the brain at ordinary doses, which is why it is available over the counter. It is not harmless. Taken in the massive doses seen in opioid misuse, loperamide blocks cardiac sodium and potassium channels and causes QT prolongation, torsades de pointes, and cardiac arrest — the reason for an FDA warning and packaging limits. Reinforce the ceiling: 8 mg/day over the counter, 16 mg/day by prescription.

Diphenoxylate with atropine is a Schedule V opioid; the subtherapeutic atropine is there purely to make overdose unpleasant, so anticholinergic symptoms are the tell that someone took too much. Bismuth subsalicylate is mild and useful for traveler's diarrhea, with the salicylate cautions that come with it. Octreotide handles severe secretory diarrhea from carcinoid syndrome, VIPoma, or refractory chemotherapy-related diarrhea. Cholestyramine treats bile-acid diarrhea. And for most acute infectious diarrhea, the actual treatment is oral rehydration solution — water plus sodium plus glucose, exploiting the sodium–glucose cotransporter that keeps working even when the gut is inflamed.

Now the contraindication that shows up on every exam and, more importantly, at every bedside. Do not give an antimotility drug to a patient with bloody diarrhea, high fever, or suspected C. difficile. Diarrhea is how the body evacuates a toxin; stopping the motility keeps the toxin in contact with the mucosa and can precipitate toxic megacolon — a dilated, paralyzed, perforating colon. If a patient on antibiotics develops new watery diarrhea, the answer is a C. difficile test, contact precautions, soap-and-water hand hygiene (alcohol does not kill spores), and a call to the provider — not loperamide.

Count stools, don't just chart "diarrhea." Number, volume, and character — watery, bloody, greasy, black — plus potassium and creatinine tell the story of both the disease and the drug. A patient with three liters of stool output and a potassium of 2.8 has a cardiac problem, not a bowel problem.
Interactive Tap each laxative class onto an onset timeline — from minute-fast rectal rescue to days-slow bulk-forming — to see how it works and how quickly, then flip to the antidiarrheals that do the reverse. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Bulk-forming laxatives are the safest for chronic use but demand a full glass of water; PEG 3350 is first-line for chronic constipation.
  • Lactulose in hepatic encephalopathy is titrated to 2–3 soft stools per day to trap and excrete ammonia.
  • No laxative in undiagnosed abdominal pain, suspected obstruction, or possible appendicitis.
  • No antimotility agent in bloody diarrhea, fever, or C. difficile — risk of toxic megacolon. High-dose loperamide causes torsades.

Section 4IBD and other GI drugs

Inflammatory bowel disease

Inflammatory bowel disease (IBD) is chronic immune-mediated inflammation of the gut, and it comes in two shapes. Ulcerative colitis is continuous, superficial, and confined to the colon and rectum. Crohn disease is patchy and transmural and can strike anywhere from mouth to anus, which is why it causes fistulas, abscesses, and strictures. Therapy is built in two moves: induce remission with something fast, then maintain it with something safe enough for years. Steroids induce; they never maintain.

Aminosalicylates (5-ASA) are topical anti-inflammatories delivered to the bowel lumen. Mesalamine comes in oral delayed-release forms and, importantly, as enemas and suppositories — for proctitis, a suppository puts the drug exactly where the disease is with almost no systemic exposure. Sulfasalazine is a prodrug that colonic bacteria split into 5-ASA (the active half) and sulfapyridine (the half that causes most of the side effects): it is a sulfonamide, so ask about sulfa allergy; it stains urine, skin, and soft contact lenses orange-yellow; it causes reversible oligospermia; and it blocks folate absorption, so patients take folic acid alongside it. Mesalamine can be nephrotoxic — check renal function at baseline and periodically.

Corticosteroids break a flare. Budesonide is the clever one: extensive first-pass hepatic metabolism means it acts on the gut with far fewer systemic effects, making it attractive for mild-to-moderate ileal Crohn disease. Systemic prednisone or IV methylprednisolone handles bigger flares. Watch glucose, blood pressure, mood, and infection risk; taper rather than stop; and address bone protection early.

Immunomodulators hold remission. Azathioprine and 6-mercaptopurine require TPMT and NUDT15 testing before the first dose — a patient with low enzyme activity will develop profound, potentially fatal myelosuppression on a normal dose. Monitor CBC and liver enzymes, and know that pancreatitis and an increased lymphoma risk belong to this class. Methotrexate is used mainly in Crohn disease, with folic acid supplementation, weekly (never daily) dosing, and absolute contraindication in pregnancy.

Biologics changed the disease. Anti-TNF agents — infliximab (IV), adalimumab, certolizumab, golimumab — are highly effective and carry a boxed warning for serious infection and malignancy. Before the first dose, every patient is screened for latent tuberculosis (PPD or interferon-gamma release assay) and hepatitis B, because TNF blockade reactivates both. Live vaccines are contraindicated during therapy; get inactivated vaccines up to date beforehand. Newer agents are more targeted: vedolizumab blocks gut-specific integrin trafficking (so its infection risk is largely confined to the gut), ustekinumab hits IL-12/23, and risankizumab and mirikizumab hit IL-23. Oral JAK inhibitors — tofacitinib, upadacitinib — work quickly but carry a broad boxed warning for serious infection, malignancy, thrombosis, major cardiovascular events, and death.

ClassPrototypeKey effectNursing alert
AminosalicylateMesalamine, sulfasalazineTopical gut anti-inflammatory; mild-to-moderate UCSulfasalazine: sulfa allergy, orange-yellow staining of urine and contact lenses, give folic acid. Mesalamine: monitor renal function.
CorticosteroidPrednisone, budesonideInduces remission in a flare — never for maintenanceGlucose, blood pressure, mood, infection, bone loss. Taper; budesonide has fewer systemic effects.
ImmunomodulatorAzathioprine, 6-mercaptopurineSteroid-sparing maintenanceCheck TPMT/NUDT15 before the first dose. Monitor CBC and LFTs; pancreatitis and lymphoma risk.
Anti-TNF biologicInfliximab, adalimumabInduces and maintains remission; heals fistulasScreen for latent TB and hepatitis B first. Boxed warning: serious infection, malignancy. No live vaccines. Monitor infusion reactions.
Anti-integrinVedolizumabGut-selective blockade of lymphocyte traffickingFavored when systemic infection risk is a concern; onset is slower than anti-TNF.
JAK inhibitorTofacitinib, upadacitinibOral, rapid onset for ulcerative colitisBoxed warning: infection, malignancy, thrombosis, MACE, death. Check lipids, CBC, and clot risk.

The rest of the GI cabinet

Prokinetics speed a stomach that will not empty. Metoclopramide is the mainstay for diabetic gastroparesis — the same tardive dyskinesia warning applies, and the 12-week ceiling is not a suggestion. Erythromycin is borrowed from the antibiotic shelf because it agonizes motilin receptors, though tolerance develops within weeks.

Antispasmodics — dicyclomine, hyoscyamine — are anticholinergics that relax intestinal smooth muscle in irritable bowel syndrome. Their side effects are pure anticholinergic burden: dry mouth, blurred vision, urinary retention, constipation, and confusion in older adults. Other IBS agents include rifaximin, a non-absorbed antibiotic; eluxadoline, contraindicated after cholecystectomy or in heavy alcohol use because of pancreatitis risk; and alosetron, restricted to women with severe diarrhea-predominant IBS under a REMS program because it causes ischemic colitis.

Pancrelipase replaces the enzymes a fibrotic or resected pancreas no longer makes. The teaching is specific: take it with every meal and every snack, swallow the capsule whole, and if it must be opened, sprinkle the beads on a small amount of acidic soft food such as applesauce — alkaline foods dissolve the enteric coating and destroy the enzymes. Do not let the beads sit in the mouth (they irritate mucosa), and do not exceed the prescribed dose, since very high doses have been linked to fibrosing colonopathy. Ursodiol dissolves cholesterol gallstones and improves cholestatic liver disease. Orlistat, a lipase inhibitor used for weight management, causes oily stools and flatus with discharge and blocks absorption of vitamins A, D, E, and K — a supplement taken at a different time of day is standard.

Before the first dose of a biologic, ask two questions: has this patient been screened for TB, and for hepatitis B? Blocking TNF takes the immune brakes off a latent infection. Catching a missing IGRA result is a small act of chart-reading that prevents disseminated tuberculosis.
Interactive Tap through IBD agents (5-ASAs, steroids, immunomodulators, biologics) and other GI drugs (laxatives, antidiarrheals) to light up where each acts along the gut and see its key nursing focus. Open full ↑
Key points
  • IBD therapy induces remission with steroids and maintains it with 5-ASAs, immunomodulators, or biologics — steroids never maintain.
  • Sulfasalazine: sulfa allergy, orange-yellow staining, folic acid supplementation. Mesalamine: monitor kidneys.
  • TPMT/NUDT15 before azathioprine; latent TB and hepatitis B screening before any anti-TNF biologic; no live vaccines on biologics.
  • Pancrelipase goes with every meal and snack, capsule whole or sprinkled on acidic food only.

Section 5GI nursing care

Timing is the drug

More than any other body system, GI pharmacology is governed by the clock and by food. The same tablet given at the wrong hour is a different drug. Learn this table once and you will carry it for your whole career.

DrugWhenWhy
Proton pump inhibitor30–60 min before the first mealIt is a prodrug — it only activates in pumps that are actively secreting.
H2 blockerWith or after meals; at bedtime for nocturnal acidBlunts the postprandial and overnight acid rise.
Antacid1–2 h after meals; separate from all other drugs by 1–2 hFood buffers acid first; the antacid binds and blocks other drugs.
Sucralfate1 h before meals and at bedtime, empty stomach; other drugs 2 h apartIt needs acid to polymerize and it binds anything else present.
PancrelipaseWith every meal and every snackThe enzymes must be present when the food is.
Bulk laxativeAny time, with a full glass of waterWithout fluid it swells into an obstruction.
Scopolamine patch4 h before travel, behind the ear, change q72hSlow transdermal onset; wash hands afterward to avoid pupil dilation.
Chemotherapy antiemeticsBefore the chemotherapy, on a schedulePrevention is far more effective than rescue.

Alongside timing sits formulation. Many GI drugs are delayed-release or enteric-coated — PPIs, bisacodyl, mesalamine, pancrelipase — and crushing them destroys them or dumps the entire dose at once. When a patient cannot swallow, do not improvise: check a do-not-crush reference, and ask the pharmacist for an alternative formulation. Some products have specific, approved workarounds (certain PPI capsules may be opened onto applesauce; certain PPI granules may be given through a tube in a specific diluent), but those are product-specific instructions, not general permission.

What to teach

Most GI drugs treat symptoms that lifestyle also treats, and the patient who changes both does far better than the patient who changes neither. For reflux: smaller meals, nothing to eat within three hours of lying down, the head of the bed raised 6–8 inches on blocks (extra pillows bend the abdomen and make reflux worse), weight loss, and honest conversation about tobacco, alcohol, caffeine, chocolate, peppermint, and late-night eating. For ulcers: no NSAIDs without a protective strategy, and if H. pylori was found, finish every dose. For constipation: fiber, fluid, movement, and a routine — the gastrocolic reflex is real, and sitting on the toilet 20 minutes after breakfast works better than most drugs. For anyone on a chronic laxative, name the goal out loud: a soft, comfortable stool, not a daily one. The belief that a bowel movement every day is mandatory drives an enormous amount of unnecessary laxative use.

Then teach the red flags, in plain language, and make sure the patient can repeat them back: black tarry stool or coffee-ground vomit (bleeding), bright red blood, trouble swallowing, unintended weight loss, vomiting that will not stop, severe or worsening abdominal pain, or new dyspepsia after age 60. Those symptoms mean investigation, not another antacid. Over-the-counter availability makes GI drugs feel trivial; part of your job is making clear that self-treating heartburn for months can mask something that needed a scope.

What to monitor

Watch the labs the drug touches. On chronic PPIs: magnesium (hypomagnesemia presents as tremor, tetany, arrhythmia, and refractory hypokalemia), vitamin B12, iron, and creatinine. On vomiting or diarrhea of any cause: potassium, chloride, bicarbonate, creatinine, intake and output, daily weight, and orthostatic vital signs — daily weight is the most honest fluid-balance number you have. On ondansetron, especially IV or combined with other QT-prolonging drugs: ECG, potassium, and magnesium. On sulfasalazine or azathioprine: CBC and liver enzymes. On methotrexate: LFTs and CBC. Before any anti-TNF: TB and hepatitis B screening. And on any patient at all: bowel sounds, abdominal distention, last bowel movement, and stool character — documented as a number and a description, not as the word "normal."

Finally, hold the safety alerts close, because these are the ones that hurt people. Promethazine IV causes gangrene on extravasation — dilute, run slowly, large vein, stop at the first report of burning; it is contraindicated under age 2. Metoclopramide causes tardive dyskinesia — screen for abnormal movements and respect the 12-week limit. Loperamide in high doses causes torsades. Antimotility drugs plus C. difficile equals toxic megacolon. Misoprostol plus pregnancy equals miscarriage. And any antiemetic or antihistamine with sedating properties is a fall risk in an older adult — put the bed low, the call light close, and the patient's expectations straight before you give it.

Assess before you medicate, every time. Nausea can be a bowel obstruction, a heart attack, or rising intracranial pressure. Constipation can be an obstruction. Diarrhea can be C. difficile. GI complaints are so common that the reflex is to reach for the PRN — but the ninety seconds you spend listening to the abdomen and asking one more question is the part of this chapter that saves a life.
Interactive Tap each GI drug class to see when a nurse gives it, what to teach the patient, and what to monitor — from PPIs before breakfast to loperamide after each loose stool. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Timing is the therapy: PPI before breakfast, sucralfate on an empty stomach, pancrelipase with food, antiemetics before chemo, antacids separated from everything.
  • Do not crush delayed-release or enteric-coated GI drugs; ask the pharmacist for an alternative formulation.
  • Teach red flags — melena, coffee-ground emesis, dysphagia, weight loss, new dyspepsia after 60 — and the lifestyle measures that work alongside the drug.
  • Monitor Mg2+ and B12 on chronic PPIs, K+ and volume with vomiting or diarrhea, ECG with IV ondansetron, and TB/HBV before any biologic.

The chapter in one breath

  • PPIs irreversibly block the parietal cell proton pump and must be given 30–60 minutes before the first meal; chronic use costs magnesium, B12, and infection resistance, and pantoprazole is the safe partner for clopidogrel.
  • H2 blockers are faster and weaker with tolerance and renal dosing; antacids neutralize instantly, bind other drugs, and choose your side effect by cation; sucralfate is a bandage that needs acid and an empty stomach.
  • Bismuth quadruple therapy is first-line for H. pylori, and adherence teaching — including the alcohol–metronidazole reaction — is what makes it work.
  • Vomiting has four inputs: CTZ, vagal gut afferents, vestibular system, and cortex — match the antiemetic to the road, and give it before the trigger, not after.
  • Metoclopramide (tardive dyskinesia), promethazine (extravasation gangrene), ondansetron (QT), and high-dose loperamide (torsades) are the GI drugs most likely to cause real harm.
  • Bulk laxatives need a full glass of water, PEG is first-line for chronic constipation, lactulose is titrated to 2–3 soft stools daily in hepatic encephalopathy, and no laxative goes into an undiagnosed abdomen.
  • Antimotility drugs are contraindicated in bloody or febrile diarrhea and in C. difficile — toxic megacolon is the price of stopping a protective reflex.
  • IBD care means steroids to induce, something else to maintain, TPMT/NUDT15 before azathioprine, and TB and hepatitis B screening before any anti-TNF biologic.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Aminosalicylate (5-ASA)
A topical gut anti-inflammatory such as mesalamine or sulfasalazine, used mainly for mild-to-moderate ulcerative colitis.
Antacid
A base that chemically neutralizes acid already in the stomach; fast onset, short duration, and it binds other oral drugs.
Antiemetic
Any drug that prevents or relieves nausea and vomiting by blocking one of the four inputs to the vomiting center.
Bulk-forming laxative
Fiber or a water-holding polymer (psyllium, methylcellulose, calcium polycarbophil) that absorbs water and stretches the bowel; among the safest classes, but it must be taken with a full glass of water.
Chemoreceptor trigger zone (CTZ)
A region in the area postrema of the medulla that sits outside the blood–brain barrier and senses emetic chemicals in the blood; rich in D2, 5-HT3, and NK1 receptors.
Delayed-release (enteric-coated)
A formulation with a coating that dissolves past the stomach; crushing it destroys the drug or dumps the whole dose at once.
Dyspepsia
Upper abdominal discomfort, fullness, or burning; new-onset dyspepsia after age 60 is an alarm symptom, not a candidate for self-treatment.
Gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD)
Symptomatic backflow of gastric contents into the esophagus; treated with acid suppression plus positional and dietary measures.
H2-receptor antagonist
A drug such as famotidine that blocks histamine at the parietal cell; faster but weaker than a PPI, with tolerance and renal dose adjustment.
Hepatic encephalopathy
Confusion caused by ammonia accumulating in liver failure; treated with lactulose titrated to 2–3 soft stools per day, often with rifaximin.
Inflammatory bowel disease (IBD)
Chronic immune-mediated bowel inflammation — ulcerative colitis (continuous, colonic, superficial) or Crohn disease (patchy, transmural, mouth to anus).
Melena
Black, tarry stool from digested blood, signaling upper GI bleeding; an urgent finding, never a medication side effect to shrug off (except harmless bismuth or iron darkening).
Osmotic laxative
An agent such as polyethylene glycol or lactulose that pulls water into the bowel lumen to soften stool.
PAMORA
Peripherally acting mu-opioid receptor antagonist (methylnaltrexone, naloxegol) that relieves opioid-induced constipation without reversing analgesia.
Parietal cell
The gastric cell that secretes hydrochloric acid through the H+/K+-ATPase, and intrinsic factor for B12 absorption.
Peptic ulcer disease (PUD)
A break in the gastric or duodenal mucosa, caused most often by H. pylori or NSAIDs rather than by excess acid alone.
Prokinetic
A drug such as metoclopramide that accelerates gastric emptying and gut transit.
Proton pump inhibitor (PPI)
A prodrug that irreversibly inhibits the parietal cell H+/K+-ATPase; the most potent acid suppressor, given before the first meal of the day.
Quadruple therapy
The preferred H. pylori regimen — a PPI plus bismuth, tetracycline, and metronidazole for 10–14 days.
Rebound acid hypersecretion
A surge of acid production after abrupt discontinuation of a long-term PPI; the reason to taper rather than stop.
Stimulant laxative
An agent such as senna or bisacodyl that irritates enteric nerves to trigger peristalsis; effective in 6–12 hours orally, with cramping.
Sucralfate
A drug that polymerizes in acid to form a protective paste over an ulcer; requires an empty stomach and separation from all other oral drugs.

Check yourself

1. A patient reports that their omeprazole "never helped." On questioning, they take it every night with a bedtime snack. What is wrong, and what will you teach?
The timing is wrong — a PPI must be taken 30–60 minutes before the first meal of the day. PPIs are prodrugs that only activate inside actively secreting proton pumps. Taken at bedtime after eating, most pumps are quiet and the dose is largely wasted. Move it to before breakfast.
2. A patient on clopidogrel after a coronary stent needs a PPI for reflux. Which PPI would you expect, and why does it matter?
Pantoprazole. Omeprazole and esomeprazole inhibit CYP2C19, the enzyme that converts clopidogrel from a prodrug into its active antiplatelet form. Blunting that conversion risks stent thrombosis. Pantoprazole has minimal CYP2C19 effect.
3. Name four risks of long-term PPI therapy and one consequence of stopping it abruptly.
Any four: hypomagnesemia, vitamin B12 deficiency, iron malabsorption, C. difficile infection, community-acquired pneumonia, acute interstitial nephritis, and fractures of the hip, wrist, or spine. Stopping abruptly causes rebound acid hypersecretion, which mimics relapse and traps patients on the drug — so taper.
4. A patient is on a bismuth quadruple regimen for H. pylori. What three things must your teaching cover?
Complete every dose for the full 10–14 days even after symptoms resolve; avoid all alcohol because metronidazole causes a disulfiram-like reaction (flushing, vomiting, palpitations); and expect harmless black stool and black tongue from the bismuth. Also: a confirmatory breath or stool antigen test at least 4 weeks after the antibiotics finish, with the PPI held 1–2 weeks beforehand so the result is not falsely negative.
5. Why must sucralfate be given on an empty stomach, and what does that mean for the patient's other medications?
Sucralfate needs gastric acid to polymerize into the sticky paste that coats the ulcer, and food buffers that acid. Give it 1 hour before meals and at bedtime. Because it binds other drugs, separate all other oral medications by about 2 hours.
6. A pregnant patient with rheumatoid arthritis on chronic NSAIDs is prescribed misoprostol for ulcer prevention. What is your action?
Hold the drug and contact the prescriber — misoprostol is contraindicated in pregnancy. As a prostaglandin E1 analog it stimulates uterine contractions and can cause miscarriage or preterm labor. Confirm pregnancy status and contraception before any patient of childbearing potential starts it.
7. Name the four inputs to the vomiting center and one drug class that blocks each.
CTZ (D2 antagonists such as prochlorperazine, or NK1 antagonists such as aprepitant); vagal gut afferents (5-HT3 antagonists such as ondansetron); vestibular apparatus (antihistamines such as meclizine, or anticholinergics such as scopolamine); cerebral cortex (benzodiazepines such as lorazepam for anticipatory nausea). This is why ondansetron does nothing for motion sickness — wrong road.
8. Why is IV promethazine treated as a high-alert drug, and how do you give it safely?
It carries a boxed warning for severe tissue injury, including gangrene requiring amputation, if it extravasates or enters an artery. Dilute it well, infuse slowly into a large, patent, free-flowing vein (never a small hand or wrist vein), stop immediately if the patient reports burning or pain, and prefer deep IM into a large muscle. It is also contraindicated under age 2 because of fatal respiratory depression.
9. A patient started on psyllium calls to report chest tightness and difficulty swallowing. What likely happened?
The bulk-forming laxative was taken with too little fluid and swelled into an esophageal obstruction. Bulk laxatives absorb water to work; without a full glass of water with each dose they can obstruct the esophagus or bowel. Never give them to a patient who cannot swallow reliably or take adequate fluid.
10. A patient with cirrhosis is on lactulose and had no bowel movement for 24 hours. Why does that matter, and what is the therapeutic goal?
In hepatic encephalopathy, lactulose acidifies the colon so ammonia is trapped as ammonium and excreted in stool — no stool means no ammonia clearance and rising confusion. The dose is titrated to 2–3 soft stools per day. Report the lack of output rather than simply holding the next dose.
11. A patient on antibiotics develops new watery diarrhea and a fever of 101.8°F. They ask for loperamide. What do you do, and why?
Do not give it — antimotility drugs are contraindicated in febrile, bloody, or suspected C. difficile diarrhea because slowing the gut keeps toxin against the mucosa and can precipitate toxic megacolon. Instead: notify the provider, send a C. difficile test, place the patient on contact precautions, and use soap and water for hand hygiene (alcohol does not kill spores).
12. What two screening tests must be completed before a patient's first dose of infliximab, and why?
Latent tuberculosis screening (PPD or interferon-gamma release assay) and hepatitis B screening. TNF blockade removes the immune control that keeps both infections dormant, allowing reactivation and dissemination. Live vaccines are also contraindicated during therapy, so inactivated vaccines should be brought up to date beforehand.
Pharmacology · Chapter 14

Reproductive System Drugs

Almost none of these drugs cure a disease. They prevent a pregnancy, permit one, soften a menopause, restart a stalled labor, shrink a prostate, or restore an erection — and every one of them touches something a patient may find intensely private. That makes reproductive pharmacology unusual: the science is demanding and the conversation is delicate, and you have to be good at both at the same time. This chapter walks through the hormones and the drugs that mimic, block, or trick them, and then does what matters most — it names exactly what the nurse watches, teaches, and refuses to let slide. Two of the drugs in this chapter are on the high-alert list, one of them kills people who were given nitroglycerin in a chest-pain workup, and one is dangerous to a pregnant person who merely picks up a broken tablet.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain how estrogen and progestin prevent ovulation, and compare combined with progestin-only contraception.
  • Identify the patients in whom estrogen-containing contraception is contraindicated because of clot risk, and teach the ACHES warning signs.
  • Describe the role of hormone replacement therapy, why a uterus requires added progestin, and what the boxed warnings say.
  • Explain how clomiphene induces ovulation and which adverse effect means stop the drug and call.
  • Titrate and monitor oxytocin safely, recognize tachysystole, and state the emergency response.
  • Compare tocolytics and describe magnesium sulfate monitoring and its antidote.
  • Teach testosterone safety (including transfer risk) and explain why PDE-5 inhibitors plus nitrates can be fatal.
  • Contrast alpha-1 blockers and 5-alpha reductase inhibitors in BPH, including onset, PSA effects, and pregnancy handling precautions.

Section 1Female hormones and contraception

The two hormones behind almost everything in this section

Estrogen (the body's main one is estradiol) builds: it thickens the endometrium in the first half of the cycle, maintains bone density, keeps vaginal tissue plump and lubricated, and shapes the lipid panel favorably. Progesterone stabilizes: after ovulation it converts that proliferating endometrium into a quiet, secretory lining ready to receive an embryo, thickens cervical mucus into a plug, and calms uterine muscle. When progesterone falls, the lining sheds — that withdrawal is a period.

Both are steroid hormones, which explains a great deal of their pharmacology. Being lipid-soluble, they diffuse straight through the cell membrane and bind receptors inside the cell that then act on DNA. Effects therefore take days to build and days to fade — nobody feels a birth control pill work. It also explains why they are given orally at all: a synthetic tweak (adding an ethinyl group to make ethinyl estradiol) protects the molecule from first-pass destruction in the liver. That same liver pass is where the trouble starts, because the liver responds to oral estrogen by making more clotting factors.

How contraception actually works

A combined hormonal contraceptive contains a synthetic estrogen (almost always ethinyl estradiol) plus a progestin (norethindrone, levonorgestrel, norgestimate, drospirenone, and others). Steady hormone levels feed back on the hypothalamus and pituitary and flatten the mid-cycle LH surge. No surge, no ovulation. The progestin adds two backup layers: it thickens cervical mucus so sperm cannot swim through, and it thins the endometrium so implantation is unlikely. The estrogen's other job is unglamorous but important — it stabilizes the lining so the patient does not spot constantly.

A progestin-only method drops the estrogen entirely. The classic norethindrone "mini-pill" relies mostly on mucus and endometrial effects and suppresses ovulation only inconsistently, which is why it has an unforgiving three-hour window — more than three hours late is a missed dose, take it and use backup for 48 hours. A newer drospirenone-only pill has a friendlier 24-hour window. The injection, implant, and hormonal IUD are progestin-only too, and because the patient cannot forget them daily, they are far more effective in real use.

MethodPrototypeKey effectNursing alert

Transdermal patchNorelgestromin + ethinyl estradiolWeekly patch ×3, then a patch-free weekHigher estrogen exposure than pills — greater VTE risk; less effective at higher body weight; rotate sites, never the breast

Clot risk, and the conversations that flow from it

This is the part of the chapter that saves lives. Oral estrogen passes the liver and pushes it to make more procoagulant factors while lowering some natural anticoagulants. The net result is roughly a three- to fourfold rise in venous thromboembolism risk — still a small absolute number in a healthy young person (and lower than the clot risk of pregnancy itself, which is worth saying out loud to a frightened patient), but not small at all once another risk factor stacks on top.

Estrogen-containing methods are contraindicated in a patient who smokes and is 35 or older, who has migraine with aura at any age, who has had a VTE or has a known thrombophilia, who has uncontrolled hypertension, who is less than 21 days postpartum, who has active breast cancer, who has lupus with antiphospholipid antibodies, or who has vascular complications of diabetes. Notice how many of these are things you learn only by asking. "Do your headaches come with flashing lights, zigzag lines, or a blind spot before the pain starts?" is a question that changes a prescription. For nearly all of these patients a progestin-only method is the answer, because it does not carry the estrogen clot risk. The one exception worth memorizing: current breast cancer contraindicates progestin-only methods too — the tumor may be hormone-responsive, so that patient needs a nonhormonal method (copper IUD, barrier, or sterilization), not a switch to the mini-pill.

Teach the ACHES warning signs and make the patient repeat them back: Abdominal pain (severe), Chest pain or shortness of breath, Headache (severe, sudden, or new with neuro symptoms), Eye problems (vision loss, blurring), Severe leg pain or swelling, especially one calf. Any of these means stop the pill and be seen today.

One more teaching point that gets mangled constantly: most antibiotics do not reduce contraceptive efficacy. The real offenders are strong CYP3A4 inducers — rifampin and rifabutin, carbamazepine, phenytoin, phenobarbital, primidone, higher-dose topiramate, some HIV protease inhibitors, and St. John's wort. They speed hormone metabolism and can drop levels below the contraceptive threshold. Ask about herbals by name; patients rarely volunteer them.

Ask the aura question. Migraine with aura plus estrogen roughly doubles an already-elevated ischemic stroke risk, and the only way to find it is to ask specifically about visual symptoms before the headache. A patient who says "I just get bad migraines" has told you nothing yet. Ninety seconds of history here prevents a stroke in a twenty-six-year-old.

Hormone therapy at menopause

When the ovaries retire, estrogen falls and some people are miserable: hot flashes, night sweats, sleep destruction, vaginal dryness and painful sex (now called genitourinary syndrome of menopause), and accelerating bone loss. Menopausal hormone therapy is the most effective treatment for vasomotor symptoms that exists, and it is also the drug class the Women's Health Initiative made everyone afraid of. The honest modern position sits between the extremes: for a symptomatic patient under 60 or within 10 years of menopause with no contraindications, benefits generally outweigh risks; started later, the arithmetic flips.

The single rule you must never forget: a patient with a uterus needs a progestin. Unopposed estrogen drives the endometrium to proliferate without ever shedding, and that is a straight road to endometrial hyperplasia and cancer. If the uterus has been removed, estrogen alone is appropriate and actually carries a better risk profile. Systemic estrogen carries boxed warnings for endometrial cancer, cardiovascular events including stroke and VTE, probable dementia when started in older women, and (with added progestin) breast cancer. Give the lowest effective dose for the shortest reasonable time, and revisit the decision annually.

For a patient whose only complaint is vaginal dryness or dyspareunia, low-dose vaginal estrogen (cream, tablet, or ring) is a different animal: systemic absorption is minimal, it does not carry the same warnings in practice, and it usually does not require a progestin. Teach that it takes several weeks and that stopping means the symptoms return.

Interactive Drag through a 28-day cycle and switch between the natural cycle, the combined pill, and the progestin-only pill to see how contraceptives silence the LH surge, thin the lining, and thicken cervical mucus — and where the estrogen clot risk comes from. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Combined contraceptives work by flattening the LH surge (no ovulation), plus thick mucus and a thin endometrium.
  • Estrogen raises clot risk — contraindicated with smoking at 35+, migraine with aura, VTE history, uncontrolled hypertension, early postpartum; use a progestin-only method instead.
  • Teach ACHES; teach that CYP3A4 inducers (rifampin, several anticonvulsants, St. John's wort) — not ordinary antibiotics — can cause failure.
  • In hormone therapy, a uterus means a progestin; DMPA's boxed warning is bone loss.

Section 2Fertility and pregnancy drugs

Making ovulation happen: clomiphene and its relatives

Clomiphene citrate is a lovely piece of pharmacologic trickery. It is a selective estrogen receptor modulator that blocks estrogen receptors in the hypothalamus. The hypothalamus, deprived of the estrogen signal it uses to know that estrogen exists, concludes that levels are low and increases GnRH pulses. The pituitary answers with more FSH and LH, follicles grow, and ovulation follows. It is usually taken for five days early in the cycle (commonly days 5 through 9), with ovulation expected roughly 5 to 10 days after the last tablet — timing the patient needs, because the drug does nothing if nobody knows when to try.

The adverse effects follow logically from "temporary chemical estrogen deficiency plus stimulated ovaries": hot flashes, mood swings, breast tenderness, abdominal bloating, and ovarian enlargement. Two matter more. Visual disturbances — blurring, floaters, light flashes, afterimages — mean stop the drug and report immediately; they are usually reversible but not always. And clomiphene raises the chance of multiple gestation to roughly 5 to 10 percent (mostly twins), which is a real conversation, not a footnote. Letrozole, an aromatase inhibitor borrowed from oncology, has quietly become first-line for ovulation induction in polycystic ovary syndrome — higher live-birth rates and fewer multiples than clomiphene.

When those fail, injectable gonadotropins (menotropins, follitropin alfa) stimulate the ovary directly and an hCG trigger finishes maturation and releases the egg. Their signature danger is ovarian hyperstimulation syndrome: ovaries swell, capillaries leak, and fluid pours into the abdomen and chest. Teach the patient to call for rapid weight gain (more than about 2 pounds a day), abdominal distension, severe pain, nausea and vomiting, decreased urine output, or shortness of breath. Severe OHSS is a hospitalization with a genuine thrombosis risk, not a bad week.

Oxytocin: the drug that demands a nurse

Oxytocin binds receptors on uterine smooth muscle and makes it contract. Receptor density climbs steeply toward term, which is why the same dose that does nothing at 20 weeks can hyperstimulate at 40. It is a high-alert medication — the kind where an error is more likely to cause serious harm — and it earns that label honestly, because there is no antidote and no way to un-ring the bell of a contraction that has already compressed the cord.

Give it as a dilute secondary (piggyback) infusion into a mainline IV, always on a programmable pump, connected at the port closest to the patient so it can be shut off instantly with minimal drug still in the line. Doses are ordered in milliunits per minute and titrated up slowly against contraction response. Postpartum, the goal flips: a much larger dose runs to clamp a boggy uterus down and stop atony-driven hemorrhage.

What you watch: contraction frequency, duration, intensity, and resting tone, plus the fetal heart rate pattern. Tachysystole means more than five contractions in 10 minutes averaged over 30 minutes — and if it comes with a nonreassuring FHR pattern, act. The sequence to have memorized: stop the oxytocin, reposition to left lateral, give an IV fluid bolus, give oxygen if indicated, notify the provider, and anticipate a tocolytic such as terbutaline to relax the uterus. Oxytocin also resembles ADH structurally, so prolonged high-dose infusions with hypotonic fluid can cause water intoxication — watch intake and output, headache, confusion, and falling sodium.
